[Page v]
Preface 
It has been another eight years since the second edition of this book was published, 
and 16 years since the ﬁrst edition was published in 2008. I was told by Springer that 
by end of April 2023 nearly 800 copies of the books have been sold, plus 41,000 
downloads from Springer Link by worldwide educational and academic users, which 
is not bad considering that nanofabrication is a relatively narrow ﬁeld of engineering 
discipline. Last May, the editor of Springer invited me to write the third edition. I 
thought it is the time to update the book, as a lot has happened in nanofabrication 
technology since the second edition was published. 
The most signiﬁcant development in the last eight years was the revival of 
Moore’s law which has been a law of predicting the trend of integrated circuit 
(IC) for more than 60 years. I remembered that an article published in Nature in 
February 2016 with the heading of “The chips are down for Moore’s law.” While it is 
natural to think that the features dimension of an IC cannot go down forever, which 
was then predicted that the Moore’s law would come to an end by early 2020s, the IC 
industry came up with a new way to interpret the Moore’s law since then. The 
current “3 nm generation of ICs” (Moore’s law) actually need only to have 12 nm 
half-pitch minimum features to be made (Sect. 11.2 of Chap. 11), which is perfectly 
possible thanks to the advances in nanofabrication happened in the last 8 years, 
namely the high-NA EUV lithography (Chap. 2). Now it looks like the Moore’s law 
will go on viable at least for the next 15 years! 
Although IC manufacturing represents the apex of nanofabrication, there is only a 
small group of people who actually get involved in this ﬁeld because only a handful 
companies around the world can afford to conduct such hugely expensive business. 
Most readers of this book may work in diverse ﬁelds other than IC manufacturing. 
Same as the last edition, the new edition maintains the wide coverage of 
nanofabrication. Most techniques one may encounter in work or in scientiﬁc litera-
tures are included, from various top-down lithography and pattern transfer to variety 
of bottom-up self-assembly approaches. There are also chapters devoted to focused 
ion beam, nanoimprinting, and scanning probe fabrications. A content unique only 
to this book is about indirect nanofabrication, which introduces many non-traditional
v

[Page vi]
March, 2024 
techniques often called “tricks.” As the book title implies, it is about the principles, 
capabilities, and limits of these nanofabrication techniques which should be helpful 
and informative to a wide spectrum of nanofabrication practitioners and novices. 
vi
Preface
In the last eight years, my own career had a major change again. I have retired 
since 2020 from my post at the Suzhou Institute of Nanotech and Nanobionics, 
Chinese Academy of Sciences. Although I have not completely cutoff connections 
with the Institute, now working as an advisor, one good thing is that I have plenty 
time to work on the third edition. With the time in hand, I thoroughly read through 
past and present literatures and carefully reviewed all the chapters in the second 
edition. The third edition not only has included the most updated information and 
new knowledges but also has the angles of some views readjusted. Meanwhile, I am 
now more involved in industry by participating some startup companies, which 
enables me to have a new perspective on the nanofabrication technologies I am 
familiar with. All these have helped me to reﬁne the third edition. 
Finally, my sincere gratitude goes to the Institute I was working with and all 
funding bodies which gave me the opportunities, resources, and the platform in the 
past and present. I am also indebted to my wife, Ling, who put up with me for the 
time I buried myself working on the book. 
Suzhou, China
Zheng Cui

[Page 1]
Chapter 1 
Introduction 
1.1 
What Is Nanofabrication? 
Since mankind invented tools, fabrication has been the major production activity 
throughout human history. The so-called fabrication is to modify raw materials into 
desired shapes and structures with various tools to acquire certain functionalities. 
Fabrication is traditionally associated with mechanical processing, i.e., cutting, 
milling, or molding a work piece into a work part. Many machined work parts are 
assembled into a complex working system. Some of the conventional mechanical 
processing can be of nanoscale precision. For example, some of the high precision 
machining, such as grinding and polishing, can achieve accuracy of a few nanome-
ters. However, these fabrications cannot be called nanofabrication. The fundamental 
difference between conventional mechanical processing and the nanofabrication 
described in this book is the dimension of structures or parts to be fabricated. 
Nanofabrication refers to the fabricated structures and parts themselves are in the 
dimension of below 100 nanometers. No conventional machine tools can achieve 
such feats. It is only possible by a fabrication process called planar processing. Jack 
Kilby, the inventor of integrated circuits and Nobel laureate for his invention, 
conceived the planar processing method more than 60 years ago, which laid the 
foundation of modern integrated circuit (IC) manufacturing processes and the 
associated microfabrication and nanofabrication technologies. 
Comparing to conventional mechanical processing, planar processing is a 
completely different way to make individual parts. There are three distinctive 
characteristics in the planar processing: 
1. Individual parts or structures are formed by planar patterning, instead of conven-
tional direct interaction between a machine tool and raw materials. The planar 
patterning process is schematically illustrated in Fig. 1.1, which is essentially of 
two stages: patterning and pattern transfer. A pattern is formed by lithography. In 
this case it is optical lithography similar to photography whereby the negative is a 
layer of energy-sensitive polymer called resist coated on a planar supporting 
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_1
1

[Page 2]
2
1
Introduction
Fig. 1.1 Schematic of planar fabrication process (patterning + pattern transfer) 
substrate. The resist layer is exposed and developed, and the developed resist 
relief pattern is then transferred onto the planar substrate by either etching or 
additional ﬁlm deposition. By this way of fabrication, the dimension of a structure 
is no longer limited by the capability of a machine tool, but by the resolution of a 
lithography system. 
2. Planar process can only make 2D planar structures, not a true 3D object. It at best 
can make 2.5D structures by superimposition of a series of 2D structures in 
vertical direction. In contrast to conventional mechanical processing in which 
individual parts are machined ﬁrst and a complex system is assembled afterward, 
planar processing makes a complex system not by assembling individually 
fabricated parts but by the planar fabrication process itself. The assembly of 
individual components is realized at different stages of planar processing. As an

[Page 3]
1.2
Classiﬁcation of Nanofabrication
3
Fig. 1.2 An example of 
mechanical system 
fabricated by planar process. 
(Courtesy Sandia National 
Laboratories, SUMMiT™ 
Technologies, www.sandia. 
gov/mstc) 
example, Fig. 1.2 shows a complete mechanical system fabricated by the Sandia 
National Laboratories of the USA, using planar fabrication processes. It looks 
like a conventional mechanical system with drive mechanism, which can push a 
planar structure out of surface plane. What is different from a conventional 
mechanical system is that the gears are less than 1 mm in diameter, which is 
beyond the capability of any current precision machining technologies. The 
individual gears and assembly of drive mechanism were formed in the course 
of layer by layer planar processing. 
3. Planar fabrication is generally a batch process. A batch of devices is made on the 
same planar substrate through many steps of processes. An 8-inch planar silicon 
wafer may contain hundreds of individual ICs, such as CPU or memory chips. An 
example of batch-processed silicon wafer is shown in Fig. 1.3. All the fabrica-
tions are done at the wafer level where the hundreds of ICs are made simulta-
neously with repeated lithography, etching, or deposition processes. Once it is 
done, individual IC, called “die,” is diced and packaged. The batch processing 
allows mass manufacturing of devices or components. 
1.2 
Classiﬁcation of Nanofabrication 
The key technology that allows the planar processing to make 2D structures beyond 
the capability of conventional mechanical machining is the lithography patterning 
technology. The minimum dimension of feature patterns formed by lithography 
determines whether a planar processing is microfabrication, i.e., feature dimensions 
below a millimeter and above 100 nanometers, or nanofabrication, i.e., feature 
dimension below 100 nanometers. Depending on how a pattern is formed, the 
lithography can be classiﬁed into three types: projection lithography, probe lithog-
raphy, and mold lithography, as schematically illustrated in Fig. 1.4.

[Page 4]
4
1
Introduction
Fig. 1.3 Batch-processed 
silicon wafer containing 
hundreds of individual ICs 
(dies) 
Fig. 1.4 Schematics of three types of lithographic patterning 
Projection lithography involves projecting a mask image onto a polymer resist 
layer coated on a planar substrate. The irradiation that comes through the mask can 
be a shining light (optical projection lithography), electrons (electron projection 
lithography), ions (ion projection lithography), and atoms (atom projection lithog-
raphy). The irradiation causes the exposure of polymer resist which is then devel-
oped to become the pattern of mask image for subsequent pattern transfer, as shown 
in Fig. 1.1. The minimum pattern features that can be exposed depend on the 
wavelength of irradiation source or the feature size on a mask. Projection lithography 
is a parallel patterning process, meaning that all the features contained in a mask are 
imaged simultaneously. High-volume manufacturing of ICs is exclusively done by 
projection lithography patterning process.

[Page 5]
1.2
Classiﬁcation of Nanofabrication
5
Probe lithography can be considered as the extension of conventional mechanical 
processing, in which various probes of micro- or nanometer sizes replace the 
conventional mechanical cutting or milling tools. The probes can be nonsolid probes 
such as a focused electron beam, an ion beam, or a laser beam. The probes can also 
be solid types, such as the probes in scanning tunneling microprobes or atomic force 
microscopes. Nonsolid probes are basically irradiation probes which expose poly-
mer resist to create patterns. Different from the aforementioned projection lithogra-
phy, exposure of polymer resist performed by an irradiation beam is in sequential 
manner. The beam probe scans sequentially to create a pattern instead of projecting 
the whole pattern at once. Therefore, probe lithography is a low-throughput pattern-
ing process. Solid probes directly manipulate atoms to create trenches or dents or 
selectively form oxidation patterns on a substrate material. Alternatively, the solid 
probes can work like a “fountain pen,” transferring liquid to a solid surface to form 
nanometer-size dots or patterns of only a monolayer of molecules. Nonsolid probes 
can also directly pattern substrate surface, such as sputtering removal of substrate 
materials by a focused ion beam or ablation removal of substrate materials by a laser 
beam, without the help of polymer resist pattern transfer. Whether the fabrication is 
microscale or nanoscale is mostly dependent on the probe size. 
Mold lithography makes patterns by replication of molds. Mold lithography 
includes nanoimprint, hot embossing, and microinjection molding. Nanoimprint 
uses master stamps to make nanopatterns by pressing the stamps into a layer of 
thermally softened or liquid polymer materials. Although both projection and probe 
fabrications can make micro- and nanostructures as aforementioned, nanoimprint 
performs the same task at very low cost, because once a stamp is made, it can be used 
repeatedly to replicate large number of same nanostructures. However, to make the 
nanoimprint stamps, the projection patterning or probe patterning process has to be 
used. Nanoimprint has many derivatives, such as thermal nanoimprint, UV-cured 
nanoimprint, soft contact print, etc. Hot embossing is based on the same principle as 
the nanoimprint but conﬁned to press a master stamp into thermoplastics only. It is 
the same technique as for making compact disks. Microinjection molding is for 
molding of duroplastics or metals. It is an extension of conventional molding process 
to micrometer range. Both hot embossing and microinjection molding are for 
replication of microscale structures and objects. Only nanoimprint is qualiﬁed as a 
nanofabrication process. 
In addition to the above mentioned planar, probe, and mold patterning technol-
ogies, all of which can in one way or another fabricate sub-100 nm structures, there 
are also self-assembly processes to build nanostructures. These two classes of 
nanofabrication techniques clearly distinguish each other by their “top-down” and 
“bottom-up” approaches. In the top-down approach, nanostructures are made by 
patterning from top. Bottom-up approach relies on self-assembly of nanoscale 
entities such as nanospheres, nanocrystals, or molecules through spontaneous attrac-
tion forces between nanoentities. Bottom-up process can potentially break the 
feature dimension limit of top-down nanofabrication processes. Self-assembly pro-
cess generally forms lattice-like structures or periodical arrays. To make useful

[Page 6]
nanostructures, self-assembly is normally guided or directed by top-down fabricated 
template structures. 
6
1
Introduction
In general, fabrication of structures larger than 100 nm is considered to be 
microfabrication. Nanofabrication is referred to fabrication of sub-100 nm struc-
tures. Most of nanofabrication technologies, except scanning probes, are developed 
from microfabrication technologies. Although there is no clear distinction between 
microfabrication and nanofabrication, from micrometer to nanometer represents not 
a simple downsizing of structure dimension, but a major advance in fabrication 
technology. For example, optical lithography used to have its resolution limited by 
the wavelength of illuminating light. At each wavelength, there is a minimum feature 
size that can be realized. However, with the advances in optical lithography, its 
resolution limit has been lowered down continuously in the last several decades. The 
theoretical limit of optical diffraction to the patterning resolution has already been 
broken. Now features of less than 10 nm can be fabricated by optical lithography. 
1.3 
Purpose of the Book 
Nanofabrication is a multidisciplinary technology domain covering a wide range of 
knowledge. Different nanofabrication processes involve different physical or chem-
ical principles. For example, reactive ion etching by plasma discharge involves both 
physical and chemical processes. Wet etching of materials by acids or alkaline 
solutions is entirely a chemical process. Although each nanofabrication technology 
has its theoretical basis in physics or chemistry, it is not the purpose of this book to 
discuss these theories. The emphasis of this book is to introduce the practical aspects 
of different nanofabrication technologies. From application point of view, a mature 
and successful processing technique may not have, or does not need, a thorough 
theoretical base. In most cases, a successful processing technique is established 
through many experimental trials and errors. It does not matter if the technique 
cannot be theoretically explainable, as long as it works successfully to produce the 
desired results. Basic theoretical knowledge in physics, chemistry, or other branches 
of science may act as guidance in developing or optimizing a new process technique. 
While the basic principle of each technology is explained in this book, most of the 
contents are devoted to practical use of each fabrication technique. It is the author’s 
belief and experience that learning nanofabrication is very much a practical and 
hands-on process. It is important to know the ﬁrst principles. The rest is very much 
down to practicing and mastering the various technologies by using them. 
Nanofabrication technologies introduced in this book are essentially about pat-
terning and pattern transfer, as illustrated in Fig. 1.1. Patterning is the most critical 
step in nanofabrication, which determines how small structures can be made. A 
nanoscale pattern can be made indirectly by exposure of a polymer layer as a 
patterned mask. The exposure can be carried out with photons (Chap. 2), electrons 
(Chap. 3), and ions (Chap. 4). The polymer patterns can also be made without 
exposure but by scanning a probe (Chap. 5) or imprinting a master template

[Page 7]
(Chap. 6). Polymer patterns can be formed even without any tools but by polymer 
molecules themselves in a process called self-assembly. The typical example is the 
self-assembly of block copolymers (Chap. 10). 
1.3
Purpose of the Book
7
In most cases, a patterned polymer layer itself is not the ﬁnal product but an 
intermediate step. A subsequent pattern transfer step is needed to complete the 
nanofabrication. Pattern transfer is essentially divided into two types, transfer by 
etching (Chap. 7) which is subtractive process and transfer by deposition (Chap. 8) 
which is an additive process, as illustrated in Fig. 1.1. Both the pattern transfer 
processes rely on the polymer pattern as a mask. The ﬁnal structure is either in the 
substrate material (etching) or a thin ﬁlm material (deposition) as the replica of 
polymer mask pattern. Some tools and techniques can make patterned structures 
directly without the aid of polymer masks, such as focused ion beam sputtering 
(Chap. 4) or deposition/removal of materials with scanning probes (Chap. 5). Some 
nanoscale structures may not be made straightforwardly with a patterning tool but 
made indirectly to bypass the limitation in resolution for an existing tool (Chap. 9). 
There are vast application domains where nanofabrication techniques are perva-
sively used. This book is not intended to cover all the applications but to select a few 
most relevant areas where nanofabrication processes described in this book play the 
key roles. The application areas covered by this book are integrated circuits, 
photonics, and nanotechnology (Chap. 11). 
Though there are a vast number of literatures and published research papers 
which give details of each nanofabrication technology, they tend to present a 
positive picture of the technology rather than pointing out what are the shortcomings 
or telling the negative side of the story. A nanofabrication practitioner knows from 
his/her own experience that there are no perfect one-suits-all fabrication technolo-
gies. Each technology has its own weakness. For example, the optical lithography, 
especially the latest extreme UV lithography, though is capable of sub-100 nm 
patterning, is too expensive and not easily accessible; electron beam lithography is 
of high resolution and high ﬂexibility but low throughput; scanning probes are 
simple and low-cost systems for sub-100 nm patterning, but the pattern area is 
small, speed is low, and patterning depth is shallow; nanoimprinting is best suitable 
for making high density periodical patterns in large quantity; soft lithography is 
limited in sub-100 nm resolution because of the softness of stamp and lateral 
diffusion of inks; molecular self-assembly has to be guided with other patterning 
techniques in order to make any useful pattern structures with reduced defect 
density. The present book tries to present a balanced view. For each technology 
introduced, both its advantages and weaknesses are described, so that the readers are 
made aware of both positive and negative aspects of a nanofabrication technology 
and aware of its limits. 
With the ever expansion of information sources on the Internet, acquiring any 
knowledge from the Internet has never been so easy, particularly with the help of the 
latest artiﬁcial intelligence (AI). Though the Internet has provided the convenience 
of ﬁnding required information at a stroke of keyboard, it still needs a trained eye to 
spot the correct and best knowledge among the vast amount of information. Since the 
book covers so wide topics regarding the nanofabrication, it is written such that the

[Page 8]
essential principles of each nanofabrication technique are explained and key knowl-
edge points are described, along with their source of references. Readers can always 
explore further for more detailed information. The book is intended to be a practical 
guide and ﬁrst-hand reference for anyone who wishes to learn about different 
nanofabrication techniques or to consult with when facing to choose a suitable 
technique from a “tool box” for a speciﬁc application.
8
1
Introduction

[Page 9]
Chapter 2 
Optical Lithography 
2.1 
Introduction 
Since the planar processing for integrated circuit (IC) manufacturing started more 
than 60 years ago, optical lithography, or often called photolithography, has become 
the convenient choice of making planar microstructures. In optical lithography, a 
mask or photomask, also called reticle, is imaged onto a ﬂat substrate surface coated 
with a thin layer of polymer material called photoresist. The photon energy is 
focused into the photoresist, causing polymer chain scission (positive tone photore-
sist) or cross-linking (negative tone photoresist). The mask pattern is then delineated 
into the photoresist after development. 
In the early days when the feature size of integrated circuits was a few microme-
ters, optical lithography was done with visible light and was no different from 
copying photos in a photographic workshop. As the circuit feature dimension 
shrank, illuminating light with shorter wavelength was employed. This was the 
time when mercury lamps were used as the illumination source, with UV wavelength 
emission at g-line (λ = 436 nm) and i-line (λ = 365 nm) being the choices of 
wavelengths for exposure of photoresists. When the circuit dimension shrank to 
sub-micrometer dimension, short illuminating wavelength became necessary 
[1]. Deep UV (DUV) at λ = 248 nm generated from the KrF excimer laser and 
λ = 193 nm generated from the ArF excimer laser became the new illumination 
sources for optical lithography. Many attempts have been tried to explore even 
shorter wavelengths, such as 157 nm and 126 nm, for optical lithography. They all 
but failed because there were simply no viable materials to make the refractive lenses 
to focus them. In the end the extreme UV (EUV) at 13 nm using mirror-based 
reﬂective optics became the workhorse for the latest IC manufacturing. 
The advances of optical lithography were not only about using short wavelength 
illumination. Many innovations in the last 20 years have kept optical lithography 
alive for making generations of IC chips, such as using high numerical apertures 
(NA), introducing immersion lithography, multiple patterning processes, and a range
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_2
9

[Page 10]
of resolution enhancement techniques (RETs). Optical lithography was able to 
achieve patterning circuit structures well below the 193 nm illumination wavelength. 
Now the same innovations are being repeated at the EUV wavelength, which enables 
the IC manufacturing to enter the sub-3 nm technology node.
10
2
Optical Lithography
Apart from the mainstream development in optical lithography for high-volume 
IC manufacturing, there is always an army of researchers who are trying to ﬁnd 
low-cost alternatives. Interferometric lithography and near-ﬁeld optical lithography 
are among the few. These techniques are trying to beat mainstream optical lithog-
raphy on the cost front but still able to deliver sub-100 nm patterning capability. 
There is also the maskless optical lithography technique, which promises to deliver 
sub-100 nm patterning without using the increasingly expensive masks. 
Optical lithography has been the subject of many published literatures including 
previous edition of the book published by the author [2]. The basic issues of optical 
lithography have been well described in these literatures. The present chapter will 
not simply repeat those basics but review the photon-based lithography techniques 
with emphasis on those issues related to producing sub-100 nm structures on a 
photon-sensitive polymer layer. While the basic principles of various optical lithog-
raphy techniques are still being mentioned, most of the technical details about the 
basic optical lithography processes have been omitted. The content has been struc-
tured around the central theme of sub-100 nm patterning, the capabilities and limits 
of current photon-based lithographic techniques, and the ways of overcoming these 
limits. 
2.2 
Principle of Optical Projection Lithography 
IC manufacturing started in the early days with contact optical lithography, it was 
soon migrated to projection optical lithography. In contact mode, an optical mask is 
in direct contact, or having a proximity gap, with a photoresist-coated substrate, and 
features in the mask are imaged onto the photoresist in 1:1 ratio without reduction. 
The contact photolithography is performed with a mask aligner and mostly for 
microscale patterning [3]. Projection photolithography can project a demagniﬁed 
image onto photoresist (5:1 or 10:1), capable of patterning much smaller features as 
well as eliminating the mask damage and contamination problems associated with 
contact photolithography. For submicron patterning, project photolithography 
prevails. 
The basic projection optical lithography system is schematically shown in 
Fig. 2.1, where the mask is illuminated by a light source (mercury lamp) through a 
condenser lens and the light passing through the transparent patterns in the mask is 
focused by a projection lens onto the photoresist layer. A mercury lamp can only 
generate g-line or i-line wavelength illumination. For deep UV (248 nm or 193 nm 
wavelength), excimer lasers are used as the light sources. Because of the limited 
beam size of an excimer laser, the mask is scanned by the laser beam (normally the

[Page 11]
size of 25 × 33 mm) and projected onto a wafer. For a DUV scanner, the 
demagniﬁcation ratio is 4:1. 
2.2
Principle of Optical Projection Lithography
11

The theory of optical imaging in projection photolithography can be very com-
plicated. A comprehensive mathematical description of the theory has been given in 
a book written by Wong [4]. However, the concept of projection photolithography 
can be understood easily. A simple conceptual view of diffraction-limited projection 
photolithography is shown in Fig. 2.2. 
A mask feature, which is a transparent opening, can be considered as a slit from 
which passing light is diffracted in the same way as Young’s single slit diffraction. 
Diffraction is a phenomenon which involves the bending of waves around obstacles. 
It is described by Huygen’s principle that every point on a wave front acts as a source 
of tiny wavelets that move forward with the same speed as the wave. These wavelets 
interfere with each other, causing light intensity varies with maxima and minima 
which are bright and dark fringes, as shown in Fig. 2.3. The angle of each fringe 
relative to the source can be determined by a simple equation (Eq. 2.1). 
sin φ = m λ 
a 
m = 1, 2, 3, . . . :
ð2:1Þ 
In Eq. 2.1, ϕ is the diffraction angle of light passing through the slit, λ is the 
wavelength of light, and a is the width of slit. It is apparent that the spread or 
expansion of light, represented by the diffraction angle ϕ, is the function of wave-
length and the width of slit. For a ﬁxed wavelength, the smaller the slit, the wider the 
spread of light, as the simulated light intensity distributions shown in Fig. 2.4a for 
three different widths of slits. The narrower the slit, the wider the distribution. On the 
other hand, light with shorter wavelength can have narrower distribution of

[Page 12]
diffraction, as shown in Fig. 2.4b where the light intensity distribution is much 
narrower for 248 nm wavelength than that for 436 nm wavelength. Referring the slit 
to a feature opening on a mask, if two of such slits are close to each other, then they 
may merge into each other, making them irresolvable. Figure 2.5a shows exactly 
such a case that two light images have merged into one because the slits where the 
lights come from are too close to each other. They can only be resolved as separate 
spots providing the two slits are well apart, as shown in Fig. 2.5b.

Light diffraction from a slit, or a mask feature, is only half of the process in a 
projection optical lithography. Referring to Fig. 2.1, the light coming out of a mask 
opening has to be focused by a projection lens onto a substrate surface. An optical 
lens made of glass or quartz is always of limited diameter. The diameter of a lens 
aperture determines how much or how wide the diffracted light from the mask can be

[Page 13]
accepted into the lens system. The lens diameter is deﬁned by the numerical aperture 
(NA) expressed as Eq. 2.2 where n is the refractive index of the medium between the 
lens and an imaging plane and θ is the half-angle of the maximum cone of light that 
can enter or exit the lens, or the angle of outmost ray of light entering or exiting the 
lens as illustrated in Fig. 2.2.

Skipping the complicated optical imaging theory, a simple equation can deﬁne 
the resolution limit of a projection lithography system, as expressed in Eq. 2.3, 
where λ is the illumination wavelength, NA is the numerical aperture of imaging 
optical system, and k1 is a factor related to a speciﬁc imaging process (k1 < 1). 
R = k1 λ 
NA
ð2:3Þ 
From the above equation, one can intuitively work out that shorter wavelength 
results in narrow distribution of light diffraction, as proved in Fig. 2.4b. Therefore, 
more lights can be focused by the lens of ﬁxed diameter (NA). The same applies to 
larger numerical aperture at a ﬁxed wavelength, i.e., more diffracted lights can be 
admitted into the lens and be focused onto a substrate if the NA of lens is bigger. 
Both can result in improved resolution in imaging, because higher-order diffractions 
(light of large diffraction angle) passing the lens result in more details of the mask 
feature to be imaged onto the substrate. The effects of wavelength and numerical 
aperture can be quantitatively evaluated by computer simulation of partially coherent 
imaging in projection optical lithography. Figure 2.6 shows the simulated light 
intensity of a mask feature of 0.5 μm linewidth at different illumination wavelengths 
(Fig. 2.6a) and different NAs (Fig. 2.6b). The simulation was performed by the 
author’s own optical lithography simulation program COMPARE [5]. 
The k1 in Eq. 2.3 is a factor that can further enhance the resolution of imaging 
process by the collective action of many techniques. Sections 2.4, 2.5, 2.6 and 2.7 
will describe how the resolution of optical lithography has been improved over the 
years by using shorter wavelengths, larger numerical apertures, and reduction of the 
k1 factor.

[Page 15]
2.3
Basics of Photoresists
15
2.3 
Basics of Photoresists 
2.3.1 
Process of Optical Lithography 
Projection of photomask described in previous section is only one step of optical 
lithography. The photomask image projected from the optical lens system has to 
become the surface relief image in photoresist. The whole photoresist imaging 
process involves 10 steps. 
1. Pre-treatment of Substrate 
The substrate surface, which in most cases is a silicon wafer, has to be 
thoroughly cleaned with chemicals to remove all the stains and particles. 
Then, it is placed in oven to bake at 150–200 °C for 15 min to half hour to 
dry completely. Another measure is to coat a thin layer of HMDS 
(hexamethyldisilazane), which can promote the adhesion between photoresist 
and silicon surface. Without a good adhesion, photoresist patterns after devel-
opment may peel off at later stage of process. 
2. Coating Photoresist 
Photoresist is coated onto silicon wafer by spin or spray coating process. In a 
spin coater, photoresist drops at the center of the wafer. By high-speed spinning, 
the photoresist drops uniformly spreading to the whole surface. The thickness of 
photoresist can be controlled by different spin speeds and viscosity of 
photoresist. 
3. Pre-exposure Bake 
After coating photoresist, the wafer should be baked. The purpose of 
pre-exposure bake (pre-bake) is to drive out all the solvent in the photoresist 
layer so that the photoresist layer becomes completely dry and solid. Pre-bake is 
also called soft bake, with temperature and baking time speciﬁed by photoresist 
vendors. 
4. Optical Exposure 
Silicon wafers coated with photoresist are placed in an optical lithography 
system for exposure. If it is not the ﬁrst layer exposure, alignment has to be 
performed to ﬁnd the alignment marks and to make sure the overlay of current 
mask with previously patterned structures on the wafer is accurate. The next step 
is to choose an appropriate exposure dose. The actual exposure can be done 
automatically by the stepper. For optical lithography by a mask aligner, a proper 
contact pressure or proximity gap should be selected before the exposure [3]. 
5. Post-exposure Bake 
Light wave can be reﬂected at the interface between photoresist layer and 
silicon surface during the exposure. Reﬂected light wave can interfere with the 
incoming light wave to form standing wave. Such a standing wave can leave its 
mark in the exposed photoresist. Post-exposure bake (post-bake) can partially 
eliminate the standing wave effect. However, post-bake may promote lateral 
diffusion of photoactive compounds in the photoresist, resulting in slight dete-
rioration of photoresist image quality. In recent years, various top and/or bottom

[Page 16]
antireﬂection coatings have been introduced into wafer process. By applying a 
layer of antireﬂection coating, the standing wave formation can be effectively 
prevented. 
16
2
Optical Lithography
6. Development 
There are three basic development methods: immersion, spray, and puddle. 
Immersion development is the simplest, which does not need any special 
equipment. The exposed wafer is immersed in a developer-ﬁlled container for 
certain time and then taken out to wash off the residual developer. Spray 
development requires spraying developer onto a spinning wafer. Washing and 
dry processes are also carried out during wafer spinning. Puddle development 
combines both the immersion and spray methods. The wafer is ﬁrst immersed in 
developer for a certain time and then will undergo spinning for further spray 
development, cleaning, and drying. Both spray and puddle developments need 
special equipment. 
7. Descum 
Development and washing cannot completely remove the photoresist. There 
is likely a very thin layer of residual photoresist, called scum, remaining on the 
silicon surface. Such scum is more apparent for deep and narrow pattern 
structure where developer is at the bottom of the pattern structure difﬁcult to 
reach, leaving more scum on silicon surface. The scum, though only a few 
nanometers, may prevent subsequent pattern transfer process, particularly in wet 
etching process. Descuming is generally required after photoresist development. 
The descuming is performed by placing the developed wafer in a plasma etcher 
for a short-time oxygen plasma etching, normally half minute, to remove the 
residual photoresist. A caution should be taken that descuming also partially 
reduces the photoresist thickness and may have detrimental effect on developed 
photoresist pattern structure. 
8. Hard Bake 
Hard bake is to enhance the adhesion of photoresist pattern structure to the 
silicon wafer by high temperature baking of the developed wafer in oven. Hard 
bake can also enhance the etch resistance of photoresist patterns. Not all 
processes require hard bake. It should be aware that hard bake could make 
photoresist patterns more difﬁcult to remove after pattern transfer. 
9. Pattern Transfer 
The purpose of optical lithography is to transfer the mask design onto silicon 
wafer. Photoresist only serves as a masking layer, i.e., protecting the substrate so 
that only those areas where photoresist has been developed and substrate surface 
has been exposed can be further processed, either by etching or by deposition. 
The pattern transfer techniques will be introduced in Chaps. 7 and 8. 
10. Removal of Photoresist 
After pattern transfer, the photoresist has fulﬁlled its purpose and needs to be 
removed. Photoresist removal can be a wet process or dry process. Wet process 
involves various acidic or alkaline or solvent to dissolve the remaining photo-
resist. The most commonly used solvent is acetone. Acetone can dissolve most 
of photoresists. Dry process is to remove the photoresist by oxygen plasma 
etching.

[Page 17]
2.3
Basics of Photoresists
17
2.3.2 
Characteristics of Photoresists 
Photoresist is the name for all photon-sensitive macromolecule polymers. The name 
of “resist” indicates its function as an etching-resistant material to protect substrate. 
They are photon sensitive; therefore, they can be optically deﬁned into pattern 
structures. Photoresists can be classiﬁed into positive tone and negative tone resists, 
according to the polarity of pattern structures after photon exposure. Figure 2.7 
illustrates the difference between positive and negative photoresists. The photo-
chemistry in a positive photoresist is such that long chain of molecules can be broken 
by photon energy into short chains through a “chain secession” process, while for 
negative photoresist initially short chains of molecules can join together to form long 
chains after absorbing photon energy. Macromolecule polymers with long chain are 
insoluble in developer, whereas polymers with short chain molecules are soluble in 
developer. Therefore, as illustrated in Fig. 2.7, exposed part is removed by developer 
for a positive photoresist, while it remains after development for a negative 
photoresist. 
However, there are exceptions that a positive tone resist can create negative tone 
images. One way is to reverse the tone of mask from dark ﬁeld (features are 
transparent on the mask) to a bright ﬁeld mask (features are opaque on the mask). 
Another is to use negative tone developer. Standard developer for a positive tone 
photoresist is alkaline based. Negative tone development (NTD) uses solvent-based 
developer. As illustrated in Fig. 2.8, the solvent developer reverses the tone of 
exposed positive resist. The reason to use NTD is that the negative tone photoresists 
for 193 nm DUV photolithography are always inferior to its positive tone counter-
parts. Using a positive tone photoresist combined with a bright ﬁeld mask and NTD 
can create better quality of exposed positive tone pattern structures, particularly at 
sub-50 nm resolution [6]. 
Many types of photoresists have been developed over the years for IC 
manufacturing. There are four basic constituents in all types of photoresist: resin, 
solvent, photoactive compound (PAC), and additives. Resin is the main part of a 
photoresist, which provides the etch-resistant property. Solvent keeps the resin in 
liquid form so that it can be spin-coated onto a substrate. The PAC determines the 
photoresist only sensitive to certain wavelength of photon irradiation. For chemi-
cally ampliﬁed resists (CARs), there is also a constituent called photoacid generator
(1) Before exposure                (2) After development 
Positive 
resist 
Negative 
resist 
Fig. 2.7 Difference in image polarity between positive and negative tone photoresists

[Page 18]
(PAG) which helps to enhance the exposure sensitivity. Additives control light 
absorbance and solubility of a photoresist. It should be noted that a photoresist 
may not just be sensitive to photons. Some photoresists are also sensitive to 
electrons, ions, or X-ray.
18
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.8 Difference between positive and negative tone development 
Fig. 2.9 Deﬁnition of sensitivity and contrast for positive (left) and negative (right) photoresists 
A photoresist is characterized by the following parameters: 
1. Sensitivity 
Sensitivity determines the exposure speed of a photoresist. The deﬁnition of 
sensitivity is illustrated by the resist development curves which are the 
remaining resist thickness versus exposure dose, shown in Fig. 2.9. It should 
be noted that the sensitivity for positive resist and negative resist is deﬁned 
differently. The higher the sensitivity of a photoresist, the less the exposure dose 
it needs, hence the faster the exposure. Exposure dose is in the unit of mJ/cm2 .

[Page 19]
The chain secession or cross-linking in a photoresist normally takes place upon 
absorbing photon energy of certain wavelength. However, these processes can 
take place after exposure and during post-exposure baking for CARs, which 
enables the CARs to have much higher sensitivity because less photons are 
needed to expose them [7]. 
2.3
Basics of Photoresists
19
2. Contrast 
Photoresists with high contrast can produce pattern structures with vertical 
sidewall and high aspect ratio (ratio of pattern height to pattern width). The 
deﬁnition of contrast for both positive and negative photoresists is deﬁned as the 
slope of development curve shown in Fig. 2.9. The steeper the slope of the 
curve, the higher the photoresist contrast. Resist contrast has a direct impact on 
optical lithography resolution. 
3. Etch Selectivity 
A high etch selectivity is desired if the photoresist is to be used as a masking 
layer in pattern transfer by plasma etching. Etch selectivity is deﬁned by the ratio 
of substrate etch rate and photoresist etch rate. If a photoresist has an etch 
selectivity of 10 with silicon, it implies for a silicon etch rate of 1 μm/min; the 
photoresist loss is 100 nm/min. Etch selectivity determines how thick resist layer 
is needed for a certain etch depth of substrate material. 
4. Resolution 
The achievable resolution of a photoresist depends on a number of factors; 
(a) the resolution of optical exposure system; (b) the molecule weight, molecule 
weight distribution, and contrast and resist thickness; and (c) development 
condition, pre- and post-bake temperature, etc. In general, thin resist layer 
makes it easy to achieve high resolution patterns. However, the etch selectivity 
has to be taken into account when deciding the resist thickness. Overdevelop-
ment for positive resist and underdevelopment for negative resist will all result 
in reduced resolution in photoresist patterns. High post-baking temperature will 
also affect the resolution of resist patterns because of its softening effect on 
developed resist pattern structures. 
5. Exposure Latitude 
If the critical dimension (CD) of exposed pattern changes less when exposure 
dose deviates from the optimum dose, we say the photoresist has larger exposure 
latitude. The exposure latitude is best illustrated in Fig. 2.10 where linewidth 
varies differently with exposure dose for resist A and resist B. The designed 
linewidth is 0.45 μm. The exposure latitude is quantitatively deﬁned as the 
exposure dose range within ±10% of CD variation. By comparison, resist A has 
larger exposure latitude than resist B because resist A allows larger variation of 
exposure dose while still maintaining CD variation within ±10%. In an ideal 
situation, the exposure dose is ﬁxed and a substrate receives uniform exposure 
everywhere. In reality, the light source of an optical lithography system may 
vary due to external factors. A photoresist with larger exposure latitude will be 
affected less when exposure energy ﬂuctuates or is not uniform across the wafer.

[Page 20]
20
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.10 Comparison of 
linewidth variation with 
exposure dose variation 
6. Process Latitude 
Variations of pre- and post-baking temperatures, development time, concen-
tration of developer, and temperature are all inﬂuential over the quality of ﬁnal 
resist patterns. Each set of process technology has its optimum conditions. When 
process conditions deviate from their optima, the performance of a photoresist 
should vary as little as possible, i.e., having a larger process latitude. A photo-
resist with large process latitude allows certain ﬂuctuation of process parameters 
during manufacturing and hence will have higher yield compared to a photore-
sist with small process latitude. 
7. Thermal Stability 
Each photoresist has a glass transition temperature (Tg), above which the 
resist is in molten state and thermal ﬂow of patterned resist structure may 
happen. The thermal ﬂow will deform the resist structure and hence deteriorate 
the pattern quality and resolution. 
8. Swelling 
Swelling can happen in some of negative photoresists during development. 
This is mainly due to the developer molecules that are embedded in the polymer 
chains, causing volume increase. Swelling can also deform the patterned resist 
structures. 
9. Viscosity 
The viscosity of liquid-form resist determines the ﬂowing ability of the resist. 
A photoresist with different viscosities will produce different thickness of 
coating layer, which is important in optical lithography of thick resists. Viscos-
ity can normally be controlled by the solid content of polymer in the resist liquid. 
The same resist can have different viscosities depending on the polymer con-
centration. For solvent-based resists, gradual evaporation of solvent after the 
bottle is opened for some time can increase the polymer concentration and, 
therefore, increase the viscosity. At the same spin coating condition, fresh resist 
and old resist may produce different coating thickness due to the change of resist 
viscosity.

[Page 21]

Active gases
Relative power
)
2.4
Optical Lithography at Shorter Wavelengths
21
10. Shelf Life 
Every photoresist has its own shelf life, because photoactive compounds may 
lose its photoactivity with time and solvent may gradually evaporate. Some 
resists can change property at or above room temperature and must be stored at 
low temperature. Photoresists are in general sensitive to ambient lights, partic-
ularly the UV constituent in the sunlight or artiﬁcial light. They have to be 
contained in a dark brown bottle and optical lithography process line has to be 
housed in an amber lighting environment. 
2.4 
Optical Lithography at Shorter Wavelengths 
2.4.1 
Deep UV 
In projection photolithography, the mask is the object to be imaged onto a substrate 
which is the image plane. The light passing through the mask is the light from the 
illumination source in an optical lithography system. Therefore, the wavelength 
appearing in Eq. 2.3 is the wavelength of illumination source. In the early days of 
optical lithography, mercury lamps were the illumination source. The spectra line at 
wavelength of 436 nm, called g-line, was ﬁrst utilized. As the mask features shrank, 
shorter wavelength at 365 nm (i-line) became the choice of illuminating source. 
Further demands in imaging small mask features made the mercury lamp unsuitable 
as the illumination source, because there is simply not enough photon energy at 
shorter wavelength to meet the requirement of volume production of ICs. 
Fortunately, excimer lasers can meet the requirements of both high photon energy 
and shorter wavelength. Excimer lasers are pulsed gas discharge lasers which 
produce light output in the ultraviolet region of the spectrum. The commonly used 
excimer laser sources are listed in Table 2.1, each emitting different wavelength of 
UV light. 
The KrF excimer laser has the highest relative power and was the obvious choice 
of illumination source to go down the scale of wavelength for volume IC 
manufacturing. The introduction of excimer laser marked the beginning of deep 
UV (DUV) optical lithography. The next illumination source for DUV optical 
lithography was the ArF excimer laser at 193 nm wavelength. It could have gone 
down even shorter wavelength with excimer lasers. There are the molecular ﬂuorine 
(F2) excimer laser emitting photons at 157 nm wavelength and Ar2 excimer laser at
Table 2.1 Excimer lasers and

126 nm wavelength. The semiconductor industry did start concerted effort from 
1998 to develop 157 nm optical lithography technology, aiming at IC feature size of 
70 nm.

2
Optical Lithography
Table 2.2 Reduction of wavelengths in optical lithography 
Minimum feature 
size (nm)

For optical lithography at 157 nm wavelength, there are three critical technology 
issues: material for making optical lenses, transparent and radiation-durable pellicle 
for masks, and photoresists. All these technical barriers are stemmed from high 
absorption of photons at short wavelength such as 157 nm or below. The lens 
materials at 248 nm and 193 nm wavelengths used to be fused silica, which is no 
longer suitable at 157 nm because of the high absorption or poor transmission. The 
high absorption nature at 157 nm wavelength has also prevented the use of pellicle 
which is a thin ﬁlm stretched on a frame to protect a photomask from particle 
contamination. The same problem applies to photoresists. Resists designed for 
248 nm and 193 nm lithography are too absorptive at 157 nm. New resist materials 
have to be developed. 
Given the huge efforts needed to make the 157 nm wavelength optical lithogra-
phy production worthy and the fact that 157 nm can only last one generation of IC 
manufacturing, the semiconductor industry had to ponder whether to pursue 157 nm 
or to go directly to much shorter wavelength which is the extreme UV (EUV) at 
13.5 nm. The picture became clear in 2004 when immersion optical lithography at 
193 nm started to mature and could well take over 157 nm wavelength for 70 nm IC 
manufacturing or even below [8]. Optical lithography at 157 nm became unneces-
sary. On the other hand, EUV can play a key role in several generations of IC ahead 
from 32 nm to 22 nm or below, because of its much shorter wavelength. Table 2.2 
has listed the minimum IC feature sizes in the history of semiconductor industry 
development, and the corresponding wavelengths of optical lithography. There is a 
big jump from 193 nm to 13.5 nm. Entering 13 nm wavelength regime, optical 
lithography has come to a completely new era. 
2.4.2 
Extreme UV 
EUV at 13 nm wavelength is in strict sense no longer optical irradiation. It is often 
called soft X-ray. To differ it from hard X-ray lithography, the semiconductor 
industry still considers EUV lithography (EUVL) as an optical lithography. In 
many aspects, EUVL retains the look and feel of optical lithography as practiced 
so far. However, conventional refractive optics ceases to function at EUV because of 
its strong absorption in virtually all materials. Instead, reﬂective optics has to be used

[Page 23]
for both mask and focusing optics. The basic system of EUV lithography is sche-
matically shown in Fig. 2.11. It shows that the EUV radiation from an EUV source is 
collected by a set of reﬂective mirrors (illumination optics) and projected onto a 
reﬂective mask. The reﬂected mask image is then focused by another set of reﬂective 
mirrors (projection optics) and projected onto a substrate to expose the EUV resist 
layer. In addition, EUV imaging must be carried out in a near vacuum condition to 
reduce atmospheric absorption of EUV light. 
2.4
Optical Lithography at Shorter Wavelengths
23
EUV source 
Illuminator optics 
Projector optics 
EUV mask 
Wafer 
Fig. 2.11 Schematic of EUV lithography system 
There are four critical components in EUVL: EUV source, EUV projection 
optics, EUV mask, and EUV resist. The state-of-the-art developments of the four 
critical components are described as follows. 
2.4.2.1 
EUV Source 
EUV radiation can be generated by two methods: plasma and synchrotron radiation 
source. Synchrotron radiation source (SRS) is a large-scale instrument which can 
only be built and owned by a national government and is mainly for fundamental 
scientiﬁc research. Industrially dedicated EUV sources for lithography purpose are 
mostly based on plasma-generated EUV radiation. There are two types of plasmas 
that can generate EUV wavelength radiation: laser-produced plasma (LPP) and 
discharge-produced plasma (DPP). Some elements such as Li, Xe, and Sn, after

[Page 24]
being ionized, can emit short wavelength photons when electrons in the atomic 
structure jump from higher energy band to lower energy band. For example, ionized 
Li atom has a radiation spectrum from 10 to 22 nm, with the highest intensity of 
spectra line at 13.5 nm [9]. This has been the reason that the central wavelength for 
EUVL is ﬁxed at 13.5 nm. After years of investigations, LPP of liquid Sn droplets 
became the prevailing choice of EUV source, and maximum 6% conversion efﬁ-
ciency has been achieved [10]. ASML subsidiary Cymer and Japanese company 
Gigaphoton are currently the major players in the EUV source technology. In the last 
10 years, the EUV source power has been steadily increasing, from 40 W in 
2014 to 250 W in 2017, achieving 125 WPH (wafer per hour) exposure throughput 
in the ASML’s NXE:3400B EUV scanner [11]. Later in the NXE:3400C scanner, 
250 W/170 WPH at 30 mJ/cm2 exposure dose was achieved [12]. The industry has 
now made progress in developing 600 W source and in the next steps toward even 
higher powers [13]. 
24
2
Optical Lithography
2.4.2.2 
EUV Optics 
The key component of EUV optics is the multilayer reﬂective mirror. EUV reﬂec-
tivity of any single materials at near-normal incidence is very low. In order to 
achieve reasonable reﬂectivity near-normal incidence, the mirror surface must be 
coated with multilayer thin ﬁlm coatings known as distributed Bragg reﬂectors. 
These coatings consist of a large number of alternating layers of materials having 
dissimilar EUV optical constants, and they provide a resonant reﬂectivity when the 
period of the layers is approximately λ/2 of EUV wavelength. The most developed 
and best understood reﬂective multilayers for EUV are made of alternating layers of 
Mo and Si, and they have the highest reﬂectivity at wavelengths of about 13 nm. 
Figure 2.12 shows the theoretical reﬂectivity of a mirror consisting of 50 pairs of Mo 
(2.76 nm)-Si (4.14 nm) multilayer at normal incidence of EUV [14]. In practice,
Fig. 2.12 Theoretical reﬂectivity of a mirror consisting of Mo-Si multilayer at normal incidence of 
EUV radiation

[Page 25]
around 68% reﬂectivity can be achieved by a multilayer Mo-Si mirror. The causes of 
decrease in reﬂectivity are absorption of EUV energy by impurities in the deposited 
ﬁlm, index change due to formation of interdiffusion of layers, and scattering due to 
interface roughness. The major issues for EUV optics are the contamination control 
and lifetime under EUV irradiation. The latest development in EUV optics is to 
increase the numerical aperture (NA). Current EUV scanners in use have the NA of 
0.33. The next generation of scanner developed by ASML has the NA of 0.55, which 
is capable of single-exposure at sub-8 nm half-pitch resolution for 2 nm logic node of 
ICs [15]. In December 2023, ASML shipped its ﬁrst high-NA (0.55) EUV scanner to 
Intel, and next generation of hyper-NA (>0.7) EUV scanner is being developed 
according to the ASML’s 2023 Annual Report.
2.4
Optical Lithography at Shorter Wavelengths
25
2.4.2.3 
EUV Mask 
EUV mask works the same way as the EUV mirror. It has to be reﬂective. There are 
two stages of processes involved in making EUV masks: ﬁrst is the making of mask 
blank and second is the patterning of absorber layer. Figure 2.13 shows the cross 
section of an EUV mask. The blank is similar to an EUV mirror except that there is 
an absorber layer on top of the multiple pairs of Mo-Si layers. The substrate must be 
a low thermal expansion material with ﬂatness better than 50 nm and free of defects. 
A number of metals can be used as the absorber layer, such as Al, Cr, Ta, and W, 
with the Ta-based material to be the favorite [16]. Because EUV projection optics is 
reduction optics with reduction ratio of 4:1, the patterning of EUV absorber is less 
demanding. Conventional electron beam lithography and reactive ion etching are 
adequate for the absorber patterning. The most difﬁcult part in EUV mask 
manufacturing is the defect inspection and repair [17]. There are two groups of 
defects, the defects in a mask blank and defects in patterned absorber. As EUV 
imaging relies on reﬂective surface of mask, any bumps can cause phase error in the 
ﬁnal image. It has been characterized that a 65 nm × 1.5 nm (width × height) bump in 
the multilayer surface can cause 20% deviation of dimension to a 25 nm line 
[18]. Defects as small as 50 nm on the absorber patterns can be printable [19]. For 
repair of defects in conventional optical mask, focused ion beam has been the 
universally used repair tool. However, focused ion beam repair of opaque defect in
Fig. 2.13 Cross-sectional 
view of EUV mask 
Patterned absorber of Al, Cr, 
Ta or W material at around 
100nm thickness 
50nm buffer layer (SiO2) 
Reflective multi-layers (40-50 
pairs ~300nm total thickness) 
Low thermal expansion 
substrate

[Page 26]
EUV masks can easily cause both damage and gallium staining to the substrate, and 
gallium is very absorbent at EUV wavelength of 13.5 nm. In this case, electron beam 
repair method is preferred. Electron beam irradiation assisted by reactive gases can 
either etch or deposit materials with high resolution and high accuracy, which makes 
it a perfect tool for defect repair in EUV masks [20].
26
2
Optical Lithography
2.4.2.4 
EUV Resists 
Resists for EUV lithography are required to have higher sensitivity and low line edge 
roughness (LER). The traditional chemically ampliﬁed resists (CARs) used in DUV 
lithography are no longer suitable for EUV because they are more transparent at 
13 nm wavelength than at 193 nm wavelength. Less absorption of EUV radiation 
results in low sensitivity. The CARs also have worse LER for features below 22 nm 
because of the photoacid diffusion process after exposure. For this reason, new resist 
formulations consisting of atoms with enhanced absorptivity in EUV regime, such as 
metallic atoms of Hf, Zr, Zn, and Sn, have been proposed as alternative to traditional 
CARs [21]. In addition to better absorption of EUV photons, the metal-containing 
inorganic photoresists, which are non-chemically ampliﬁed, have another advantage 
over organic resists. For EUV lithography of sub-32 nm generation ICs, the resist 
thickness is less than 35 nm. Such thin layer of resist has to have good etch 
selectivity to substrate materials in order to transfer the resist patterns to substrate. 
The metal-containing inorganic resists have much better etch resistance in most 
plasma etching processes [22]. An all-dry processed negative tone EUV resist with 
resolution down to sub-10 nm lines was also demonstrated, which can be coated and 
developed by vacuum sublimation [23]. 
After years of efforts, EUV lithography reached a signiﬁcant milestone: readiness 
for high-volume production of advanced semiconductor devices. An EUV source 
power of 250 W has been realized, providing a tool throughput capability exceeding 
140 wafers per hour at a dose of 20 mJ/cm2 . The full-wafer critical dimension (CD) 
uniformity is less than 0.5 nm for multiple systems and matched-machine overlay is 
at 1.1 nm [24]. 2019 was the year the EUV patterning technology was deployed for 
the ﬁrst time for the mass production of logic chips of the 7 nm technology 
generation. 
2.4.3 
X-Ray 
The ultimate short wavelength photon radiation would be the X-ray. X-ray is the 
radiation wavelength spanning from 1 nm of the soft X-ray to 0.1 nm of hard X-ray. 
The fundamental difference between X-ray wave and optical wave is that X-ray can 
penetrate the majority of materials. Only materials with high atomic number can 
absorb X-ray. X-ray cannot be focused because the refractive index of all materials 
to X-ray is the same (n ≈ 1). Therefore, even if X-ray enters from free space to a

[Page 27]
material or from one material to another, there is no refraction. X-ray exposure of 
mask image can only be 1:1 with a proximity gap between mask and resist surface. 
The opaque and transparent features on an X-ray mask are deﬁned by heavy metal 
absorber and thin silicon membrane (1–2 μm thickness). 
2.4
Optical Lithography at Shorter Wavelengths
27
Because of its short wavelength, X-ray lithography once was a very hopeful 
candidate technology to replace optical lithography for IC manufacturing. IBM 
started the technology development in 1980. Motorola, NEC, NTT, and Mitsubishi 
all had invested in the technology. The beginning of and mid-1990s were the 
heydays of X-ray lithography R&D. As early as in the mid-1990s when optical 
lithography was still struggling to get down to 0.25 μm circuit dimension, X-ray 
lithography had already achieved 0.12 μm experimental DRAM chips and 0.1 μm 
logic chips [25]. However, X-ray lithography eventually did not succeed to replace 
optical lithography. The ﬁrst hurdle is the fabrication of mask. As X-ray exposure is 
1:1 pattern transfer, the heavy metal absorber has to have the same dimension as the 
resist feature. In addition, local stress caused by heavy metal absorber and internal 
stress of supporting membrane all can have inﬂuences on the pattern resolution. 
The second hurdle is to accurately control the mask-wafer gap. High resolution 
X-ray lithography requires the gap to be controlled within 10 μm. Variation of the 
gap will cause variation of image resolution. This requires the ﬂatness of silicon 
wafer to be within ±0.25 μm and ﬂatness of the mask within ±0.5 μm [26]. These are 
very stringent requirements for a large-size silicon wafer and a large area of 
membrane. The gap control is also required to be within ±0.5 μm accuracy. Bearing 
in mind that the mask membrane is only 1–2 μm thick and very easy to crack, it 
makes the gap control a lot harder. 
The third hurdle is the photoelectrons and Auger electrons generated by high 
energy X-ray irradiation in resist. These low energy electrons are more efﬁcient to 
interact with resist polymer molecules, causing exposure of the resist. Upon acquir-
ing energy from X-ray, these electrons scatter to some distance within the resist 
layer. The consequence of electron scattering is the expansion of exposure pattern 
dimension, hence degradation of lithography resolution. 
On top of all the technical challenges is the economical factor. For industrial-scale 
manufacturing, the X-ray “light” has to be from a synchrotron radiation source 
(SRS). Construction of a large-scale SRS costs over $100 million or a compact 
size SRS costs about $20 M. Although one SRS can have 10–20 beam lines 
equivalent to 10–20 optical steppers, they all reply on proper working condition of 
the SRS. If the SRS breaks down, all the beam lines will stop working. Had the 
optical lithography not kept the pace of shrinking down in IC feature dimension, the 
semiconductor industry would have had no choice but to adopt X-ray lithography. 
However, optical lithography has been continuously advancing. New resolution 
record has been broken time and again beyond anyone’s dream decades ago. 
Fabrication of optical masks is much simpler and easier than fabrication of X-ray 
masks. X-ray lithography has eventually failed to win favors from the semiconductor 
industry to become the mainstream manufacturing technology [27]. X-ray lithogra-
phy is now mainly used not for high resolution but for high aspect ratio features 
because of its powerful penetration capability [28], particularly for the LIGA 
process [29].

[Page 28]
28
2
Optical Lithography
2.5 
Optical Lithography at High NA 
The second parameter inﬂuencing the resolution of optical lithography, in addition to 
the wavelength as seen in Eq. 2.3, is the numerical aperture (NA). As explained 
earlier, NA is equivalent to the diameter of a lens. While lights passing through a 
small mask feature (transparent opening) diffract in wide angle as illustrated in 
Fig. 2.2, a lens with large diameter can collect more diffracted lights and focus 
onto the image plane. It is crucial to collect the wide-angle diffracted lights because 
they contain the information about the details of the mask feature. The light 
diffraction through a small feature has the characteristics that the ﬁner the details 
of the feature, the further the lights diffract from the central optics axis. Or in terms of 
Fourier optics, smaller spatial detail can be referred to as being of higher “spatial 
frequency.” Such characteristics can be best explained in Fig. 2.14a, b. 
Figure 2.14a is square wave function, representing slits or a series of line features 
in a mask, which have the ideal distribution of light intensity (without diffraction). 
After diffraction at the image plane, the square wave function is transformed into a 
series of sinusoidal wave functions ranging from the base spatial frequency (m = 1 in  
Eq. 2.1) to higher orders (m > 1 in Eq. 2.1). The superimposed sine waves of 
different spatial frequency become the image of original square function (object) at 
the image plane. The more the high spatial frequency waves are included, the better 
the ﬁnal image approximates the original object, as shown in Fig. 2.14b. As seen in 
the plots, the superimposed image of up to 200 sinusoidal waves is almost identical 
to the original square wave function. It is therefore understandable that only an 
optical lens system with larger NA can allow diffracted lights of much higher spatial 
frequency to reach the image plane, resulting in better image of the mask features. 
High NA also means more lights coming through the lens system, hence a brighter 
image on the image plane. Computer simulation of optical imaging conﬁrms the 
inﬂuence of NA, as shown in Fig. 2.15, where a group of 0.2 μm lines is imaged
Fig. 2.14 Mask images (a) without diffraction and (b) with diffraction

[Page 29]
through a projection optical system at 248 nm wavelength with three different 
numerical apertures (NA = 0.5, 0.6, 0.8). The lines imaged at high NA are not 
only resolved better (high image contrast) but also brighter (high image intensity).
2.5
Optical Lithography at High NA
29
Although it is so obvious that high NA can improve the resolution of optical 
lithography, resolution is only one requirement for high-quality optical lithography. 
Another important requirement is the depth of focus (DOF), or commonly known as 
depth of ﬁeld in photography. DOF is also related to the wavelength of illumination 
and numerical aperture of an optical system by Eq. 2.4, where k2 is a constant 
(k2 < 1) related to a speciﬁc lithography system and photoresist process. 
DOF = k2

degradation of image quality, as seen in Fig. 2.16.

Optical Lithography
The DOF deﬁned by Eq. 2.4 is only valid for NA less than 0.5. For higher NA, the 
equation has to be modiﬁed and take the form of Eq. 2.5 (where k3 is a constant 
similar to k2), which causes DOF to drop much more steeply at high NA value [30] 
DOF = k3

p
ð2:5Þ 
It must be pointed out that DOF is actually a more important parameter in the 
lithography of ICs. ICs are always fabricated on large size of silicon wafers, 
normally in 8″ or 12″ diameter. The wafers in such size are impossible to be perfectly 
ﬂat, plus there is topography formed by circuit structures in previous process steps. 
One can imagine that if the DOF is very small, the optical imaging can only be in 
focus in a very small range of height variation. There would be many places in a 
wafer where defocus would be inevitable because of the topography or wafer 
bowing. 
Every layer of 2D circuit layout on an IC chip has a group of critical minimum 
features which dimension is called “critical dimension,” or CD. This CD has to be 
controlled within a certain range (less than ±10% variation from nominal dimension) 
during fabrication to ensure the ﬁnal circuit working properly. For example, for a 
100 nm critical feature dimension, any errors during fabrication should increase or 
reduce the feature dimension no more than 10 nm. The DOF of a lithography system 
has to be large enough to ensure the CD variation is within ±10% everywhere on a 
wafer. For scientiﬁc experiment, resolution may be the only important target to 
achieve, as the researchers are only interested in a small device area where there are a 
few pattern structures with minimum dimensions. For large-scale production, high 
yield can only be possible when all the minimum circuit features across the entire 
wafer are controlled within the target CD variation. In this case, the highest resolu-
tion is only meaningful within certain DOF. 
The increase in NA used to be restricted to ensure the DOF is above 1 μm at a  
speciﬁc wavelength, which would give much ease to IC processing. With advances 
in all aspects of optical lithography, particularly in wafer ﬂatness, introduction of 
autofocus and auto leveling systems, thinner resist processes, and reduced surface 
topography via chemical mechanical polishing (CMP), the requirement on DOF has 
been gradually reduced to below 0.5 μm. This has enabled gradual increase of NA 
over the years. Figure 2.17 shows the evolution of NA in optical lithography from 
the 1980s up to mid-2000s. While the wavelength has increased from g-line to deep 
UV at 193 nm, the NA also increased from 0.3 to above 0.8. By 2009, the DUV 
TWINSCAN system made by ASML reached 0.93NA which was the highest NA 
that could be possible [31]. The higher the NA, the more complex, bigger, heavier, 
and more expensive the lens has become. In the 1980s, a g-line stepper lens at 
0.35NA weighed only 14 kg. By the mid-1990s, an i-line stepper lens at 0.63NA 
weighed over 500 kg [32]. 
Increasing NA above 0.8 not only encountered the difﬁculty in lens design/ 
building and left optical lithography with very small DOF but also had the problem

[Page 31]
of polarization effect at high oblique incidence of light, which degrades the image 
contrast [34]. High NA has forced optical lithography into an optical imaging regime 
where the approach angles of the imaging rays are so wide that the light coming out 
of the lens can no longer be considered as a scalar. Instead, one must consider the 
vector components of light and take into account the separation between the electric 
and magnetic ﬁeld components of light, the so-called 
s-polarization and 
p-polarization. When there is incident light in a large oblique angle from air into a 
polymer resist layer, part of the light (s-polarization) can be reﬂected from the 
surface if the angle is greater than Brewster’s angle. Reﬂection of polarized light 
will reduce the photon energy that goes into the photoresist, causing low contrast of 
image and low image intensity. Brewster’s angle θB at which the polarization 
reﬂection occurs is expressed as Eq. 2.6, when light from one medium with refrac-
tive index n1 goes into another medium with refractive index n2. In the case of 
optical lithography, light goes from air (n1 = 1) to a photoresist layer (n2).

High refractive index photoresist will be able to tolerate imaging with high-NA 
optics. For example, if a photoresist has refractive index of 1.5, Brewster’s angle θB 
will be 56°, which translates into NA of 0.83 based on Eq. 2.2. Theoretically, further 
increase in NA can be tolerated by increasing the refractive index of photoresist, 
ignoring the difﬁculty and cost of making high-NA lenses. However, the refractive 
index of photoresist is not a property that can be easily adjustable. One way to solve 
the polarization problem is to illuminate the mask with a deliberately polarized light 
source, for example, illumination containing only the p-polarization light. In this 
way, there will be no reﬂection of light from the photoresist surface, even with very 
high-NA optics. Improvement in DOF and lithography quality has been observed 
using polarized illumination at lens NA of 0.92 [35].

Optical Lithography
2.6 
Immersion Lithography 
The most important development in the history of optical lithography is the intro-
duction of immersion imaging, the so-called immersion optical lithography 
[36]. Immersion imaging scheme came just at the time when optical lithography 
has hit the limit at 193 nm wavelength and the semiconductor industry is facing the 
choice of whether to go for the problematic 157 nm option or to go for the so-called 
next generation lithography (NGL) which includes EUV, electron projection, ion 
projection, and nanoimprinting. 
It is obvious that if the refractive index n in Eq. 2.2 is not for air (n = 1) but for a 
liquid (n > 1), then the NA can be more than 1. The immersion imaging, also called 
“wet” imaging, is not a new concept. The medical and biological research has for a 
century used immersion imaging in optical microscope to enhance the image 
resolution. The wet imaging is illustrated and compared with conventional “dry” 
imaging in Fig. 2.18. The liquid between the objective lens and imaging plane has 
effectively reduced the angle of light coming out of the lens. Although the NA of 
optical lens is the same as for dry imaging, the effective NA seen from the imaging 
plane side has increased to a value greater than actual NA of the lens. Table 2.3 
shows the angle of refracted light in different mediums for a lens with a ﬁxed NA. 
The light, which used to be entering photoresist at 58° angle in the case of dry 
imaging, is now entering at 36° angle, equivalent to the NA value of about 1.22. 
However, the incident light angle cannot be increased by merely placing an immer-
sion ﬂuid between an objective lens and the image plane. The objective lens has to be 
speciﬁcally designed to allow larger angle of light interfacing with the immersion
Fig. 2.18 Schematic 
comparison of “wet” (right 
half) and “dry” (left half) 
imaging

ﬂuid. The most frequently used form of an immersion lens is a prism or hemispher-
