2.6
Immersion Lithography
33
Immersion imaging at 193 nm (193i) has indeed made a leap forward in terms of 
sub-100 nm IC manufacturing using optical lithography. Its advantage to extend 
further into sub-50 nm resolution regime is obvious. For example, assuming 
k1 = 0.25 and the numerical aperture of lens is 0.75, the dry lithography at 
193 nm wavelength can achieve resolution of 65 nm, whereas the same optical 
system with immersion imaging using water can achieve resolution of 45 nm. While 
immersion imaging is such a simple and mature concept, why did it take so long for 
the semiconductor industry to seriously consider the merits of replacing air with a 
liquid? This was because to realize immersion optical lithography, there were many 
technical difﬁculties. In the past, it was not worth the effort to solve these technical 
difﬁculties, as conventional optics was more than adequate to meet the resolution 
requirement of IC production above 100 nm. As optical lithography was 
approaching its end of life, any measures that could revive it would be seriously 
considered. 
One of the most serious technical difﬁculties is how to move the wafer stage 
rapidly relative to the objective lens while in water without stirring up bubbles. In 
order to achieve high throughput, the wafer stage must accelerate rapidly and move 
accurately to each exposure ﬁeld location, then settle, and scan the image before 
stepping to the next location, all in a short period of time. Maintaining a consistent 
bubble-free liquid between the lens and the wafer is very difﬁcult. There are 
basically three approaches to solving the problem [33]. The ﬁrst approach is to 
submerge the whole chuck, wafer, and lens in a pool of water. The issue with the 
pool approach is that a complex system of servomotors and laser interferometers is 
required to accurately move the chuck. Submerging this whole system would require 
signiﬁcantly complex engineering. The second approach is to limit the pool size to 
the top of the chuck. This technique would keep all of the chuck control mechanisms 
dry but would add considerable mass to the chuck that must rapidly accelerate. The 
third and currently being implemented technique is to dispense the water between 
the lens and the wafer with a nozzle and rely on surface tension to maintain a water 
“puddle” while the wafer stage moves to each die location during step-scan process. 
Because the water puddle moves with the lens, no bubbles are stirred up during the 
step-scan process. 
Another critical issue is the defects that are likely to form on photoresist surface 
due to water marks and drying stains [37]. Conventional 193 nm photoresists that are 
optimized for “dry” exposures may not be suitable for immersion exposure since 
resist components show some solubility in water and thus leach out, thereby 
contaminating the water and creating defects. The contaminated water can further 
degrade the front lens and wafer stage of the scanner. Water can also permeate into 
the resist ﬁlm, causing resist swelling and changing its photochemical properties. An 
additional topcoat layer is an effective solution to block both the leaching of resist 
components into the water and the water permeation into the photoresist. The topcoat 
is spin-coated on the resist and is transparent at 193 nm wavelength. The topcoat 
layer is removed after exposure and post-exposure bake (PEB) but before pattern

[Page 34]
development. There are two types of topcoats: those only soluble in some solvent 
and those that dissolve in resist developer (aqueous trimethyl ammonium hydroxide 
(TMAH)). The trend is toward developer-soluble topcoats, because the additional 
process complexity associated with a solvent-removed topcoat is too high. As 
193 nm immersion lithography has been introduced to volume production of ICs, 
many technical issues associated with topcoat are being investigated. Whether a 
topcoat is needed is still debatable [38]. In the meantime, non-topcoat photoresists 
have been developed, since the additional topcoat will inevitably complicate the 
resist process and increase the cost of manufacturing [39, 40]. 
34
2
Optical Lithography
The immersion imaging using water (n = 1.44) can achieve NA at 1.35 and is 
capable of 45 nm resolution with 193 nm wavelength. This is about the limit with 
water as the immersion liquid. Further improvement in resolution has to seek an 
immersion liquid with higher refractive index (n = 1.8 ~ 2.0). At present water has 
similar refractive index as the glass as shown in Table 2.3. To maintain index 
matching, high refractive index glass material is also needed for making the objec-
tive lens. In 2006 IBM demonstrated 29.9 nm line and space features achieved by 
193i lithography using a high index ﬂuid (n = 1.64) and a quartz crystal prism 
(n = 1.67) at the end of objective lens to match the high index ﬂuid [41]. 
The optical glass for making lenses and water as the immersion ﬂuid are all well 
known for their physical properties. Introduction of new materials for both the 
immersion liquids and lens materials will lead optical lithography into unknown 
territory. For high index immersion lithography to enable 1.70NA imaging, a high 
index lens element with an absorbance <0.005/cm, a ﬂuid with an index of ≥1.80, 
and a resist with an index >1.9 are needed [42]. Though high index immersion 
lithography was attractive, the IC industry did not pursue down this road, not 
because there were fundamental obstacles which were hard to surpass but because 
the industry wanted something which could be quickly adopted for 32 nm and 
below. The alternative technology is the multi-patterning method which will be 
described in Sect. 2.7.5 of this chapter. Since 2009, the 193i lithography has been 
staying with water as the high index liquid [42]. 
2.7 
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor 
The third parameter inﬂuencing the resolution of optical lithography, as seen in 
Eq. 2.3, is the k1 factor. The k1 factor encompasses any measures that can improve 
the resolution of imaging, not only in the optics but also in masks and photoresist 
layer. Apart from developing shorter wavelength illumination and high-NA optics as 
introduced in previous sections, any other efforts in the development of optical 
lithography over the last 40 years have been concentrated on lowering the k1 factor. 
Figure 2.19 shows the reduction of k1 factor from g-line optical lithography to EUV 
lithography [43], which pushed the IC feature dimension from 1 μm in the 1980s to 
below 5 nm node at present day.

[Page 35]
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
35
Fig. 2.19 Reduction of IC feature size enabled by reduction of k1 factor. (Courtesy of David 
Abercrombie, Semiconductor Engineering [43]) 
Since the optical lithography is based on diffraction optics, there is a theoretical 
resolution limit. Traditionally, the resolution limit is deﬁned by Rayleigh criterion. A 
recent article argued that Abbe diffraction limit gave a more accurate description 
[44]. In 1873, the German physicist Ernst Abbe discovered that the resolution of a 
microscope is not controlled by how good the microscope can be but by the 
wavelength of light used and the aperture of its optics. A microscope cannot resolve 
two objects located closer than λ/2NA, which translates into Eq. 2.7 where R is the 
distance between the centers of two separate line opening, λ is the wavelength of 
light, and NA is the numerical aperture of the imaging lens. 
R = 0:5 λ 
NA
ð2:7Þ 
Before 2000, the technology nodes in IC industry were deﬁned by the minimum 
achievable circuit feature dimension, i.e., linewidth. For example, when speaking of 
0.35 μm, 0.25 μm, or 0.18 μm IC technologies, they implicate the minimum 
linewidth of circuit feature in an IC chip. However, minimum linewidth cannot 
represent the true integration level of an IC because it does not indicate how dense 
the circuit features are in an IC. In fact, it is much easier to achieve high resolution 
for single isolated line than for dense line/space pattern. The true representation of 
circuit integration density is the pitch, not the linewidth. The pitch is the separation 
distance between two lines, as shown in Fig. 2.20 which shows two lines of same 
width but different pitches (P). The simulated optical intensities of two separate lines 
on a mask shown in Fig. 2.21 demonstrate that different separation distances (pitch) 
result in different optical images. The smaller the pitch, the worse the resolvability of

[Page 36]
the lines even though their linewidth is the same. For this reason, the semiconductor 
industry started to use “half pitch” (hp) as illustrated in Fig. 2.20, to  deﬁne the 
technology node since the 2001 version of International Technology Roadmap for 
Semiconductors (ITRS) [8]. By this deﬁnition, the theoretical limit of k1 factor 
would be 0.25 which is shown by the dashed line in Fig. 2.19.

In the early days of semiconductor manufacturing, k1 factor was much larger. 
With the continuous shrink of circuit dimension, the resolution of optical lithography 
was restrained by available illumination wavelength and numerical aperture of

[Page 37]
optical systems. Lowering k1 factor became the focus of development. There had 
been innovations in every step of imaging process from the top at the illumination 
source to the middle at the mask level, and down to the bottom at the photoresist 
layer. They were all under the name of “resolution enhancement technique” (RET) 
and contributed to lowering the k1 factor from 0.6 downward and even breaking the 
resolution limit (k1 < 0.25), as seen in Fig. 2.19. Table 2.4 summarizes the RETs that 
have been developed to improve image resolution, contrast, and depth of focus. The 
following sections will introduce each of these innovative techniques. 
2.7

2.7.1 
Off-Axis Illumination (OAI) 
Illumination light through a photomask will be diffracted, as described in Sect. 2.2. 
The smaller the mask feature, the larger the angle of diffracted light (Fig. 2.2). An 
optical system has limited resolution because the large angular diffractions are cut 
off by ﬁnite NA of a lens. However, if the illumination is deliberately tilted through 
an off-axis aperture instead of an on-axis aperture, light passes through the mask 
feature in an oblique angle, while the zero-order illumination is blocked. Figure 2.22 
compares the conventional illumination (a) and off-axis illumination (b). In this way 
the high orders of diffraction can pass through the mask feature to be imaged. It 
should then enhance the image resolution. 
OAI was ﬁrst proposed in the early 1990s with two schemes: annular OAI [45] 
and quadrupole OAI, also called SHRINC (Super High Resolution by Illumi-Nation 
Control) [46]. Both schemes are illustrated in Fig. 2.22b. Quadrupole OAI is most 
effective for line and space (L/S) pattern but less for isolated features. With quad-
rupole OAI, 0.275 μm L/S pattern can be resolved with i-line lithography. Quadru-
pole OAI is also dependent on the orientation of L/S patterns. It gives the best results 
for vertical or horizontal L/S pattern but less optimum for L/S with 45° angle. 
Annular OAI, on the other hand, does not have the discrimination of pattern 
orientation, but the resolution enhancement effect with annular OAI is rather weak 
compared to quadrupole OAI. 
OAI is the easiest and requires the minimum cost to implement in an optical 
lithography system among other resolution enhancement techniques (RETs). It

[Page 38]
certainly is the most widespread used RET in commercial optical lithography 
systems. Because the OAI effect is dependent on pattern density and orientation, 
the state-of-the-art OAI apparatus are programmable [47]. It can be tuned to a 
particular set of mask patterns to achieve the optimum performance. For example, 
the state-of-the-art DUV scanners made by ASML are equipped with a FlexRay 
illuminator which has 4000 micromirrors, and the illumination conditions can be 
self-deﬁned [48]. This is particularly useful as the mask patterns become increas-
ingly complex due to the incorporation of many phase shift and proximity correction 
features. The illumination can be adapted to particular mask patterns through the 
source-mask optimization (SMO) [49], which has become a standard practice in IC 
design process.

2.7.2 
Phase-Shifting Mask (PSM) 
The concept of phase-shifting was proposed in 1982, whereby the light diffraction of 
a mask feature can be suppressed by introducing light wave with 180° phase 
difference surrounding the mask feature [50]. Figure 2.23 schematically shows 
how the light intensity distribution from a mask feature can be made narrower by 
introducing light of opposite phase in comparison with imaging of a conventional 
binary photomask. There are a number of ways to realize phase-shifting on a mask, 
depending on where and how to place the phase shifters. Figure 2.24(i) shows ﬁve 
different types of PSM in comparison with a conventional photomask. The shaded 
region on a PSM is 100% transparent to light but with 180° phase difference from 
unshaded pattern region, except the attenuated PSM (Fig. 2.24i–f) where the shaded

[Page 39]
region is only 5–15% transparent to light but has 180° phase-shifting. Figure 2.24 
(ii) conceptually compares the phase and intensity produced by binary and PSMs. 
Different phase-shifting methods have different performance on resolution enhance-
ment, with the alternating PSM and chromeless PSM being the strongest phase-
shifting methods which produce the greatest improvement on imaging resolution. 
Figure 2.25 shows the simulated light intensity distributions (aerial image) of L/S 
pattern for conventional mask, alternating PSM, and attenuated PSM. The linewidth 
is 0.2 μm and exposed with 248 nm wavelength and 0.5NA. It can be seen that the 
alternating PSM has the most signiﬁcant improvement in aerial image over the 
imaging of conventional mask. The attenuated PSM has improvement over conven-
tional mask but not as pronounced as the alternating PSM. 
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor

Fig. 2.23 The concept of phase-shifting mask 
Although the alternating PSM has superb performance in resolution enhance-
ment, there are three critical issues with this type of PSM. First, alternating PSM is 
only suitable for patterns with high periodicity. They are very difﬁcult to apply to a 
random design layout. Some memory cell layouts have been modiﬁed to be ideally 
suited for this approach, but this is not possible for logic applications like micro-
processors or application-speciﬁc integrated circuits (ASICs). There are dedicated 
software tools which can deal with placing phase-shifting patterns to random design 
layout, but not all issues have been solved. 
The second issue is the phase conﬂict. Each 180° phase-shifting region has a 
boundary. If the phase shifter is adjacent to a transparent 0° phase region, the 
transition between regions of 180° and 0° phase will always print out as a dark

[Page 40]
narrow line whether this is intended or not, because of the cancellation of light at the 
transition region. For most of IC designs, it is very difﬁcult to avoid these undesir-
able features created by the phase conﬂict. There are two ways to overcome this 
problem. One way is to apply a second exposure with a trim mask to eliminate these 
dark lines. This approach will increase the exposure time and require an additional 
mask. Another way is to design some intermediate phase shifters at the transition
40

region so that the transition of phase is not from 180° abruptly to 0°, but to 120°, 60°, 
and 0°, for example. Both methods require extra space around the critical features 
and hence are less suitable for densely packed pattern layouts, such as memory cells.

The third issue is mask manufacturing complexity. Phase-shifting layer has to be 
produced by additional exposure and deposition or etching of the mask. This 
additional manufacturing step not only increases the mask cost but also requires 
accurate alignment of two different patterns and increases the possibility of defects. 
Inspection and repair of phase defects are complicated. The overall cost of a PSM is 
at least ten times higher than a conventional binary mask. 
Although alternating PSM has the best performance, the ﬁrst and most 
widely used PSM in optical lithography for IC manufacturing was the attenuated 
PSM, solely because it is relatively easy to make and of low cost. Attenuated PSM 
(attPSM) is also called half-tone PSM or embedded PSM. As shown in Fig. 2.24i–f, 
the attenuated PSM is not much different from a conventional binary mask 
(Fig. 2.24i–a), except that the chrome layer on a conventional mask is replaced by 
a thin ﬁlm which gives 5–15% light transmission with 180° phase shift. The 
transmission of attenuation ﬁlm has to be carefully controlled because of the side 
lobes which become unwanted features in photoresist [52]. Attenuated PSM is 
particularly suitable for isolated or low density features, which makes it the best 
phase-shifting option for optical lithography of logic ICs or ASICs. Even though 
attPSM is not an aggressive or strong phase-shifting scheme, as has been demon-
strated in Fig. 2.25, the economical factor alone has kept it alive all the way down to 
65 nm node. As the photomask manufacturers pointed out, few IC manufacturers 
have required alternating PSMs. They are too expensive to make and the beneﬁt out 
of that is not justiﬁable [53]. Process engineers can get by with attPSM and optical 
proximity correction (OPC) without using the alternating PSMs. The attPSM can be

[Page 42]
used not only in DUV but also in EUV lithography. Because the EUV masks are not 
refractive but reﬂective, the phase shift and attenuation are created by varying the 
absorber material and layer structure [54]. 
42
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.26 Comparison of CPL mask (right) with conventional binary mask (left) 
As mentioned earlier, alternating PSM is good for highly repetitive features such 
as the memory cell in DRAM-type ICs, but not for randomly distributed features 
which are common in logic-type ICs. To tackle this challenge, a variation of phase-
shifting scheme shown in Fig. 2.24i–e, called chromeless phase lithography (CPL), 
was developed in the early 2000s [55]. Imagining that the transmission of 
phase-shifting layer on an attPSM becomes 100% instead of 5–15%, then at any 
edges of a mask feature, there will be dark lines because of the cancellation of light 
where 0° phase and 180° phase regions meet. If the two phase edges come reason-
ably close, then the two dark lines become merged into one dark line, which is 
equivalent to a dark line produced by a conventional binary mask (clear ﬁeld mask) 
but with much higher contrast, because of the phase-shifting. This is illustrated in 
Fig. 2.26 where the aerial image of an opaque line from a binary mask is compared 
with that of an all-transparent mask but with a phase shift. The all-transparent mask 
produces the same dark line aerial image as the conventional chrome-on-glass mask 
but has much high image contrast, shown by the simulated light intensity in 
Fig. 2.26. As it is known that negative tone resist image in IC manufacturing is 
always realized by positive tone photoresist combined with a clear ﬁeld mask, the 
CPL can well replace the conventional clear ﬁeld mask to create such negative tone 
resist image with substantially improved resolution. 
Dark aerial images from CPL are always formed by two phase edges in close 
proximity. The image formation is intrinsically symmetrical with no unwanted

[Page 43]
phase-edge images. The CPL layout needed to form a resist pattern on the wafer is 
not substantially different from that of a binary chrome mask. CPL can be made by 
etching the quartz substrate of a conventional chrome-on-glass mask blank to create 
π-phase depth after the initial chrome patterning. The chrome is then removed in 
subsequent steps to reveal non-phase-shifting areas. Both “π-phase background 
areas” and “0-phase patterning areas” share the same quartz substrate. It looks like 
a conventional mask but without chrome patterns. By careful design of π-phase 
patterns, dark random patterns can be formed in photoresist. An example of CPL 
mask and its resist image are shown in Fig. 2.27 where irregular resist patterns are 
formed by phase-shifting features instead of chrome features. For design layouts 
with both large and small features on the same mask, a hybrid CPL mask can be 
used. A hybrid CPL mask contains both phase-shifting features and chrome features. 
For large patterns, chrome features can be used as it does not require high resolution. 
For small patterns, π-phase patterns can be used to improve image resolution. 
Because of the mixture of phase-shifting regions and chrome regions, two steps of 
processes are needed to fabricate the hybrid CPL mask. CPL performance can be 
further enhanced by high-NA imaging optics and off-axis illumination. In combina-
tion with OAI and 0.85NA optics, 70 nm features with minimum pitch of 160 nm 
have been produced at 193 nm wavelength illumination, which corresponds to k1 
factor of about 0.3 [56].

Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor

Fig. 2.27 (a) CPL mask and (b) printed resist patterns. (Reprint from Ref. [56] with permission)

[Page 44]

Optical Lithography
2.7.3 
Optical Proximity Correction (OPC) 
Because of diffraction, ﬁne details of an object could be lost through an optical 
imaging process. As illustrated in Fig. 2.14b, the ﬁne details of an image are 
contained in those high spatial frequencies of diffracted lights. The ﬁdelity of an 
image can only be maintained if those diffractions of high spatial frequency can be 
imaged. Loss of ﬁne details implies the image is no longer sharp but blurred. In 
optical lithography of IC patterns, this blur of mask pattern image is translated into 
corner rounding and line-end shortening and is called optical proximity effect. Such 
effect is demonstrated in Fig. 2.28a, where the simulated optical image is shown to 
compare with the original mask pattern. Line-end shortening and corner rounding
Fig. 2.28 (a) Optical proximity effect results in corner rounding and line-end shortening; (b) 
optical proximity correction modiﬁes the mask design to restore the pattern ﬁdelity

[Page 45]
are apparent. Increasing numerical aperture (NA) and reducing illumination wave-
length (λ) are able to reduce the proximity effect. However, as described in previous 
sections, there are limits as to how short wavelength and how high NA can be 
employed. Failing all that, the remedy is to modify the original design layout to 
compensate the line-end shortening and corner rounding. This is the idea of optical 
proximity correction (OPC) [57]. OPC is to deliberately distort the original design to 
balance the image intensity. For example, overexposure region can be reduced by 
taking away part of the pattern; underexposure region can be enhanced by adding 
some extra features. Figure 2.28b shows the design pattern after OPC and its 
intensity contours. Comparing with Fig. 2.28a, the OPC has made the image closer 
to its mask design. OPC can also be realized by modifying the transmission of 
different parts of a mask pattern using the so-called grayscale mask technique 
[58]. Although the OPC design looks different from the original design, those 
added features are all sub-resolution, meaning that they are not resolvable by the 
imaging optics and hence will not be printable in photoresist.
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
45
Fig. 2.29 Mixture of dense 
and isolated lines with 
added scattering bars
Scattering bars 
Another compensation technique is to use the so-called scattering bars, or 
sub-resolution assist features (SRAF) [59]. These are narrow lines or spaces placed 
adjacent to a primary feature in order to make a relatively isolated line behaving 
more like a dense line lithographically. For example, Fig. 2.29 is a group of lines. 
The lower half of the central line can be considered as dense line because it is closely 
adjacent to other lines. The upper half of the central line, however, is an isolated line. 
Upon optical exposure, the resultant CD (critical dimension) of the central line 
would be different in its upper section from its lower section. The upper section 
will be narrower than its lower section. This is the optical proximity effect. To make 
up the difference, one can either deliberately widen the upper section of the central 
line, which is called biasing, or add two narrow bars on both sides of the upper 
section, as shown in Fig. 2.29. These two narrow bars are called scattering bars or 
SRAF. They are too narrow to be imaged on photoresist (which is the reason why

[Page 46]
they are called sub-resolution features), but the light passing through these bars can 
compensate the low intensity of light of the isolated line, so that the CDs of both 
dense lines and the isolated line are of the same width. SRAF is a complementary 
technique to the OPC methods mentioned earlier. SRAF is only workable if there are 
spaces nearby to allow placing them. 
46
2
Optical Lithography
OPC has become a standard technique in sub-100 nm optical lithography. The 
implementation of OPC faces two challenges: one is the placing and routing of OPC 
features, and the other is the fabrication of OPC mask. OPC features are added after 
the IC design is done. It is a complicated task to determine where to place what type 
of OPC features or how to modify the original design. In the early days of employing 
OPC technique, placing the OPC features was based on some rules which were 
derived from experience and computer simulation of aerial images of some typical 
shapes [60]. Rule-based OPC has the advantage of fast correction because patterns 
are modiﬁed according to a set of rules. However, the accuracy is poor as the number 
of rules is limited, which cannot cover all the possible situations (variety of pattern 
shapes). The other method is the model-based method [61]. The execution of model-
based OPC is to ﬁrst ﬂatten the hierarchy mask design layout into millions of 
segments; modelling is performed on each segment (1–2 μm range which is the 
distance of proximity interaction); the simulated pattern edge is compared with target 
pattern edge; difference called the edge placement error (EPE) is determined, and 
then the edge movement is performed to minimize the EPE. The ﬁnal step is to 
resemble all the corrected segments back to the mask layout [62]. A post-OPC 
veriﬁcation is also needed to check the validity of mask layout. In most cases, 
veriﬁcation with a speciﬁc exposure tool and a speciﬁc wafer process is necessary. 
The model-based method is obviously more accurate but very time-consuming. With 
the advances in computer technology, simulation power has increased exponentially 
in recent years. Now the mainstream OPC methodologies are mostly model-based 
and different OPC software is readily available from commercial vendors. 
All OPC features are sub-resolution, which means they are much smaller than the 
normal mask patterns and not imageable by the optical system. An OPC feature 
should be at most half the size of a normal feature. For 65 nm generation IC, the 
mask feature will be 265 nm for 4:1 reduction optical lithography. The OPC feature 
on mask should then be less than 130 nm. Making such small patterns on a mask 
plate can be considerably challenging. Currently there are two mainstream technol-
ogies for mask manufacturing, laser direct write and electron beam direct write. 
Laser tools are fast and have high throughput, while electron beam technique has the 
high resolution but slow. Figure 2.30 shows part of an OPC mask made by electron 
beam lithography, where the smallest features (serifs) are only 100 nm. Any 
distortions introduced during mask making can potentially degrade the OPC perfor-
mance [63]. Other challenges are mask inspection and repair for these very small 
OPC features, and data handling of OPC design. The data ﬁle for a full mask OPC 
design at 90 nm technology is as large as 263GB and increases to 729GB at 65 nm 
technology [64]. With such a large data volume, not only computer processing time 
but also hard disk accessing time has become limiting factors to the mask making

[Page 47]
throughput. All have contributed to the escalating cost of masks for the sub-100 nm 
generation IC manufacturing. 
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
47
Fig. 2.30 SEM image of an 
OPC mask showing OPC 
features added to normal 
mask features 
The ultimate solution for achieving the best optical lithography result is to design 
the photomask by working out how an ideal image is generated and then reversing 
back to the “perfect” mask layout. This is the concept of “inverse lithography 
technology” (ILT) which was ﬁrst proposed more than 30 years ago [65]. ILT is a 
rigorous mathematical approach to the design of photomasks for optimal lithography 
and can be viewed as an ultimate solution for OPC, or replacement of OPC, because 
the mask design has been optimized pixel by pixel to achieve the perfect balance of 
light intensity distribution on image plane. To reduce the difﬁculty in mask fabrica-
tion, the algorithm of ﬁnding the mask design can be tuned to different degree of 
aggressiveness. Less aggressive optimization can have a mask design which is easier 
to fabricate. Figure 2.31a shows two mask patterns from ILT design. The top one is 
an aggressive design and the bottom one is a nonaggressive design. The 
nonaggressive design lacks ﬁne details; therefore, the mask is easier to make. 
Figure 2.31b shows the relevant resist images printed on wafer with the ILT masks. 
There have been two obstacles hindering the adoption of ILT into mainstream IC 
manufacturing: (1) huge amount of computer power needed to process the mask data 
and (2) making curvilinear mask features which are preferable for the best imaging 
result. In recent years, two newly developed technologies provided solutions to 
overcome the obstacles: parallel graphic processing IC, i.e. graphic processing 
unit (GPU), and multi-e-beam writer [66]. GPU enabled fast calculation of imaging 
data, and multi-e-beam lithography (see Sect. 3.7.2 of Chap. 3) allowed fast 
manufacturing of complicated ILT masks with curvilinear features. These technol-
ogies enabled full-chip ILT for the ﬁrst time in 2019 [67, 68]. The optimizations 
from illumination to mask, including PSM, OPC, and ultimately ILT, are all based 
on computer simulations. The ﬁelds have been uniﬁed under the term of “computa-
tional lithography” [69], which has become an integral part of optical lithography

[Page 48]
process. Without computational lithography, it would be impossible for chipmakers 
to manufacture the latest technology nodes. Considerable effort and cost have been 
saved by doing computational optimization ﬁrst before going into experimental 
trials. A survey conducted in 2022 indicated that more than 90% of respondents 
said they have used ILT in some critical layers of masks [70]. Adoption of machine 
learning 
technology 
can 
further 
enhance 
the 
efﬁciency 
of 
computational 
lithography [71].

from aggressive design (top) and nonaggressive design (bottom). (Reprint from Ref. [72] with

2.7.4 
Photoresists 
All the techniques described in previous sections are merely forming a light image 
on the surface of a photoresist layer. The mask pattern that represents an IC design 
will have to transform into a photoresist pattern so that it can be further transferred

[Page 49]
into the substrate material. The description of basic techniques for processing 
photoresists, such as exposure, baking, development, etc., can be found in many 
literatures [3, 73, 74]. Section 2.3 of this chapter has also given a general overview of 
the basics of photoresists and lithography process. Although the basic process steps 
are more or less the same whether for patterning of sub-micrometer features or for 
patterning of sub-100 nm features, more technical difﬁculties and challenges are 
encountered when the photoresist patterns are less than 100 nm. The four key 
parameters for photoresists and their processes at sub-100 nm resolution regime 
are sensitivity, contrast, line edge roughness (LER), and etch resistance. The etch 
resistance will be discussed in Chap. 7. This section will focus on sensitivity, 
contrast, and line edge/linewidth roughness. 
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
49
2.7.4.1 
Sensitivity 
Since the optical lithography moved to deep UV wavelength at 248 nm, chemically 
ampliﬁed (CA) resists, which are distinctively of high sensitivity, have become the 
working resists. The CA resist technology was pioneered by IBM in the 1980s in 
order to accommodate early deep UV light sources [75]. At the time, the choice of 
exposure light sources was still mercury lamps which were widely used for g-line 
and i-line optical lithography. Although excimer lasers emerged as new sources of 
deep UV wavelength radiation, they were in a very early development phase, and 
there was concern over their ability to operate reliably. Mercury lamps, already 
proven in manufacturing, were deemed a more reasonable approach. However, the 
deep UV spectrum in a mercury lamp was rather weak and hence required very fast 
resists, leading to the development of CA resists. 
The high sensitivity characteristics of CA resists rely on a substance called 
“photoacid generator” (PAG). In these resists, the lithographically important chem-
istry is not driven directly by photons absorbed during the exposure step. Instead, an 
acid formed upon exposure of a PAG catalyzes chemical changes in the resist during 
the thermal baking step, which is called post-exposure baking (PEB). Since the 
reaction is catalytic, the acid is regenerated after each chemical reaction; the same 
acid molecule can participate in further reactions, hence named chemical ampliﬁca-
tion. Each PAG can induce 800–1200 more photoacid molecules after the PEB step 
[76]. These photoacid molecules convert the exposed region of the CA resist into 
soluble or insoluble, depending whether it is a positive or a negative resist. Because 
only a small amount of photon energy is needed to generate the initial photoacid 
“seeds” and the major chemical process occurs during PEB, they are therefore of 
very high sensitivity. While conventional i-line resists need more than 100 mJ/cm2 
photon energy, CA resists need only 10–20 mJ/cm2 photon energy. 
The very high catalytic chain length in CA resists, which provides the high 
sensitivity, is also problematic because the generated photoacid molecules can be 
deactivated by airborne amine diffusion into the resist, which results in “T-topping” 
or swelling in resist pattern. Amine concentrations as small as 5 ppb in air are enough 
to induce the T-topping, depending on the length of time the resist is exposed to

[Page 50]
ambient air having trace of amine. For example, Fig. 2.32 compares the CA resist 
patterns when the exposed resist was developed immediately without delay 
(Fig. 2.32a) and when there was a delay of 45 min in clean room ambient before 
development (Fig. 2.32b). T-topping is formed because of the exposure to ambient 
air. When the wafer was delayed for 45 min but kept in the exposure tool where the 
environment is enclosed and well ﬁltrated, no T-topping was formed as shown in 
Fig. 2.32c. Improvement in resist chemistry to make it less sensitive to amines is a 
possible solution. But the most straightforward approach is to control airborne 
contamination with robust ﬁlters in the exposure tool or in the clean room. Chemical 
ﬁlters, in some cases, have moved from traditional carbon-based media to new 
formulations, including highly porous polymeric materials that can provide 
improved removal efﬁciency and capacity. High rate of chemical ampliﬁcation 
during the PEB step also increases process sensitivity to resist baking cycles, 
necessitating extremely tight control of baking temperatures and times. From the 
process control point of view, too high sensitivity of a CA resist may not be 
beneﬁcial. A compromise in sensitivity could render a CA resist more stable in a 
production process environment. 
50
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.32 SEM images of CA patterns (a) without delay, (b) with 45 min delay in clean room 
ambient, (c) with 45 min delay but kept inside exposure tool (150 nm CD and ArF lithography). 
(Reprint from Ref. [77] with permission) 
Coming to the EUV era, the chemically ampliﬁed resists (CARs) used in DUV 
lithography are no longer suitable because they are more transparent at 13 nm 
wavelength than at 193 nm wavelength. A series of non-CA resists containing 
metallic elements have been developed. Details of EUV resists have been described 
in Sect. 2.4.2.4. 
2.7.4.2 
Contrast 
High contrast is the key to achieve high resolution in a photoresist. There are two 
different contrasts: one is the contrast of optical imaging, and the other is the contrast 
of photoresist, each of which has different deﬁnition [3]. However, one can tell the 
high contrast optical imaging or high contrast resist structure simply by comparing 
the aerial images (Fig. 2.33) or the resist proﬁles (Fig. 2.34a, b). It must be pointed 
out that high contrast of optical imaging does not necessarily lead to high contrast

[Page 51]
resist proﬁle, whereas a high contrast photoresist can, to some extent, compensate a 
low contrast optical imaging to produce high resolution resist patterns. That is why 
the photoresist is a vital part of resolution enhancement technology.

To achieve high contrast, the resist material should be less absorptive to UV 
radiation. With low UV absorption, it is easier to achieve uniform distribution of 
photon energy through the depth of photoresist layer. Too absorptive will result in 
more photon energy being absorbed at the top and less at the bottom. As a 
consequence, the development of resist will be fast at the top and slow down through 
the depth, resulting in slopped pattern proﬁle instead of a sharp and vertical resist 
proﬁle. Unfortunately, materials are always more absorptive at shorter wavelength

[Page 52]
radiation. Unlike i-line resists which work at 1 μm thickness, CA resists for deep UV 
have to work at less than 0.5 μm thickness in order to compensate for the high 
absorption of photons. Alternatively, a bilayer resist system can be used, with a thin 
layer of resist on top as the imaging layer [78]. 
52
2
Optical Lithography
2.7.4.3 
Line Edge/Linewidth Roughness (LER/LWR) 
For very small feature dimension, the edge roughness of developed resist lines can 
be a signiﬁcant fraction of the linewidth. A measure of the line edge roughness 
(LER) is the 3 sigma standard deviation of the actual line edge relative to an average 
line edge. The roughness can be quantitatively measured by a scanning electron 
microscope (SEM) or an atomic force microscope (AFM). Figure 2.35 shows some 
CA resist lines with average linewidth of 111 nm. The roughness, however, is 37 nm 
as measured by SEM. The LER for CA resists at sub-100 nm feature dimension has 
become a serious issue. LER was included in the International Technology Roadmap 
for Semiconductors (ITRS) since 2001, as one of the key requirements on photore-
sists. ITRS indicates that the LER should be below 5% of the critical dimension 
(CD). For the 90 nm IC technology node, the LER should be less than 4.5 nm. While 
the functional lines patterned for ICs have shrunk below 22 nm, LER did not shrink 
accordingly. This is particularly true for CA resists. 
It is still unclear what the causes of LER are. A number of statistically ﬂuctuating 
effects at these small dimensions may contribute to the LER, such as shot noise 
(photon ﬂux variations), statistical distributions of chemical species in the resist 
(such as photoacid generators), the random walk nature of acid diffusion during 
chemical ampliﬁcation, and the nonzero size of resist polymers being dissolved 
during development [79]. Careful measurements and evaluation of LER data have 
ruled out the correlation between polymer molecule size and LER [80]. There have 
been attempts of using statistical modelling to explain the LER measurement data. 
The general trend is that the higher the resist sensitivity, the rougher the resulting line
Fig. 2.35 SEM image of 
CA resist lines with average 
linewidth of 111 nm and line 
edge roughness of 37 nm

[Page 53]
edge. CA resists have not been able to meet the requirements of both high sensitivity 
and low LER. By analytical modelling of three key resist processing steps, the 
exposure, PEB, and development, it was demonstrated that the observed trade-off 
between resist sensitivity and LER is fundamental, which implies the basic limitation 
of achieving simultaneously low roughness, low dose, and high resolution in any 
standard CA resist process [81].
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
53
Fig. 2.36 Schematic 
illustration of linewidth 
roughness (LWR) caused by 
line edge roughness (LER). 
(Reprinted from Ref. [82] 
with permission) 
At the feature dimension below 16 nm, LER can lead to linewidth variation. A 
new term called linewidth roughness (LWR) was adopted to reﬂect the seriousness 
of problem. Figure 2.36 schematically illustrates the LWR. At the nominal linewidth 
of 10, the LER results in the variation of linewidth from 9 to 11. Correlations 
between the LER and LWR exist statistically [82], and the LWR can deﬁnitely 
lead to the variation of device property [83]. The current IC manufacturing requires 
the resist to satisfy LWR <15%, in addition to resolution <10 nm and exposure dose 
as low as 20 mJ/cm2 [84]. Fortunately, the LER became less pronounced in EUV 
lithography, because of using non-CA resists with inorganic components [85]. 
In addition to the aforementioned properties, there are a few other factors that can 
critically inﬂuence the production worthiness of 193 nm resists. They are the 
linearity of translating mask pattern to wafer, line slimming due to electron radiation 
during SEM inspection, and pattern collapse during wet development. They are 
brieﬂy described in the following. 
Usually, the mask features are linearly imaged onto a wafer. A variation of mask 
CD will cause variation of wafer CD, except 4 times smaller if it is a 4:1 reduction 
optics. However, at sub-100 nm resolution regime with low k1 factor, small varia-
tions of CD on the mask can cause disproportionately large changes in wafer CDs. 
This ampliﬁcation of mask errors is called the mask error enhancement factor 
(MEEF). For a linear imaging, MEEF = 1. If MEEF >1, the errors in mask CDs 
are nonlinearly transferred to the wafer even if the mask CD itself gets geometrically 
reduced by the proper ratio (4:1). For the dense line/space pattern, both theory and 
experiment indicate that mask errors are ampliﬁed by 2.5 times at k1 = 0.35 
[86]. Fundamentally, the nonlinear imaging originates from the decrease of aerial

[Page 54]
image contrast because of the loss of high spatial frequencies in projection imaging. 
An easy solution to counter the large MEEF can be simply biasing the mask feature. 
In the meantime, mask errors and defects have to be reduced at low k1 optical 
lithography, to avoid the detrimental effect of MEEF. 
54
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.37 Resist pattern 
collapse 
With IC manufacturing entering sub-100 nm regime, wafer inspections have 
widely adopted CD-SEM, a scanning electron microscope-based CD inspection 
and measurement, because of its much higher resolution capability than optical 
inspection methods. However, it has been observed that patterns of 193 nm resist 
shrink under electron irradiation during CD-SEM inspection, although very low 
electron beam energy (below 1 keV) and beam current (below 10pA) are used in 
CD-SEM. The shrinkage due to electron irradiation ranges from 0.2 nm to 0.6 nm 
per measurement scan for different groups of 193 nm resists [87]. The longer the 
electron beam dwells on the resist pattern, the more signiﬁcant the feature shrinkage 
becomes. Among the two common polymer systems of 193 nm CA resists, acrylate 
and methacrylate, the methacrylate system seems to suffer more severe shrinkages. 
After 30 scan measurements at 800 eV electron energy, the methacrylate-based resist 
shrinks up to 30 nm for contact hole patterns [88]. The exact mechanism of e-beam-
induced shrinkage is not clear. While the 193 nm resists will still be improved, a 
reasonable approach to this problem is to readjust the CD measurement procedure to 
minimize resist deformation. 
Collapse of high aspect ratio resist patterns is a well-known phenomenon. After 
development and rinsing, capillary forces are present between closely packed resist 
structures. Unbalanced capillary forces lead to a net force on the pattern during 
drying process. Pattern collapse occurs if the capillary forces exceed the elastic 
restoring forces of the polymer [89]. Figure 2.37 demonstrates the typical resist 
pattern collapse phenomenon. A ﬁgure of merit for the collapse behavior is the 
critical aspect ratio of collapse (CARC), which is deﬁned as the aspect ratio for a 
given width and pitch with 0% of line collapse [90]. The worst line collapsing 
happens for asymmetric structures consisting of an asymmetric pitch. On the other 
hand, pattern collapse occurs only for unbalanced forces. Therefore, the collapsing

[Page 55]
effect is more probable in the middle part of a line than at their end, because water 
level is easier to equalize at the line end. For the same reason, the probability of line 
collapse is higher for longer lines in comparison to shorter lines. Because the 
capillary force is proportional to the surface tension of the rinsing liquid, using a 
supercritical ﬂuid, such as liquid CO2, instead of water for rinsing can eliminate the 
capillary force effect [91]. The simplest approach to reducing pattern collapse is to 
reduce the resist ﬁlm thickness. However, for single layer 193 nm resists, the lower 
limit for the resist thickness has to be in the range of 300–500 nm because of the etch 
resistance consideration. In this case, using multilayer resist systems can be an 
option. 
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
55
2.7.5 
Multi-patterning 
Before the EUV lithography was ready for production, the IC industry was left with 
the only choice of 193 nm immersion lithography (193i). All the aforementioned 
RETs were employed, which helped to reduce the k1 factor. However, these were not 
enough for the IC industry to keep pace with the demand for further reduction of 
circuit feature dimensions. Starting from 20 nm node downward, the circuit features 
became too dense on the mask, making it more difﬁcult to pattern discernible 
features on the wafer. Particularly when OPC and sub-resolution assist features 
(SRAF) were used, as seen in Fig. 2.38 [43], there were simply not enough spaces 
to place these OPC features. On the other hand, isolated features or less dense 
features are much easier to pattern, as has been illustrated in Fig. 2.21. The solution 
to the dense feature problem is to split the circuit layout into two separate masks and 
image them in two patterning process, the so-called double patterning (DP). 
There are three variations of double patterning methods: litho-freeze-litho-etch 
(LFLE); litho-etch-litho-etch (LELE); and self-aligned double patterning (SADP). 
Figure 2.39 schematically compares the LFLE and LELE with single patterning 
(Fig. 2.39a). In the LFLE patterning process (Fig. 2.39b), the exposed and developed 
resist structures are chemically modiﬁed (freeze) before applying the second layer
Fig. 2.38 Example of original mask layout to OPC/SRAF-added mask layout to ﬁnal resist image. 
(Courtesy of David Abercrombie, Semiconductor Engineering [43])

[Page 56]
resist and exposure. The “freeze” makes the resist no longer respond to the second 
exposure. The combination of both exposures produces dense resist patterns. In the 
LELE patterning process (Fig. 2.39c), two layers of hard masks are used. Each hard 
mask is patterned by photoresist exposure separately. The composition of both 
masking patterns becomes the ﬁnal dense masking patterns. Both LFLE and LELE 
double the pattern density. However, both require two sets of masks and two 
exposures, which have to be accurately aligned to each other within 1.5–3 nm.
56
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.39 Comparison of (a) single patterning with (b) LFLE and (c) LELE 
While the LFLE and LELE require very stringent alignment accuracy for the 
two-step exposure, self-aligned double patterning (SADP) is much simpler. As 
illustrated in Fig. 2.40, SADP only involves single exposure. The doubling of pattern 
density is realized by spacers. As the deposition of spacer material is naturally 
conformable to the resist pattern, no alignment is required. The directional plasma 
etching can remove the top and bottom spacer material to create spacer patterns 
which double the density of original resist pattern. The spacer then acts as mask to

[Page 57]
pattern the hard mask layer. Finally, the spacer is removed by isotropic etching. 
Section 9.2 of Chap. 9 will further elaborate this type of indirect nanofabrication. 
Although the SADP is simple to implement and less process steps are involved, it is 
only suitable for line/space type of pattern. For nonperiodic pattern such as logic ICs, 
the patterns created by spacer may not be the patterns needed in real circuits. In 
practice, all the above three double patterning methods are being used. 
2.7
Optical Lithography at Low k1 Factor
57
Fig. 2.40 Self-aligned double patterning (SADP) process. (Courtesy of Cepheiden’s artwork from 
Wikimedia Commons) 
Double patterning enabled the 193i lithography to go down 20/16/14 nm IC 
technology nodes. For 10 nm, 7 nm, or 5 nm nodes, triple patterning (TP) or 
quadrupole patterning (QP) has to be used. By repeatedly using the spacer concept 
eight times (octuple patterning), 6.25 nm half-pitch line/space patterns were created 
by 193i lithography [92]. The multi-patterning has truly broken the theoretical limit 
of k1 factor, and even with EUV lithography, the multi-patterning will be needed 
when IC processing comes down to sub-5 nm regime (Fig. 2.19). 
2.7.6 
Design-Technology Co-optimization (DTCO) 
As described in previous sections, to lower the k1 factor, many RETs have to be used. 
The original mask layout for an IC design has changed considerably for the k1 < 0.3 
regime, after applying PSM, OPC, SRAF, and ILT, which can be vastly different

[Page 58]
from the printed photoresist image on a wafer. In the earlier above 100 nm process 
technologies, IC designers do not have to know the lithography processes. They only 
need to follow a set of design rules which deﬁnes how small a feature should be and 
how close two features to each other should be, etc. Since IC manufacturing entered 
90 nm node and below, the traditional implementation of more sophisticated RETs 
has seen design rules exploding. For example, the number of rules at 90 nm node 
rose four to ﬁve times, many of which are never explained to a designer whether they 
are related to lithography, etch, CMP, other manufacturing needs, or all of the above. 
58
2
Optical Lithography
On the other hand, complicated RET measures and addition of RET features to 
the original design have put considerable pressure on IC process engineers. Some of 
the RET modiﬁcations may be sound from the optimization of optical imaging, but 
may be completely unmanufacturable, or with very low manufacturing yield. At 
90 nm, IC manufacturers ﬁnd that only less than half of IC designs operate as 
expected and more than half of them need a complete mask “re-spin” for acceptable 
yield and performance. With the design costs approaching $10 million or more and 
mask costs alone nearly $1 million, Design for Manufacturability (DFM) has 
become a key issue, and IC designers must consider manufacturability issue 
throughout the design cycle [93]. 
The methodology of DFM is to start with identiﬁcation of manufacturability and 
then goes back to designers to tune the design in order to achieve the maximum 
manufacturing yield. One of the DFM approaches favored by IC manufacturers is to 
predict, during the design stage by model-based computer simulation, how the ideal 
design layout will print as contours in silicon. These contours are equivalent to 3D 
structure proﬁles produced through the ﬂow of manufacturing processes, which are 
accurate representation of the ﬁnal manufacturing results. From that, designers can 
predict the “hot spots” where the circuits tend to fail. Once the hot spots are 
identiﬁed, the design layouts will be manipulated to make sure those potential failure 
spots are eliminated [94]. This model-based prediction is different from previously 
described OPC simulation. It has incorporated not only optical imaging but also 
manufacturing parameters. A new set of lithography-friendly design rules can be 
established by the manufacturing process-related computer simulation [95, 96]. 
While the DFM is the design community responding to the manufacturing 
community, the introduction of double patterning below 20 nm node puts consider-
able constrains on designing process, and the optimization is not only required at the 
designer side but also required at the manufacturer side. Design-technology 
co-optimization (DTCO) became the norm [97]. DTCO encompassed DFM. How-
ever, the optimization at the manufacturer side is not only on the existing process 
technology but also on the choice of different technologies and different IC archi-
tectures, with the end goal to improve performance, power efﬁciency, transistor 
density, and cost. It should be pointed out that the situation became relaxed since the 
introduction of EUV lithography which led to the k1 factor to jump to as high as 0.56. 
The EUV lithography enabled a different meaning of DFM (Design Friendly 
Manufacturing) [98]. However, the luck will soon run out as multi-patterning will 
be needed even for EUV lithography at sub-5 nm node, as indicated in Fig. 2.19.

[Page 59]
2.8
Near-Field Optical Lithography
59
2.8 
Near-Field Optical Lithography 
Projection optical lithography has been well developed over the course of more than 
40 years. It has become the mainstream lithography technology for high-volume IC 
manufacturing. However, one should not forget that optical lithography was origi-
nated from contact printing. Contact printing relies on a mask aligner to print a mask 
pattern onto a photoresist in 1:1 ratio. Although mask aligner is no longer used in 
modern IC manufacturing for many reasons such as low throughput, no image 
reduction, and easy damage to mask plate, it still has a role to play in large feature 
size lithography, particularly in manufacturing of MEMS (microelectromechanical 
system) devices. In recent years, contact optical lithography has been revitalized as 
an alternative low-cost technique for sub-100 nm patterning technique. 
It is known that projection optical lithography is a far-ﬁeld optical imaging 
process, which has a fundamental resolution limit of λ/2 where λ is the wavelength. 
Contact optical lithography is by nature a near-ﬁeld optical imaging process. There is 
effectively no space for light wave to travel between the mask opening and photo-
resist layer, except within the photoresist layer. At such an intimate contact, light 
waves no longer propagate as sinusoidal wave but as evanescent wave, which decay 
exponentially with distance. Therefore, near-ﬁeld optical lithography is also called 
evanescent near-ﬁeld optical lithography (ENFOL) [99]. The evanescent wave no 
longer behaves as sinusoidal wave which diffracts through small openings, and the 
imaging resolution is diffraction limited. The resolution of near-ﬁeld optical lithog-
raphy is only limited by the mask opening, not by optical wavelength. That is the 
reason that near-ﬁeld optical lithography can achieve sub-100 nm resolution. One 
only needs to make the mask features small enough. 
Near-ﬁeld optical lithography evolved from the contact printing but is not the 
same as a conventional contact printing. Instead of using a hard glass mask, a thin 
conformable mask is used. Figure 2.41 shows the setup of near-ﬁeld optical lithog-
raphy, where the mask is made of 2-μm-thick silicon nitride membrane with chrome 
patterns. The membrane mask is supported by a silicon frame and mounted onto a 
vacuum chuck. The intimate and conformable contact is realized by vacuum 
between the membrane mask and photoresist-coated substrate. Though not a trans-
parent mask, the thin nitride membrane can transmit 61% of g-line wavelength 
(436 nm) and 29% of h-line wavelength (405 nm) irradiation from a broadband 
mercury arc lamp [100], which is adequate to expose commercial g-line photoresists. 
UV illumination
Membrane mask 
Vacuum 
Resist-coated 
substrate 
Fig. 2.41 Setup of near-ﬁeld optical lithography. (Reprint from Ref. [100] with permission)

[Page 60]
60
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.42 Computer-
simulated contours of 
squared electric-ﬁeld 
intensity |E|2 /|E0|2 where | 
E0|2 is the incident intensity. 
(Reprint from Ref. [100] 
with permission) 
Because the evanescent wave can only travel very short distance, the ENFOL can 
only be performed on very thin photoresist, normally less than 100 nm thickness. 
Such a characteristic is best explained by the computer simulation of electromagnetic 
ﬁeld distribution, as shown in Fig. 2.42. It shows contour plots of the squared 
electric-ﬁeld intensity for a cross section through a 140-nm-period and 40-nm-
thick Cr mask illuminated from above with 436 nm light. The exposure is into a 
60-nm-thick photoresist layer on silicon. The black and white regions correspond to 
the ratios of |E|2 /|E0|2 from <0.1 to >1.3, where |E0|2 is the incident intensity. 
Two properties are revealed for the evanescent wave propagation. The ﬁrst is that 
the penetration of evanescent wave is only about the top 40 nm of the resist layer, as 
seen in the bright region from the Cr mask opening into the resist layer, which 
implies that complete resist clearance of exposed patterns will be difﬁcult to achieve. 
In this case, a short O2 plasma etching will have to be performed to remove this 
residual resist layer prior to transferring the resist pattern into the underlying silicon. 
The second is the large ﬁeld enhancements seen at the edges of the mask pattern 
made of metal conductor, where the bright regions represent |E|2 /|E0|2 > 1, indicating 
that the local electrical ﬁeld intensity can be greater than the incident intensity. The 
local enhancement is strongly dependent on the sharpness of the edges, which is a 
possible explanation that patterns produced by evanescent near-ﬁeld lithography are 
always of high line edge roughness. 
The fact that electrical ﬁeld of incident light has been enhanced or ampliﬁed 
indicates there is something else happening in the evanescent near-ﬁeld imaging 
process. This is the surface plasmon polariton (SPP) phenomena. SPP is a charge 
density wave on the surface of material which has plenty of free electrons, such as 
silver or gold, with silver being the best material for exciting the SPP. It is an

[Page 61]
interaction between the electromagnetic ﬁeld of incident light wave and the 
conducting free electrons on the surface of metal thin ﬁlm or nanostructures 
[101]. The SPP wavelength depends on the wavelength of incident light and the 
permittivity of conducting material, which can be much shorter than the incident 
light wavelength [102]. 
2.8
Near-Field Optical Lithography
61
Fig. 2.43 (a) Computer simulation of near-ﬁeld distribution, (b) experimental lithography result. 
(Reprint from Ref. [103] with permission) 
Since the SPP wave can propagate along the interface of the conducting material 
and a dielectric material, interference can occur, resulting in standing waves with 
periodically distributed maxima and minima. Figure 2.43a shows the computer-
simulated near-ﬁeld distribution for two well-separated mask opening aperture 
illuminated by g-line (λ = 436 nm) light wave [103]. The mask material is silver. 
The separation of the two apertures is 300 nm. The simulation reveals clearly that the 
electrical ﬁeld maxima exist not only at the mask apertures but also between the 
apertures where there are no openings to the incident light. This is the result of 
interference of SPP waves from both sides of the aperture. The computer simulation 
has been proved by experiment with exposure using a g-line illumination source and 
50-nm-thick photoresist layer. A vacuum chuck is used to pull the mask onto the 
resist-coated substrate to ensure intimate contact. Figure 2.43b shows the exposed 
50 nm resist line patterns. Although the pitch of line grating on mask is 300 nm, the 
actual photoresist pattern has a pitch of only 100 nm. The two extra lines between the 
mask features are created by the interference standing wave of SPP. It is noticed that 
the resist feature size is only 1/10 of the exposure wavelength. Similar SPP inter-
ference can also happen within the opening of a mask feature, which creates smaller 
features in photoresist than the mask feature itself [104]. It is also noticed that the 
LER (line edge roughness) is quite signiﬁcant. This is caused by local enhancement 
of electrical ﬁeld. 
Because photoresist can be exposed by SPP, not necessarily by incident light, 
then it is not necessary to have the mask in intimate contact with photoresist for 
patterning. A pattern can be printed onto photoresist, as long as there is a mechanism 
to generate the SPP pattern. This is the concept of silver superlens [105]. An example 
of superlens structure is illustrated in Fig. 2.44. A conventional photomask (not the

[Page 62]
membrane-type 
conformable 
mask) 
is 
coated 
with 
30-nm-thick 
polymeth-
ylmethacrylate (PMMA) as a spacer; then 50-nm-thick silver is coated as the 
superlens, as well as 30-nm-thick SiO2 as a protective layer. Although light is not 
directly shining on a photoresist layer, exposure of the photoresist can still happen by 
the SPP radiation excited in the silver layer with the photomask. With this superlens, 
110 nm line pattern has been transferred to photoresist with an i-line illumination 
[106]. Along this direction, plasmonic reﬂective lens and plasmonic cavity lens have 
also been developed for near-ﬁeld optical lithography [107]. 
62
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.44 Schematic of SPP 
superlens structure. 
(Reprinted from [106] with 
permission) 
As described above, near-ﬁeld optical lithography can indeed realize sub-100 nm 
patterning of photoresist using much longer illumination wavelength. However, 
there are a number of inherent drawbacks in this technology:
•
Intimate contact is required. Because the near-ﬁeld optical lithography relies on 
evanescent wave which decays exponentially with distance, the mask has to be 
intimately in contact with photoresist. This is the reason that a membrane-type 
conformable mask has to be used. A membrane mask is fragile. The requirement 
of conformable contact with photoresist-coated substrate makes it even more 
vulnerable to break. Membrane mask is also difﬁcult to be made into large size. 
The superlens approach can alleviate the awkwardness of using a membrane 
mask. It still requires the metallic superlens layer to be in intimate contact with the 
photomask, which makes the reusability of patterned photomask greatly reduced. 
Some research has demonstrated that with the help of plasmonic cavity lens and 
off-axis illumination (OAI as introduced in Sect. 2.7.1), up to 80 nm air gap 
between the plasmonic lens and photoresist layer can be allowed [108].
•
Exposure depth is very limited. Both evanescent wave and SPP wave decay very 
rapidly in photoresist, resulting in a very shallow exposure depth. The thickness 
of photoresist for near-ﬁeld optical lithography should be no more than 100 nm. 
Such a thin photoresist layer makes subsequent pattern transfer by etching very 
difﬁcult. Multilayer resist structure is commonly used, whereby the near-ﬁeld 
optical lithography only patterns the top imaging layer and the resist pattern is 
transferred to underlayer by reactive ion etching [106].
•
LER is signiﬁcant. Because the charge density induced in the metal mask by 
incident light is highly concentrated at any sharp edges of a mask feature opening, 
the locally enhanced intensity can cause signiﬁcant line edge roughness (LER) in 
the exposed photoresist features. There are two approaches that can reduce the

[Page 63]
2.9
Talbot Optical Lithography
63
Fig. 2.45 Soft PDMS mask 
for near-ﬁeld optical 
lithography [110] 
LER: one is to improve the mask making process to reduce the LER of mask 
features; the second is to improve the photoresist material to make it less sensitive 
to the speckles of image intensity. 
Nevertheless, near-ﬁeld optical lithography is a simple and low-cost technology 
for making sub-100 nm patterns. There have been attempts to extend the technology 
for high-throughput patterning. Reported approaches include employing bowtie 
antenna (see Sect. 11.4.3 of Chap. 11) apertures as plasmonic lens array [109], or 
scanning an optical ﬁber probe in near ﬁeld (see Sect. 5.3.2 of Chap. 5). Even if it 
may never become viable in mass manufacturing of ICs, this does not stop the 
technology to be used in laboratory for research wherever there are requirements for 
making sub-100 nm structures. Its similarity to conventional contact printing using a 
mask aligner makes the technology much more accessible than the state-of-the-art 
optical projection lithography. 
The key to realize high resolution by near-ﬁeld lithography is the near ﬁeld which 
requires the mask in intimate contact with substrate. None of the aforementioned 
masks are able to be used repeatedly. There is a simple way to realize repeatable 
intimate contact between mask and substrate, which is to use a soft mask. The typical 
material for making soft mask is polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS). The softness of 
PDMS enables the intimate contact with substrate, as illustrated in Fig. 2.45 
[110]. Since the PDMS is soft and elastic, it can be rolled onto a glass tube. By 
installing a UV light inside the glass tube, it becomes a rolling mask to carry out 
near-ﬁeld optical lithography in a roll-to-roll manner [111]. A company named 
Rolith, which was acquired by Canadian company Metamaterial Technologies Inc. 
in 2016, commercialized the technology and built industrial-scale lithography 
systems. 
2.9 
Talbot Optical Lithography 
There is an interesting optical phenomenon which was discovered by Henry Fox 
Talbot almost 200 years ago. He found that when a monochromic coherent light is 
shining through a mask with periodical openings, the images of mask openings 
reappeared at different distances from the mask and at some distance these images

[Page 64]
can be smaller than the dimension of mask opening, which was later named after him
as the Talbot effect [
]. The Talbot effect is actually the interference of waves
behind the grating mask, as illustrated in Fig.
where light waves coming
through the mask openings interfere with each other and form black and white
nodes. As seen in Fig.
, at ZT distance from the mask, the interference images
2.46
2.46
112
64
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.46 Optical interference images of a grating mask due to Talbot effect

[Page 65]
of same pitch as the mask grating reappear itself; at ZT/2 position the interference 
images are also of the same pitch but have a 1/2 pitch shift laterally. The interesting 
phenomenon is that at the ZT/4 position, the interference images have half the pitch 
of original mask grating or double the resolution. This is the key that Talbot effect 
can obtain high resolution grating patterns with a low resolution mask. These 
particular distances where the interference images are formed are called Talbot 
length and are expressed as Eq. 2.8, where a is the period of grating and λ is the 
wavelength of incident light.
2.10
Interferometric Optical Lithography
65
ZT = 2a2 
λ
ð2:8Þ 
The Talbot effect can be utilized to pattern structures much smaller than mask 
features. The implementation of Talbot optical lithography is rather simple. It is 
similar to the proximity optical lithography using a mask aligner. The only differ-
ence is that the distance between mask and substrate has to be precisely controlled 
[113]. It was lately found that the distance between the mask and the substrate could 
be changed by a Talbot period during the exposure without altering the aerial image. 
So it does not require precise positioning at the Talbot distances but only a displace-
ment of the mask or substrate by a Talbot period independently of the start and end 
positions. The new optical lithography technique is called displacement Talbot 
lithography (DTL), which has been commercialized based on the setup of mask 
aligner [114]. 75 nm gratings have been achieved by the technique with a 150 nm 
mask patterns exposed by a deep UV source of 266 nm wavelength [115]. Complex 
patterns, as long as they are periodical, can also be imaged by Talbot lithography 
[116]. Though it is simple, the Talbot lithography has limited capability for high 
resolution lithography. It only works for periodical patterns. The contrast of Talbot 
image is poor and for sub-100 nm patterning, high resolution mask and short 
wavelength illumination are necessary. 
2.10 
Interferometric Optical Lithography 
Another simple, but capable of sub-100 nm resolution, and photon-based lithogra-
phy is interferometric optical lithography. Optical interference refers to the interac-
tion of light waves which are correlated or coherent with each other, either because 
they come from the same source or because they have the same or nearly the same 
wavelengths. The most famous example of optical interference is Young’s double-
slit experiment. Two beams from the double slit are both of same wavelength and 
same phase because they emit from the same light source. The interference of the 
two beams causes distribution of light intensity with periodical maxima and minima. 
Interferometric lithography (IL) is the use of a small number of coherent optical 
beams (minimum of two) incident from different directions onto a 2D (thin) or 3D 
(thick) photosensitive layer to produce an interference pattern whose intensity

[Page 66]
distribution is recorded in the photosensitive layer, as illustrated in Fig. 2.47. IL is a  
well-established lithographic technique. The use of IL for photoresist patterning 
goes back to the 1970s when it was mainly used to produce hologram plates 
[117, 118]. The alternative names are interference lithography or holographic 
lithography. 
66
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.47 Schematic of 
interferometric lithography 
θ 
Beam 1
Beam 2 
photoresist 
substrate 
The intensity of interference pattern at the photoresist surface can be expressed as 
Eq. 2.9 [119]. 
I x
ð Þ = 2 E
j j2 1 þ σpol cos 2kxsin θ
ð
Þ
 
, 
k = 2nπ 
λ
ð2:9Þ 
where σpol = 1 for TE (transverse-electric) polarization or σpol = cos(2θ) for TM 
(transverse-magnetic) polarization. λ is the illumination wavelength, θ is the angle of 
incident beam relative to normal as indicated in Fig. 2.47, and n is the refractive 
index. For light passing through air into photoresist, n = 1. The period of the 
interference pattern along the x direction is λ/2nsin(θ). For a ﬁxed wavelength, 
periodical lines with different pitches can be obtained by varying the angle of 
incident beams. At a ﬁxed angle of incidence, further increase of resolution can be 
achieved by using short wavelength of illumination. It is apparent that at the 
maximum oblique angle of incidence (θ = 90°), the minimum possible pitch is λ/ 
2, which represents the resolution limit of IL. For half-pitch feature, the limit is λ/4. 
Increasing n or decreasing λ can both increase the resolution. The refractive index 
can be increased by immersion lithography. There has been demonstration of 
29.9 nm half-pitch lines by IL with the combination of 193 nm wavelength and 
high index ﬂuid immersion imaging [41]. IL with EUV wavelength has demon-
strated 12.5 nm half-pitch features [120]. The IL certainly provides a very simple 
solution to push down further the resolution of optical lithography without using a 
mask and its associated complications such as phase-shifting and OPC. 
The basic setup for IL is very simple. It only needs a laser, some lenses, and 
mirrors [121]. Figure 2.48 shows schematically a setup of IL system. A laser beam is

[Page 67]
focused through a pinhole (to improve coherence) and collimated through a colli-
mator lens and then split into two beams which are then reﬂected by two mirrors onto 
a photoresist-coated substrate. An interference light intensity distribution is formed 
and the photoresist is exposed by the interference light. For the lasers, early work 
was based on continuous-wave (cw) single-mode lasers such as the Ar ion laser at 
488 nm (Ar+) and the He-Cd laser at 442 nm. With the commercial development of 
i-line lithography (365 nm), attention shifted to the cw Ar++ lines at 364 and 352 nm 
and to the 355 nm third harmonic of a YAG laser to take advantage of the availability 
of commercial i-line resists. Further pursuing of short wavelength has seen the use of 
the intra-cavity doubled Ar ion line at 244 nm and ﬁfth-harmonic YAG at 213 nm. 
At wavelengths shorter than 257 nm, it is difﬁcult to obtain sources with temporal 
and spatial coherence and output power sufﬁcient for conventional IL. ArF excimer 
laser has the necessary output power but is poor in temporal and spatial coherence. 
Hence, to expose gratings and grids of sub-100 nm period, achromatic interferomet-
ric lithography (AIL) is used. AIL utilizes a group of phase grating plates to 
modulate the incoming laser light, so that the spatial period of the printed grating 
is dependent only on the period of the parent gratings used in the interferometer 
regardless of the optical path or the wavelength and coherence of the source [122]. 
2.10
Interferometric Optical Lithography
67
