Fig. 2.48 Schematic of interferometric lithography setup 
IL has two distinctive advantages: One is its simplicity. In fact, any laboratories 
can install an IL system on an optical bench. Another is no need for a mask. The 
interference pattern is generated directly on a photoresist-coated substrate. The 
downside is that the interference pattern can only be in the form of straight lines 
from a single exposure, or dots if two exposures are carried out on the same substrate

[Page 68]
with the second exposure rotating 90° to the ﬁrst one. Whether straight lines or dots, 
they are periodical patterns with the pitch determined by the beam incident angle or 
wavelength of the laser. Although IL can only pattern periodical features, there are 
many applications which only require periodical features, for example, 2D and 3D 
photonic crystal structures [123], magnetic storage devices [124], etc. There was a 
report using seven laser beams shining from all directions through a thick lump of 
SU-8 photoresist, and complex 3D interference structures were formed as a photonic 
bandgap material [125]. 
68
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.49 Photoresist 
pattern produced by 
interference and projection 
hybrid lithography. (Reprint 
from Ref. [126] with 
permission) 
IL is not completely useless in IC manufacturing. It can be adapted to patterning 
IC design layouts. One of the ideas is hybrid maskless optical lithography, which 
uses IL to produce periodical patterns ﬁrst and then uses a conventional photomask 
to trim the interference patterns into desired arbitrary pattern shapes. Figure 2.49 
gives an example of the hybrid lithography, where a line/space pattern has been 
trimmed in the middle with a coarse dark ﬁeld mask. The trim mask is usually of low 
resolution. One can work out many ways of designing a trim mask to tailor the 
interference pattern into a desired IC layout. The key advantage of the hybrid 
approach is that the most difﬁcult part of lithography, i.e., the highest resolution 
patterning, is done by the maskless IL, which saves many troubles from using those 
RET masks mentioned earlier. 
The IL should, however, not be confused with imaging interferometric lithogra-
phy (IIL) technique. IIL is basically a projection optical lithography with a photo-
mask but combined with interference from a reference beam to enhance the 
resolution of projection lithography [127].

[Page 69]
2.11
Maskless Optical Lithography
69
2.11 
Maskless Optical Lithography 
“The devil is in the mask.” This was the message delivered by Burn Lin, the world 
renowned optical lithography expert, at the SPIE 20th Annual Conference on Optical 
Lithography in March 2007 [128]. That message still holds true at the present day. 
Optical lithography, since it started from contact printing, has always been relying 
on photomasks to deliver pattern designs to substrates. With IC manufacturing going 
into sub-50 nm feature dimension, photomasks have increasingly become a bottle-
neck as more and more RET features are incorporated into the photomasks, such as 
phase-shifting, SRAF, OPC, and masks generated by inverse lithography, as have 
been described in previous sections. With the introduction of multi-patterning, the 
number of masks needed has also increased considerably. For example, at 16 nm 
node the total number of masks is as high as 75 [129]. The adoption of EUV 
lithography has reduced the number of masks, but total cost of a mask set has greatly 
increased. Ten years ago, at the most advanced nodes, a leading-edge mask set is at 
the price of $2 million to $3 million, or roughly $100,000 for a single mask at critical 
feature level [130]. Today at 7 nm node, a single EUV mask can cost over 
$0.5 million and the whole set of masks costs up to $15 million. The escalating 
mask cost can have serious impact on the overall cost of low-volume IC production 
such as foundry runs and fabrication of application-speciﬁc integrated circuits 
(ASICs). For small-volume IC manufacturing, maskless lithography (ML2) was 
favored [131]. ML2 includes a number of optical and nonoptical technologies. The 
nonoptical ML2 techniques use electron or ion beams, which will be described in 
Chaps. 3 and 4. For optical ML2, a number of techniques exist. The interferometric 
lithography (IL) introduced in the previous section is a ML2 technique. However, as 
mentioned above, IL does not offer the total ﬂexibility because only periodical 
patterns can be generated by IL. In this section, some other optical ML2 techniques 
are introduced. 
Maskless optical lithography has been around for more than three decades. It was 
started in 1987 by the introduction of ﬁrst laser direct write tool [132]. It used a 
scanning laser beam to perform direct exposure of photoresist. The technology 
deemed to be slow and low throughput, therefore, was not able to compete with 
conventional projection optical lithography. However, it was highly suitable for 
lithography in making photomasks. At the time, mask making was dominantly using 
electron beam (e-beam) raster scan system. The ﬁrst laser tool CORE 2000 offered 
multi-laser beams with raster scan, which greatly increased the throughput of 
exposure. Since then, laser direct write tools have evolved from the ﬁrst generation 
of CORE 2000, CORE 2564 to ALTA 3000, ALTA 4300, and the latest model of 
ALTA 4700DP supplied by Applied Materials Inc. While the ALTA 4700 has 
comparable resolution as a 50 keV shaped e-beam mask writer, the writing time is 
nearly half of that of a 50 keV e-beam writer [133]. Over 75% of masks are now 
produced by laser tools. 
A simple schematic drawing of direct laser scan is shown in Fig. 2.50. A single 
laser beam from a frequency-doubled continuous-wave (cw) Ar ion laser at

[Page 70]
wavelength of 257 nm is split into 32 individually addressable beams, like a light 
brush. The brush is scanned across the plate by a 24-facet polygonal mirror. A single 
facet of polygon mirror scans the brush over an area of 4096 pixels wide by 32 pixels 
long. While the brush is scanned across the reticle by the facet of mirrors, the reticle 
stage is driven by a linear motor stage moving at constant velocity in the direction 
perpendicular to the scan. A set of brush strokes made during one stage move is 
called a stripe. A stripe is always 4096 pixels wide, but its length varies up to the 
length of the reticle depending on the pattern data. After a stripe is written, the stage 
performs a rapid retrace and moves to the starting position of the next stripe. This 
stage movement lines up each successive “brush stroke” to provide complete area 
coverage. One should remember that a mask feature is 4 times larger than the feature 
on a wafer, because of the 4:1 reduction of project optical lithography system. For 
ALTA 4700, the pixel size is 96 nm in the x-axis and 160 nm in the y-axis. 
Therefore, the resolution capability of a laser direct tool is adequate for patterning 
the masks for latest generation of IC manufacturing, including all the sub-resolution 
features used in RET. 
70
2
Optical Lithography
Fig. 2.50 Schematic of 
laser scan in making reticles 
In parallel to the aforementioned multi-beam laser tools, there is another class of 
optical maskless lithography which is based on micromirror array to reﬂect or deﬂect 
light [134]. This new scanning scheme was beneﬁted from the development of 
MEMS. A micromirror is a MEMS device in which the mirror surface is tilted by 
applying electrostatic ﬁeld between the mirror plate and an underlying electrode, as 
schematically shown in Fig. 2.51a. The dimension of a mirror is only a few tenths of 
square micrometers. A large closely packed mirror array can be fabricated by silicon 
surface micromachining process, such as the digital micromirror device (DMD) 
mass produced by Texas Instruments (TI). A typical DMD from TI (DLP4500) is 
measured 11.43 mm diagonal with 912 × 1140 array of aluminum-coated 
micromirrors and 7.6 μm mirror pitch. The micromirrors can have ±12° tilt angle 
(relative to ﬂat state). Because the deﬂection of each micromirror is individually 
controlled by a pair of underlying electrodes, it acts as a pixel. Light shining onto the 
mirror surface can be reﬂected to different angles, depending on the deﬂection of 
mirror surface. An aperture in an optical imaging system can control which angle of 
reﬂected light can pass through. A ﬂat mirror represents a “white” pixel, and a mirror 
deﬂected by one fourth of wave length at the edge of mirror represents a “black” 
pixel. Deﬂection in between represents a “gray” pixel, as schematically illustrated in

[Page 71]
Fig. 2.51b. Therefore, the micromirror array is commonly called spatial light mod-
ulator (SLM). There are other types of SLM that have been adopted for optical 
maskless lithography, including grating light valve (GLV) for phase modulation 
[135] and liquid crystal light valve for intensity modulation [136]. 
2.11
Maskless Optical Lithography
71
Fig. 2.51 (a) Micromirror structure, (b) white, gray, and black pixels, represented by different 
deﬂections of mirror surface 
Fig. 2.52 Setup of micromirror-based maskless lithography system. (Reprinted from [137] with 
permission) 
The micromirror-based laser lithography tool works like an optical stepper. 
Figure 2.52 shows a schematic of exposure system setup [137]. A DMD chip, 
consisting of an array of micromirrors, is controlled by a pattern generator in 
which pattern data is converted into electrical signal to control the deﬂection of

[Page 72]
each micromirror. The SLM is uniformly illuminated by light from a pulsed excimer 
laser which has been processed through a beam homogenizer. The reﬂected light 
pattern is imaged through a demagnifying optical system onto the photoresist-coated 
substrate. An interferometer-controlled air-bearing stage moves continuously in the 
x direction, and the laser is triggered at the exact time to stitch the exposed ﬁeld with 
the previous exposure. In the time between two sequential exposures, the SLM is 
reloaded with new data corresponding to the pattern to be written. After a stripe of 
ﬁelds has been exposed over the full length of the substrate, the stage moves in the y 
direction, and a new stripe is exposed next to the previous one. 
72
2
Optical Lithography
The ﬁrst generation of SLM laser tool had reduction of 160× [134]. Later it was 
developed to reduction of 267×. With a micromirror size of 8 × 8 μm, the achievable 
minimum pixel size was 30 nm [138]. The commercial SLM-based lithography tools 
are represented by the Sigma-7000 series from Micronic Laser System AB. Their 
Sigma-7500 tool was able to make masks for 45 nm generation of IC manufacturing 
[139]. However, mask making for IC industry is a very competitive market. With 
only a few companies left for manufacturing the latest generations of ICs, only the 
ALTA tools from Applied Materials Inc. survived to serve the IC industry, while 
other vendors such as the Micronic and Heidelberg Instruments are providing 
maskless laser lithography tools for a broad scope of applications other than ICs, 
for example, masks for making display panels [140] or lithography for 
microelectromechanical systems (MEMS) using the so-called maskless aligner 
as opposed to the traditional mask aligner [141]. Other microscale patterning 
needs include microoptics, microﬂuidics, computer-generated hologram (CGH), 
etc. The pattern features are no longer in nanoscale but in micrometer size, with 
emphasis on large substrates which can be up to 1400 mm × 1620 mm for display 
applications [142]. 
2.12 
Two-Photon Polymerization Lithography 
While the above optical ML2 technologies are well developed by industry and very 
much geared toward volume production of photomasks or ICs, there is another laser-
based lithography technique developed in research laboratories, which can directly 
form 3D structures without using a photomask. This is the two-photon polymeriza-
tion (2PP) lithography. 2PP is based on photon-induced polymerization process 
to solidify a liquid polymer upon being irradiated by a laser beam. It is very similar 
to a microscale photon polymerization technique, called microstereolithography, 
which 
is 
commonly 
referred 
to 
as 
3D 
printing 
nowadays 
[143]. 
While 
microstereolithography uses a UV laser beam to scan a surface layer of liquid 
polymer to form a 2D pattern and build up 3D structures layer by layer, 2PP 
lithography uses infrared femtosecond laser beams to form a focus point inside a 
pool of liquid polymer, and the polymerization only takes place at the focal point. 
Microstereolithography is a single-photon polymerization process, in which the 
short wavelength of UV laser has enough energy to trigger the polymerization of

[Page 73]
some photon-sensitive polymers. Therefore, the UV laser polymerization only takes 
place at the surface of liquid polymer, as illustrated in Fig. 2.53a. A 3D object is built 
up by lowering the polymer liquid surface layer by layer. In two-photon polymer-
ization process, the energy of a single infrared photon is not enough to trigger the 
polymerization. Two photons that overlap double the energy or shorten the wave-
length by half, which is equivalent to the absorption of UV photons as illustrated in 
Fig. 2.53b. The polymerization is only triggered at the location of overlapped two 
photons [144]. The two-photon polymerization is only possible with a femtosecond 
infrared laser at its focal point. The polymerization can happen inside the liquid 
polymer because the polymer is transparent to infrared laser. 
2.12
Two-Photon Polymerization Lithography
73
(a)
(b)

hvIR

Polymer liquid
Polymerization 
Fig. 2.53 Schematic comparison of (a) microstereolithography (one-photon polymerization) and 
(b) two-photon polymerization lithography 
By moving around the two-photon focus point, any 3D polymerized structures 
can be formed. The nonlinear absorption of photons with controllable threshold 
intensity allows the 2PP lithography to have very high resolution at the level of λ/10 
to λ/50 [145]. Making 3D structures with sub-100 nm details is possible. Figure 2.54 
shows a chain-like 3D structure formed by two-photon polymerization of a liquid 
resin. The smallest structure dimension is only 120 nm. 2PP lithography can induce 
not only polymerization but also variety of other photochemical processes. 
For example, 3D metallic microstructures were successfully created in the polymer 
matrix containing homogeneously doped metal ion by photon-induced reduction 
process [146], as well as a variety of other materials [147]. Commercial 2PP 
lithography tools, the Quantum X series, are available at the German company 
Nanoscribe [148]. 
The key condition to realize 2PP is that the laser beams do not cause signiﬁcant 
absorption of photon energy in the liquid polymer except at the focus point of two 
laser beams. In the past infrared (IR) lasers were used for 2PP lithography because 
only IR wavelength has the minimum absorption in the polymer. Recently, new 
photon-sensitive polymers have been discovered, which can have peak absorption of

[Page 74]
photon energy at shorter wavelength. Visible femtosecond lasers at wavelengths of 
520 nm and 730 nm were used. By controlling the laser exposure dose, 3D polymer 
“woodpile” structures have been formed with the minimum line diameters around 
60–70 nm, as is shown in Fig. 2.55. Such structure can be useful as a photonic crystal 
material.

structure formed by

of liquid resin. (Reprint

Fig. 2.55 “Woodpile” structure formed by two-photon exposure of liquid polymer. (Reprint from

[Page 75]
References
75
Fig. 2.56 Polymerization of a bull sculpture by (a) raster scan of the whole body or (b) only the 
surface shell and subsequent solidiﬁcation by ﬂood UV exposure from a mercury lamp. (Reprint 
from Ref. [151] with permission) 
Though complicated 3D polymer structures can be made by 2PP lithography, the 
process is extremely slow and inefﬁcient because the structure is formed point by 
point with focused laser. One remedy for this problem is to form a shell of 3D object 
and then polymerize the inside by ﬂood exposure of UV light, as the example shown 
in Fig. 2.56. For the bull sculpture to completely solidify by 2PP raster scan, it would 
take 183 min. By polymerization of only the surface proﬁle (the shell) and subse-
quently solidifying by a mercury lamp, the same bull sculpture could be made in 
13 min [151].

Chapter 3 
Electron Beam Lithography 
3.1 
Introduction 
Electron beam lithography is the most important and also the most versatile 
nanofabrication technique. Electrons work very much like the photons for 
nanofabrication. They carry energy and transfer the energy into the energy-sensitive 
polymer materials called electron resists. The resists exposed by electron beam are 
developed into surface relief patterns which act as masks for subsequent transfer into 
the substrate materials. 
Electron beam (e-beam) lithography appeared almost around the same time as the 
optical lithography in the early 1960s. It evolved from scanning electron microscope 
(SEM). An e-beam lithography system is very much like a SEM. The SEM could 
evolve to e-beam lithography only because of the discovery of an electron-sensitive 
polymer material, called polymethylmethacrylate (PMMA) [1]. Exposure of PMMA 
resist by e-beam is very much like exposure of photoresists by photons. Electron resists 
are the same group of macromolecular polymers as those photoresists. The exposure of 
electron resists is the same process as that of photon exposure of photoresists, only that 
the e-beam lithography can have much higher resolution. As already revealed by Eq. 2. 
3 in Chap. 2, the resolution of optical lithography is limited by optical wavelength. In 
order to increase the resolution, the wavelength of light source in optical lithography 
system has decreased from g-line to i-line, deep UV and extreme UV. Electrons, 
though are charged particles, are also of wavelike behaviors known as wave particle 
duality. The electron wavelength can be calculated by Eq. (3.1)  [2]. 
λe = 1:226 
V
p
nm
ð
 Þ
ð3:1 Þ
The original version of the chapter has been revised. A correction to this chapter can be found at 
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_12 
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024, 
corrected publication 2025 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_3
83

[Page 84]
84
3
Electron Beam Lithography
where V is the energy of electrons in electron volt unit. From Eq. (3.1) one can 
calculate that the wavelength of electrons with 100 eV energy is only 1.2 angstrom. 
It is apparent that the higher the electron energy, the shorter the wavelength, which 
can be hundreds of thousands times shorter than optical wavelength. It is known that 
high voltage electron transmission microscopes can see individual atoms. This is 
because of the ultrashort wavelength the high energy electrons have, hence the 
ultrahigh resolution. In fact, the electron wavelength is not the resolution-limiting 
factor in e-beam lithography. The factors which limit the achievable resolution in 
e-beam lithography are various electron aberrations and electron scattering in resists, 
which will be discussed in the following sections of this chapter. 
Nanofabrication realized by e-beam lithography was much earlier than photons did. 
Because of the ﬁne beam enabled by electron optics, e-beam lithography using 
PMMA resist immediately demonstrated much higher resolution capability than 
optical lithography could dream of around the time. In the early 1970s, e-beam 
lithography was already able to pattern features as small as 60 nm [3]. In the early 
1980s when the doom of optical lithography was predicted, e-beam lithography was 
proposed as the replacement for large-scale IC manufacturing. However, this has never 
been materialized because of the continuous advances in optical lithography which is 
inherently high throughput [4]. While patterning by optical lithography is mostly done 
through photomasks, electron beam can directly expose and pattern on resist, i.e., 
“direct write.” Its high resolution patterning capability has enabled the technology to 
survive over six decades and has always been a versatile nanoscale patterning tool with 
wide applications in many ﬁelds other than IC manufacturing. With the boom of 
nanotechnology in the last 30 years, e-beam lithography has become an indispensable 
nanopatterning tool in research laboratories and universities. Excluding all other 
factors, the ultimate resolution of e-beam lithography will only be limited by the 
diameter of electron beam, which has been demonstrated as small as 1.5 nm [5]. 
There have been attempts to introduce projection e-beam lithography or multiple 
e-beams for small-volume IC manufacturing. Limited success has been achieved, by 
which it means that the throughput has been much improved but still not on par with 
optical projection lithography. The maturity and adoption of EUV optical lithogra-
phy for sub-10 nm IC manufacturing completely killed the hope for e-beam direct 
write entering IC production. One thing that e-beam lithography has a ﬁrm position 
in the technology chain of IC mass production is the fabrication of photomasks. 
Before the advent of laser direct write lithography, e-beam direct write used to be the 
only tool for making photomasks. Even with today’s widely used laser tools, nearly 
half of all photomasks worldwide are still made by variable shaped e-beam tools. 
With the increasing complexity of RET (resolution enhancement technology) masks, 
multi-beam writers became the favored choice, not to mention that e-beam tools are 
indispensable in EUV mask repair. 
This chapter will ﬁrst introduce the basics of electro-optics, explaining how 
electrons are focused and deﬂected. Then the classiﬁcation of electron beam lithog-
raphy systems is described, including key speciﬁcations. The data formats for 
e-beam lithography are discussed as part of lithography pattern design process. 
The exposure process is introduced together with the analysis of electron resists 
and their characteristics. The difference between e-beam lithography and optical

[Page 85]
lithography lies in the fact that electrons are charged particles. They scatter in solid 
materials, resulting in some special features unique in e-beam lithography, such as 
the proximity effect caused by electron scattering and how to overcome or correct 
the effect. Same as the optical lithography, resists play a key part in either high-
throughput or high resolution e-beam lithography. Recent developments in e-beam 
resists and processes are discussed. Finally, progresses in high-throughput e-beam 
lithography are introduced. 
3.2
Principle of Electron Optics
85
3.2 
Principle of Electron Optics 
3.2.1 
Electron Lens 
The core part of an e-beam lithography system is the electron optical system which 
focuses and deﬂects an electron beam. To understand the principle of e-beam 
lithography, one has to ﬁrst understand the principle of electron optics. Electron 
optics describes the movement of electrons in an electromagnetic ﬁeld. The name of 
electron optics implies that it has many similarities with light optics. It is known that 
light wave has different propagation speeds in different mediums, resulting in light 
refraction. Electrons can also change speed and direction of movement in different 
electromagnetic ﬁelds, which is an analogy to refraction. There are, however, major 
differences between electron optics and light optics [2]: 
1. Refractive index in optical mediums is a discontinuous function of space coordi-
nates, i.e., light entering from air to an optical lens undergoes a discontinuous 
refraction. The refractive index of electron optics is a continuous function of 
space coordinates because electrical potential always distributes continuously in 
space. 
2. Refractive index of optical mediums is between 1 and 2.5. The refractive index of 
electron optics depends on the space potential, which can be very large. 
3. Geometrical aberrations in light optics can be eliminated by changing arbitrarily 
the curvature of refractive surface (shape of a lens). However, electrical potential 
in space cannot be arbitrarily changed. Correction of aberration in electron optics 
is completely different from that in light optics. 
4. Electrons repel each other because they are carrying the same polarity of electrical 
charge. For a high current density electron beam, there is a space charge effect 
which can deteriorate the focusing of electron beam. Light optics does not have 
the space charge effect. 
Once understood the relation between electron refraction and space distribution 
of electrical potential, it becomes easier to understand the principle of electron lens. 
Figure 3.1 shows the space potential distribution inside two cylindrical electrodes 
when different voltages are applied to them. It can be seen from observing the 
potential distribution that the equipotential distribution shapes, spreading from the 
gap between two cylindrical electrodes, are very similar to the curved surface of an

[Page 86]
optical lens. The difference is that an optical lens only has one ﬁxed curvature of 
shape, while the curved equipotentials inside the cylindrical electrodes are distrib-
uted continuously. An electron coming from one end of the cylindrical electrodes 
will experience an axial force around the gap region, which can force the electrons 
from different incident angles and positions to converge toward the cylinder axis. 
Therefore, the electrical ﬁeld at the gap region is equivalent to an electron lens. 
Figure 3.2 shows the two computer-simulated electron trajectories passing through 
the cylindrical electrode. They come in different angles from the left side of cylinder 
and converge toward the axis after passing the equivalent lens region. 
86
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.1 Space potential distribution inside two cylindrical electrodes with different voltages 
(Cylinder A, 0 volt; Cylinder B, 100 volts) 
Fig. 3.2 Calculated electron trajectories in the cylindrical electrodes (two trajectories with different 
incident angles) 
Focusing by electrical ﬁeld is called electrostatic lens and focusing by magnetic 
ﬁeld is called magnetic lens. The electrodes which generate electrical ﬁeld distribu-
tion can either be cylinders or apertures. They will have lens effect as long as the 
spatial potential distributions produced are in the form of Fig. 3.1. The structure of 
magnetic lens is shown in Fig. 3.3. The magnetic ﬁeld is normally generated by

[Page 87]
ð
 
electrical current ﬂowing through coils which wind over a soft iron core, called pole 
pieces. 
3.2
Principle of Electron Optics
87
Fig. 3.3 Magnetic lens where the magnetic ﬁeld is generated by electrical current passing through 
coils 
Though the complete theories of electron optics are complex, it can be concep-
tually understood from the Lorentz force equation as shown in Eq. (3.2). The 
equation expresses the force exerted to a particle with charge q and speed v in a 
combined electrostatic ﬁeld E and magnetic ﬁeld B. 
F = q E þ v × B

rotationally symmetric, which is true in most of the electron optical systems, the 
movement of charged particles can be described by the following equations [6]. 
For an electrostatic lens with a rotationally symmetric electrostatic ﬁeld E(z,r), 
Eq. (3.2) can be expressed as the force experienced by electrons in r and z directions 
as Eqs. (3.3) and (3.4).

Eqs. (3.3) and (3.4) as Eq. (3.5), from which the focal length of an electron lens can 
be derived as in Eq. (3.6).

Equations (3.5) and (3.6) reveal three characteristics of focusing charged particles 
in an electrostatic ﬁeld. 
1. The focusing does not depend on charge or mass of a particle. Whether electrons 
or ion of different mass will experience the same focusing action. 
2. The focusing is directly proportional to the variation of potential distribution 
along z direction, as indicated by Eq. (3.6). This is the reason that electron 
focusing only takes place where there is a rapid change of electrical ﬁeld, 
which only happens at the gap between two electrodes with different electrical 
potentials. 
3. Equation (3.6) also reveals that high potential V(r, z) reduces the focusing action 
of a lens because the charged particles pass through the lens in high speed. 
For a magnetic lens with a rotationally symmetric magnetic ﬁeld B(z, r),

For electrostatic lenses, the focusing power depends on how much the difference 
is of the voltage applied to the lens elements and how rapid is the change of potential. 
This requires high voltage and very narrow gap between the lens elements, for

[Page 89]
example, the two cylinders in Fig. 3.1. Then there is the risk of electrical breakdown 
if the voltage difference is too great. Therefore, electrostatic lens is in general limited 
in focusing power. On the other hand, high magnetic ﬁeld can be generated by high 
current passing through coils. The heat generated by the high current can be cooled 
by forced circulation of water or air. In this way, high focusing power can be 
achieved with a magnetic lens. This is the reason that all the e-beam lithography 
systems, as well as electron microscopes, use magnetic lenses to focus electron 
beams. However, magnetic lens cannot be used for focusing an ion beam because of 
its dependence on charge and mass of ions. A magnetic lens will focus ions of 
different charges or masses to different position on an image plane. Such a property 
has been employed for separation of ions in mass spectrometers. For focusing a ﬁne 
ion beam, electrostatic lenses are generally used. 
3.2
Principle of Electron Optics
89
3.2.2 
Electron Source 
A ﬁne beam spot focused by an electron optical system is in fact the image of an 
electron source. To achieve a very small size of beam, the size of its source has to be 
small. Before the advent of ﬁeld emission (FE) electron sources, tungsten thermal 
cathodes were the commonly used electron emission source. Lately, LaB6 com-
pound was employed as thermal cathode material which performs better than 
tungsten cathodes. The fundamental drawback of thermionic cathode is their large 
emission area. In order to reduce the source size, a strong lens in front of the cathode 
has to be used to focus the electrons emitted from the cathode into a much small 
cross-over, as illustrated in Fig. 3.4a. This cross-over acts as a virtual source seen by 
the downstream lenses, so that a ﬁne spot size can be obtained through the focusing 
system. The problem with this method is that it is difﬁcult to achieve a very small 
diameter of cross-over because of the Coulomb repulsion of electrons arising from 
the high concentration of electrons at the cross-over. 
Field emission sources have completely overcome this problem. A ﬁeld emission 
source does not rely on high temperature heating of the source to generate electron 
emission. The electrons are directly extracted from the material surface by extremely 
high electrical ﬁeld (>108 V/m). A ﬁeld emission source is a ﬁne needle. Very high 
electrical ﬁeld can be created at the sharp end of needle with relatively low applied 
voltage [7]. Because electrons only emit at the points where the electrical ﬁeld 
exceeds threshold strength, the emission area can be ﬁnely controlled and very 
small. With a ﬁeld emission source, there is no need to have a cross-over. The 
virtual source extends to the back of real source, as illustrated in Fig. 3.4b. The 
virtual source is only a few nanometers. Very high resolution can be achieved using 
such small virtual source as the object of electron optical imaging system. 
Table 3.1 gives a comprehensive list of technical parameters for the four main 
electron sources which are widely used in various electron beam systems [8]. First, it 
can be seen that ﬁeld emission (FE) sources have much small sizes of both the real 
cathode geometry and the virtual source. Second, their emission current density is

[Page 90]
several orders of magnitude higher than the thermionic sources. The cold ﬁeld 
emission source appears to be the best among the four types of sources. All high-
grade electron microscopes nowadays are using cold ﬁeld emission sources. How-
ever, it is rarely used as the electron emission source in an e-beam lithography 
system. This is because the emission surface of a cold cathode can easily absorb

emission current. As seen in Table 3.1, the noise and emission current drift of cold 
FE source are the highest among the four types of sources. These effects are not 
serious problems for electron microscope because a ﬂickering of image does not 
matter very much. But they are unacceptable to e-beam lithography application. 
E-beam lithography relies on stable emission current to deliver consistent exposure 
dose. Any emission current drift or noise can seriously deteriorate the quality of 
resist exposure. This has been the reason to develop thermal ﬁeld emission cathodes 
for e-beam lithography systems. The thermal ﬁeld emission cathode, also called 
Schottky cathode, is to heat up the conventional ﬁeld emission cathode. At high 
temperature, absorption of gaseous atoms at cathode emission surface is greatly 
reduced, resulting in stable working of the cathode. As seen in the table, Schottky 
cathode has the most characteristics of a ﬁeld emission source but much lower in 
beam noise and emission current drift.
3.2
Principle of Electron Optics
91
3.2.3 
Aberrations 
A small size of source does not guarantee a ﬁnal small size of beam. An electro-
magnetic lens system must have as small as possible aberrations in order to achieve a 
ﬁne beam size. Similar to light optics, an electron optical system also has geomet-
rical aberrations, such as spherical aberration, coma, ﬁeld curvature, astigmatism and 
distortion, and nongeometrical aberration such as chromatic aberration. As afore-
mentioned, the fundamental difference between electron optics and light optics is 
that the refractive index in an optical lens is a discontinuous function of space 
coordinates, i.e., light entering from air to an optical lens undergoes a discontinuous 
refraction, while the refractive index of an electromagnetic lens is a function of 
electromagnetic ﬁeld distribution, which is a continuous function of space coordi-
nates. The geometrical aberrations in light optics can be eliminated by changing 
arbitrarily the curvature of refractive surface (geometrical shape of an optical lens). 
However, electromagnetic ﬁeld in space cannot be arbitrarily changed. An optical 
imaging system may consist of a hundred pieces of lenses to correct all the aberra-
tions. An electromagnetic imaging system has only a few lenses. A typical e-beam 
lithography column is schematically illustrated in Fig. 3.5 [9]. The electron gun is at 
the top, which consists of an electron source and some lenses to initially focus the 
electrons, as illustrated in Fig. 3.4. The condenser lens further focuses the beam. In 
conjunction with the beam blanker and selectable aperture, the beam can be switched 
on or off (pass or not pass the aperture). The deﬂectors perform the beam scanning 
function. The ﬁnal objective lens focuses the beam onto a substrate surface. 
Due to aberrations, an electron beam spot on axis (off-axis deﬂection is not 
considered here) at the substrate (image plane) is not simply a demagniﬁed electron 
source but includes other aberration components, as expressed in Eq. 3.13 [10].

3.2
Principle of Electron Optics
93
Spherical aberration is caused by large angle of beam. Electrons entering a lens 
with different angles will be focused differently, as the trajectories shown in Fig. 3.2. 
Although an electron optical system can be designed to minimize the spherical 
aberration coefﬁcient Cs, the most effective way of reducing the spherical aberration 
is to reduce the angle α, because the aberration is proportional to the cubic power of 
α. The α can be reduced by using an aperture to prevent wide angular electrons from 
entering the objective lens. The selectable aperture in Fig. 3.5 performs exactly this 
function. For the objective lens, the aperture is the source. Its size has great inﬂuence 
on the ﬁnal spot size on the substrate. An e-beam lithography system is normally 
equipped with a series of different sizes of apertures. They are selectable and 
exchangeable to suit different requirements on beam current and resolution. It is 
obvious that for ultimate high resolution e-beam lithography, the smallest aperture 
should be selected, albeit the usable beam current is also greatly reduced. Unfortu-
nately, reduction of convergent angle will cause increase of electron diffraction 
aberration. Although the wavelength of high energy electrons is extremely small, 
as indicated in Eq. 3.1, the diffraction can be noticeable if the aperture is also 
extremely small. Obviously, increasing beam energy can signiﬁcantly reduce the 
diffraction effect. 
Chromatic aberration is caused by energy spread of emitted electrons, and by the 
instability of applied voltage in the case of electrostatic lenses or instability of 
current in the case of magnetic lenses. While the ﬂuctuation of external power 
supply to an electromagnetic lens system can be well controlled to minimum, the 
energy spread of an emission source is, however, beyond anyone’s control. There-
fore, the chromatic aberration is always there. The energy spread can be exasperated 
if there is a beam cross-over in the case of thermal emission source. At the cross-
over, high concentration of electrons creates signiﬁcant repulsion among the charged 
particles, causing increase of the energy spread. The evidence is displayed in 
Table 3.1, where it indicates that electrons leaving a thermionic cathode have energy 
spread of 0.59 eV but increase signiﬁcantly to 1.5–2.5 eV at the electron gun exit, 
because of the cross-over formed in front of the cathode. Apparently, the cross-over 
has made the energy spread much worse. Not only the cross-over at the electron gun 
side, any cross-over along the axis of an electromagnetic lens system can create 
additional energy spread, hence the chromatic aberration. Therefore, an advanced 
electron optical system will try to avoid any cross-over. For example, the GEMINI 
column developed at ZEISS does not have any cross-over along the path from a ﬁeld 
emission source. The only cross-over is the focus point at the substrate surface where 
the beam lands [8]. 
Another aberration which is unique in electron optics is caused by space charge 
effect. As mentioned earlier, electrons at a cross-over will experience Coulomb 
repulsion force. The repulsions among electrons not only increase the energy spread 
but also increase the diameter of beam, resulting in defocus and radial blur of the 
beam [11]. The higher the emission current, the greater the space charge effect, 
hence more difﬁcult to achieve small beam diameter in an electromagnetic lens 
system. E-beam lithography has much greater demand on beam current than a SEM, 
because a resist can only be exposed above a threshold exposure dose, whereas the

[Page 94]
exposure dose is the product of beam current and beam dwelling time. High beam 
current can reduce the beam dwelling time, resulting in high throughput in e-beam 
lithography. However, high beam current makes the focusing of electron beam more 
difﬁcult. That is partly the reason that a SEM can have much small size of electron 
beam than can an e-beam lithography system. The other reason is that SEM can use a 
cold ﬁeld emission source while e-beam lithography system cannot, as explained 
previously. A modest scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM) in the 
1970s had already been able to provide a 0.5-nm-diameter electron beam. The 
current at this beam size was 1pA (10-12 A) [12]. In contrast, an e-beam lithography 
system rarely operates below 100pA beam current. The normal beam current is 
0.1 nA ~ 100 nA. 
94
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.6 Beam

The only way to reduce the space charge effect without compromising the beam 
current is to increase the beam energy, in other words, operating at high acceleration 
voltage in an e-beam lithography system. High beam energy can also minimize the 
chromatic aberration caused by beam energy spread, because both the space charge 
induced beam enlargement and the chromatic aberration are inversely proportional 
to the beam energy. This is partly the reason why high-end e-beam lithography 
systems always operate at 100 keV beam energy. The relation between beam current 
and beam diameter is best demonstrated in Fig. 3.6, where beam diameters vs. beam 
current at 20 keV and 50 keV beam energies are compared [13]. Therefore, either 
smaller beam current or higher beam energy, or both, is preferred when high 
resolution e-beam lithography is required. 
Besides the abovementioned aberrations which are associated with beam focus-
ing, there are also aberrations related to beam deﬂection. They are mainly distortion 
and astigmatism which increase in cubic power proportion with the distance of 
deﬂection from the center axis. An e-beam lithography system normally has scan 
ﬁelds from a few hundreds of micrometers to a few millimeters. The beam spot is no 
longer small and circular at the corners of deﬂected ﬁeld. They become bigger and 
elliptical, and a square scanning ﬁeld becomes distorted [14]. For example, a 
previously reported prototype e-beam lithography system had achieved beam spot

[Page 95]
diameter of 2.7 nm on axis, but the beam spot deteriorated to 6.5 nm at the edge of 
500 μm  deﬂection ﬁeld [9]. 
3.3
Electron Beam Lithography Systems
95
The above analysis indicates that a high resolution e-beam lithography requires 
high beam energy, small scanning ﬁeld, low beam current, and a stable environment. 
These are the prerequisites to enable low aberrations and ﬁne focusing of a beam. 
3.3 
Electron Beam Lithography Systems 
3.3.1 
Basic Conﬁguration 
Though the basic components of an e-beam lithography system have been illustrated 
in Fig. 3.5, a practical system is much more complex, as shown in Fig. 3.7, which is 
generally referred as electron column. An electron column consists of the following 
subsystems:
(a)
(b) 
Electron gun 
Gun alignment 
Condenser lens 
Beam blanker 
Zoom lens 
Stigmator 
Beam aperture 
Projection lens 
Deflector 
Work stage 
Backscattering electron 
detector 
Fig. 3.7 (a) Photograph of an e-beam lithography system (VB6-HR from Leica Microsystems) and 
(b) schematic of complete electron optical system in an e-beam lithography column

[Page 96]
96
3
Electron Beam Lithography
1. Electron gun. The electron gun has been described in detail in the last section. 
What needs to be mentioned here is that electron gun is always at very high 
voltage. Electron beam in an e-beam lithography system is normally accelerated 
to a kinetic energy between 10 keV and 100 keV. The acceleration voltage is 
always applied to electron gun, i.e., the gun is kept at negative high voltage 
while the exposure stage is at ground potential, so that a high voltage difference 
is established between the gun and the stage with which electrons emitted from 
the gun are accelerated to land at the stage. If the cathode is ﬁeld emission 
cathode, a separate vacuum system is needed at the gun section in order to 
maintain a high vacuum for ﬁeld emission of electrons. 
2. Electron gun alignment system. The whole electron column is assembled section 
by section from each part of subsystems. The assembled column can be as 
1 meter high. Any slight error in machining or assembling of sections can lead to 
misalignment between the cathode tip in the gun and the last stage of lens 
aperture. A deﬂection system at the electron gun section is normally installed to 
collimate the electron beam if an alignment is required. 
3. Condenser lens. It has the same function as the condenser lens in an optical 
lithography system, to converge the emitted electrons from a cathode so that 
they arrive at the exposure surface in maximum numbers. 
4. Beam blanking. This is to switch on and off the electron beam so that the beam 
can only pass through the column to reach substrate when an exposure is 
needed. It functions like a shutter in a camera, by deﬂecting the beam off the 
electron optical axis so that it cannot pass through a central aperture. 
5. Zoom lens. This is for adjusting the focal plane, including dynamic focusing of 
electron beam. 
6. Stigmator. Astigmatism occurs when focusing of a beam is different in x and y 
directions, resulting in an elliptical shape of beam instead of a circular beam. It is 
mostly caused by the errors in mechanical machining of the lens components. 
Stigmators are generally composed of multipole lenses, which can focus a beam 
laterally from different directions. 
7. Apertures. Several apertures are used in an electron column. One of the impor-
tant functions of an aperture is to limit the angle of electron beam spread. The 
main aberration of an electron lens is the spherical aberration which is directly 
proportional to the cubic power of beam spread angle. Limiting the angle is an 
effective way to improve electron optical resolution. However, the use of small 
aperture can reduce beam current by cutting off large-angle electrons. In prac-
tice, the size of aperture can be adjusted (using a series of apertures of different 
diameter). A smaller aperture is used when high resolution is required and a 
larger aperture is used when high beam current at low resolution is needed. The 
position of aperture can be ﬁnely tuned to ensure the center of aperture coincides 
exactly with the axis of electron column. 
8. Projection (objective) lens. This is the ﬁnal lens of an electron column, which 
focuses the electron beam coming out the aperture to form the ﬁnal beam spot at 
the exposure surface.

[Page 97]
9. Deﬂectors. Electron beam deﬂection and scanning are realized by deﬂectors. 
The deﬂectors can be placed before, after, or inside the projection lens. A 
deﬂector can be electrostatic or magnetic. Magnetic deﬂector is a coil which 
generates magnetic ﬁeld by electrical current ﬂow. A magnetic deﬂector has 
small aberration and distortion but slow deﬂection speed, whereas electrostatic 
deﬂection is of high speed but larger aberration. An e-beam lithography system 
is usually equipped with two sets of deﬂection system, responsible for main ﬁeld 
and sub-ﬁeld deﬂection. An exposure ﬁeld (main ﬁeld) is normally divided into 
many sub-ﬁelds. Main ﬁeld requires large deﬂection angle but at low speed, 
whereby a magnetic deﬂector can be used. Sub-ﬁeld deﬂection is of small angle 
and high speed, which can be carried out by an electrostatic deﬂector. 
10. Electron detector and work stage. An electron detector is equipped at the exit of 
ﬁnal lens to detect the backscattered electrons from the substrate. This is to 
detect the alignment mark when aligned exposure is required. Unlike a SEM 
which detects secondary emission electrons, backscattered electrons are less 
efﬁcient for imaging. A work stage is where the substrate is mounted. The stage 
moves in synchronization with beam deﬂection to enable exposure of large-size 
substrate.
3.3
Electron Beam Lithography Systems
97
Other peripheral components include vacuum systems, high voltage power 
source, and pattern generator which converts a design pattern data into electrical 
signals to drive beam scanning deﬂectors. 
3.3.2 
Key Speciﬁcations 
When choosing an e-beam lithography system, one should pay attention to the 
following key speciﬁcations: 
1. Minimum spot size, which is the indication of system resolution. It directly 
inﬂuences the minimum size of exposed features. 
2. Acceleration voltage, which is the indication of electron beam energy. A system 
with high acceleration voltage also has higher resolution because of smaller 
aberration (mentioned earlier) and smaller electron proximity effect (discussed 
later). High energy beam can also expose thicker resist layer. 
3. Beam current. A system with larger beam current can produce higher speed of 
lithography, i.e., less time is needed to expose a pattern at the same exposure dose 
than a low beam current system. However, the exposure speed is limited by the 
scanning speed of a system, or the response speed of beam deﬂectors. Besides, a 
high current beam always has large spot size. In general, a small beam current is 
used for high resolution lithography and large beam current for large pattern 
exposure which gives high throughput. 
4. Scanning speed. High scanning speed results in shorter exposure time. Scanning 
speed is expressed in frequency. The maximum frequency of current vector scan

[Page 98]
98
3
Electron Beam Lithography
systems is 50 MHz, while the maximum frequency for raster scan systems is up to 
500 MHz. Vector scan and raster scan will be discussed in the next section. 
5. Scanning ﬁeld. For a large scanning ﬁeld, most patterns can be exposed within the 
ﬁeld, which can avoid ﬁeld stitching errors. However, large ﬁeld has low scan-
ning speed and high deﬂection aberration. 
6. Other speciﬁcations include the accuracy of work stage, accuracy of overlay 
between different exposure levels, accuracy of ﬁeld stitching, etc. 
3.3.3 
Vector and Raster Scanning 
The difference between vector scan and raster scan is that in vector scan electron 
beam only scans the exposure patterns (Fig. 3.8a), whereas in raster scan the electron 
beam scans the whole ﬁeld all the time, but the beam blanker is switched on only 
where there are patterns (Fig. 3.8b). Vector scan systems only scan when there are 
patterns. The electron beam is a Gaussian beam, or a circular beam. It is commonly 
called Gaussian beam because the current distribution in the circular beam spot is 
close to a Gaussian distribution function. Vector scan systems generally have high 
resolution but low scan speed comparing to raster scan systems. The fastest vector 
scan system so far is only 50 MHz scanning speed. However, the high resolution 
capability (≤3 nm beam spot size) makes these systems as powerful tools for 
nanoscale lithography. 
In a practical raster scan system, the electron beam only scans in one direction, 
because of the limit of scanning ﬁeld size, while the work stage moves in other 
direction. They are combined to obtain a large ﬁeld size. Raster scan e-beam 
lithography was exclusively used for making photomasks. These systems, 
represented by MEBES series, were typically working at 10 keV and minimum 
feature size around 350 nm. Because of the low resolution, they were used for mask 
making until the 130 nm node IC technology. The masks for 130 nm node onward 
were exclusively patterned by 50 keV variable shaped e-beam systems (see 
Sect. 3.7.1). 
Fig. 3.8 Schematic illustration of (a) vector scan and (b) raster scan

[Page 99]
3.3
Electron Beam Lithography Systems
99
3.3.4 
Pattern Fragmentation 
The deﬂector in an e-beam lithography system has limited deﬂection range usually 
from a few hundred micrometers of sub-ﬁeld to a few millimeters of main ﬁeld, 
which is adjustable. Patterns bigger than the ﬁeld size have to be fragmented. 
Figure 3.9 illustrates that a big pattern is divided into a number of fragments to ﬁt 
into different deﬂection ﬁelds. The exposure of whole pattern is completed by 
stitching together these fragments. There are two types of stitching, sub-ﬁeld 
stitching within a main ﬁeld and main ﬁeld stitching between movements of work 
stage. Modern commercial e-beam lithography systems are equipped with laser-
controlled work stage which has very high accuracy of movement. Both sub-ﬁeld 
stitching and main ﬁeld stitching can be controlled within 25 nm accuracy [15]. One 
must be aware of the ﬁeld size and stitching accuracy of an e-beam lithography 
system before designing very ﬁne patterns. During the design process, patterns 
should be grouped as much as possible within an exposure ﬁeld. For those patterns 
bigger than the exposure ﬁeld, care should be taken to avoid putting critical parts of 
the pattern at the ﬁeld boundary. 
Apart from setting the ﬁeld size, pixel size and step size also need to be speciﬁed. 
Note the difference between pixel size and step size, illustrated in Fig. 3.10. In short, 
electron beam stops at each pixel and moves to next pixel in steps. The step size can 
be equal to pixel size or a few times bigger than pixel size. By adjusting the pixel size 
and step size, the way a pattern is ﬁlled by electron beam can be adjusted. 
As most of e-beam lithography systems can only scan a pattern in x and 
y directions in a Cartesian coordinate system, any patterns, no matter how complex 
they are, have to be fragmented to basic shapes of rectangles, trapeziums, or 
parallelograms. Electron beam scans in raster scan mode inside each of the basic 
shapes and moves from one basic shape to another in vector scan mode, as sche-
matically shown Fig. 3.8a. The fragmentation is much straightforward if the expo-
sure pattern is a rectangular shape or can be decomposed into a number of 
rectangular shapes. If a pattern is a curved shape, the curved boundary has to be
Fig. 3.9 Pattern 
fragmentation according to 
exposure ﬁeld (I, II, III, and 
VI are the exposure ﬁelds 
covered by the pattern) 
I 
II
III 
IV

[Page 100]
approximated by a large number of rectangles or trapeziums, resulting in a large 
number of fragmentations. Figure 3.11 is an example of fragmentation on a ring 
shape pattern. If the curved shape is big, the rectangle and trapezium fragmentation 
can generally approximate the curved boundary in good precision, as shown in 
Fig. 3.11a. However, if the curved shape itself is very small and comparable to the 
resolution limit of the e-beam system, approximation by rectangles can be very crude 
because of the limit of pixel size and step size, as shown in Fig. 3.11b.
100
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.10 Deﬁnitions of

Fig. 3.11 (a) Fragmented ring shape pattern with outer diameter 1 μm and (b) fragmented ring

It indicates that Cartesian scan systems cannot obtain high precision curved 
patterns. This is not a major problem in most e-beam lithography applications 
because most of the e-beam systems are for making electronic devices which are 
mostly rectangular patterns. Curved patterns are encountered mainly in making

[Page 101]
optical elements such as waveguides. There is a ﬁxed beam moving stage (FBMS) 
technique which can pattern any curve-shaped patterns and without stitching errors 
[16]. As the name implies, FBMS is a scanning mode that the electron beam is 
stationary and an exposure pattern is formed by continuously moving the work stage. 
In this way, any pattern shapes can be exposed without the need for pattern 
fragmentation and without any stitching errors, such as long curved optical 
waveguides [17]. 
3.3
Electron Beam Lithography Systems
101
3.3.5 
Commercial E-Beam Lithography Systems 
For high-quality sub-100 nm patterning, a commercial e-beam lithography system is 
necessary. There are used to be a number of providers of commercial systems. Over 
the years, the market has been consolidated into two major players and one small 
player. The two major players are Raith represented by its top grade e-beam 
lithography system EBPG Plus [18] and JEOL represented by its top grade system 
JBX-9500FS [19]. Raith used to be a middle range e-beam lithography system 
provider, while Vistec Lithography (originally Leica Cambridge) was in the top 
league in direct competition with JEOL. In 2013, Raith acquired the part of Gaussian 
beam system of Vistec to become the top league. Vistec is now focusing on variable 
shaped beam systems for advanced mask making [20]. The small player is 
NanoBeam which is specialized in high resolution e-beam direct write systems. Its 
nB5 system is capable of patterning 6 nm features with good uniformity over a large 
area [21]. All these top grade commercial e-beam lithography systems operate at 
100 keV beam energy and with scanning speed of at least 50 MHz, and all of them 
are capable of below 10 nm patterning resolution. 
The aforementioned professional e-beam lithography systems are of high perfor-
mance and multifunction but also very expensive with multimillion US$ price tags. 
Not many universities or research institutes can afford such expensive systems. On 
the other hand, most of scientiﬁc researches do not need a top grade e-beam 
lithography system. There is another large group of Gaussian beam vector scan 
systems which are modiﬁed from scanning electron microscopes. It functions both as 
a SEM and an e-beam lithography system. Raith is the main supplier of such 
combined systems. The company still has this main product line represented by 
Raith150 which operates at maximum 30 keV beam energy and 20 MHz speed and 
can pattern sub-10 nm feature size. 
If one has already got a SEM, there is an even cheaper solution, that is, to modify 
the existing SEM into an e-beam lithography machine. As mentioned earlier, a SEM 
is fundamentally the same as an e-beam lithography system, except that a SEM does 
not have a pattern generator, a laser-controlled work stage, and a beam blanker. 
Therefore it can be converted into a lithography tool once these kits are installed. 
Raith, apart from selling high-end e-beam lithography systems, also provides the 
ELPHY upgrade kits, known as “lithography attachments.” The ELPHY kits enable 
virtually any SEM to be converted into an e-beam lithography tool without

[Page 102]
sacriﬁcing any of its original functionality [22]. It must be pointed out that the 
functionalities of these SEM converted systems are very limited because [23]: 
102
3
Electron Beam Lithography
1. A SEM normally does not have a beam blanking function. Without beam 
blanking, the electron beam will not be switched off when moving from one 
exposure pattern to the next, resulting in some unwanted exposure features in 
resist. Nowadays, many SEM manufacturers have provided the option to equip a 
beam blanker. 
2. SEM works in raster scan mode, whereas SEM converted e-beam lithography 
system must be able to work in vector scan. The deﬂection coil in a SEM cannot 
completely adapt to vector scan mode. As seen in Fig. 3.8a, vector scan mode is 
needed when jumping from one pattern to another. A SEM deﬂection coil is not 
designed for such a jump. When a pulse signal is applied to the coil, it needs a 
fairly long settling time to become stable. For example, JEOL840 SEM needs 
10 μs settle time and Hitachi S800 SEM has settling time up to 1 ms, which can 
severely hamper the lithography speed. 
3. The work stage movement in a SEM is normally manual. Even if there is a step 
motor, the accuracy of stage movement is not better than 1–5 μm. When the 
exposure pattern is larger than the scan ﬁeld, ﬁeld stitching becomes a serious 
problem. It can be improved by adding a laser-controlled work stage. However, a 
laser-controlled stage is normally quite expensive. 
In general, the main limitation of a SEM converted e-beam system is its inability 
for large area and high-throughput e-beam lithography. Nevertheless, it is still a 
useful tool for scientiﬁc research wherein only small-size samples with a few devices 
or structures may be needed. Longer exposure time is not a serious problem and no 
quantity of devices is required. In such circumstance, the SEM converted systems 
become very attractive because they are much less expensive. 
3.4 
Scattering and Proximity Effect 
To realize nanoscale lithography or direct nanofabrication with electron beam, a 
small beam size is necessary but not enough. Electrons have to interact with a 
polymer resist, so that the energy carried by the electrons is transferred to the 
polymer material, causing changes of molecular chains in a polymer. The energy 
transfer process takes place during collisions between the electrons and the atoms in 
the polymer. The process is called scattering, which can lead to a much larger area of 
energy deposition than the original size of beam landing area, creating the so-called 
proximity effect. The proximity effect is the major obstacle in the process of 
achieving high resolution nanofabrication by e-beam lithography.

[Page 103]
3.4
Scattering and Proximity Effect
103
3.4.1 
Electron Scattering 
High energy electrons penetrating into a solid material will collide with atoms and 
the electrons surrounding an atom. As we have learned from general physics, two 
things can happen when two rigid balls collide with each other. If a small ball 
collides with a massive ball, the small ball will only change the direction of 
movement without losing any energy it carries. If a small ball collides with another 
small ball of same mass, the small ball will transfer half its energy to the other ball. 
The similar scenario happens in electron scattering in solid. The massive balls are the 
atoms and the small balls are electrons. There are two types of scattering taking place 
in the solid: elastic and inelastic scatterings. Collision of incident electrons with 
atoms in the material is an elastic scattering. As the mass of an electron is at least 
1/1840 of atomic mass, the collision is an extremely small ball with an enormous 
ball. An electron undergoing elastic scattering does not lose its energy but simply 
change its direction. Collision of incident electrons with the electrons surrounding 
the atoms of solid material is inelastic scattering, during which the incident electron 
will pass part of its energy to the electrons of solid material. It is this part of 
transferred energy that causes chain reaction of resist polymer material, resulting 
in the exposure of resist. Therefore, the electron scattering process in a solid material 
can be pictured as this: electrons from an incident electron beam undergo continuous 
elastic scattering by material atoms and continuously change their ﬂying directions. 
In between two elastic scatterings, there are inelastic scatterings with material 
electrons whereby the beam energy is gradually deposited into the material. 
The rigid ball collision is only an analogy to describe the scattering process taking 
place during electron beam exposure of resist. Electrons and atoms are not rigid 
balls. Their collisions are through the interaction of energy ﬁeld around the atoms 
and electrons. The interaction radius of energy ﬁeld is described by scattering cross 
section. For elastic scattering there is Rutherford’s single electron differential scat-
tering cross-section model. It describes that if a solid material consists of N different 
elements, the differential scattering cross section per solid angle of the ith element 
can be expressed as Eq. (3.14).

Inelastic scattering can be described by Bethe’s continuous slowing down 
approximation model [24], as shown in Eq. (3.16).

where dE/ds is the rate of energy loss of unit travel length (eV/m), NA is the 
Avogadro constant, ρ is the density of solid material (kg/m3 ), Ai is the atomic weight 
of ith element, a is a constant (1.1658), Ci is the weight fraction of the ith element in 
the material, and Ii is the mean excitation energy (eV). 
The above two equations can be used to calculate the electron scattering and 
energy deposition in solid material. The collision of electrons with atoms in solid is a 
random process. Which atom the electron collides with and which direction it is 
deﬂected are all random. Therefore, the scattered electron trajectories are also 
random, which can be modelled by Monte Carlo simulation [25, 26]. Monte Carlo 
method is a numerical technique for solving mathematic equations by a random 
process. Here it is not to use the method to solve mathematic equations but to borrow 
its name to reﬂect the random nature of electron scattering process. Figure 3.12a 
shows two electron trajectories (primary electron A and B), when they travel inside a 
resist layer on top of substrate material. The resist is PMMA of 0.1 μm thickness and 
the substrate is silicon. It was simulated by a Monte Carlo simulation program 
MOCASEL [27]. Each dot along a trajectory represents a scattering event. One 
can see that the position, direction, and free travel distance of each scattering event 
are totally random. Yet the probability of a scattering event taking place and the 
energy loss of a primary electron are governed by the physical law of electron-atom 
collisions. Although each individual scattering is random, simulation of a large 
number of electron scatterings will generate the general picture of the extent of 
energy deposition due to the collision and loss of energy from the primary electrons. 
Figure 3.12b plots out 100 electron trajectories. The electron energy is 20 keV with 
1 μm resist layer on top of a silicon substrate. This plot gives a rough idea how deep 
and how wide the electrons travel inside resist and substrate. Most of electrons 
scatter forward into substrate but some electrons scatter back into resist layer. These 
forward scatterings and backward scatterings (backscattering) are the cause of 
broadening of exposure area in resist. 
To generate statistically correct and reliable results, the number of electrons to be 
simulated has to be between 50,000 and 100,000. This number does not represent the 
real number of electrons in an electron beam, but only represents all the possible 
scattering directions of a single electron. Its energy deposition is represented by the 
so-called point spread function (PSF) which is the deposited energy distribution in 
resist from single point of incidence. Once the PSF is obtained, the energy deposition 
by a real electron beam can be calculated by convoluting the PSF with a beam spread 
function, for example, a Gaussian function for a Gaussian beam. The typical PSF is

[Page 105]
shown in Fig. 3.13, where forward scattering part and backscattering part of energy 
spread are indicated. Figure 3.13 also compares the PSFs at 10% and 90% depth of 
resist. It clearly displays a signiﬁcant increase of broadening in deposited energy by 
forward scattering, as well as accumulated energy by backscattering, as electrons go 
deeper.

There are three factors that strongly inﬂuence the electron scatterings:

1. Electron energy 
Higher energy of primary electron has much smaller scattering angle, therefore, 
less broadening of the beam due to small-angle forward scattering. Figure 3.14 
compares the PSFs of 50 keV, 70 keV, and 100 keV electron energies. The higher 
the electron energy is, the narrower the PSF becomes. This is another proof that high 
energy electron beam is preferred in e-beam lithography, in addition to previously 
mentioned beneﬁts in lowering chromatic and space charge aberrations. 
2. Density of substrate material 
More electrons will scatter back from substrate if the substrate material is of 
higher density. Figure 3.15 compares the PSFs for silicon and GaAs substrates. 
Electron energies are both at 50 keV and the resist thickness is the same as 0.5 μm. 
However, the deposited energy level due to backscattering electrons is signiﬁcantly 
higher for GaAs substrate than for silicon substrate, because the density of GaAs is 
2.3 times higher than that of silicon. Even if the substrate is a light material such as 
silicon, the backscattering can still be signiﬁcant if there is a thin ﬁlm of heavy metal, 
such as gold or tungsten, coated at the interface between silicon and resist layer. 
Therefore, it is more difﬁcult to achieve high resolution e-beam lithography on high 
density substrate.

Electron Beam Lithography
3. Resist thickness 
This has been apparent from Figs. 3.12b and 3.13 that electrons scatter wider 
forwardly as they go deeper. Therefore, for high resolution e-beam lithography, thin 
resist layer is preferred. 
3.4.2 
Proximity Effect and Correction 
Like the optical proximity effect which is caused by light diffraction, the direct 
consequence of electron scattering in e-beam lithography is the so-called electron 
proximity effect. Because the deposited energy from scattering electrons can extend 
to adjacent areas, dense features will become overexposed, and very isolated feature 
will be underexposed, creating the so-called intershape and intrashape proximity 
effect. An example of proximity effect from computer simulation is shown in 
Fig. 3.16. It shows the cross-section proﬁle of PMMA resist of 1 μm thickness, 
exposed by 20 keV electron beam. The pattern is 100 nm line grating with 500 nm 
pitch. PMMA is a positive tone resist. As described in Sect. 2.3.2 of Chap. 2, resist is 
removed after exposure and development for positive resist. The ideal proﬁle is

shown by the shaded area which is unexposed resist with 100 nm line openings. The 
actual resist proﬁle is shown by the contours which represent the evolution of resist 
cross section at different exposure doses. The higher the exposure dose, the deeper it 
goes until it reaches the full depth of 1 μm. It is apparent that the actual resist proﬁle 
(contour lines) is signiﬁcantly deviated from the ideal proﬁle (shaded area), because 
of the proximity effect. The result manifests the effect of electron forward scattering, 
which becomes wider as the electrons go deeper in the resist.
3.4
Scattering and Proximity Effect
109
Fig. 3.17 (a) Exposure patterns with same exposure dose, (b) computer-simulated 3D resist proﬁle 
without proximity correction 
For high resolution e-beam lithography, particularly when dealing with irregular 
pattern size and density, correction of proximity effect is absolutely necessary. 
Proximity correction has always been part of e-beam lithography development and 
can be dated back to more than 40 years ago [28]. Many research results have been 
published on this subject. In this section, the three principal correction methods are 
brieﬂy introduced. They are dose correction method, pattern size compensation 
method, and background exposure compensation method. 
1. Dose correction is the most widely used correction technique and also a technique 
giving the best correction results. As previously shown, electron scatterings 
create nonuniform distribution of electron energy in resist, which extends beyond 
the exposed pattern area and is the root cause of proximity effect. The principle of 
dose correction is to adjust the exposure dose at different parts of a pattern so that 
all patterns receive the same exposure energy. For example, Fig. 3.17a shows a 
group of patterns. They all receive the same exposure dose, represented by the 
same shading color. Because of the electron scattering and proximity effect, the 
line between the two big pads cannot be resolved, as the simulated 3D resist 
proﬁle shown in Fig. 3.17b. If the patterns are given different exposure doses, as 
shown in Fig. 3.18a where the light color shading represents lower exposure dose, 
the line in the middle can be fully resolved as shown by the simulated 3D resist 
proﬁle in Fig. 3.18b. In this case, the big pads receive less exposure dose, and the 
middle line receives different exposure doses at different sections depending on 
whether it is between the pads or outside the pads.

[Page 110]
110
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.18 (a) Exposure patterns with different exposure doses at different locations, (b) computer-
simulated 3D resist proﬁle with proximity correction 
The above example is a very simple case, for which one can intuitively alter the 
exposure dose on different parts of the patterns. For much complicated pattern 
designs, proximity correction has to be done by intensive computation. A self-
consistent method has been developed to ﬁnd the correct dose allocation for a design 
pattern [28]. The method ﬁrst assumes an initial exposure dose applying to all 
patterns. Energy distribution of all the patterns, including contributions from neigh-
boring patterns due to electron scattering, is calculated. Naturally, the distribution 
will not be uniform. The next step is to adjust the exposure dose on various regions 
of the exposure area and various parts of each pattern based on the known energy 
deposition. Now the exposure doses are no longer uniform. Calculation of energy is 
carried out again according to the new exposure doses. The uniformity of energy 
distribution should be improved, but may not be good enough. Another round of 
dose adjustment and energy calculation may be needed. The cycle is repeated until 
the deviation of energy distribution between two calculations is within an error limit 
(tolerance). It is then considered a self-consistent solution has been reached and the 
iteration can be terminated. The ﬁnal result will be a map of exposure dose which has 
different value at different part of exposure patterns. Theoretically, the self-
consistent method requires calculating the electron scattering effect for all 
the patterns. If the pattern number is N, there will be N sets of equation to solve. 
The amount of computing is enormous and may not be necessary. As we know that 
the electron scattering range is limited around the point of incidence, the self-
consistent method can be simpliﬁed by zoning the exposure area, i.e., the calculation 
of scattering and energy deposition is only carried out in certain zones where 
electron scattering effect is signiﬁcant [29]. 
The procedure of getting correct exposure doses by self-consistent solution is of 
direct physical meaning, hence called a physics-based correction method. Its solu-
tion is also relatively accurate. However, to ﬁnd self-consistent solutions for an IC 
design where there are millions of pattern shapes, the simulation procedure becomes 
very complicated, and the computation is very time-consuming. There is another 
simple geometry fraction method, which gives much faster proximity correction, 
though the correction result is relatively coarse. The difference of the two methods is

[Page 111]
that the physics-based correction fractures a pattern according to the self-consistent 
calculation of electron energy at every point of the pattern. So the fractured patterns 
are irregular. Geometry fraction method ﬁrst divides a pattern into regular 
sub-shapes according to the radius of electron scattering range and then calculates 
the electron energy deposition from which appropriate doses are assigned to each 
sub-shape [30]. It is a faster proximity correction method because there are no 
iterative computations. The electron energy in all patterns is only calculated once. 
3.4
Scattering and Proximity Effect
111
Fig. 3.19 An exposure pattern (a) without geometry fraction; (b) with geometry fraction 
The above proximity correction requires complicated computer algorithms. There 
are a number of commercial proximity correction software available. Usually the 
proximity correction software comes as part of software tool sets when an e-beam 
lithography system is purchased, for example, the NanoPECS proximity effect 
correction package supplied by Raith. These commercial software tools can process 
any pattern designs with reasonably accurate correction results. Fig. 3.18 is a simple 
example of geometry fraction, where the exposure doses are assigned manually. An 
example of proximity correction by computer is shown in Fig. 3.19. The original 
pattern with single exposure dose (single color) is fractured and assigned with 
different exposure doses to different parts of the pattern (different colors). 
2. The proximity correction can be done by resizing the pattern geometry, which is 
the pattern size compensation method. The resizing, also called biasing, is best 
suitable for simple and highly repeated patterns, such as lines and squares. For a 
group of repeated lines, the lines in the middle of pattern area can be resized 
narrower (negatively biased) than nominal design to compensate the higher than 
average energy contributed from neighboring lines, or the lines at the outer rim of 
pattern area can be resized wider (positively biased) to compensate the lower than 
average energy due to electron scattering. The overall result is a relatively 
uniform distribution of energy across the whole pattern area. The amount of

biasing can be determined empirically or from simulation of electron energy 
distribution. An example is shown in Fig. 3.20. It shows deposited energy for a 
group of seven lines with linewidth of 150 nm and pitch of 450 nm and exposed 
by 20 keV electron beam into 0.5-μm-thick resist on silicon. If all lines are 
exposed at equal width, the lines in the middle receive higher energy (solid line 
plot) because electron scatterings from both sides have contributed energy to the 
middle lines. If the middle lines are negatively biased by 10 nm to 25 nm, all the 
lines receive more or less the same energy (dashed line plot). 
3. Proximity correction can be done using background exposure to compensate the 
nonuniformity of electron energy distribution. As aforementioned, electron scat-
tering always causes excess of energy at dense pattern region and less energy at 
sparse pattern region. If a second exposure is carried out at all non-pattern regions 
(background), the pattern density for the second exposure is exactly reversed. 
The originally sparse pattern region now becomes dense pattern region and 
receives more energy. Meanwhile, the original dense pattern region where there 
are less blank areas now receives less energy. The superimposition of the two 
exposure can well balance the energy distribution so that it becomes uniform 
everywhere. Figure 3.21a shows the energy distribution of a group of seven lines 
(150 nm width and 450 nm pitch). The ﬁrst exposure produces an unbalanced 
energy proﬁle, with the lines in the middle receive higher energy. If a second 
background exposure, with energy proﬁle shown by the dashed line plot in 
Fig. 3.21a, is added to the ﬁrst exposure, the overall energy distribution is well 
balanced, as the energy proﬁle plot shown in Fig. 3.21b. The background 
exposure method has a name called “GHOST” [31]. It is simple and does not 
need special proximity correction software and does not need to calculate the 
electron energy distribution. All it needs is to have a second exposure of reverse 
polarity of the original pattern design. The second exposure can be carried out 
with a defocused electron beam and at relatively low exposure dose, with only the
