purpose of compensating the nonuniformity of energy. The drawbacks of this 
method are increased pattern data volume, increased total exposure time, and 
deterioration of image contrast due to the background exposure. This technique is 
only practical in a raster scan e-beam system. A raster scan system scans the 
whole exposure ﬁeld all the time. The beam is only switched on where there is a 
pattern shape to be exposed. Therefore, the background exposure is just another 
scan with beam switched on in opposite polarity. For a vector scan system, the 
beam only scans over a pattern area. The background exposure requires another 
set of design data with all the background patterns and the exposure time can be 
exceedingly long. 
All the above proximity correction techniques require additional process and 
special software tools. A simple and effective measure to reduce the proximity effect 
is to increase beam energy as well as to decrease resist thickness. High beam energy 
can greatly reduce the beam expansion caused by forward scattering and reduce 
localized backscattering. As forward scattering is wider with electrons going deeper, 
a thin resist layer can also minimize the effect of beam expansion. An example is 
shown in Fig. 3.22, which is a direct comparison with Fig. 3.16. The pattern design 
and resist thickness are the same in both cases. The only difference is the beam 
energy for the exposure. While in Fig. 3.16 the resist is exposed at 20 keV, the beam 
energy in Fig. 3.22 is 100 keV. No proximity effect is seen for 100 keV exposure and 
resist proﬁle is almost the same as the ideal proﬁle (shaded area). 
All the above discussion on electron scattering and proximity effect has favored 
using high energy electron beam. High beam energy results in narrower forward 
scattering and lower backscattering. Although the higher, the better, too high voltage 
makes the design and construction of electron optical column very difﬁcult. Besides, 
electron energy above ~120 keV can damage silicon lattice and semiconductor 
devices. Therefore, it has been concluded that 100 keV is the best suitable energy 
for e-beam lithography [32]. However, one must be aware that high beam energy 
requires high exposure doses. The exposure dose increases linearly with the increase 
of beam energy, because majority of primary electrons have gone deep into substrate 
and very few of them contribute to the exposure of resist.

3.4.3 
Effect of Secondary Electrons 
So far the discussion about electron scattering and proximity effect has only been 
focused on primary electrons. Although the major contribution of proximity effect 
comes from scattering of primary electrons, secondary electrons can play a vital role 
when e-beam lithography is conducted close to the beam diameter, namely, around 
10 nm or below. The secondary electrons are electrons knocked off from the atoms 
of solid material by high energy primary electrons. While traveling inside the resist, 
primary electrons transfer their energy to the electrons around the atoms of materials. 
These secondary electrons acquiring up to kilovolts of energy become mobile and 
can travel a short distance. For example, electrons of 400 eV energy can travel about 
12 nm and 200 eV electrons can travel about 5 nm distance [33]. They scatter the 
same way as primary electrons, only in a short distance, but always in directions 
perpendicular to the path of primary electrons, and leave their energy along the path 
of scattering. The consequence of the scatterings of these “fast” secondary electrons 
is a broadened energy deposition in resist. Figure 3.23 gives a comparison of point 
spread functions (PSFs) with and without contributions from secondary electrons by

[Page 115]
Monte Carlo simulation. It is apparent that secondary electrons made a noticeable 
contribution to the low energy part of PSF. 
3.4
Scattering and Proximity Effect
115
Fig. 3.23 Comparison of 
PSFs with and without 
secondary electron 
contribution 
It was concluded decades ago that on average the secondary electrons scatter 
about 10 nm distance around the main path of a primary electron, which limits the 
e-beam lithography resolution to no better than 10 nm, no matter how small the 
e-beam spot size is [34]. However, this conclusion is not completely true, because 
e-beam lithography depends not only on electron energy deposition but also on resist 
material and process. The latter can to some extent suppress the energy broadening 
effect caused by secondary electrons and enable lithography resolution to go below 
10 nm. The e-beam resist process will be discussed in a later section of this chapter. 
3.4.4 
Low Energy E-Beam Lithography 
Although high beam energy has been favored in e-beam lithography, its rather 
inefﬁcient use of energy, i.e., the need of high exposure dose and possible damage 
to substrate material or to buried structures, has prompted investigation on very low 
energy exposure of resists. At very low end of beam energy (0.5–5 keV), primary 
electrons cannot travel very far. Figure 3.24 compares the electron scattering trajec-
tories at 10 keV energy (Fig. 3.24a) and 3 keV energy (Fig. 3.24b). The resist 
thickness is 100 nm on a silicon substrate. At 10 keV, most of the electrons have

arise: one is substrate heating and another is substrate charging. For nonconductive 
substrate materials, such as quartz and glass which are commonly used for making 
photomasks, both heating and charging exist. Charging problem can be avoided by 
using a metallic underlayer between resist and nonconductive substrate [35]. Resist 
heating due to deposition of large amount of electron energy in a substrate of poor 
thermal conductivity can cause change of resist sensitivity and severe proximity 
effect [36], particularly in e-beam lithography of photomasks [37], and there is no 
effective way to overcome the heating problem.

Electron Energy (keV) 
With low energy electrons, there would be little energy deposited into substrate if 
a correct combination of resist thickness and beam energy is chosen. Figure 3.25 
shows experimentally measured penetration depth in resist at different beam energy. 
The resist was ZEP-520 positive e-beam resist. A series of large square patterns were 
exposed by an e-beam with different energies. The resist was then developed and the 
remaining thickness was measured, from which the penetration depth was worked 
out [38]. 
Because the electron penetration is shallow at low energy, it brings two advan-
tages: One is small range of forward scattering. If the resist thickness is just about the 
penetration depth of low energy electrons, then there will be no backscattered 
electrons, 
hence 
no 
backscattering-related 
proximity 
effect. 
For 
example, 
sub-100 nm lines were produced in 66-nm-thick PMMA layer at 2 keV beam energy, 
and the deviation of linewidth between dense pattern region and sparse pattern 
region was found to be less than 10 nm [39]. Another advantage of shallow 
penetration of electrons at low energy e-beam is the high concentration of energy 
deposition in resist layer so that fewer electrons are needed for resist exposure. 
Resists become much more sensitive to low energy electrons. Figure 3.26 is the 
measured resist sensitivity vs. electron energy [38]. The resist (ZEP-520) was 
180 nm thick and electron energy ranged from 3 keV to 20 keV (the electron

[Page 118]
penetration depth is over 180 nm at 3 keV energy as shown in Fig. 3.25). The resist 
sensitivity is deﬁned as the exposure dose needed to enable the 180-nm-thick resist 
layer to be fully developed. As seen from the plot, the decrease of resist sensitivity 
(increase of required exposure dose) is linearly proportional to the increase of beam 
energy. In fact, low energy electrons are much more efﬁcient at exposing a resist 
polymer, because the majority of polymer chain scission or cross-linking happens 
during interaction with electrons of below 100 eV energy. For high energy e-beam 
lithography, it is the low energy secondary electrons, excited by high energy primary 
electrons, which are responsible for the exposure of resist [33].

Electron Beam Lithography

Electron energy (keV) 
Resist sensitivity (μC/cm2) 
The shallow penetration of low energy e-beam lithography is also a drawback, 
because only very thin resist can be used for lithography. The resist thickness should 
normally be below 100 nm, depending on the beam energy. As is known from 
previous chapter, thin resist is less resistant to reactive ion etching, making resist 
pattern transfer to substrate more difﬁcult. In this case, multilayer resist structure has 
to be used, whereby the top thin layer resist acts as an imaging layer [40]. Another 
drawback of low energy e-beam lithography is the difﬁculty to achieve ﬁne size of 
beam. As has been described in previous section, both chromatic aberration and 
space charge effect become severe at low beam energy. The performance of a 
conventional e-beam lithography system falls rapidly below 6 keV acceleration 
voltage. The only way to maintain relative focusing quality of the electron optical 
column is to introduce a retarding ﬁeld after the objective lens, to slow down high 
energy e-beam to a low energy before landing on the substrate [41]. 
A more promising low energy e-beam lithography technology was proposed in 
the late 1990s, which was based on projection of low energy electrons using a stencil 
mask [42]. The resolution of low energy e-beam projection lithography (LEEPL) is 
no longer limited by the beam spot size but by the resolution of stencil mask. A 
70 nm feature dimension has been achieved by LEEPL at 2 keV electron energy on 
50-nm-thick ZEP-520 resist with no proximity effect [43]. However, the requirement 
of a stencil mask stopped the LEEPL from further development due to the same

[Page 119]
reason as for X-ray lithography or other projection-based e-beam lithography. 
Presently, the only commercial system that offers low energy e-beam lithography 
is the Raith150 Two which can set beam energy from 100 eV to 30 keV, although 
low energy e-beam lithography can also be implemented simply using a SEM 
converted e-beam lithography tool for scientiﬁc research purpose. 
3.5
Resist Materials and Processes
119
3.5 
Resist Materials and Processes 
The energy of electrons deposited in a polymer resist will have to be converted into 
molecular chain reaction in a resist to eventually form a lithographic pattern. On the 
one hand, a small beam size and small scattering range are not necessarily translated 
into a small size of resist features. On the other hand, a poor resist material and 
non-optimum resist process can certainly result in poor resolution and much 
enhanced proximity effect. Resist materials and processes play a vital role in 
achieving the ultimate resolution capability of e-beam lithography. The basics 
of polymer resists and speciﬁcs for optical lithography have been mentioned in 
Sects. 2.3.2 and 2.7.4 of Chap. 2. The present section deals with speciﬁcs of e-beam 
resists and the factors inﬂuencing the ﬁnal achievable e-beam lithography resolution. 
3.5.1 
Sensitivity 
The most important properties of a resist are sensitivity and contrast, which are 
directly linked to the resolution capability of the resist. The sensitivity and contrast 
are derived from a resist development curve which typically looks like the plots in 
Fig. 3.27 (more information about the resist development curve can be found in
(a)
(b)

Exposure dose

Fig. 3.27 Illustration of resist development curves: (a) resist A is of higher sensitivity than resist B; 
(b) resist A is of higher contrast than resist B, and resist C is negative resist

[Page 120]
Fig. 2.9 of Chap. 2). Similar to photoresists, long molecule chains are broken by 
energized electrons into short chains, called chain secession process in a positive 
tone resist, so that the exposed parts of polymer become soluble in developer. For a 
negative tone resist, the initial short chain molecules are joined upon exposure to 
form long chains, called cross-linking process, so that the exposure parts of polymer 
become insoluble in developer. The development curves, also called contrast curves, 
are obtained by exposure and development of a series of large square patterns with 
different exposure doses. The remaining resist thickness is measured against the 
exposure dose, which generates the curves shown in Fig. 3.27. For positive resist, 
there is an exposure dose at which the resist layer is fully developed to bottom. This 
exposure dose is deﬁned as the resist sensitivity. The smaller this threshold dose, the 
higher the resist sensitivity. In Fig. 3.27a, resist A is more sensitive than resist B. For 
negative resist, the development curve looks like the plot C in Fig. 3.27b. With the 
increase of exposure dose, the degree of cross-linking increases, and more resist 
thickness remains after development, until the whole resist layer is completely cross-
linked. The sensitivity of negative resist is deﬁned by the exposure dose which 
results in more than half the resist thickness remaining due to cross-linking. A 
practical example of development curves is shown in Fig. 3.28, where the depth of 
resist development at different exposure dose is plotted for a negative tone resist, 
HSQ [44]. The development curves at different beam energy are also shown.

One must be aware that the above deﬁnition of resist sensitivity is only for 
comparison purpose. It is deﬁned by exposure of much larger pattern area, which 
is rarely representative in actual e-beam lithography. The actual exposure dose 
needed for a particular pattern exposure can be quite different. It depends on the 
following factors:
.
Electron energy. Higher electron energy requires higher exposure dose, resulting 
in low resist sensitivity, as is shown in Fig. 3.28.
.
Resist thickness. The thinner the resist, the lower the resist sensitivity, because 
fewer electrons deposit their energy within the thin resist layer.

[Page 121]

Resist Materials and Processes

Table 3.2 Sensitivity and resolution of some e-beam resists

.
Pattern density. Exposure of dense patterns requires less exposure dose, because 
electron scattering from neighboring exposure areas contributes to the overall 
energy deposition. Similarly, isolated patterns require high exposure dose, 
because a portion of electrons have scattered outside the pattern area.
.
Substrate material. High density substrate materials result in more backscattering 
electrons into resist layer, contributing to the resist exposure. Therefore, resist 
coated on a high density material appears more sensitive than on a low density 
material.
.
Process conditions. The post-exposure baking conditions, in the case of chemi-
cally ampliﬁed resists, the strength of developer, and the temperature of developer 
can all have inﬂuence on the resist sensitivity, which will be discussed later. 
For high resolution e-beam lithography, low sensitivity resists are preferred and 
also inevitable. Table 3.2 shows some widely used e-beam resists, which indicates 
the correlation between sensitivity and resolution capability. PMMA, the positive 
tone resist discovered more than 50 years ago, is still being used today although it is 
of very low sensitivity. PMMA is by far the only positive e-beam resist which has the 
highest resolution capability. The smallest feature size produced by e-beam lithog-
raphy in PMMA is 4–5 nm, which was demonstrated more than 20 years ago 
[45, 46]. It must be noted that PMMA resist comes with different molecular weight. 
Typical commercial PMMA resists are of 950 K and 495 K molecular weights 
[47]. High molecular weight is generally of low sensitivity but high resolution 
capability. Another positive tone e-beam resist alternative to PMMA is the ZEP 
series (520A and 530A) developed by ZEON of Japan [48], which are of similar 
resolution capability but higher sensitivity than PMMA. Only one third of exposure 
dose is needed for ZEP resists, as shown in Table 3.2. They also have better etch 
resistance than PMMA, which is a key property for resist pattern transferring to 
substrate. 
The best negative tone e-beam resist is hydrogen silsesquioxane (HSQ). HSQ is a 
liquid form of silicon dioxide initially developed at Dow Corning with the product 
name of ﬂowable oxide (Fx-12). HSQ was ﬁrst reported for e-beam lithography in 
1998 [49]. Nowadays sub-10 nm resist line features can be achieved routinely with 
HSQ. There was also a report of 5–6 nm isolated line features produced in 30-nm-
thick HSQ by e-beam lithography at 100 keV [9], as well as sub-10 nm dense line 
features in ultrathin (10 nm) HSQ layer [50, 51]. Although PMMA has similar or

[Page 122]
slightly better resolution capability than HSQ, the line edge roughness (LER) in 
PMMA at sub-10 nm dimension is very high, whereas the LER in HSQ at sub-10 nm 
resolution is much lower [52]. The etch resistance of HSQ is also much higher than 
that of PMMA because of its inorganic nature. In these aspects, HSQ is a better 
e-beam resist for extremely high resolution e-beam lithography. Due to the success 
in e-beam lithography, HSQ is nowadays supplied as a commercial e-beam resist 
from a number of vendors worldwide. One should be aware that commercial HSQ 
has a storage lifetime of 6 months, beyond which the resist property may change. 
E-beam lithography experiments on aged HSQ revealed an increase of sensitivity, 
although the resolution capability was not notably compromised [53]. A dry powder 
form of HSQ (AQM-HSQ [54]) was developed, which has inﬁnite long shelf life but 
same properties as the Dow Corning HSQ [55]. It should be mentioned that HSQ is 
not only widely used as e-beam resists but also being used for EUV optical 
lithography [56]. 
122
3
Electron Beam Lithography
If lithography throughput is not a critical issue, particularly in R&D where 
generating nanoscale patterns is paramount, then there are some inorganic resist 
materials which are of very low sensitivity but very high resolution capability. Use 
of inorganic materials as resist in e-beam lithography dated back more than 40 years 
ago, when 8 nm features were made for the ﬁrst time by e-beam exposure of metal 
thin ﬁlm to form a carbon contamination pattern which acted as an ion sputter 
etching mask [57]. The exposure dose required to generate the carbon contamination 
feature was, however, as high as 107 μC/cm2 . A number of other inorganic materials 
have been tried for e-beam lithography over the years, including metal halides (LiF, 
AlF3, CaF2, MgF2, NaCl) with threshold exposure dose >106 μC/cm2 and metal 
oxides (AlOx, ZnO, TiOx) with threshold exposure dose >103 μC/cm2 [52]. Even ice 
has been tried as an e-beam resist [58]. The process of ice lithography is rather 
simple: introducing water vapor into an e-beam tool equipped with a cryostage to 
form a layer of ice on the substrate surface and exposing with e-beam to evaporate 
the ice selectively to form an ice pattern. The ice pattern can be further transferred to 
substrate by a lift-off process which will be introduced in Chap. 8. Rising the 
chamber temperature to room temperature, the remaining ice evaporates and the 
fabrication is completed. Although very high exposure dose is needed, for example, 
to evaporate 80-nm-thick ice, it needs 106 μC/cm2 exposure dose at 20 keV [59], the 
advantages of ice lithography are also remarkable. These include in situ patterning 
without taking samples out of vacuum chamber, no need for solvent development 
and patterning on nonﬂat substrate, etc. [60]. By repeating the freezing-evaporation 
cycle, 3D nanostructures can be built [61]. Ice lithography can be done not only with 
water vapor but also with organic vapor as long as they can freeze [62]. Lower 
exposure dose is needed for organic ice than for water ice [63]. There were also 
attempts to mix organic resists with inorganic materials to create quasi-inorganic 
resists, for example, fullerene molecules (C60) mixed with ZEP-520 [64], PMMA 
[65], or NEB-22 [66] and silica nanoparticles mixed with ZEP-520 [67], which have 
improved the resolution capability and etch resistance of the resists, but at the cost of 
reduced resist sensitivity.

[Page 123]
3.5
Resist Materials and Processes
123
Fig. 3.29 SU-8 resist 
patterns exposed by e-beam 
at 50 keV energy 
To understand why low sensitivity resists have high resolution capability, one 
should look at the PSF shown in Fig. 3.23. The long tail of PSF caused by secondary 
electrons and backscattered electrons will not have signiﬁcant exposure effect if the 
resist is less sensitive. In other words, these low energy electrons will only show up 
their impact on resist pattern proﬁle if the resist sensitivity is very high. An example 
is shown in Fig. 3.29 which is the SEM image of exposed SU-8 resist [68]. SU-8 is a 
photoresist but also sensitive to electrons. The sensitivity of SU-8 is around 1μC/cm2 
at 50 keV beam energy, which is extremely high compared with PMMA. The 
consequence of such a high sensitivity is the residues of SU-8 resist shown between 
the exposed resist patterns. The residues are caused by exposure of backscattering 
electrons. They only exist between the exposure patterns because of the accumulated 
effect from both sides of electron scattering. If the resist had not been so sensitive, 
the residues would not have occurred. 
Normally a pattern is exposed by e-beam lithography through multi-pass scan to 
ﬁll up the exposure area, because the beam diameter and scan grid are much smaller 
than the pattern dimension. For very high resolution lithography of line features 
(<50 nm), a single pass exposure is often employed, whereby the electron beam 
scans only a single pass. The linewidth is then determined by the exposure dose of 
this single pass. Although a single pass exposure should theoretically be only the 
width of beam diameter, the actual line in resist can be much wider if exposed at 
high dose. 
Because the accuracy of resist pattern is so much dependent on exposure dose, it 
is a common practice that e-beam lithography of any new pattern designs is always 
started with a dose test, i.e., running a series of exposure for the same pattern design 
at different exposure doses, from which the optimum exposure dose range is 
determined.

[Page 124]
124
3
Electron Beam Lithography
3.5.2 
Contrast 
Resist contrast is deﬁned by the slope of a development curve and expressed in 
Eq. 3.17. D0 is the exposure dose at which the resist starts to be exposed, also 
sometimes called threshold dose (Dth), and D1 is the exposure dose at which the 
resist is fully exposed. They are marked in Fig. 3.27a. The steeper the slope is, the 
higher the resist contrast. For example, the resist A in Fig. 3.27b has higher contrast 
than resist B. Resist contrast is an inherent property of a resist material. However, 
development conditions can have some inﬂuence on it. 
γ = 
1 
log 10D1 - log 10D0 
ð3:17Þ 
By this deﬁnition, the contrast of negative tone resist HSQ shown in Fig. 3.28 is 
2.8 [44]. Another negative tone resist, SU-8, has a contrast of only 0.92, as measured 
from contrast curve shown in Fig. 3.30 [68, 69]. By the appearance of contrast 
curves of these two resists, one can immediately tell that the HSQ resist has higher 
contrast. Apart from measuring the slope of resist contrast curves, another direct way 
of judging the resist contrast is to observe the resist proﬁle after development. An 
example to compare the effect of resist contrast on resist proﬁle is shown in 
Fig. 3.31, where computer-simulated resist proﬁles are shown for 50 keV e-beam 
exposure of 0.5-μm-thick resist. Figure 3.31a is the proﬁle of low contrast resist, 
while Fig. 3.31b is the proﬁle of high contrast resist. Although electron scattering 
and proximity effect are the same in both cases, high contrast resist can produce the 
most desirable proﬁle. High contrast enables resist proﬁles to have vertical sidewalls 
and high aspect ratios (feature height to feature width), while low contrast resist will 
have tapered sidewall. As an example, e-beam exposure of low contrast SU-8 resist 
with sensitivity of 1μC/cm2 resulted in sidewall broadening which is proportional to

was as large as 2.7 μm when exposed at 6μC/cm2 and became 5.5 μm  at  18μC/ 
cm2 [70].

As previously mentioned, generally low sensitivity resists have high contrast and 
high sensitivity resists have low contrast. However, there is an exception that some 
chemically ampliﬁed (CA) resists are of both high sensitivity and high contrast. CA 
resists have been introduced in Sect. 2.3.2 in Chap. 2. They are highly sensitive 
because a few photoacid generators (PAGs) can be ampliﬁed in numbers at later 
stage of post-exposure baking. The CA resists were originally developed for deep 
UV optical lithography but later were found also sensitive to electrons, hence used as 
e-beam resists as well. Figure 3.32 shows high aspect ratio patterns in negative tone 
CA resist AZPN114 (Fig. 3.32a)  [71] and positive tone CA resist UV3 (Fig. 3.32b) 
[72] exposed by 100 keV electron beam. Similar results have been demonstrated for 
other CA resists, such as SAL601 [71] and NEB-22 [73]. These CA resists are 
generally capable of resolution below 50 nm. Although their resolution capability is 
not as high as PMMA or HSQ, their very high sensitivity, two to three orders of

[Page 126]
magnitude higher, makes them very attractive in high-throughput e-beam lithogra-
phy. For example, Fig. 3.33 shows the 100 nm hole array exposed by 100 keV 
electron beam with PMMA at the exposure dose of 1450μC/cm2 (Fig. 3.33a) and 
with AZPF514, a positive tone CA resist, at the exposure dose of 25μC/cm2 
(Fig. 3.33b)  [72]. The CA resist is nearly 60 times more sensitive than PMMA. 
126
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.33 100 nm holes exposed with (a) PMMA at 1450μC/cm2 and (b) AZPF514 at 25μC/cm2 
In the early days when optical lithography stopped at 193 nm wavelength, the IC 
industry was seriously looking at the possibility of using high-throughput e-beam 
lithography for further technology nodes. Unfortunately, optical lithography 
marched on with immersion and multi-patterning technologies and e-beam direct 
write for mass production of ICs never took off. On the one hand, the CA resists have 
many issues such as highly sensitive to post-exposure baking condition, post-
exposure delay effect, and susceptible to exposure environment [71, 74, 75]. On 
the other hand, scientiﬁc community only cares about high resolution, not produc-
tivity. Researchers worldwide mostly contend with a few mature e-beam resists such 
as PMMA and HSQ for nanoscale patterning, though there are still people interested 
in research on new CA resists purely from scientiﬁc point of views [76–78]. One 
must be aware that resists of too high sensitivity may not be suitable for some slow 
scanning e-beam systems. If the scanning speed of an e-beam system is slow, for 
example, below 10 MHz, the beam current has to be extremely small in order not to 
overexpose some very high sensitivity CA resists. Extremely small beam current 
makes the focus calibration and registration extremely difﬁcult because of low 
secondary electron image. Some systems may have a lower limit on attainable 
beam current, which renders the exposure of CA resists by these systems impossible. 
3.5.3 
Resolution Enhancement Processes 
The resist sensitivity and contrast are partly dependent on the resist material prop-
erty, as well as partly dependent on the resist process conditions. Although every

[Page 127]
commercial resist comes with a guideline process conditions given by resist vendors, 
the conditions are meant only for general applications and may not be tuned to 
achieve the highest resolution capability. The process conditions are also tied to 
pattern density and exposure condition, such as the beam energy and exposure dose. 
Therefore, to achieve the highest resolution for a particular resist, the optimum 
process conditions should be investigated. In some cases, exotic methods were 
developed to enable very high resolution of e-beam lithography which has previ-
ously been unthinkable. These exotic methods include ultrasonic assisted resist 
development and development at lower or higher temperature. 
3.5
Resist Materials and Processes
127
The enhancement effect by ultrasonic assisted development on e-beam lithogra-
phy resolution with PMMA resist was discovered in 1993 [79]. Previously it had 
been generally accepted that e-beam lithography has a resolution limit at around 
10 nm because of secondary electron scattering effect [80]. However, one fact was 
overlooked in considering the resolution limit, that is, the behavior of polymer 
molecules during the development process. The development is realized by short 
chain polymer molecules dissolved in a developer solution after chain scission action 
by electron exposure. When the exposure region becomes very small (<10 nm), the 
molecules, which for PMMA is of a size around 2 nm, can be trapped in solid 
PMMA due to intermolecular forces which increase rapidly within the radius of 
10 nm. More electron interaction with the polymer is needed to release the trapped 
polymer molecules, which means higher exposure dose. The exposure region then 
becomes wider at the increased exposure dose. With ultrasonic assisted develop-
ment, the ultrasonic agitation can provide additional energy and help to loosen up the 
trapped molecules to dissolve into the developer solution. With the assistance of 
ultrasonic agitation, exposure lines as small as 4 nm in PMMA has been achieved 
[45]. The ultrasonic agitation was also proved to reduce the exposure dose signiﬁ-
cantly at feature size below 20 nm, as shown in Fig. 3.34. It becomes no difference to 
conventional development process for big feature size because the intermolecular
Fig. 3.34 Comparison of

[Page 128]
force is no longer dominant at above 10 nm line opening. There were also other 
explanations as to why the ultrasonic agitation can help enhance the resolution of 
PMMA, such as reduction of developer viscosity or reduction of resist swelling 
[81]. However, it must be pointed out that the resolution enhancement by ultrasonic 
agitation only works for PMMA resist with high molecular weight, which demon-
strates that resist development process is very much dependent on individual resist 
materials.
128
3
Electron Beam Lithography
Fig. 3.35 Comparison of edge roughness of ZEP-520 resist lines (40 nm wide) developed at (a) 
room temperature and (b)  at -4 °C temperature. (Reprint from [82] with permission) 
Another method to enhance resolution is to develop the exposed PMMA in a cold 
developer. Sub-10 nm lines were achieved at 30 keV beam energy with MIBK+IPA 
(1:3) developer which was maintained at 4–8 °C temperature [46]. Unlike ultrasonic 
agitation, which promotes the activity of PMMA molecules, cold developer actually 
suppresses the activity of PMMA molecules. In order to develop the exposed 
PMMA resist at low temperature, exposure dose much higher than development in 
room temperature is needed. High exposure dose is known to result in wider 
exposure region, as indicated by previous electron scattering analysis. However, 
energy level deposited in resist is rapidly reduced at the location away from the 
center of exposure region, as shown by the point spread function for very thin resist 
layer in Fig. 3.13. In a cold developer, those PMMA molecules exposed by low level 
electron energy cannot be dissolved because of their low level of kinetic activity at 
low temperature. Only the very central region where the deposited electron energy is 
the highest can be developed, resulting in high resolution exposure features. The 
similar resolution enhancement at low temperature development was also found in 
e-beam lithography of ZEP-520 resist. With the developer temperature as low as -
17 °C, 13 nm lines in ZEP-520 exposed at 30 keV beam energy have been obtained 
[82]. A particular beneﬁt of low temperature development for ZEP-520 is the much 
improved line edge roughness, as demonstrated in Fig. 3.35. The ZEP-520 resist 
lines in Fig. 3.35a were developed at room temperature, while the lines in Fig. 3.35b 
were developed at -4 °C. It clearly shows the ZEP-520 resist molecules have much 
lower kinetics in low temperature developer solution. 
The reason that cold development works for both PMMA and ZEP-520 resists is 
believed to be due to the variation of molecule weight distribution before and after

[Page 129]
electron exposure. Upon electron exposure, chain scissions occur, resulting in short 
chains and low-weight molecules in the exposed region. Low temperature developer 
reduces the kinetic energy of molecules. Only those of molecules with very short 
chain can be dissolved by the developer, which effectively suppressed the inﬂuence 
of scattering electrons because the polymer molecules exposed by low energy 
scattering electrons cannot participate the dissolving process. The same mechanism 
can explain why low strength developer can help to achieve high resolution resist 
patterns. Table 3.3 shows qualitatively the relation between developer concentration 
and resolution of PMMA resist. On the other hand, positive chemically ampliﬁed 
resists such as UV3 do not show any improvement in low temperature developers 
because there is no molecular weight degradation process during e-beam exposure of 
these resists [82]. However, cold development does show better results for some 
negative chemically ampliﬁed resists such as NEB-22 [83] and SAL-601 [84].

While low temperature development helps to enhance resolution of PMMA and 
ZEP-520, it works the opposite for HSQ resist. The contrast of HSQ resist is actually 
enhanced at high development temperature, as shown in Fig. 3.36, where develop-
ment temperature varies from 18 °C  to  50  °C, including ultrasonic assisted devel-
opment [85]. The sensitivity of HSQ is decreasing but the contrast is increasing with 
the increase of development temperature. It is not clear exactly why the high 
temperature development has caused the decrease of e-beam exposure sensitivity 
of HSQ, as most other resists become more sensitive at high development

[Page 130]
temperature. However, the beneﬁt of high temperature development of HSQ is 
apparent. High resolution dense patterns can be resolved when developed at high 
temperature. However, there is a limit on how high the development temperature 
should be. It is found there is a signiﬁcant destruction of the exposed structures in the 
resist at development temperature of 60 °C  [86]. 
130
3
Electron Beam Lithography
3.6 
Ultimate Resolution of E-Beam Lithography 
E-beam lithography is by far of the highest resolution, most versatile, and most 
widely used lithography technique for nanoscale patterning. No wonder that every 
laboratory around the world which claims to be a nanofabrication lab will be found 
to have an e-beam lithography system, whether it is a multimillion dollar complex 
machine or a simple SEM converted system. There are many factors, in addition to 
the hardware capability, that inﬂuence the ultimate resolution an e-beam machine 
can achieve. These factors have been analyzed in previous sections. Here a summary 
of conditions or preferences is given if one intends to achieve the highest possible 
resolution of e-beam lithography. 
1. Using high electron energy. It can reduce the spread of forward scattering 
electrons and enlarge the spread of backscattering electrons. The latter can 
avoid local concentration of backscattering electron energy deposition and 
both can effectively reduce the proximity effect. At high beam energy, both 
the chromatic aberration and space charge effect in the electron lens system are 
reduced, which helps to obtain small beam spot. High energy electrons also 
penetrate deeper in a resist layer, which facilitate the exposure of thick resist and 
to achieve high aspect ratio resist pattern structures. 
2. Choosing small aperture. The aperture that limits the angle of electron beam 
spread in an e-beam system is generally selectable. For high resolution e-beam 
lithography, small aperture is preferred, which can effectively reduce the spher-
ical aberration since the aberration is proportional to the cubic power of beam 
spread angle. 
3. Using low beam current. Low beam current can reduce the aberration caused by 
space charge effect and helps to reduce the beam spot size. However, the adverse 
effect of low beam current is the increase of exposure time as well as reduced 
electron signal intensity which is used to detect the focusing and overlay marks. 
4. Choosing small scanning ﬁeld. Small scanning ﬁeld means small-angle deﬂec-
tion of beam, which can reduce the aberrations associated with deﬂection. 
5. Using low sensitivity e-beam resists. Low sensitivity resists can effectively 
suppress the exposure of resist by secondary electrons and low energy 
backscattered electrons. For lithography below 20 nm, low sensitivity resists 
such as PMMA (positive tone) and HSQ (negative tone) are commonly used.

[Page 131]
3.6
Ultimate Resolution of E-Beam Lithography
131
6. Using thin layer resist. Thin resist can reduce the proximity effect caused by 
forward scattering electrons. 
7. Optimizing resist processes. If the above conditions cannot all be met, optimized 
resist processes including resist coating, adhesion, development condition, and 
pre-/post-baking can to some extent help to improve the lithography resolution. 
8. Avoiding high density patterns. As previously explained, the most difﬁcult 
pattern to achieve high resolution is the high density pattern. A single 10 nm 
line pattern is far easier than dense pattern of 10 nm half pitch to obtain. Even 
proximity effect correction is powerless for high resolution periodic dense 
patterns. 
9. Avoiding high density or poor conducting substrates. High density substrates 
generate more backscattering electrons, which means more severe proximity 
effect; poor conducting substrate causes accumulation of electrical charge which 
generates local electrical ﬁeld, causing distortion of exposed patterns, as well as 
excessive heat if the beam current is high. 
10. The last but not the least is to maintain a stable environment for the e-beam 
system to operate. The stable environment includes low surrounding vibration, 
low electromagnetic interference, and low ﬂuctuation of ambient temperature. 
These conditions become necessary when exposing sub-100 nm patterns. 
What is the ultimate resolution of e-beam lithography? Such a question has been 
asked over and over, and many researchers have been trying to demonstrate the 
smallest feature one can achieve [5, 34, 80]. Clearly, the beam size is not a limiting 
factor, as the electron optical system in a scanning transmission electron microscope 
(STEM) can achieve the beam size well below 1 nm. An experiment has demon-
strated 1.7 nm line feature in PMMA using an aberration-corrected STEM [87]. In 
the experiment, the electron energy was 200 keV which enabled 0.5 nm beam size; 
the substrate was a 5-nm-thick SiNx membrane which minimized the electron 
backscattering effect; the PMMA is 7 nm thick which minimized the forward 
scattering effect. Another experiment using aberration-corrected STEM at 200 keV 
and beam size of 1.5 nm demonstrated 2 nm feature size in HSQ which was 10 nm 
thick on a 10-nm-thick SiNx membrane [88]. Theoretically the ultimate resolution 
should only be limited by beam diameter [5]. In practice, many factors can inﬂuence 
the resolution, such as the aforementioned ten factors. One should bear in mind that 
the goal of electron beam lithography is for nanofabrication. In most cases, the 
patterned resist features have to be transferred to substrate either through etching or 
lift-off [89], which limits the ultimate resolution of patterning common e-beam 
resists PMMA or HSQ by e-beam lithography around 4–5 nm, though there are 
indirect techniques which can pattern even smaller features as will be introduced in 
Chap. 9.

[Page 132]
132
3
Electron Beam Lithography
3.7 
High-Throughput E-Beam Lithography 
Although an electron beam can be focused into a very small spot and, therefore, can 
do very high resolution lithography, its fundamental limit is the low throughput 
because of its sequential nature in exposure. While optical lithography has standard 
throughput of at least 60 wafers per hour, a single 50 nm electron beam has to have a 
scanning speed of 20 km/s and beam dwelling time of 2.5 ps in order to compete 
with this throughput [90]. None of the single electron beam lithography systems can 
ever dream of such a high scanning speed, and none of the e-beam resists have such 
high sensitivity. At the current level of scanning speed and beam current for a 
Gaussian beam system, an 8 inch wafer would need tens of hours to expose. To 
make a photomask for 22 nm node of IC patterning with 193 nm photolithography, 
the e-beam writing time would be 80 hours [91]. 
The key motivation to develop high-throughput e-beam lithography systems in 
the early days was to prepare for the next generation lithography (NGL) after optical 
lithography ran out of steam for 16 nm generation or even single digit feature size 
of ICs. By 2010, the semiconductor industry considered that 193 nm optical lithog-
raphy with immersion imaging and multi-patterning could go as far as 22 nm node of 
IC patterning. Below 22 nm, EUV was not ready to take over and e-beam direct write 
might have the chance to play a role in mass production of ICs at 16 nm or even 
11 nm [92]. A single beam system can never meet the challenge. Electron beam 
projection lithography became the favorable option. While optical lithography relies 
on mask projection, e-beam can do the same. In e-beam mask projection lithography, 
a broad beam is used as the “light” source to illuminate a mask. Electrons passing 
through the mask are focused into high resolution image to expose a resist layer. 
Several types of e-beam projection lithography (EPL) systems were developed 
including the cell projection system from Hitachi (HL-800D) [93], the projection 
reduction exposure with variable axis immersion lens (PREVAIL) system from IBM 
[94], and the scattering with angular limitation projection electron-beam lithography 
(SCALPEL) system from Lucent Technologies [95]. All of these e-beam lithography 
technologies rely on exposure through a mask. However, the mask, whether stencil 
type or membrane type, is difﬁcult to make, to inspect, and to repair. This has been 
an age-old problem when X-ray lithography was in its peak development era. Mask 
projection lithography has failed to be adopted in industrial manufacturing of ICs. 
The situation has changed fundamentally since 2016. While 193 nm immersion 
lithography combined with multi-patterning continued to extend ICs to 10 nm node, 
EUV technology became mature and started IC production in 2019, taking over from 
7 nm node onward [96]. High-throughput e-beam lithography is now only for 
making masks. There are two types of e-beam lithography technologies active in 
advanced mask making: variable shaped beam (VSB) and multi-beam mask writer 
(MBMW).

[Page 133]
3.7
High-Throughput E-Beam Lithography
133
3.7.1 
Variable Shaped Beam Lithography 
The high-throughput e-beam lithography started with shaped beam system in the 
1970s [97]. The so-called shaped beam is to use a shaped aperture, usually a square 
shape so that the actual pixel exposed onto a wafer is no longer a single Gaussian 
beam of very ﬁne size but a large beam. As mentioned in Sect. 3.3.4, a pattern is 
always fragmented into basic shapes of rectangles, trapeziums, or parallelograms in 
e-beam lithography. Instead of scanning a Gaussian beam to ﬁll the shapes, the 
shaped beam can ﬁll them much quick, which considerably increases the exposure 
speed, hence the throughput. To enable the ﬂexibility of exposing various pattern 
size, variable shaped beam (VSB) was developed [98]. VSB is an extension of the 
ﬁxed shape beam concept. As illustrated in Fig. 3.37, the beam spot is shaped by 
projecting two square apertures simultaneously. By shifting the image of the ﬁrst 
aperture laterally with respect to the second aperture, a compound beam spot is 
formed, which is subsequently demagniﬁed and projected onto the target. By the 
combination of two square apertures, the beam spot is tailored to ﬁt each pattern 
segment while it steps from one segment to the next. A typical VSB system operates 
at 50 keV beam energy and basic beam size of 2 × 2 μm. Originally VSB was 
developed mainly to prepare for high-throughput patterning of ICs. Lately VSB 
became the powerful tool for mask making as laser direct write could not meet the 
mask requirements below 130 nm IC technology. Currently, there are three major
Fig. 3.37 Combination of 
apertures for variable 
shaped beam lithography

[Page 134]
types of VSB systems worldwide, i.e., JBX series from JEOL [99], SB series from 
Vistec [100], and EBM series from NuFlare [101]. These VSBs aim at masks for 
22 nm node down to 7 nm node, noted that mask feature is four times of IC feature.
134
3
Electron Beam Lithography
3.7.2 
Multi-E-Beam Lithography 
The advance in optical lithography (193 nm immersion + multi-patterning and EUV) 
has left the high-throughput e-beam lithography with the only option: mask writing. 
While e-beam mask writers were traditionally monopolized by VSB technology as 
described in previous section, the ever-increased complexity of 193 nm and EUV 
lithography add more and more sub-resolution features onto the masks; in particular, 
the adoption of inverse lithography technology (ILT) prefers curvilinear instead of 
rectangular features [102]. To pattern the curvilinear features, the size of shaped 
beam in a VSB has to be extremely small, which leads to considerable increase of 
shots (exposure points), hence the exposure time. It is estimated that the e-beam 
writing time for an ILT mask would be more than 30 hours, whereas the writing time 
could be reduced to less than 10 hours by using a multi-beam mask writer 
[103]. Three different types of multi-e-beam lithography systems were being devel-
oped in the past, i.e., projection maskless lithography (PML2) [104], reﬂective 
e-beam lithography (REBL) [105], and high-throughput maskless lithography 
(MAPPER) [106]. Fast-forward to present day, the company which developed the 
MAPPER system was bankrupt in 2018 and taken over by ASML in 2019. 
KLA-Tencor which was behind the REBL is no longer supporting its development. 
Only the PML2 was continuously developed by IMS (IMS Nanofabrication AG, 
Austria) which gave up on e-beam direct write for IC patterning but teamed up with 
JEOL to focus on mask making. The system later became MBMW-101, world’s ﬁrst 
high-throughput multi-beam mask writer [107]. 
The MBMW system uses array of microscale beam blanking apertures, called 
programmable Aperture Plate System (APS), as schematically shown in Fig. 3.38 
[108]. The APS is the combination of an aperture plate and a blanking plate with 
IC control electronics. The aperture plate splits a broad telecentric 5 keV electron 
beam into thousands of 2.5-μm-sized beamlets. These individual beamlets then pass 
through the blanking plate where each opening aperture is integrated with a deﬂec-
tion electrode controlled by the IC electronics. Unwanted beamlets are slightly 
deﬂected and ﬁltered out at the second cross-over of the projection optics. Therefore 
only beamlets which are unaffected by the blanking plate are projected to the wafer 
substrate with 200 times reduction to achieve 12.5 nm spot size and accelerated to 
50 keV beam energy [109]. Unlike the VSB which only has a single beam, MBMW 
has the total number of beams from 256 k to one million, depending on the number 
of apertures, which allow it to pattern millions of features simultaneously and 
therefore can considerably increase the throughput. 
Another multi-beam technology (MBW series) is developed by NuFlare Tech-
nology, as a direct competition to MBMW-101. The MBW system is based on the

[Page 135]
same concept of dividing a broad beam into multiple individual beams (260,000) by 
a shaping aperture array (SAA) and deﬂecting individual beam by a blanking 
aperture array (BAA) [110]. The only difference is that the MBW system deﬂects 
50 keV beams, while the MBMW system deﬂects 5 keV beams. Direct blanking of a 
high energy beam enables the system to be less sensitive to external noise in all beam 
paths. It also enables simple and robust electrical insulation design because it does 
not need an additional high voltage area that may cause unexpected discharges. 
References
135
Fig. 3.38 Schematics of multi-beam mask writer developed by IMS [108] 
With wide adoption of ILT from 5 nm node, multi-e-beam technology becomes 
the only option enabling both EUV and curvilinear photomasks. The eBeam Initia-
tive’s 11th annual Luminaries survey in 2022 reported strong purchasing predictions 
for multi-beam mask writers, and the multi-beam mask writing technology is hailed 
as “a game changer” [111].

Chapter 4 
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam 
4.1 
Introduction 
Ions are different from electrons because of their heavy mass. A hydrogen ion is 
1840 times heavier than an electron. The heavy mass of ions makes them particularly 
suitable for nanofabrication by direct structuring of material, rather than by exposure 
of a polymer resist as e-beam lithography does. Ions were discovered and made use 
of in many applications other than nanofabrication, and many years earlier than 
electrons. The application of ion beam has nearly a hundred years of history. Since 
Thomson developed gas discharge ion source in 1910, ion beam technology was 
mainly used in material analysis, separation of isotopes, and modiﬁcation of material 
property by ion implantation. In the early days, fabrication utilizing ion beam in 
nanometer scale was hampered because a ﬁne size of ion beam was difﬁcult to 
achieve from the early gas discharge ion sources. Focused ion beam (FIB) really 
became a nanofabrication tool following the introduction of liquid metal ion sources 
(LMIS) [1]. The LMIS made it possible to focus an ion beam to less than 5 nm in 
beam size. However, there is a drawback of using LMIS: the metallic ions in a LMIS 
can implant into a processed material. Plasma ion sources using xenon or argon 
gases were then developed. With properly designed ion optical system, sub-100 nm 
size of focused ion beam can be achieved. For even smaller beam size, there is the 
gas ﬁeld ion source (GFIS) which can produce sub-1 nm beam, enabling even higher 
resolution than a LMIS for nanofabrication. On the one hand, a focused ion beam 
can machine a material by sputtering away the material. On the other hand, a focused 
ion beam combined with chemically active gases can deposit materials onto a 
substrate. A focused ion beam can also implant different atomic species into a 
substrate by using ion sources consisting of different elemental materials. All the 
above processes are based on the high resolution capability of a FIB system, which 
enables the processed structures to be in micrometer and nanometer dimensions. FIB 
system is truly a ﬂexible and versatile nanofabrication tool. 
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_4
141

[Page 142]
142
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
4.2 
Ion Sources 
An ion source is at the heart of a FIB system. Early ion sources were developed for 
mass spectrometry and nuclear physics research. Since the 1960s, ion implantation 
application for semiconductor manufacturing further pushed ion source develop-
ment. It was the demand for nanoscale structuring that pushed development of 
various ion sources to be suitable for focused ion beams. Currently there are three 
types of ion sources being used for FIB applications: liquid metal ion source, plasma 
ion source, and gas ﬁeld ion source. 
4.2.1 
Liquid Metal Ion Source 
Liquid metal ion source (LMIS) is based on ion emission from liquid metal under a 
strong electrical ﬁeld. LMIS was developed as a by-product when Clampitt and his 
colleagues were studying cesium ion sources for satellite thrusters in the 1970s 
[2]. The basic structure of a LMIS is illustrated in Fig. 4.1. A tungsten wire of about 
0.5 mm diameter is electrochemically etched into a needle with the tip radius of only 
5–10 μm. The tungsten needle is then wetted with molten metal. A small amount of 
liquid metal adheres to the needle. When applied to a high voltage, an electrostatic
Fig. 4.1 Schematic of 
liquid metal ion source 
structure 
Extractor 
Metal film 
Emitter 
Metal ions 
Liquid metal
Reservoir

[Page 143]
force exerts to the liquid metal, pulling it into an extremely small apex (only about 
5 nm in size). The electrical ﬁeld strength at the liquid apex can be as high as 1010 V/ 
m. At such extremely high ﬁeld, metal atoms at the liquid apex become ionized and 
escape from the liquid metal surface in the form of ﬁeld evaporation, resulting in ion 
emission. Although the total emission current may be only a few microamperes, the 
current density can be as high as ~106 A/cm2 because of the extremely small 
emission area. The angular intensity of ion emission is up to ~20 μA/sr [3].
4.2
Ion Sources
143
The ion emission process in a LMIS is a very complex dynamic process. For ﬁeld 
electron emission discussed in Sect. 3.2.2 of Chap. 3, the electrons emit from a solid 
surface, while for ion emission in a LMIS, the emission surface is a liquid metal tip 
which changes shape with electrical ﬁeld and emission current. In addition, the 
liquid metal tip has to have a continuous supply of material in order to compensate 
the loss of liquid metal due to emission. The entire emission process is the interplay 
between electrohydrodynamics of liquid metal and ﬁeld ion emission. There had 
been analytical model [4] and numerical model [5] in attempt to describe such a 
dynamic process. The analysis indicated that there are three conditions which have to 
be satisﬁed in order to maintain a stable ion emission in a LMIS [6]: 
1. The emission surface must have a certain shape in order to establish surface 
electrical ﬁeld. 
2. The surface electrical ﬁeld must be high enough to maintain a certain level of ion 
emission current and a certain rate of liquid metal supply. 
3. The liquid metal ﬂow rate must be enough to compensate the loss of material 
corresponding to the ion emission current so that the stable shape of emission 
surface is maintained. 
The three conditions have reﬂected the interdependence of LMIS emission 
surface shape, electrical ﬁeld, emission current, and liquid metal ﬂow. In a practical 
LMIS, the most critical condition for a sustained stable emission is to ensure a good 
wetting of tungsten needle by the liquid metal. A well-wetted needle can maintain a 
continuous liquid metal ﬁlm over the needle surface, which not only helps to 
establish a liquid emission tip but also guarantees constant supply of liquid metal 
material to the tip. 
Experiments have revealed the following characteristics of LMIS: 
1. There exists a threshold voltage at which the onset of ion emission occurs. The 
threshold voltage depends on liquid metal property, radius, and sharpness of 
supporting tungsten needles. It is averagely above 2 kV. Once applied voltage 
exceeds the threshold value, the emission current increases rapidly. 
2. The emission angle from a LMIS is relatively large, which is generally around 
30° based on various experimental measurements. The higher the emission 
current, the larger the emission angle. However, the usable current will decrease 
with the increase of emission angle. 
3. The angular distribution of emission current is relatively uniform.

[Page 144]
144
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
4. The energy spread of emitted ions is relatively large, depending on the emission 
current. The higher the emission current, the larger the energy spread [7]. Large 
energy spread will cause large chromatic aberration in ion optical system. 
5. Mass spectrometry analysis of LMIS emission has shown that at low emission 
current (≤10 μA), the emission consists of almost 100% of singly charged ions. 
With the increase of emission current, the proportion of multiple charged ions, 
charged molecules, ion clusters, and charged liquid droplets in total emission 
current increases, which has detrimental effects for the application of FIB 
technique. 
The above characteristics indicate that for practical FIB applications, it is not 
desirable for a LMIS to work at large emission current condition. The optimum 
working current should be less than 2 μA. At small emission current, both the ion 
energy spread and emission angle are small, resulting in efﬁcient use of emitted ions. 
The actual usable ion current is not necessarily lower than working at large emission 
current. 
LMIS started with gallium as the emission material because the melting point of 
gallium is at 29.8 °C. Liquid gallium can adhere to tungsten surface very well. 
Gallium is presently still the source material for majority of LMIS used in FIB 
systems. Apart from gallium, LMIS made of other materials had also been investi-
gated. The materials that had been tried for LMIS were Al, As, Au, B, Be, Bi, Cs, Cu, 
Ge, Fe, In, Li, Pb, P, Pd, Si, Sn, U, and Zn [8]. Some of the above elements can be 
directly made into single material LMIS. Some have to be made into eutectic alloys 
which are of low melting point. For liquid metal alloy ion source (LMAIS), the 
different ion species can be separated by magnetic ﬁeld. Among those elements, 
As, B, Be, and Si are of particular interests because they are the main doping 
elements for semiconductor transistors. In the early years when LMIS-based FIB 
technology was developed, alloy LMIS consisting of these elements were used for 
direct ion implantation to make transistors. Though LMAIS was no longer used to 
make transistors, there are renewed interests in LMAIS recently, mainly because ion 
species other than gallium can avoid the gallium staining problem which will be 
introduced later in this chapter, as well as adding special functionalities to a FIB 
fabricated structure [9]. 
The lifetime of a LMIS is measured by μA•hour. It is obvious that the larger the 
emission current, the shorter the lifetime of a LMIS. Current commercial gallium 
LMIS has lifetime up to 6000 μA•hour. The limiting factors for longer lifetime 
include not only the supply of liquid metal material but also the contamination 
caused by backscattered substrate materials which redeposit onto the LMIS emission 
surface. Such redeposition of alien materials can change the ﬂow characteristics of 
liquid metal. In particular, when a FIB is used for chemical gas-assisted etching or 
deposition, some organic molecules can absorb onto liquid metal surface. Upon 
bombardment of secondary electrons, these organic molecules can form a carbon 
layer, causing signiﬁcant increase in ﬂow resistance of liquid metal [10]. Some 
LMIS using high vapor pressure metals are also of short lifetime because of their 
volatility at molten state.

[Page 145]
4.2
Ion Sources
145
4.2.2 
Plasma Ion Source 
Ion species generated by a plasma have long been used in mass spectrometry. They 
were not used for FIB applications because of low current density and large energy 
spread, which is difﬁcult to focus into a ﬁne beam. The renewed interest in plasma 
ion source for FIB started with the emergence of inductively coupled plasma (ICP). 
Figure 4.2 shows the schematic of ICP ion source. With the assistance of induction 
coil outside the plasma chamber, the plasma density inside the chamber is greatly 
enhanced. The density of Xe+ plasma can be as high as 1013 cm-3 [11]. The ions 
inside the chamber are extracted by the extraction electrode at 4 kV and accelerated 
by an ion optical column to 20–30 keV energy. Plasma ion source is a broad source 
and the source size is the extraction aperture (~200 μm) which cannot be too small. 
Therefore, even with a focusing lens, the ﬁnal spot size at the substrate target is much 
larger than the ion beam from a LMIS, ~50 nm compared to ~5 nm of LMIS 
[12]. Also due to the source size, the current density is two orders of magnitude 
lower than LMIS, ~104 A/cm2 compared to ~106 A/cm2 of LMIS. However, the 
angular intensity of ICP source is much higher than a LMIS, ~10 mA/sr compared to 
~15 μA/sr of LMIS, which makes the ICP source much advantageous in high current 
operation [11]. Using a 1 μA Xe+ beam can have 80 times higher sputtering removal 
rate for silicon than a Ga beam operating at 20 nA [13]. Another advantage of ICP 
source compared to LMIS is it being free from Ga staining, because it uses gas 
species such as Xe, Ar, or O2. 
Fig. 4.2 Schematic of 
inductively coupled plasma 
(ICP) ion source. (Reprinted 
from [11] with permission)

[Page 146]
146
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
4.2.3 
Gas Field Ion Source 
While the plasma ion source avoids using heavy metal ion species but does not have 
a ﬁne beam size, a gas ﬁeld ion source (GFIS) emits gaseous ions but has extremely 
small beam size. Gaseous atoms, mostly helium atoms, absorbed on the surface of a 
sharp needle can be ionized by the extremely high electrical ﬁeld at the apex of 
needle, very much like the LMIS where Ga atoms are ﬁeld-ionized. Field-induced 
ion emission was discovered more than 70 years ago and has been utilized ever since 
as ﬁeld ion microscope (FIM) to observe atomic structure of needle materials 
[14]. The advance of FIM to become GFIS for FIB applications originated from 
the work reported by Ward et al. in 2006 [15]. A conventional FIM will have many 
gas atoms absorbed on the emitter tip. Ward et al. managed to control the electrical 
ﬁeld and gas inlet so that only three atoms are absorbed and ﬁeld-ionized on the tip 
apex, as illustrated in Fig. 4.3 [16]. The extraction ﬁeld is then conﬁgured to allow 
only one ionized atom to produce the beam while the other two are dumped 
[15]. Such an ion source can produce the ultimate focused ion beam size of 
0.25 nm, energy spread <0.5 eV, and angular current density of 2.5 μA/sr. With 
such a small beam spot, the source brightness is estimated to be ~109 A/cm2 sr, which 
is three orders of magnitude better than a gallium LMIS, and many nanofabrication 
challenges can be met by helium FIB [17]. Apart from helium, other heavier inert 
gases such as neon [18] and xenon [19] have also been used in GFIS. 
He0

4.3
Focused Ion Beam Systems

4.3 
Focused Ion Beam Systems 
Focused ion beam (FIB) systems have no fundamental difference from electron 
beam lithography systems. They all consist of emission source (electron or ion 
source), lens column (electron or ion optics), work stage, vacuum, and control 
systems. The core part of focusing emitted ions into a ﬁne beam is the ion optical 
system. Electron optics has been introduced in Chap. 3. Ion optics is very much like 
electron optics. The only difference is that ions can have different masses and 
charges. The force exerted on charged particles in electrical ﬁeld depends only on 
the charge a particle carries, as shown in Eq. (4.1a), whereas the force exerted on 
charged particles in magnetic ﬁeld depends not only on charge but also on mass, as 
indicated by Eq. (4.1b). 
Fe = qE
ð4:1aÞ 
F 
* 
m = qv 
* × B 
* ,

2qV 
m
ð4:1bÞ 
where ν is velocity and V is acceleration voltage. 
Electrical charge over mass is commonly called charge-mass ratio. It is apparent 
that ions with different charge-mass ratio will experience magnetic force differently 
in a magnetic ﬁeld. Such a property is useful for some applications but detrimental 
for some other applications. For example, mass spectrometry utilizes this feature to 
select ions of different mass in a magnetic ﬁeld. For FIB application, ions of different 
mass will be focused to different focal planes. Therefore, FIB systems are univer-
sally using electrostatic lens and deﬂectors, which have been described in Chap. 3, to  
focus and deﬂect ion beam. 
A typical FIB system is schematically shown in Fig. 4.4a. The focusing lenses are 
electrostatic lenses. Ion beam deﬂector is an electrostatic octopole deﬂector. The 
typical acceleration voltage of FIB system is between 1 and 30 kV, which is also the 
energy the ion beam carries to sputter at a target substrate. The working current is 
different from the emission current of an ion source. The typical working current is 
0.2 pA to 50 nA for a Ga+ LMIS, 0.1 pA to 10 μA for a Xe+ ICP source, and 0.1 pA 
to 10 pA for a He+ GFIS [11], which is adjusted by using an aperture. 
There are a number of commercial FIB system manufacturers worldwide. FEI 
Company was the ﬁrst to use LMIS to build commercial FIB systems. The company 
was acquired by Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc Inc. in 2017. Most of the commercial FIB 
systems are not stand-alone FIB but combined with SEM (scanning electron micro-
scope). A schematic of dual-beam system is shown in Fig. 4.4b, which enables in 
situ observation of FIB sputtering or deposition progress. Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc 
offers Helios series dual-beam systems which use either a gallium LMIS or a plasma 
ion source [21]. Other commercial dual-beam systems include the VELION from 
Raith [22], NX9000 from Hitachi [23], and JIB systems from JEOL [24]. Currently,

[Page 148]
only ZEISS offers the GFIS-based FIB system: the ORION NanoFab system [25], 
which not only has a GFIS with helium and neon ions but also has a gallium LMIS to 
complement.

4.4 
Ion Scattering in Solid Materials 
As charged particles, high energy ions also scatter in solid material. The difference 
between electron scattering and ion scattering is that ions are much heavier than 
electrons; therefore, they scatter very little. Ion scattering can also be classiﬁed as 
elastic scattering and inelastic scattering. Elastic scattering changes the direction of 
incident ions without loss of energy. Inelastic scattering causes loss of energy of 
incident ions. The scattering angle can be calculated by nuclear differential scatter-
ing cross section expressed by Eq. (4.2) [26]:

scattering in which electrons do not lose energy because electrons are far smaller

because of their comparable masses. 
4.4
Ion Scattering in Solid Materials
149
There are two types of energy losses during ion scattering in solid. One is nuclear 
losses, i.e., an incident ion scattering with atomic nucleus transfers part of its energy 
to the solid atom, causing the atom to either dislocate its position to become recoiling 
atoms or escape from the solid surface. The phenomenon of incident ions knocking 
solid atoms out of solid target is called sputtering. Another is electronic losses, i.e., 
the incident ion transfers part of its energy to the electrons surrounding an atomic 
nucleus. These electrons can either be excited to produce secondary electron emis-
sion from solid surface or be stripped off from the atom, resulting in the ionization of 
atom and secondary ion emission. Atomic nucleus losses can be calculated by 
Eq. (4.3), and electronic losses can be calculated by Eq. (4.4) [27], where L is the 
mean free path between two collisions and N is the atomic density of solid material.

Like Monte Carlo simulation of electron scattering introduced in Chap. 3, ion 
scattering can also be simulated by Monte Carlo method. The simulation process is 
similar, i.e., the total scattering cross section is calculated by integration of Eq. (4.2); 
the probability of scattering with a particular atomic species is determined by the 
ratio of their total scattering cross sections; scattering angle and mean free path of 
scattering are chosen randomly; atomic nucleus and electronic losses are calculated 
by Eqs. (4.3) and (4.4); the ion scattering trajectory is followed until it loses all the 
energy and stops at some location inside the solid material. Figure 4.5 shows the
Fig. 4.5 Gallium ion scattering trajectories calculated by Monte Carlo method, (a) ion 
energy = 50 keV, (b) ion energy = 100 keV (dimension unit: μm)

[Page 150]
gallium ion scattering trajectories in silicon, calculated by Monte Carlo method 
[28]. As it is shown in Fig. 4.5a, 50 keV gallium ions can only penetrate in silicon 
up to 50 nm deep because of its heavy mass. Even at 100 keV energy, the penetration 
is only around 100 nm as shown in Fig. 4.5b. Compared with electron scattering, 
electrons with the same energy and in the same substrate material have scattering 
depth of 10 μm. The ion penetration depth is also called stopping range. The 
stopping range increases with the increase of ion energy and the decrease of ion 
mass. Unlike electron scattering, incident ions will eventually rest in the solid 
material after losing all their energy and become part of the material. This is the 
so-called ion implantation. Ion implantation can modify the material property and 
will be described in later section.
150
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
For Monte Carlo analysis of ion scattering and sputtering, there is a free down-
loadable computer program “SRIM” (Stopping and Range of Ions in Matters) 
[29]. The program was initially called “TRIM” [30]. Since published in 1980, it 
has been upgraded several times [31]. The latest version is SRIM-2013. The program 
can calculate 3D distribution implanted ions in solid, damage of target material 
caused by ion bombardment, sputtering, generation of secondary ions, phonon 
production by ion excitation, etc. The target material can be single material or 
composite material consisting of up to eight different materials. Sputtering yield 
and ion scattering trajectories can be displayed during simulation. 
The above analysis has assumed that the target material is amorphous material 
where atoms are randomly arranged. The scattering of incident ions in an amorphous 
material is a random process. If the target material is a crystalline material where 
atoms are orderly arranged, incident ions may penetrate much deeper along the low 
indexed axis of crystalline. The penetration depth can be several times of that for 
amorphous target. Such a phenomenon is called ion channeling effect. In this case, 
ion energy loss is mainly electronic loss, and the yield of sputtered target atoms is 
low because of low probability of incident ions colliding with target atoms. This can 
be a serious issue when sputtering a polycrystalline material with ion beam. Because 
different crystalline structures coexist in a polycrystalline material, there is different 
indexed axis on the target surface, leading to different sputtering yields at different 
locations. The ﬁnal sputtered surface can be very rough, as shown in Fig. 4.6a which 
is the surface topography of sputtered copper layer by FIB. Copper on semiconduc-
tor substrate is always in the form of polycrystalline structure. The highly rough 
surface after FIB sputtering is caused by differentiated sputtering yield across the 
surface. One way to reduce the sputtering roughness is to deliberately block the ion 
channels by doping the copper layer with impurity atoms. The doped copper layer 
will be like amorphous layer and sputtering uniformity can be greatly improved, as 
shown in Fig. 4.6b [32].

[Page 151]
4.5
Ion Sputtering and Deposition
151
Fig. 4.6 FIB sputtering of polycrystalline copper, (a) with channeling effect (b) after doping 
4.5 
Ion Sputtering and Deposition 
Because of the heavy mass of ions, a FIB tool can do much more than e-beam 
lithography in nanoscale fabrication. The main functions of FIB are sputtering 
(removing 
materials) 
and 
deposition 
(adding 
materials). 
These 
are 
direct 
nanofabrication, without having to expose resist ﬁrst and etching or deposition 
subsequently, like the electron beam lithography introduced in Chap. 3. 
4.5.1 
Ion Sputtering 
Ion sputtering is a process where incident ions transfer their energy to atoms in a 
solid material, and upon acquiring enough kinetic energy from incident ions, these 
atoms escape from solid material surface. Target materials can be removed layer by 
layer with ion sputtering, which is also called ion milling. Ion sputtering is not a one 
to one process. An ion beam bombarding a target material can generate a large 
number of recoiling atoms. These recoiling atoms can further pass their kinetic 
energy to surrounding atoms, generating more recoiling atoms. Some of the recoiling 
atoms at the vicinity of target surface may have enough kinetic energy to break off 
from the binding of surface energy into free space, becoming sputtered atoms. The 
most important parameter of ion sputtering is sputtering yield, i.e., the number of 
sputtered atoms every incident ion can generate. The sputtering yield can be 
calculated 
by 
Sigmund’s 
“linear 
collision 
cascade” 
model, 
as 
shown 
in 
Eq. (4.5)  [33].

The model predicts that ion sputtering yield depends not only on incident ion 
energy but also on incident angle, atomic density, and mass of target material. 
Although ion sputtering yield can be calculated theoretically, most of sputtering 
yield data are experimentally measured at speciﬁc sputtering conditions. Table 4.1 
gives the sputtering yields for some of the target materials with gallium, aluminum, 
and indium ions at 30 keV initial energy [34]. A collection of sputtering yields for 
different target materials using gallium FIB of different energies can be found 
in [35]. 
Sputtering yield Y is a constant and only related to ion species and energy, target 
material property, and sputtering angle. Once the sputtering yield is known, the 
actual sputtering rate can be calculated by multiplying R in Table 4.1 with ion dose. 
For example, the sputtering yield for aluminum target is known at 0.3 μm3 /nC, and 
the aluminum sputtering rate will be 0.03 μm3 per second for an ion beam current of 
100 pA. If a 5 × 5 μm hole with 5 μm depth is to be machined by FIB on an 
aluminum substrate, the total volume of removed material is 125 μm3 . According to 
the above sputtering rate, the rate of sputtering depth is 7.2 nm per minute, and it 
needs 69.4 min to machine the 5 × 5 × 5 μm hole. The total sputtering time can be 
changed by changing the ion beam current. 
From theoretical analysis and experimental investigation, it is revealed that the 
ion sputtering yield has the following characteristics: 
1. Ion sputtering yield rises with glancing angle of beam incidence and reaches its 
maximum at about 80°. Further increase of the angle toward 90° results in rapid 
drop of sputtering yield. The sputtering yield versus incident angle is plotted in 
Fig. 4.7, which indicates that sputtering at vertical incidence of beam (0°) does 
not generate the maximum sputtering yield. This is because vertically incident 
ions can penetrate much deeper due to the channeling effect, which produces less 
recoiling atoms, hence low sputtering yield. 
2. High energy ions do not necessarily generate high sputtering yield. For gallium 
ions, the sputtering yield becomes saturated above 30 keV ion energy, which is

one of the reasons that most commercial FIB systems work at 10–30 keV beam 
energy. 
3. Redeposition of sputtering material can reduce the net sputtering rate. At a ﬁxed 
beam current, a slow scan appears to have high sputtering rate because more ions 
are injected into target; in reality slow scan reduces the sputtering rate as the 
experimental results of the sputtering rate vs. ion beam dwelling time shown in 
Fig. 4.8 [36]. Longer dwelling time (slow scan) does not produce higher 
sputtering yield because of the redeposition effect. There is a saturation point 
beyond which the sputtering removal of material and redeposition reach a 
balance. It is found in experiment that the sputtering yield is a constant for ion 
beam scanning speed varying from 0.05 cm/s to 1.0 cm/s [37]. This phenomenon

is most signiﬁcant when sputtering deep holes with FIB. As the hole depth 
increases, more and more sputtering atoms redeposit on the sidewalls of the 
hole. One method to reduce the redeposition is to reduce the dwelling time of 
an ion beam at each sputtering point, for example, to sputter with fast and 
multiple scans of ion beam. 
4. Ion bombardment combined with chemically active gases can greatly enhance the 
sputtering yield. The chemical gas-assisted sputtering process is essentially a 
combination of ion sputtering and chemical gas phase etching, because ionized 
chemically active gases such as chlorine and ﬂuorine can violently react with 
silicon, gallium arsenide, or aluminum to produce volatile compounds. The 
process works as the following: a small amount of chemically active gas, called 
precursor gas, is introduced into the sputtering chamber; gas molecules then 
absorb to target surface; ion bombardments ionize the absorbed gas atoms; 
these gas ions react with target surface material and convert the target material 
into volatile compounds which are then evacuated by the vacuum system of FIB. 
Experiments have revealed that chemical gas-assisted ion sputtering has 
sputtering rate of a few ten times higher than normal ion sputtering [38]. The 
enhancement factor can be expressed as Eq. 4.6 [36]. 
E = 1 þ E0 - 1
