Þθ
ð4:6Þ 
1 
where θ = 
1 þ ϕJ
F
In Eq. 4.6, θ is the gas molecule coverage over the surface (θ < 1); J is the ion 
current density; ϕ is related to absorption property of material surface; F is the ﬂux of 
reaction gas ﬂow; and E0 is enhancement factor at maximum coverage of gas 
molecules (θ = 1). Table 4.2 lists the enhancement factors for some combinations 
of reaction gases and sputtered materials. Eq. 4.6 indicates that the enhancement 
factor is related to the gas molecule coverage which is directly proportional to the gas 
ﬂux and inversely proportional to ion current density. It must be pointed out that 
gas-assisted sputtering only works for some speciﬁc combinations of materials and 
gases. These materials have to be reactive to the chemical gases. 
The most effective technique to reduce the aforementioned redeposition is to 
incorporate chemical gases into the sputtering process. Figure 4.9 compares 50 nm 
interdigital ﬁnger structures patterned by ion sputtering etching without gas assis-
tance and with gas assistance. Because of the tightly conﬁned spaces between the 
ﬁngers, they are barely resolvable by ion sputtering alone due to redeposition

[Page 155]
(Fig. 4.9a). With the assistance of XeF2 gas, the 50 nm ﬁngers are clearly separated 
(Fig. 4.9b). 
4.5
Ion Sputtering and Deposition
155
Fig. 4.9 Ion sputtering patterning of 50 nm interdigital ﬁngers, (a) without XeF2 gas assistance and 
(b) with XeF2 gas assistance. (Reprint from [39] with permission) 
The above discussions are based on the assumption of using a FIB with gallium 
LMIS. There are also FIB systems based on ICP plasma ion source and gas ﬁeld ion 
source (GFIS) as introduced in Sect. 4.3. The milling rate is higher when a plasma 
ion source FIB is used because of its high ion current; the milling accuracy is better 
when a GFIS-based FIB is used because of its high resolution capability. It has been 
shown that the milling rates of a Xe ICP-FIB can be at least 50 times higher than a Ga 
LMIS-FIB with same energy of ions (30 keV) due to the availability of higher 
current [40]. For He GFIS, the milling rate is naturally low because of its extremely 
low ion current. However, the milling rate can be improved by using heavier ions 
such as neon or argon. For example, a neon FIB can have the sputter yield of ~1 
atom per incident ion for Si and 4 atoms per incident ion for Cu. This is about ~100× 
higher than that of helium FIB for the same energy. Though it is about 2× lower than 
gallium FIB, the neon beam size from the GFIS is <1.5 nm which is of higher 
resolution than gallium FIB [18]. 
When sputtering multilayer materials, end point detection is needed to stop ion 
sputtering once a layer of material has been sputtered off [37]. The end point 
detection is usually achieved by observing the contrast of secondary electron or 
secondary ion image of the sputtered surface. As different materials have different 
image brightness due to the difference in secondary electron or secondary ion yield, 
a comparison of image brightness can tell if a layer material has been completely 
removed. Another method is to employ a secondary ion mass spectrometer. By 
analyzing the variation of species of secondary ions in free space, one can tell if 
certain material has been completely removed and new material underneath the layer 
has started to appear in the mass spectrum.

[Page 156]
156
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
4.5.2 
Ion Beam Assisted Deposition 
If the precursor gas introduced into sputtering chamber is not chemically reactive to 
the substrate material, the ions produced by ion beam bombardment on the absorbed 
gas atoms will form nonvolatile compounds, which cannot be removed away by 
evacuation but stay on the target surface. This is the ion beam assisted deposition. 
While material is deposited by ion sputtering of absorbed gas atoms, ion sputtering 
itself can also remove the as-deposited atoms. The deposition process and sputtering 
process coexist and compete with each other. In this case, only ﬁne tuning of ion 
energy, ion dose, gas ﬂow rate, and chamber pressure can adjust the deposition rate 
to be greater than sputtering rate, so that the net result is material deposition, not 
sputtering. 
The purpose of ion beam assisted deposition is to make functional structures, such 
as metal interconnections in IC chips. To deposit metal ions, metal organic com-
pound gases should be used. Some commonly used metal organic compound gases 
with different metal constituents and composition of deposited metal ﬁlms are shown 
in Table 4.3. As can be seen, the deposited ﬁlm is not pure metal, which implies that 
the conductivity of FIB deposited compounds will not be as good as pure metals. 
These impurities are mostly carbon and oxygen atoms because of the organic 
compounds used. Heating the sample can remove some of the impurities and 
improve the conductivity. FIB assisted deposition can also deposit nonmetal material 
such as SiO2. SiO2 can be deposited by introducing water vapor into the FIB 
sputtering chamber. 
4.6 
Focused Ion Beam Direct Nanofabrication 
Focused ion beam is the most versatile tool for nanofabrication. Unlike the optical 
lithography or electron beam lithography which has to pattern a polymer resist ﬁrst 
as the imaging layer, FIB can directly remove materials by sputtering, or add 
materials by gas-assisted deposition as well as ion implantation, all of which are in

has become an indispensable tool in scientiﬁc research laboratories as well as in 
some industrial sectors. However, one should also be aware that high energy ions 
can implant into the target material. Gallium ions can cause staining problem [45], 
and gaseous ions can create bubbles at the subsurface of target [46].

4.6.1 
Inspecting and Editing Integrated Circuits 
High density integrated circuit (IC) chips contain up to billions of transistors with 
multilayer interconnections. It is hard to avoid mistakes in designing such complex 
circuit systems. Once a circuit design becomes an IC chip, it can no longer be altered 
even though mistakes such as circuit routing in design have been made. However, by 
using the sputtering and deposition functions of a FIB, one can connect or disconnect 
some parts of circuits on an IC chip. By altering the interconnection, errors in circuit 
design can be diagnosed, and correction of these errors (debugging) can be directly 
carried out on the chip by FIB. Figure 4.11a shows part of an IC chip that has been 
modiﬁed. There are sputtered cuts to disconnect some circuits and depositions of 
tungsten (W) wires to reconnect or bypass some circuits. There are even deposited 
insulator patches to allow crossover of FIB deposited conductive lines. Modern FIB 
systems can compare the entire IC design layout with actual IC chip image (SEM 
imaging in a dual-beam system) point to point and locate the error points as well as 
make corrections precisely to perform high precision circuit editing [47]. In addition 
to diagnosing and repairing design mistakes in IC chips, FIB technology can also 
help to diagnose problems that occurred in manufacturing processes, for example, 
cutting through the IC chips to look at the cross section of different processed layers,

[Page 158]
as shown in Fig. 4.11b which is a cross section of IC chip cut by FIB. If something 
went wrong in the process, one can ﬁnd out where or in which process steps it went 
wrong by inspecting the cross section. The above examples demonstrated that the 
FIB is truly an indispensable tool in modern IC manufacturing. Nowadays, every 
wafer fab around the world is equipped with FIB tools to assist IC manufacturing. 
158
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
Fig. 4.11 (a) Debugging IC chip by FIB; (b) cross-sectioning IC chip by FIB 
Before the 22 nm node, the transistors in an IC chip are planar structures located 
at the bottom of circuit stack. FIB editing only involves modifying the multilayer 
interconnects on top levels. From 22 nm node onward, FinFET transistors became 
dominant, and at 5 nm node the gate-all-around (GAA) transistors have been 
introduced in ICs. The transistors are no longer planar and the thin dielectric layer 
capsulating the semiconductors is more susceptible to transistor performance. IC 
editing using a FIB needs to go deeper into the device and to carefully navigate 
around the more delicate dielectric layers. For the state-of-the-art circuit editing, the 
FIB tool works at lower landing energy of ions and lower beam current so as to 
reduce damage to sensitive circuits and allows the operator to remove very thin 
layers with surgical precision [48]. 
4.6.2 
Repairing Defects of Optical Masks 
Another major application of FIB is to repair defects in optical masks. Mask defects 
are the primary cause of circuit failures in an IC chip. These defects are generated 
during fabrication of optical masks. Optical masks are glass or quartz plates with 
chrome patterns made by lithography and etching. During lithography and etching 
processes, extra spots of chrome or missing spots of chrome may form to become 
opaque defects or clear defects, as illustrated in Fig. 4.12. These defects can be

[Page 159]
imaged onto silicon wafer to become unwanted features in a circuit, leading to failure 
of the circuit. 
4.6
Focused Ion Beam Direct Nanofabrication
159
Fig. 4.12 Illustration of 
optical mask defects 
(opaque defects, a, e, d, f; 
clear defects, b, c) 
For opaque defects, the repairing technique in early days was mainly by laser 
zapping. A high energy density laser beam heats the chrome defect, which vaporizes 
the extra chrome spot. However, laser beam spot is too big to repair small defects and 
the repaired region always has rugged edges. As IC feature dimension shrinks, FIB 
repair of mask defects became dominant. The principle of FIB repair of opaque 
defects is ion sputtering to remove unwanted chrome defects. The thickness of 
chrome layer on an optical mask is normally around 80 nm. FIB sputtering of 
chrome layer must be carefully controlled to avoid over sputtering. Extra sputtering 
after the chrome defect has been removed can result in new defect in the mask. End 
point detection is necessary in FIB repair of mask defects. A common method to 
detect the end point is by secondary electron image of repaired region, because of the 
different contrast in the secondary electron images between chrome and glass. 
Repair of clear defects is relatively simple. It makes use of FIB assisted deposi-
tion process to patch up the clear defects usually with carbon material. Hydrocarbon 
gas is introduced into the vicinity of ion beam sputtering region by a small nozzle. 
Absorbent hydrocarbon molecules on target surface are dissociated upon ion beam 
bombardment, leaving carbon atoms at the target surface. Because high energy ions 
can also break up the bond of SiO2 which is the mask substrate, the silicon atoms 
with dangling bonds at surface can form a strong bond with carbon atoms, holding 
the carbon atoms ﬁrmly to the surface. FIB assisted deposition of carbon can make a 
very robust repair of clear defects. The deposited carbon patches will not come off 
the mask substrate in normal mask cleaning process. As the same with FIB assisted 
deposition of other materials, gas ﬂow rate, ion beam current, and dose must be 
precisely controlled and matched, so that gas molecules have enough time to be 
absorbed at the substrate surface. Ion beam sputtering time should be long enough to 
dissociate the absorbent molecules but not too long to over sputter the surface, 
causing removal of already deposited carbon atoms. With such a control, the 
deposition rate is higher than sputtering rate and continuous deposition of carbon

[Page 160]
is ensured. Clear defects are normally on glass or quartz surface which is an 
insulating material. Charge neutralization measure is needed to prevent ion charge 
accumulation. 
160
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
Fig. 4.13 (a) Photomask image with a defect, (b) photomask image after FIB repair, (c) photoresist 
image from the defected mask, (d) photoresist image from the repaired mask 
The biggest problem with FIB repair of mask defects is gallium staining [45]. As 
mentioned earlier, gallium ions eventually remain in a target material after losing all 
energy to become implanted ions. As gallium is a heavy metal, implanted gallium 
ions can alter the light transmission of glass, which is called gallium staining. 
Although gallium ion sputtering can remove chrome opaque defects, the repaired 
region does not resume to its original transparency because of the gallium staining, 
resulting in incompletely repaired defects as compared in Fig. 4.13 before 
(Fig. 4.13a, c) and after (Fig. 4.13b, d) FIB repair. Residual of photoresist is visible 
at the repaired side due to the Ga staining (Fig. 4.13d). An additional etching is 
required to remove the stained layer and recover the transparency of glass 
substrate [49]. 
With circuit dimension further shrinking, electron beam assisted etching and 
deposition became popular for IC photomask repair [50], particularly for the EUV 
mask repair [51]. The focused e-beam is applied in the presence of certain precursor 
gas molecules, thus triggering the local removal or deposition of materials. With 
electron beam, not only the repair resolution has increased but also the gallium ion 
staining issue has been avoided. Because the e-beam assisted repair is basically a

[Page 161]
chemical process, it has much better selectivity in removing different materials than 
the simple physical sputtering by FIB. There were investigations using the FIB 
systems based on gas ﬁeld ion source (GFIS) to repair EUV masks, as GFIS-based 
FIB is also of high resolution to be able to repair defects as small as 11 nm [52], and 
the helium or neon ions do not create staining issue. However, it was found that the 
gaseous ion sputtering could cause subsurface damage such as beam-induced mixing 
of the Mo-Si layers and nanobubble formation in the underlying Mo-Si multilayer of 
EUV mask [53]. Therefore, e-beam tool has become the only choice for EUV mask 
repair [54]. 
4.6
Focused Ion Beam Direct Nanofabrication
161
4.6.3 
Preparing TEM/STEM Samples 
Transmission electron microscope (TEM) or scanning transmission electron micro-
scope (STEM) requires very thin samples so that electrons can penetrate the sample 
to form electron diffraction patterns. Conventional method to prepare TEM samples 
is mechanical cutting and grinding. Such technique can only prepare large size of 
samples. With FIB technology, a sample can be “sliced” locally for observation and 
analysis. The preparation of TEM samples by FIB is similar to cross-sectioning 
process in IC inspection. Ion beam sputtering is carried out from front and back 
directions, eventually leaving a thin slice in the middle as the TEM sample. Fig-
ure 4.14 demonstrates a prepared TEM sample using a Thermo Fisher’s FIB tool 
[55]. By controlling the precision of sputtering area, the sample thickness can be 
made of only 100 nm. 
Because the direction of ion sputtering is perpendicular to the surface of TEM 
sample, there are no implanted gallium ions in the sample. However, other damages 
to the sample can occur, particularly the footprints left by ion beam sputtering 
parallel to the sample surface. If the ion beam diameter and scanning step size are 
large, which is often the case when high beam current is required to remove large 
quantity of material, microscale ripples can be formed along the sputtering direction
Fig. 4.14 TEM sample 
prepared by controlled FIB 
sputtering. (Courtesy of 
Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc)

[Page 162]
which is parallel to the sample surface. When under TEM, the ripple-like surface 
topography on the sample can degrade the image contrast of actual crystalline 
structure of the sample, because the periodic nature of the ripples may confuse the 
periodic crystalline structures of the sample. Such issue is particularly serious for 
ultrathin samples. Therefore, when making TEM samples with FIB, a low resolution 
ion beam is used ﬁrst to sputter in a large area to remove bulk of the material; then a 
ﬁne ion beam and small scan steps are used to carry out a ﬁnal polish of the sample 
surface. In the latest commercial FIB-SEM dual-beam tools, TEM/STEM sample 
preparation process has been programmed into the systems, and the sample prepa-
ration becomes fully automatic [55].
162
4
Nanofabrication by Focused Ion Beam
4.6.4 
Nanostructuring for Scientiﬁc Research 
Apart from the aforementioned three applications of FIB, which are the industrial 
standard processes, scientiﬁc research community also encounters the needs for 
making various micro- and nanostructures. Optical or electron beam lithography 
introduced in Chaps. 2 and 3 can only create the images of these structures in 
polymer resists, which have to be transferred to a functional material by etching or 
deposition. FIB can bypass the pattern transfer step and make micro- and 
nanostructures directly by either sputtering or deposition; in particular, FIB can 
make true 3D nanostructures. For example, with FIB-induced deposition, 80 nm 
pillar with the height of several micrometers can be made, and the pillars can be 
further manipulated into various shapes and bending angles [56]; materials which are 
not easy to structure in nanoscale by other techniques, such as diamond, can be 
milled into 3D shapes by FIB [57]; freestanding structures can be folded by FIB 
irradiation which alters the internal stress of structure locally [58]; FIB milling 
helped to break up the single grain boundary of gold ﬁlm and created a gap as 
narrow as 1–2 nm [59]. One can ﬁnd many pictures of exotic structures created by 
FIB on the Internet. A recent review article has given a comprehensive summary 
about the variety of applications realized by FIB [60]. With the unique capability of 
FIB-induced modiﬁcations, new nanomaterials with tailored properties can also be 
created, leading to many more new applications [20]. 
4.7 
Focused Ion Beam Lithography 
Like electron beam lithography, focused ion beam can also be used for lithography 
purpose. Ion beam lithography is of very high sensitivity because energy transfer 
between ions and solid atoms is much more efﬁcient than between electrons and 
solid atoms. Commonly used electron beam resists are 100 times more sensitive to 
ion beam than to electron beam. For example, a resist may need 20 μC/cm2 exposure 
dose by 20 keV electron beam lithography. The same resist only needs 0.1 μC/cm2

[Page 163]
exposure dose by gallium ion beam exposure at 100 keV [61]. In addition to high 
sensitivity, ion beam lithography almost has no proximity effect. Because ions are 
much heavier than electrons, the scattering range of ions in resist is much less than 
electrons. There are little spread of ions due to forward scattering and almost no 
backscattering ions. Figure 4.15 is the computer-simulated point spread function of 
ion scattering. Comparing to the point spread function of electron scattering 
(Fig. 3.14 in Chap. 3), the lateral energy spread caused by ion scattering is much 
less, and there is no “long tail” of energy spread due to backscattering. Experiments 
have proved that whether for dense line exposure or exposure on substrate of high 
atomic number material, which is the major cause of proximity effect in e-beam 
lithography, feature dimensions achieved by ion beam lithography are the same as 
with light substrate material and for sparse lines [61]. With ion beam lithography, the 
smallest features of 11–14 nm were demonstrated more than 30 years ago [62]. 
4.7
Focused Ion Beam Lithography

Focused ion beam lithography with liquid gallium ion sources once drew some 
interests in its early development stage. It did not prosper because gallium ions have 
very small penetration depth in resist, as is demonstrated by Monte Carlo simulation 
in Fig. 4.5. The only way to increase the penetration depth is to either increase the 
beam energy or to use lighter ions. The industry has gone along the direction of using 
helium ions generated by gas ﬁeld ion source (GFIS) instead of gallium LMIS for ion 
beam-based lithography. The simulation by SRIM program [31] indicates that while 
the penetration depth of 30 keV gallium ions in PMMA resist is only 22 nm, the 
same energy of helium ions has penetration depth of 200 nm. Helium ions are inert

[Page 164]
and will not change the property of substrate; therefore, it has no “staining” problem. 
FIB system with helium GFIS can achieve the smallest beam diameter of 0.25 nm, 
while FIB with gallium LMIS can only achieve beam diameter around 5 nm. 
Commercial helium ion microscope equipped with pattern generator from Carl 
Zeiss AG has been used as helium FIB lithography tool [63].

Fig. 4.16 (a) Comparison of sensitivity between helium beam and electron beam exposure of 
HSQ; (b) dot array obtained by helium FIB lithography of HSQ 
The advantage of helium FIB lithography has been conﬁrmed by experiment. 
Figure 4.16a shows the development curves of helium ion beam exposure of HSQ 
resist in comparison to e-beam exposure. Much less exposure dose is needed when 
using helium FIB and it has similar contrast as e-beam. It has been shown in Chap. 3 
that the highest resolution of e-beam lithography is around 6 nm and is only possible 
for sparse patterns. Helium FIB lithography can achieve the same level of resolution 
but on dense patterns, as shown in Fig. 4.16b where dense dots of 5.95 nm were 
obtained with helium FIB exposure of HSQ resist [64]. A direct comparison of 
exposure on the same resist (10 nm thickness) with both a helium ion beam (30 keV) 
and an electron beam (100 keV) showed that the helium FIB could achieve 5 nm 
dense lines with 20 nm pitch, while the electron beam could only get 6.5 nm dense 
lines with 40 nm pitch [65]. 
Although focused ion beam lithography has superior resolution capability, to 
become mainstream lithography technology, throughput is the key hurdle. Similar to 
electron projection lithography (EPL), ion projection lithography (IPL) also once 
received great attention in the late 1990s. IPL was proposed as an optional technol-
ogy competing with EPL for next generation lithography (NGL) [66]. The main 
reason to develop IPL was due to the aforementioned facts, i.e., ions scatter little in 
resist and substrate, hence no proximity effect, and resists are several orders of 
magnitude more sensitive to ions than to electrons. The ﬁrst IPL system ALG-1000 
using helium ions was made in 1996. A series of development work was carried out 
in the following years. By 2002, 50 nm pattern resolution was demonstrated by IPL 
[67]. However, IPL suffers the same drawback as the EPL, which is the need of a

[Page 165]
mask. For IPL, a stencil mask has to be used because ions cannot penetrate through a 
thin membrane. 
References
165
In order to avoid the bottleneck of mask, multiple beam approach was developed, 
which was called the Projection Mask-Less Patterning (PMLP) [68]. The PMLP tool 
can produce up to 40,000 programmable beams working parallel, using H+, He+, Ar 
+, C60-, or cluster ions. With individual beam diameter of 8.3 nm, the system is able 
to expose 12.5 nm half-pitch dense patterns on 20-nm-thick HSQ resist [69]. Though 
of very high resolution and free of proximity effect, it suffered the same fate as multi-
e-beam lithography, i.e., not able to compete with multi-patterning optical lithogra-
phy in terms of throughput. The maturity of EUV lithography ﬁnally killed the hope 
of charged beams going into IC manufacturing. The company (IMS Nanofabrication 
GmbH) who developed the PMLP tool turned its focus on developing multi-e-beam 
lithography tool since 2010 [70], and now their multi-beam mask writer (MBMW) 
becomes the workhorse for making the latest optical and EUV masks, which has 
been introduced in Sect. 3.7.2 of Chap. 3.

Chapter 5 
Tip-Based Nanofabrication 
5.1 
Introduction 
As has been described in Chaps. 2, 3, and 4, photons and charged beams (electron 
and ion) are capable of delineating sub-100 nm patterns. However, one question has 
been missing, that is, at what cost? To break into sub-100 nm scale, many “tricks,” 
apart from short wavelength and high NA, have to be used in DUV-based lithogra-
phy, and the latest high-NA EUV lithography tool costs over 350 million euros. In 
charged beam (electron or ion) lithography, very sophisticated electron or ion optical 
systems are required; so is the escalating cost to own such a system. For low-cost 
nanoscale patterning technologies and throughput is not a critical issue, scanning 
probe lithography (SPL) is deﬁnitely an alternative and accessible to majority of 
scientiﬁc research community. 
The advent of SPL was shortly after the invention of scanning tunneling micro-
scope (STM). Researchers actually stumbled onto it when they observed that some 
lines were left on the sample surface after an extended period of scanning of STM 
probe [1]. It is now known as local oxidation process initiated by a probe. At the time 
when the lines were observed, the mechanism was unclear. It was said “under the 
inﬂuence of air some oxidation process seems to occur and maybe SiO2 is formed.” 
Although the SiO2 lines drawn by the STM tip were nowhere comparable in quality 
to those patterned by optical or e-beam lithography, it was the start of drawing 
patterns at sub-100 nm scale without photons or charged beams, and a STM was a 
very simple and cheap setup. The invention of atomic force microscope (AFM) 
further fueled the development. In the last 40 years since the advent of STM, many 
new nanofabrication techniques have been developed using a scanning tip which are 
no longer necessary to be an imaging tool such as STM or AFM. It is now simply 
called tip-based nanofabrication (TBN) [2]. 
Conventional lithography using either photons or electrons always has to rely on 
a polymer material (resist) as an imaging layer. TBN, however, can be implemented 
with diverse mechanisms [3]. Thin polymer resist can be exposed by electrons
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_5
169

[Page 170]
emitted from a tip, light transmitted through a probe or simply by heating; the surface 
of a sample can be modiﬁed under the inﬂuence of very high local electric ﬁeld, such 
as local oxidation and depassivation; materials can be transported from a probe to the 
sample surface to form deposition patterns; patterns are formed simply by scratching 
the surface mechanically or by pushing surface atoms around with a tip. Though 
some of the patterning techniques have been commercially exploited as dedicated 
nanolithography tools, most of the techniques are still conﬁned to laboratories as an 
add-on function of a scanning probe microscope (SPM).
170
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
The most attractive feature of TBN is its simplicity. It makes the sub-100 nm 
patterning accessible to anyone with a simple setup of scanning tips. However, one 
has to be aware of its limitation. Firstly, tip-based lithography does not have the 
same resolution capability as the atomic resolution imaging capability of a SPM, 
because the mechanism of sensing the existence of an atom is different from making 
the structural change of surface. Secondly, the very proximal interaction between a 
tip and a sample surface has determined that the interaction is extremely “superﬁ-
cial.” Patterns made by TBN are never more than a few tens of nanometers deep. 
Therefore, special measures have to be taken when transferring the “superﬁcial” 
patterns into a substrate. Thirdly, imaging with a SPM is slow; so does the TBN. In 
fact nanofabrication using a tip is even slower because there has to be enough energy 
transfer from the tip into a substrate in order to induce a structural change of the 
surface. TBN generally has the scanning speed of micrometer to millimeter per 
second, which is many orders of magnitude slower than a conventional e-beam 
lithography. Fourthly, the working area of a TBN is small, making it suitable only 
for patterning a small area of surface. There have been continuous efforts to develop 
multiple probe systems [4] or high-speed scanning systems [5] in order to boost the 
throughput of TBN, but it can never be successful as nanofabrication tools for 
industrial-scale applications. Nevertheless, TBN is quite handy for making 
sub-100 nm structures in laboratories. 
5.2 
Principles of Scanning Probe Microscopes 
Scanning tunneling microscope (STM) was invented in 1981 by Binnig and Rohrer 
[6], who received Noble Prize in Physics for the invention in 1986. Figure 5.1 
schematically illustrates the basic conﬁguration of a STM. It consists of a sharp 
conductive tip (stylus) which is usually a tungsten wire sharpened electrochemically. 
The tip assembly is controlled by a piezoelectric transducer which moves the tip up 
and down to control the gap between the tip apex and the sample surface with 
sub-nanometer precision. Although the radius of tip apex may be a few nanometers 
or a few tens of nanometers, at the very end of the tip, there are always a few atoms of 
tip material protruding closest to the sample surface at a distance about 0.5 to 1 nm, 
as illustrated by the inset picture in Fig. 5.1. When a bias voltage (mV to a few volts) 
is applied between the tip and the sample surface, a tunneling of electrons between 
the tip atoms and sample surface atoms can occur if their gap distance is within

[Page 171]
~1 nm, as explained by quantum mechanics principle. For suitably sharpened tip, 
gap distance, and bias voltage, the electron tunneling can be conﬁned only between 
single atoms. An additional piezoelectric transducer in the tip assembly can produce 
raster scan of the tip over small region of the sample surface, creating a 2D image of 
the sample surface with atomic resolution. 
5.2
Principles of Scanning Probe Microscopes
171
Fig. 5.1 Schematic view of STM. (Reprint from Wikipedia) 
The key technique in a STM operation is the feedback control of tunneling 
current. As the tunneling current is exponentially dependent on the tip-sample 
distance, the feedback control loop can either keep a constant tunneling current or 
keep a constant tip-sample distance, with sub-angstrom precision vertically and 
sub-nanometer precision laterally. In the constant current mode, tip-sample distance 
across the scanning region is varied, creating a 2D topographical map of the surface. 
In the constant gap mode, variation of electron density of the surface atoms is 
detected. In either case, a 2D map of surface atoms can be created. 
Atomic force microscope (AFM) was invented shortly after the STM [7]. As 
schematically illustrated in Fig. 5.2, the key difference between a STM and a AFM is 
the tip assembly. In an AFM, the tip is mounted on a ﬂexible cantilever beam, 
whereas the STM tip is rigidly mounted. AFM operates by measuring attractive or 
repulsive forces between the tip and the sample surface. The forces cause the 
cantilever beam to deﬂect, which is detected optically or electronically and translated 
to surface topographical variation. A raster scan of the tip over a surface region 
generates a 2D map of the surface structure with atomic resolution. AFM can be 
operated either in contact mode or noncontact mode which is also called tapping 
mode. In contact mode, a feedback control loop can either keep a constant height of

[Page 172]
the tip over the sample surface or keep a constant contact force between the tip and 
sample surface. In the tapping mode, the AFM tip is literally tapping the sample 
surface at high frequency. In all these modes, a 2D map of surface topographical 
image can be created. 
172
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Fig. 5.2 Schematic view of 
AFM. (Reprint from 
Wikipedia) 
AFM is actually a much more versatile technique than the STM. A STM relies on 
tunneling electrons for imaging, which requires a vacuum environment. AFM can be 
operated in air. STM requires both the tip and sample are conductive, while AFM 
can be used to image any materials, even in liquid. With a conductive tip, AFM can 
be operated like a STM as well, to provide typical capabilities of a STM. Over the 
years, many other types of scanning probe microscopic techniques have evolved 
from the basic concept of AFM, such as measuring lateral force using a cantilever 
tip, magnetic force, electrostatic force, Kelvin potential, surface conductivity, capac-
itance, temperature, etc. [8]. 
In addition to STM and AFM, optical ﬁber tip can be used to transmit light, which 
is the near-ﬁeld scanning optical microscope (NSOM), or sometimes called scanning 
near-ﬁeld optical microscope (SNOM). Invented also shortly after the advent of 
STM [9], NSOM uses a sharpened light source as a scanning probe which is 
positioned only a few nanometers above the imaging surface. The light source is 
usually a laser beam transmitted through optical ﬁber to the tip aperture. A constant 
height of ﬁber probe above a sample surface is maintained by a shear force feedback 
system which employs a tuning fork commonly found in digital watches. The 
oscillation of tuning fork is damped as a result of contact shear force, and this effect 
is detected and fed back into the control electronics to adjust the height of the probe. 
NSOM can also be realized by drilling a hole on an AFM tip and shine laser light

[Page 173]
through it, which then operates the same as an AFM, as schematically shown in 
Fig. 5.3. 
5.2
Principles of Scanning Probe Microscopes
173
Fig. 5.3 Cantilever-type 
NSOM with a plasmonic 
bowtie probe 
It should be noted that the optical ﬁber tip must be coated with metal (usually 
aluminum but sometimes other metals such as gold) to prevent leakage of light 
laterally through the tip sidewall and allows only a small opening aperture of just a 
few tens of nanometers at the end of glass ﬁber to let the laser light out. However, the 
extremely small aperture can severely restrict the light output, which limits the 
energy delivered to the surface, hence the effectiveness of nanofabrication. Using 
plasmonic ﬁeld enhancement produced from a bowtie aperture (Fig. 5.3) can gener-
ate peak intensities orders of magnitude larger than that found from circular aper-
tures with similar optical conﬁnement [10]. 
Although all the three SPMs have been explored for nanofabrication in the past, 
nowadays majority of reported nanofabrications were done by AFM type of scan-
ning probes. The cantilever-based tips are mechanically ﬂexible and can work either 
as a STM or a NSOM. The progress in silicon micromachining processes allows to 
make the cantilever and tips easily. In particular, a variety of functions can be 
integrated onto the cantilever, such as piezo- or magnetic stimulation of bending, 
electronic detection of bending, and heating of the tips. Many types of TBN have 
been reported, including exposure of resists by electrons, photons, and even heat; 
modiﬁcation of surface electrically or atomically; transporting materials from the tip 
to substrate; or just scratching the surface. Although the TBN has high resolution 
capability, normally below 100 nm, and accurate alignment as well as positioning 
because they are microscopes, they all suffer the drawback of low throughput and 
limited pattern area. To overcome the drawback, considerable efforts have been

[Page 174]
devoted to developing massively parallel scanning tips to boost the throughput in the 
past decades. In the following sections, four types of TBN are introduced, together 
with some high-throughput techniques. 
174
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
5.3 
Exposure of Resists 
5.3.1 
Exposure by Electrons 
It is well known that electron emission can occur at very high electrical ﬁeld, which 
is called ﬁeld emission. The SEMs and e-beam lithography systems have all been 
utilizing ﬁeld emission cathodes to produce high current density and low energy 
spread electrons, which have been described in Chap. 3. Field emission is governed 
by the Fowler-Nordheim theory [11], as shown in Eq. (5.1). 
J = k1E2 exp
- k2 
E
ð5:1Þ 
where J is the ﬁeld emission current density, E is the ﬁeld strength, and k1 and k2 are 
constants related to the work function of ﬁeld emitter material. There is a threshold 
ﬁeld (~2 × 107 V/cm) above which electron emission starts. With the increase of ﬁeld 
strength, the emission current density increases rapidly. There are two ways to 
achieve high ﬁeld strength on an electron emitter: using a very sharp emitter or 
brining the counter electrode as close as possible to the emitter. Figure 5.4a shows an 
example of emitter and counter electrode conﬁguration with the inset SEM picture of 
an actual silicon tip (scale bar 500 nm). The emitter has a radius of 50 nm at its apex 
and the counter electrode is above the emitter with a few tens of nanometers gap. The

[Page 175]
electrical ﬁeld at the surface of emitter is numerically calculated using a 
home-developed computer simulation program [12], based on the geometry shown 
in Fig. 5.4a. Figure 5.4b shows the surface ﬁeld strength as the function of gap 
distance between the emitter and the counter electrode. It is apparent that the ﬁeld 
strength can be above the ﬁeld emission threshold (~2 × 107 V/cm) even at 30 V of 
applied voltage, as long as the emitter tip is close to the counter electrode within 
50 nm range. In the meantime, the simulation also showed that the sharper the tip, the 
higher the ﬁeld strength at a ﬁxed counter electrode distance [12]. This establishes 
the basis for electron exposure of resists using a tip.
5.3
Exposure of Resists
175
Electron exposure with a sharp tip is simply to move the tip on top of a conductive 
substrate coated with a thin layer of electron-sensitive polymer, while a bias voltage 
is applied between the substrate and the tip [13]. Exposure of PMMA resist with a 
STM was ﬁrst reported in the late 1980s. Lines of 22 nm wide were made with 
exposure of 20-nm-thick PMMA resist and scanning speed of 1 μm/s [14]. Exposure 
of chemically ampliﬁed resist SAL601 using a STM was also reported [13]. When 
exposing a resist, the STM is normally operated at constant current mode. The 
applied bias voltage to the tip is much higher, usually a few tens of volts, than it is 
operated as a microscope, which is normally a few volts. 
Exposure of resists with a ﬁeld emission tip is no different from a conventional 
e-beam lithography described in Chap. 3, except that electrons emitted from a tip are 
of very low energy and in close proximity to the resist layer. There are some unique 
features one has to be aware in tip-based e-beam lithography. 
1. Very thin resist layer (<50 nm) has to be used. As seen in Fig. 5.4a, the tip has to 
be very close to the substrate which acts as the counter electrode, in order to 
generate ﬁeld electron emission at low bias voltage. The tip and the sample 
substrate are only separated by the resist layer which has to be very thin. On 
the other hand, the emitted electrons have kinetic energy of only a few tens of 
volts. They cannot travel very far in resist, as explained in Chap. 3, which requires 
the resist layer to be thin so that it can be fully exposed. 
2. Unlike conventional e-beam system where a series of electron lenses are 
employed to focus the electrons into a very ﬁne beam, a STM does not have 
lenses between its tip and substrate. Electrons emitted from the STM tip are 
generally divergent. The degree of divergence depends on both the applied 
voltage and the separation between tip and substrate [15]. For example, a tip of 
10 nm apex radius forms about a 10-nm-diameter beam if the separation is 10 nm. 
The beam diameter becomes 30 nm if the separation is 30 nm, which almost 
linearly increases with the separation. High bias voltage also causes large diver-
gence of emitted electrons, due to the increase of tip-substrate separation in order 
to maintain constant current working mode. A 10 nm beam at 20 V of bias voltage 
becomes 40 nm at 40 V bias voltage [15]. Because there has to be a separation 
between the tip and substrate in order to accommodate the resist layer and the 
applied bias voltage has to be high enough to generate ﬁeld electron emission, the 
resultant beam of electrons is always bigger than the source size. This is the

[Page 176]
b

a
 
reason why the resolution of STM lithography can never match the atomic 
resolution of STM imaging. 
3. Low energy exposure is the key feature of tip-based lithography. In conventional 
e-beam lithography, low energy electrons (<5 keV) can increase the efﬁciency of 
resist exposure, or resists appear to be more sensitive to low energy electrons. 
However, this is not the case in STM exposure of resists. Although the ﬁeld 
emission electrons from a STM are normally below 50 eV, the efﬁciency of resist 
exposure by such low energy electrons is much lower, or STM exposure of resists 
requires much higher exposure dose than conventional e-beam lithography. 
Figure 5.5 compares the linewidth vs. exposure dose by conventional e-beam 
lithography (Fig. 5.5a) and by STM exposure (Fig. 5.5b). Different linewidth was 
created by multiple scans of beam in 40 nm grid separation. From the comparison 
it becomes apparent that STM requires 30 times higher exposure dose than 
conventional e-beam lithography to expose the same patterns. This implies a 
fundamentally different exposure mechanism in play with STM exposure of 
resists. Conventional e-beam lithography systems deliver an e-beam with high 
energy into a resist layer. The electrons undergo scattering and slow down until 
they lose all energy. The STM tip, however, emits electrons which have very low 
initial energy (a fraction of eV) when they enter the resist layer because there is 
not enough travel distance for the electrons to acquire acceleration energy. They 
are accelerated when entering the resist layer toward conductive substrate. During 
the process they lose energy to inelastic scattering with resist molecules as well as 
gain energy from the high ﬁeld. Such process is perceived less efﬁcient in 
breaking the molecular chain of a polymer resist than in the case of electrons 
with initial high energy. Extremely high exposure dose can only mean slow 
exposure. Coupled with slow scan speed (~1 μm/s), resist exposure by STM is 
of very low throughput. 
176
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Fig. 5.5 Comparison of line patterns vs. exposure dose (a) for conventional e-beam lithography at 
30 kV and (b) for STM lithography at 40–60 V. (Reprint from [16] with permission)

[Page 177]
5.3
Exposure of Resists
177
The biggest advantage of low energy e-beam lithography is its low proximity 
effect or it being free of proximity effect, as already described in Chap. 3. The 
comparison in Fig. 5.5a, b reveals that STM exposure of resist has much less 
proximity effect. This is seen from the variation of linewidth with the increase of 
exposure doses. In conventional e-beam lithography, increase of exposure dose will 
result in increase of exposed feature dimension because at higher exposure dose, 
large range scattering electrons acquire enough energy to contribute to the exposure 
of resists, as seen in Fig. 5.5a. This is not the case for STM exposure of resists. 
Increase of exposure dose shows nearly no change of linewidth, as seen in Fig. 5.5b, 
which means no electrons are found outside the exposure region. 
Tip-based e-beam lithography is no doubt capable of achieving very high pat-
terning resolution. The resolution is dependent on the tip radius and the resist 
material, together with the applied voltage between tip and sample and the exposure 
dose. Linewidths ranging from sub-5 nm to >100 nm have been obtained as well as 
dense lines with half pitch as narrow as 7 nm and dot sizes of sub-10 nm [17]. A 
range of polymer resists have been investigated, for example, the most widely used 
high resolution e-beam resist PMMA. It was found that PMMA can be exposed with 
ﬁeld emission tip as normal positive tone pattern at line dose of 200 nC/cm but 
becomes negative tone at 400 nC/cm or even self-developed at >500 nC/cm [18]. As 
for the low throughput of tip-based e-beam lithography, this would not be a serious 
problem for laboratory research in which only a small pattern area and a handful 
devices are needed. There are solutions for high throughput, which will be intro-
duced later in this chapter. 
The thin resist layer may have the difﬁculty of subsequent pattern transfer by 
reactive ion etching. Multilayer resist system obviously does not work here, because 
it increases the gap distance between the STM tip and the conductive substrate. In 
this case, resists with high etch selectivity can be used. For example, SAL601 resist 
has higher etch selectivity when used as mask to etch silicon (> 5:1) than PMMA 
(~1:1). Figure 5.6a shows a 50 nm line etched into 300 nm depth of silicon using 
SAL601 resist as the etch mask. The calixarene-based resists were also used for STM 
exposure, which have four times more endurance than PMMA for etching silicon 
[19]. When PMMA resist is exposed with STM, the only possible route of pattern 
transfer is by metal lift-off. The lift-off metal pattern can be used as the etch mask to 
further transfer into silicon. Figure 5.6b is such an example that PMMA was ﬁrst 
exposed by a STM and metal line pattern was produced by lift-off. The metal line 
was then used as a mask to etch into silicon. The pattern transfer by etching or lift-off 
will be introduced in later chapters. 
5.3.2 
Exposure by Photons 
It is quite natural to think of using an optical probe to deliver photons to realize 
optical lithography. While near-ﬁeld scanning optical microscope (NSOM) was 
invented in the mid-1980s [9, 20], its real application in exposure of photoresist

[Page 178]
was quite a few years later [21]. The exposure is based on near-ﬁeld evanescent 
photons, which has been extensively described in Sect. 2.8 of Chap. 2. There are two 
types of optical probes used for lithography, apertured and apertureless. The NSOM 
for imaging uses an apertured tip with the aperture size of 50–150 nm. The aperture 
is created by coating a metal thin ﬁlm on the surface of a sharpened glass ﬁber, so 
that light is only allowed to transmit through the end of ﬁber, as illustrated in 
Fig. 5.7a. With thin resist layer, the dimension of exposure feature is only limited 
by the aperture size of optical probe and the distance between the probe and resist 
surface. However, it is difﬁcult to make the aperture size smaller than 50 nm. On the
178

[Page 179]
other hand, the light transmission drops rapidly as the aperture gets smaller. Increas-
ing the laser intensity can damage the ﬁber. To solve the problem, the aperture can be 
made into a bowtie shape as shown in Fig. 5.3, which can be done by FIB milling 
[22]. The bowtie aperture that incorporates a small lateral gap between two metal 
edges can have both tight optical conﬁnement and high optical transmittance 
enhanced by plasmonic antenna modes (see Sect. 2.8 of Chap. 2). An overall 
dimension of 160 nm bowtie aperture generated exposed resist linewidth of only 
59 nm [10].
5.3
Exposure of Resists
179
An alternative is to use apertureless tip such as the one shown Fig. 5.7b, which is 
a sharpened optical ﬁber without metal coating. However, this type of optical probe 
is less favorable because of the less optimum light conﬁnement in the probe, unless it 
works in internal reﬂection mode [23]. The third way is that the probe is simply a 
metal tip which is illuminated by a light wave, as shown in Fig. 5.7c. Strong 
localized electrical ﬁeld can be generated at the extremity of metal tip by the 
excitation of illuminating light wave, dubbed as “lightning rod” effect [24], which 
can act as an optical probe. Metal tip is much easier than a glass ﬁber tip to be made 
extremely sharp (5–10 nm). A 10 nm resolution lithography has been achieved with 
metal tip apertureless NSOM [25]. 
5.3.3 
Exposure by Heat 
As mentioned previously, silicon micromachining allows variety of functions to be 
built onto a cantilever-type scanning probe. One of the functions is heating. Fig-
ure 5.8 shows a cantilever tip with an integrated heater [2]. The cantilever is 
U-shaped and made of differentially doped silicon such that the cantilever legs are 
high doped to carry current and the cantilever free end is low doped to form a 
resistive heater. Passing current through the cantilever causes more than 90% of the 
power to be locally dissipated at the heater tip, and the heater temperature can reach
Fig. 5.8 (a) Schematic of a cantilever with integrated tip and heater element; (b) SEM image of the 
silicon micromachined cantilever and the tip at the free end (inset)

[Page 180]
over 1000 °C. With such highly concentrated heat at the tip apex, many phenomena 
can happen by tip interacting with substrate surface, including removal of material 
by thermomechanical indentation or sublimation; conversion of a sample by local 
modiﬁcation of its physical properties such as the crystallinity or chemical reaction; 
and addition of a functional material by melt transfer from a heated tip to the 
substrate or from a gas phase such as chemical vapor deposition of a precursor 
material [27].
180
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Analogue to electron or photon exposure of resists, extreme heat can remove 
polymer materials by direct sublimation without wet development. Heat exposure of 
resists becomes a major thermal scanning probe lithography (t-SPL) technique. 
However, conventional resists used in electron or optical lithography such as 
PMMA require excessively high tip temperatures or long exposure times and 
begin to ﬂow when heated above the glass transition, which limits their application 
range. Thermal probe becomes a viable lithography process because of the self-
ampliﬁed depolymerization polymer: polyphthalaldehyde (PPA) [28]. The molecu-
lar structure of PPA is such that upon reaching the glass transition temperature 
(~150 °C), the polymer chains break and immediately unzip into small volatile 
molecules, the so-called self-ampliﬁed depolymerization. Consequently, PPA does 
not melt and ﬂow, but rather directly sublimates. The reaction is efﬁciently activated 
within a time frame of several μs when using a heater temperature of 700 °C. In 
addition, the reaction is endothermic, which keeps the depolymerization localized. 
The patterning depth is a linear function of the applied load, and a mere increase in 
the force of 3.5 nN leads to an increase in the patterning depth from 0 to 50 nm 
[29]. The patterned PPA can act as a resist mask to etch into SiO2 with the etch 
selectivity of 1:3.3 to SiO2 [30]. Once the PPA pattern is transferred into SiO2, the 
SiO2 mask then allows further reactive ion etching into silicon or into PMMA for 
metal lift-off. With this process silicon and metallic structures with sub-20 nm 
resolution have been produced [31]. 
5.4 
Surface Modiﬁcation 
5.4.1 
Hydrogen Depassivation 
More than 30 years ago, two IBM researchers Eigler and Schweizer used the 
ultrasharp tip of a STM to push 35 xenon atoms on a cold nickel surface to form 
the letters “IBM” [32]. Since then, the atomic precision of scanning probe has been 
widely recognized and used for manipulation of surface atoms. One of its successful 
applications is to move hydrogen atoms on silicon surface for lithography purpose. 
A fresh surface of silicon on the (100) crystalline plane always has dangling 
bonds of Si atoms ready to accept foreign atoms to form silicon compounds. That is 
why there is always a 1.5-nm-thin layer of SiO2, called native oxide, on the silicon 
surface once it is exposed to air. Such chemical activity can be passivated by 
covering the surface with hydrogen atoms, as shown in Fig. 5.9a. Then by selectively

[Page 181]
removing some of the H-atoms by a biased probe to expose the active Si dangling 
bonds (Fig. 5.9b), atoms of other materials can be introduced to react with the Si 
atoms to form a compound with speciﬁc functions (Fig. 5.9c). This is the so-called 
hydrogen depassivation lithography (HDL) and the passivation layer of H-atoms 
acts as the resist [2]. At the sites of exposed Si dangling bonds, atoms of other 
materials can be deposited by absorption. For example, atomic layer deposition 
(ALD) of TiO2 was used to produce an etch-resistant hard mask. Reactive ion 
etching then transfers the mask pattern onto Si with pattern height of 17 nm, critical 
dimension of approximately 6 nm, and full pitch down to 13 nm [33]. Since its 
advent, HDL has been employed by many researchers to fabricate next generation 
nano-devices such as single-electron transistors, quantum computer qubits, editable 
atomic-scale memories, and two-dimensional quantum metamaterials [34]. 
5.4
Surface Modiﬁcation
181
Fig. 5.9 (a) Hydrogen passivation of Si (100); (b) depassivation by scanning probe; (c) absorption 
of other atoms on the depassivated sites 
Although HDL can have atomic precision, it needs ultrahigh vacuum to maintain 
uncontaminated surface and exposure is very slow. As shown in Fig. 5.9b, the 
depassivation is realized by electrons transferred from the tip to the H-atoms at an 
applied voltage. There are two distinct desorption mechanisms: (i) direct electronic 
excitation of the Si-H bond by ﬁeld-emitted electrons and (ii) an atomic resolution 
mechanism that involves multiple-vibrational excitation by tunneling electrons at 
low applied voltages [35]. In the tunneling mode, the tip-sample bias voltage is 
typically 4 V and an electron current is 4 nA, which generate a line dose of 4mC/cm 
at the scanning speed of 10 nm/s. By increasing the bias voltage to 8 V and moving 
the tip away from sample surface (5 nm instead of 1 nm), electrons from the tip 
change from tunneling mode to ﬁeld emission mode. The scanning speed can 
increase to 50 nm/s, albeit the patterning resolution also deteriorates [36]. 
5.4.2 
Field-Induced Oxidation 
As described in the previous section, hydrogen-passivated silicon surface can be 
depassivated with a scanning probe under the inﬂuence of tip-emitted electrons. The 
exposed silicon dangling bonds are then ready to accept foreign atoms. Under

[Page 182]
ultrahigh vacuum condition, the foreign atoms are introduced deliberately as is the 
case of HDL. Under ambient condition, there are inevitably water molecules because 
of humidity. When the tip is brought to a few nanometers above the surface of a 
sample, even a few volts of applied voltage between the tip and sample surface can 
generate electric ﬁeld up to the magnitude of ~108 V/cm. At such a high electrical 
ﬁeld, water molecules can be broken up into ions to become reactive and form 
silicon dioxide, as schematically illustrated in Fig. 5.10. The local oxidation reaction 
takes the following steps [37], as expressed in Eq. (5.2). 
182
5

Fig. 5.10 Schematic

ð5:2Þ 
Water molecules (H2O) are ionized at the high electrical ﬁeld. Negative ions 
(OH-) are driven toward the sample surface (anode). Silicon is oxidized. Positive 
hydrogen ions are attracted to the tip (cathode) to combine with the released free 
electrons to become hydrogen gas molecules (H2). 
Local oxidation was ﬁrst discovered with STM scanning over silicon surface 
[38]. However, STM is not the best instrument for local oxidation lithography. This 
is due to the way STM operates. As described earlier, a STM operates either in 
constant current mode or constant gap mode. The bias voltage control depends on 
the feedback of either the tunneling current or the tip-sample distance. When oxide 
grows underneath the tip, both the tunneling current and the tip-sample distance 
(formation of oxide bump) are changing, which will make the bias voltage change 
because of the feedback control loop. Damage of STM tip often occurs because the 
STM often fails to recognize the increase of surface height as the oxide layer grows. 
When measuring the oxide height during oxidation process, STM can give false 
measurement, overestimating the height because of the decrease of tunneling current 
through the oxide layer. 
AFM is, on the other hand, better suited for local oxidation lithography. AFM 
holds two distinct advantages over the STM: (1) the bias voltage which is the root 
cause of oxidation can be applied independent of the feedback control of the 
tip-sample spacing; and (2) real oxide height is directly measured without involving 
tunneling current [39]. AFM working in tapping mode is even better because it 
eliminates lateral shear forces and overcomes tip-sample adhesion forces (e.g., 
capillarity). These properties avoid damages on the surface and improve imaging 
and lithography resolution. Table 5.1 compares the performances of STM, AFM in

[Page 183]
STM
CM-AFM
TM-AFM
contact mode (CM-AFM), and AFM in tapping mode (TM-AFM) for local oxidation 
lithography.

Fig. 5.11 AFM images of oxide lines produced at (a) 61% of humidity and (b) 14% humidity. 
(Reprint from [40] with permission) 
There are a number of factors that can inﬂuence the oxidation process, including 
applied bias voltage, dwelling time of tip at one location of oxidation, tip-substrate 
distance, and ambient humidity. Among them, the humidity plays an important role 
in the formation of oxide structure during tip-induced oxidation process. Figure 5.11 
compares oxide line features made by AFM-induced oxidation of silicon at different 
humidity levels. At 61% of humidity, the oxide lines were measured around 90 nm 
(Fig. 5.11a). It reduced to around 22 nm at 14% of humidity level despite all other 
conditions being the same (Fig. 5.11b). Humidity level not only can inﬂuence the 
amount of water molecules supplying to the oxidation reaction but also can inﬂuence 
the electrical ﬁeld distribution around the tip-sample region. At high humidity level, 
a water ﬁlm is formed, which can increase the lateral distribution of electrical ﬁeld, 
creating an equivalent “defocus” effect. Therefore, the oxide growth area becomes 
wider. 
The growth rate of oxide can be measured by the height of oxide grown out of 
sample surface. For oxidation of silicon, it was found that the height of SiO2 linearly 
increases with the increases of applied bias voltage (-2  V  to -20 V) to the tip and 
the increase of dwelling time (10 ms to 1000s) [40], and this has been conﬁrmed by 
an analytical model [41]. However, the oxide growth rate is high only at the 
beginning of oxidation (ﬁrst 50 ms), then slowing down and dropping. This is

[Page 184]
because the increase of oxide thickness has reduced the ﬁeld strength across the tip 
and substrate silicon. The oxide growth practically stops at ﬁeld strength <107 V/ 
cm, unless the bias voltage is further increased. 
184
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Apart from silicon, a variety of other materials can also be oxidized by a scanning 
tip, including compound III–V semiconductors, silicon carbide, and a number of 
metals such as titanium, tantalum, aluminum, molybdenum, nickel, and niobium. 
For metallic surface, the oxidation follows a slightly different reaction [42], as 
expressed in Eq. (5.3). 
M þ nH2O →MOn þ 2nHþ þ 2ne -
ð5:3Þ 
Unlike exposure of resists, patterned oxide can be directly used as a mask to etch 
into silicon, because of the high selectivity between silicon dioxide and silicon in 
reactive ion etching. Silicon nanowire transistors with a channel width of 4 nm were 
made using an AFM tip to oxidize silicon as a mask and subsequently etched into 
silicon to create the nanowire [43]. Local oxidation can also serve as dielectric 
barriers in variety of nanoscale devices such as quantum devices, nanowire ﬁeld-
effect transistors, graphene devices, MoS2 transistors, photonic devices, and silicon 
microlens [2]. There are also other ﬁeld-induced phenomena between an electrically 
biased tip and a sample surface, such as electrochemical etching assisted by acidic 
solution [44], or ﬁeld-induced decomposition [45]. Though some nanoscale struc-
tures can be generated, they are generally of poor quality. 
5.4.3 
Friction-Induced Modiﬁcation 
Perhaps the most straightforward surface modiﬁcation by a scanning probe is direct 
mechanical action on a sample surface. By applying a force on a tip, the friction-
induced mechanical plowing on hard or soft surfaces can make grooves with widths
