of tens of nm and depths of a few nm. Figure 5.12 shows three 500-nm-long grooves 
scratched by a Si3N4 cantilever tip on a two-layer GaSb/InAs ﬁlm. Figure 5.12a is 
the AFM image of scratched grooves and Fig. 5.12b is the depth proﬁles of these 
grooves. The stiffness of the cantilever is 0.37 N/m and the applied forces vary from 
100 to 150 nN with a scanning rate of 0.02 μm/s. The grooves produced on the GaSb 
(5 nm)/InAs (20 nm) substrate are about 65 nm in width and 4 nm in depth.
5.4
Surface Modiﬁcation
185
Fig. 5.13 (a) Friction-induced amorphous silicon as the etching mask; (b) KOH etching of single-
crystal silicon in (112) direction 
Friction-induced patterning by scanning probes has long been explored [47]. The 
resolution of mechanical scratching depends on the size of the tip and the depth of 
scratch. It is of the highest resolution among all the scanning probe lithography 
techniques described so far, because the geometry of a tip is ﬁxed whereas electrical 
ﬁeld or ﬁeld emission electrons spread a much larger range than the boundary of a 
solid tip apex. Even a large tip can produce a small scratch by controlling the force 
and depth. For example, 3-nm-wide groove was made using a v-shaped 30-nm-
radius Si3N4 tip with applied force of 100nN [48]. The downside of such high 
resolution scratch patterns is the very shallow depth. The mechanical scratch patterns 
made with standard AFM tips are no more than a few nanometers deep. For example, 
the aforementioned 3-nm-wide groove was only about 1 nm deep. Such shallow 
patterns are of very limited use. One way to extend the use of these mechanically 
scratched shallow patterns is to employ the top surface imaging (TSI) concept which 
has been widely used in conventional optical or e-beam lithography. One of the 
reported examples used a bilayer resist system consisting of a 3-nm-thick Ti ﬁlm on 
top of a 65-nm-thick PMMA layer. AFM with Si tip scratched the 3 nm Ti ﬁlm with 
a tip force of a few mN, and the scratched pattern is then transferred to the 
underneath PMMA layer by an isotropic O2 reactive ion etching [49]. For some 
materials, nanoscale scratch can change the surface crystalline morphology, which 
leads to the change of chemical etching resistance. For example, it was found that 
friction forms an amorphous phase on a single-crystal silicon surface that has an etch 
resistance against potassium hydroxide (KOH) (Fig. 5.13a), and high aspect ratio 
structures were made on a (110)-oriented silicon by the so-called tribo-

[Page 186]
nanolithography (Fig. 5.13b) [50]. The friction-induced selective etching can also be 
applied to a variety of other materials [47]. 
186
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Fig. 5.14 (a) AFM image of plowed PMMA groove by silicon tip, (b) proﬁle of the groove in (a). 
(Reprint from [52] with permission) 
Scratching hard surface can wear the tip and reduce its lifetime. Coating the AFM 
tips with hard materials or making the tip directly in hard materials such as diamond 
can improve its lifetime. Both carbon-coated Si3N4 tips and single-crystal diamond 
tips have been used for mechanical scratch patterning, achieving scratch depth up to 
80 nm at the applied force up to 30μN [51]. The other option is to scratch soft 
materials such as polymers. AFM has been used for direct patterning of PMMA 
resist and metal nanowires can be created by subsequent lift-off process. In this case, 
normal silicon AFM tips can be used with applied force of about ~μN. The 
scratching depth of 15 nm was achieved, and more than 50 scratches could be 
performed with the same AFM tip because of the less damage to the tip with polymer 
material [52]. However, because the mechanical scratching is simply shoveling away 
the material from the plowing path, generation of debris along the plowing path is 
inevitable. For shallow scratching, debris may be less a problem. For soft materials, 
plowing by a scanning tip may simply squeeze the material to both sides of plowing 
path, leaving additional topography on the patterned surface. Figure 5.14a is the 
AFM image of a nanogroove formed by AFM plowing on PMMA surface. The 
bright edges on both sides of the groove represent higher than surface plane 
topography. Figure 5.14b is the proﬁle of the groove. The bumping edges are over 
20 nm higher than the PMMA surface plane, which is caused by squeezed material 
from the scratched trench. 
If the ﬁlm of polymer can move away while being plowed by an AFM tip, then 
the bumping edge on the sides of groove can be avoided. Such was the idea of using 
a strained polymer ﬁlm for AFM plow patterning [53]. When an AFM tip plows 
through an internally strained ﬁlm, the strain pulls away the ﬁlm from the cutting,

[Page 187]
leaving an open trench at the plowing path. By varying the polymer ﬁlm process 
conditions and AFM plowing parameters, trenches with widths spanning from 
40 nm to 2.3 μm have been fabricated. The ﬁnal trench width is also determined 
by the ﬁlm adhesion to the substrate, which acts against the released strain. There-
fore, different trench widths can be achieved either by varying the total internal 
strain, for instance, by varying the ﬁlm thickness, or by modifying the ﬁlm adhesion 
to substrate surface. 
5.4
Surface Modiﬁcation
187
Working with self-assembled monolayer (SAM) is much easier because it 
requires less force in the mechanical plowing process and causes less wear to the 
tip. A number of studies have investigated thiol-functionalized DNA on gold surface 
with AFM. By applying force slightly higher than what is needed for AFM imaging 
(30–50 nN), DNA molecules were mechanically shaved away, and the gold surface 
was exposed [54]. In this case the patterned SAM can act as a mask either for 
subsequent patterning of gold or for adding some other molecules to the patterned 
area. One of the experiments was to immerge the SAM-covered substrate in a 
solution which contained molecules different from the SAM. Where the SAM 
molecules were shaved away by AFM, they were replaced by the reactive molecules 
in the solution [55]. There is a whole range of techniques to pattern SAMs with a 
scanning probe [56]. 
5.4.4 
Heat-Induced Modiﬁcation 
When an AFM tip is heated up, it can make dents in low melting point polymer 
materials. In this way, patterns of nanometer scale can be made into polymer surface. 
Patterning by thermomechanical indentation is different from the heated sublimation 
of PPA described in Sect. 5.3.3. The ﬁrst thermal mechanical patterning by AFM 
was reported in the early 1990s [57], in which the AFM tip was heated by a pulsed 
laser beam to above the softening point of PMMA (120 °C). The size and depth of 
indentation depend on the temperature of the tip and the force of the tip pressing into 
the polymer. At about 10-7 N pressing force, the dents were typically about 100 nm 
across and 10–20 nm deep. The AFM tip can also be heated by building a microscale 
heater integrated into the cantilever beam, which makes the thermomechanical 
indentation system much compact. An example of such integrated cantilever has 
been schematically shown in Fig. 5.8. One of the prominent examples of tip-based 
thermal mechanical indentation was the patterned media for high density data 
storage. Binary data “1” and “0” are represented by dent and no dent on polymer 
surface. With a 40-nm-thick ﬁlm of PMMA, dent sizes ranging between 10 and 
50 nm have been achieved. One drawback emerged with this approach was the 
increased tip wear, because of the frequent contact between the Si cantilever tip and 
the hard Si substrate during writing. A 70 nm layer of cross-linked photoresist 
(SU-8) was added between the Si substrate and the PMMA ﬁlm to provide a soft 
landing for the cantilever tip. With this conﬁguration, dents of 40 nm in 120 nm pitch

[Page 188]
were made in the PMMA layer, which are equivalent to 400Gb/in2 data storage 
density [58]. 
188
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
In addition to thermomechanical indentation, highly localized heat produced by a 
thermal tip can change the physical or chemical properties at the tip-surface inter-
action point. The physical properties that can be modiﬁed by thermal tip include 
local amorphization of crystalline surface, local crystallization of amorphous sur-
face, or reversion of local magnetic polarity. The chemical properties that can be 
modiﬁed by thermal tip include deprotection of functional surface molecular groups. 
For example, self-assembled monolayer which is used to protect gold surface can be 
deprotected by a heated tip so that the gold surface can be etched. Many chemical 
reactions can be activated by heat. A common strategy to obtain nanostructures of 
the desired material and property is to use a precursor material that is locally 
converted by the heated tip. One example is the reduction of graphene oxide 
(GO) ﬂakes and ﬁlms to obtain electrically conducting reduced graphene oxide 
(rGO). Thermal patterning with the heated probe locally reduces the oxygen content 
of the GO, which can increase the electrical conductivity by four orders of 
magnitude [27]. 
5.5 
Material Deposition 
5.5.1 
Dip-Pen Deposition 
It was long found that when AFM is used in damped air, condensed water is formed 
between the AFM tip and sample surface because of humidity. Depending on the 
humidity level and wetting property of substrate, water molecules could move 
between the AFM tip and substrate surface, which was difﬁcult to control and has
Fig. 5.15 Schematic of dip-pen nanolithography. (Reprint from [59] with permission)

[Page 189]
been plaguing the AFM imaging operation. In 1999, Chad A. Mirkin’s group at 
Northwestern University of the USA utilized the phenomenon and invented the dip-
pen nanolithography (DPN) [59]. Writing with a fountain pen on paper is a familiar 
example how ink is transported from the pen to a substrate. DPN works in the same 
principle. Figure 5.15 shows schematically how the ink is deposited from a probe to 
substrate. There is a liquid meniscus formed between the tip and substrate, which 
provides the route for liquid molecules to move toward the substrate surface.
5.5
Material Deposition
189
Fig. 5.16 Patterns made with DPN—(a) dots of different sizes representing tip-substrate contact 
time of 2, 4, and 16 min, (b) line patterns drawn by scanning tip. (Reprint from [59] with 
permission) 
It requires three conditions to complete the one-way molecule transport from tip 
to substrate: (1) the molecules should be able to dissolve in water; (2) there is a water 
meniscus existing between the tip apex and substrate; (3) the ink molecules can be 
self-assembled onto the substrate. In order to ensure this is one-way transport, the 
preferred choice of ink-substrate combination is the thiol-based molecular ink and 
gold substrate. Thiol group molecules are known to form stable self-assembled 
monolayer (SAM) on gold [60]. One of such thiol group molecules used in the 
ﬁrst DPN experiment was 1-octadecanethiol (ODT). The factors that can inﬂuence 
the resolution of DPN patterning are (1) the grain size of substrate, very much like 
the texture of a paper when writing with a fountain pen; (2) surface chemisorption 
property which determines the diffusion of molecules after deposition; (3) tip dwell-
ing time on the surface, and (4) humidity level which affects the size of meniscus 
formed between the tip and substrate. Figure 5.16a is the image of ODT dots 
deposited onto polycrystalline gold surface. The three dots represent AFM tip coated 
with ODT in contact with gold substrate for 2, 4, and 16 min. By moving the tip 
along the surface, line patterns can be drawn with ODT ink, as shown in Fig. 5.16b. 
Rough surface, high absorption surface, long dwelling time, and high humidity level 
can all contribute to large size of deposited patterns. DPN technique can routinely

[Page 190]
produce sub-100 nm patterns, as by all other SPL techniques. However, to make 
features below 50 nm requires optimization of aforementioned conditions. 
190
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
The three conditions to satisfy the molecule transport from tip to substrate are 
rather restrictive and limit the material deposition only to a small group of molecules. 
Many materials may not be dissolvable in water and may not be self-assembled on a 
speciﬁc substrate. For a general liquid ink, such as a molten polymer, there are 
different mechanisms for the DPN. It was found that the mass transport for a general 
liquid ink depends on (1) the viscosity of liquid which governs the ﬂow rate from the 
tip shaft to the tip apex and (2) the interfacial energy of substrate [61]. Generally, 
more viscous liquid ink and lower surface energy result in smaller liquid drop size on 
substrate. Less viscous liquid ink on a hydrophilic surface will result in larger liquid 
drop size. Of course, longer dwelling time of scanning tip will create larger feature 
size on substrate as well. 
It has been over 20 years since DPN was invented. An article was published in 
2020, reviewing the evolution of DPN in commemoration of the 20th anniversary of 
its discovery. In the last 20 years, DPN technology has evolved into many forms 
such as massively parallel 2D pens, polymer pens, beam pens, hard tip with soft 
spring pens, etc. The pens not only can deliver inks but also can be assisted by 
applied electrical ﬁeld to induce chemical reaction between the ink and substrate 
(electrochemical DPN), or the tip is heated up to melt polymers as the ink (thermal 
DPN), or a mechanical force is applied to the tip to scratch the surface so that ink 
molecules can selectively react with the material underneath (mechanical DPN), or 
the tips simply act as optical probes to deliver photons (optical DPN) [62]. 
As early as in 2003, the technology was commercialized by NanoInk, a startup 
company. Since then, the DPN instrument has been used in many research labora-
tories around the world. It was once projected as an alternative to conventional 
lithography because of its high resolution and direct material deposition capabilities. 
To tackle the low-throughput issue, massively parallel tip array tools were also 
developed. However, the DPN as a lithography tool has not been adopted widely 
by industry and it remains as a laboratory tool. The NanoInk company closed down 
in 2013 due to lack of proﬁtability. Nevertheless, compared with other patterning 
techniques, DPN can directly add materials onto a sample surface in high resolution 
and as patterns. This is particularly useful for material discovery [63]. For example, 
multispecies of biomaterials can be added onto the same spot with high precision, 
allowing localized biochemical reaction. It is a high resolution version of microarray 
which is widely used for drug screening and biomedical testing. Other material-
oriented applications such as combinatorial synthesis and heterostructure designs 
have been enabled by DPN [62]. A new company, TERA-print Technology, was set 
up in 2015, which continued the commercialization path of NanoInk with its beam 
pen lithography tool [64].

[Page 191]
5.5
Material Deposition
191
5.5.2 
Field-Induced Deposition 
It is well known that ions or atoms can be directly pulled out of material surface 
under extremely high electrical ﬁeld. The phenomenon is called ﬁeld evaporation 
[65]. The extremely high ﬁeld can be obtained from a sharp tip close to a counter 
electrode. As mentioned earlier, STM is such a system that high ﬁeld exists at the 
STM tip at only a few volts of bias voltage. Field evaporation of material from a 
STM tip was ﬁrst observed in the early 1990s. In the experiment, a gold STM tip was 
used and negative pulse voltage of 3–4 V was applied to the tip. Gold dot was 
formed on substrate surface typically 15–20 nm across its base and 2–3 nm in height 
[66]. Material deposition was easily observed from a gold tip because gold has 
relatively low threshold ﬁeld for ﬁeld evaporation (3.5 V/A) [67], and gold surface is 
inert to chemical contamination. If a tungsten tip was used in combination with a 
gold substrate, a pit was formed instead of a mound [66]. This is because tungsten 
has much higher threshold in ﬁeld evaporation (5.7 V/A). Instead of evaporation 
from tip to substrate, the substrate gold atoms evaporated onto the tungsten tip, 
forming a pit in the substrate. 
Field evaporation alone cannot completely explain the material deposition pro-
cess from a STM tip. Heating by ﬁeld emission current may also be responsible for 
the deposition. Field emission current occurs at much lower threshold ﬁeld than that 
of ﬁeld evaporation. For gold tip, the ﬁeld emission current becomes considerably 
large at the ﬁeld as low as ~0.6 V/A [68]. High ﬁeld emission current can heat up the 
extremity of STM tip, causing melting and ﬂowing of tip material. This can also 
explain why the material deposition from the tip is sustainable despite continuous 
loss of material from the tip. Melting and ﬂowing of tip material can sustain the 
supply of atoms toward the apex of tip so that the tip would not become blunt due to 
the evaporation, which is in much the same way as the working of liquid metal ion 
sources, as described in Chap. 4. For this reason, a negative bias to the tip is 
preferable because negative bias is the correct conﬁguration for ﬁeld emission to 
occur. Experimental observation also conﬁrmed that negative bias of the tip pro-
duced much stable deposition [68]. 
Another speculation about the mechanism of tip material deposition process is the 
formation of nanobridge between the tip and sample surface [69]. The extremely 
high ﬁeld at the apex of a tip can cause the tip to elongate. For metallic tip with 
50 nm radius and 0.75 nm above a metallic plane, the pulling force at the tip apex is 
of 6x10-10 N. Coupling with the heating of ﬁeld emission current, the tip can be 
pulled down to touch the sample surface, so that a liquid metal bridge is formed 
between the tip apex and sample surface. Deposition then takes place through this 
liquid bridge. This is very plausible because liquid metal ion sources work in the 
same way whereby liquid apex elongates at high ﬁeld strength to form an extremely 
sharp emitter [70]. The existence of a liquid metal bridge was also indirectly 
conﬁrmed from the measurement of resistance between STM tip and substrate. 
The elongation keeps the tip always in sharp geometry and maintains stable depo-
sition over an extended period. Because of the sharpening process at high ﬁeld, the

[Page 192]
very end of the tip becomes much smaller than its original geometry. Deposited lines 
as small as 4–5 nm in width have been made, with minimum separation of 1 nm 
between the deposited features [69]. One must be aware that ﬁeld-induced deposi-
tion is found much easier with metallic tip such as gold or platinum because they are 
soft and of low melting point and of relatively low threshold of ﬁeld evaporation. 
192
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Field-induced deposition can also be done with the assistance of organometallic 
gases [71]. Like the local oxidation by STM in ambient humidity, organometallic gas 
molecules are decomposed at the high ﬁeld around the extremity of STM tip, and 
metal atoms are deposited on substrate surface directly under the STM tip. The 
process is often called STM-induced chemical vapor deposition (CVD). The tip is 
negatively biased. There is a threshold bias voltage for different precursor gases. The 
threshold bias is 27 V voltage for iron carbonyl gas but 15 V for tungsten carbonyl. 
The deposition is normally done at emission current from 100 to 500 pA [71]. Unlike 
the local oxidation which has limit in the height of oxide pattern, high aspect ratio 
patterns can be produced by the gas-assisted deposition. The diameter of deposited 
dots is mostly dependent on the bias voltage. Higher voltage increases the gap 
between the tip and the top of the dot, which increases the effective diameter of 
ﬁeld emission electrons, as explained earlier, thus increasing the diameter of depos-
ited dots. The pressure of precursor gas within the range from 50 to 300 mTorr has 
little effect on the deposition rate or the characteristics of deposition. The deposited 
ﬁlm contains about 50% of metal, with the rest being carbon contamination and 
small amount of oxygen. A range of other materials can also be deposited, based on 
the same principle. 
AFM works the same way as STM and can transfer materials from tip to substrate 
at a biased voltage. As AFM tips are normally made of silicon or silicon nitride, 
metallic materials have to be coated onto the AFM tip in order to make the deposition 
happen. Once it is coated with metals, it becomes no different from a STM tip and 
deposition takes place under high electrical ﬁeld. As AFM can work in liquid 
solution, it is possible to initiate electrochemical deposition using an AFM tip. 
Copper has been electrochemically deposited onto GaAs surface by immerging the 
AFM tip into a mixture of CuSO4 + H2SO4 solution [72]. The electrodeposition of 
Cu is strongly dependent on the structure of the substrate, applied potential, and 
concentration of Cu2+ ions in the precursor solution. Based on the same principle, 
many other metallic materials can be electrochemically deposited with AFM. 
5.6 
High-Throughput Scanning Probe Lithography 
The superior resolution capability of scanning probe lithography (SPL) has fueled 
widespread R&D effort in the 1990s to develop it into a competing or an alternative 
technology for wafer-scale nanolithography. However, it has been pointed out that 
four criteria dictated the viability of SPL to compete with existing lithographic 
technologies [73]:

[Page 193]
5.6
High-Throughput Scanning Probe Lithography
193
•
Resolution
•
Alignment accuracy
•
Reliability
•
Throughput (wafers per hour) 
The fundamental strengths of SPL are their resolution ability, as has been 
described in the above sections, and alignment accuracy because of their atomic-
scale imaging capability. Reliability is somewhat an unproven issue as different SPL 
methodologies may have different reliability problems. The key nagging issue for 
the SPL is throughput. The throughput issue can be tackled in two aspects, increas-
ing the scanning speed of single tip or using multiple tips. 
5.6.1 
High-Speed Scanning 
Scanning probe microscope (SPM) is notoriously slow as an imaging tool. When 
SPM is compared to other real-time forms of microscopy such as the scanning 
electron microscope (SEM), searching for the desired feature on a sample is an 
extremely time-consuming process. STM or AFM normally scans at speed of 1-
10 μm per second. As a nanofabrication tool, a scanning probe is even slower, 
because the interactions between the tip and sample material to produce 
nanostructures also take time. 
On the instrumentation side, the increase of tip scanning speed encounters the 
issues of feedback control loop which has to be able to work at high frequency and of 
probe assembly itself which has to be able to move in high speed. The conventional 
piezoelectric tube which controls the height of probe has low resonance frequency 
and cannot respond to height variation at high-speed scanning of probe. One way to 
increase the speed is to operate an AFM at constant height mode so that the 
piezoelectric tube does not have to respond to height variation during high-speed 
scan. Combined with the image capturing scheme used in SEM, a scan speed of 
1 mm/s was achieved [74]. Another development was to replace the piezoelectric 
tube with a ZnO piezoelectric actuator integrated to the cantilever. ZnO ﬁlm was 
coated onto the cantilever beam, which bent the cantilever up to 4 μm in vertical
Fig. 5.17 Schematic of 
ﬂying plasmonic lens for 
high-throughput near-ﬁeld 
optical lithography

[Page 194]
direction over the topographic features on a sample surface. The integrated 
cantilever-actuator assembly could achieve much higher resonance frequency than 
a piezoelectric tube. Working at constant force mode, the integrated cantilever probe 
achieved 3 mm/s scanning speed over a sample surface containing 2 μm vertical 
steps [75].
194
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Another way to achieve high-speed scanning is to ﬁx the probe and let the 
substrate move. This approach has been implemented by emulating an optical 
compact disk, as illustrated in Fig. 5.17 [5]. The optical read head of conventional 
compact disk drive is replaced by a plasmonic lens which serves as an optical probe. 
The substrate disk coated with a thin layer of photoresist is spinning at the speed 
between 4 and 12 m/s. The high-speed spinning of substrate creates an airﬂow which 
acts as an air bearing with its aerodynamic lifting force to balance the weight of 
plasmonic lens assembly so as to maintain 10–20 nm height above the disk surface. 
The plasmonic lens is made of concentric metallic rings which generate plasmonic 
wave when irradiated by a laser beam. Photoresist exposure by surface plasmon 
polariton (SPP) has been described in Sect. 2.8 of Chap. 2. The plasmonic lens can 
focus the SPP wave into sub-100 nm beam spot. The air bearing keeps the lens at a 
distance close enough to realize near-ﬁeld optical exposure of photoresist. Features 
of 80 nm have been patterned by this method. By optimizing the plasmonic lens, 
22 nm patterning is also possible [76]. A recent development increased the spin 
speed to 8–18 m/s at the minimum ﬂying height of 15 nm. The circular type of 
plasmonic lens was also replaced by bowtie type (Fig. 5.3) to enhance the plasmonic 
ﬁeld. With this system, feature sizes of 26–35 nm were achieved in a TeOx ﬁlm by 
photon-induced phase change with depth of 2–3 nm [77]. Though it looked prom-
ising, it is yet to see this type of system to be adopted by industry. Besides, the 
plasmonic lithography only generated the resist pattern with very shallow depth, 
which is really not usable directly as pattern structure, and subsequent pattern 
transfer by other techniques has to be carried out. 
Having a high-speed scanning probe is only half of the story for high-throughput 
lithography. The surface modiﬁcations for the purpose of lithography take time, too, 
which include either exposure of resists or electrical/thermal/mechanical interaction 
with sample materials. Early work of tip-based lithography was using PMMA as the 
resist. Although it is of high resolution, PMMA is too low in sensitivity. Coupling 
with low efﬁciency of electron exposure by SPL (described in Sect. 5.3.1), much 
lower throughput can be expected. Use of high sensitivity resists such as chemically 
ampliﬁed resists is one possible route to improve the throughput. The chemically 
ampliﬁed resist SAL601 has been used for STM lithography, which is possible to 
achieve scan speed of 1 mm/s [78]. The spin-on-glass (SOG) is also highly sensitive 
to electron exposure. Line features with 100 nm width were achieved at scanning 
speed as high as 3 mm/s. The minimum 40 nm line features were obtained at 
scanning speed of 180 μm/s, 1 nA current, and 74 V bias voltage [79]. SOG has 
been commonly used in IC manufacturing as a planarization ﬁlm. Electron exposure 
of SOG can decompose the methyl group in SOG, making the exposed region 
20 times more dissolvable in buffered oxide etching (BOE) solution.

[Page 195]
5.6
High-Throughput Scanning Probe Lithography
195
The slowest interaction between a scanning tip and a substrate material is the 
tip-induced hydrogen depassivation on silicon (Sect. 5.4.1), which allows the tip 
scanning speed of only 10–50 nm/s [36]. The fastest interaction is the heat-induced 
sublimation of PPA (Sect. 5.3.3). Patterning of individual pixel at the speed of 2 μs/ 
pixel has been demonstrated in PPA by a thermal tip, which enabled high-speed 
linear scanning of 20 mm/s with an overall position accuracy of ±10 nm over a scan 
range of up to 50 μm. Pixels of 8 × 105 with feature size down to 30 nm were written 
in just 12 s [80]. 
5.6.2 
Massively Parallel Tip Array 
Even at 20 mm/s scanning speed, the mechanical movement of a probe is nowhere to 
match the electronic scanning speed of a focused electron or ion beam. The only way 
to further increase the throughput of tip-based nanofabrication is to use multiple tips 
and to pattern in parallel fashion. There is no fundamental hurdle and it is very much 
an engineering implementation issue to extend from single tip to multiple tips. The 
advance in silicon micromachining technology enabled massively parallel tip array 
to be built, including individual tip control mechanism. For example, Fig. 5.18a 
shows a 32 × 32 cantilever probe array chip named Millipede which was made by 
IBM [58]. Figure 5.18b is one of the cantilevers with a silicon tip at the top. The 
cantilever array was fabricated on a silicon-on-insulator (SOI) wafer, involving 
11 levels of photolithography patterning and multistep reactive ion etching and 
metal depositions [81]. Because of the batch processing nature similar to the 
manufacturing of integrated circuits, the resultant 1024 cantilevers were identical 
and highly uniform. The Millipede was developed not for nanofabrication but for 
data storage. Each tip can be heated electronically and can make thermal mechanical
Fig. 5.18 (a)  32  × 32 cantilever array chip, (b) individual cantilever. (Reprint from [81] with 
permission)

[Page 196]
indentation on a polymer surface to serve as a data storage media (Sect. 5.4.4). 
Although this type of data storage was not successful commercially because other 
technologies provided better solutions, the concept of parallel thermal tip array has 
been implemented for thermally converted precursor patterning [82].
196
5
Tip-Based Nanofabrication
Fig. 5.19 (a) Fabrication of polymer pen array; (b) three forms of dip-pen array 
The most successful high-throughput tip-based nanofabrication is realized from 
the evolution of DPN technology (Sect. 5.5.1). Traditional cantilever-based scanning 
tips cannot be scaled up to large numbers because each tip requires its own cantilever 
as seen in Fig. 5.18b. Chad Mirkin’s group developed cantilever-free tip array based 
on their DPN, because the DPN is mostly for delivering liquid molecules to a sample 
surface and only needs a direct contact between the tip and a substrate, which can be 
realized by a mechanical force. There is no need to control electrical ﬁeld, emission 
current, or heating current for individual tips. The fabrication of large array of DPN 
tips is relatively simple. As shown in Fig. 5.19a, pyramid-shaped pits are created in 
silicon by wet chemical etching; liquid elastomer (PDMS) is poured into the pits and 
molded into pyramid-shaped tips after curing; the slab of molded elastomer is 
attached to a glass plate; the vertical force applied to the soft tip array can be 
controlled by a piezo-scanner. Up to 28,000 polymer pens were demonstrated, and 
the polymer pen lithography (PPL in Fig. 5.19b) was capable to pattern features 
ranging in size from 90 nm to over 10 μm within 100 μm patterned area by changing 
the applied force and contact time [4]. In addition to the polymer pens, other forms of 
tip array were also developed such as the hard tip with soft spring (HSL in 
Fig. 5.19b) to enable pattern features smaller than 50 nm because the hard Si tips 
do not deform under pressure [83] and optical beam pen (BPL in Fig. 5.19b) which 
works as apertured NSOM (Sect. 5.3.2) to perform parallel optical lithography [84].

[Page 197]
References
197
It has been 40 years since the tip-based nanofabrication was ﬁrst discovered. 
Among many ways a scanning tip can provide to make nanostructures, four are 
better developed and more mature. They are the dip-pen nanolithography (DPN), 
thermal scanning probe lithography (T-SPL), hydrogen depassivation lithography 
(HDL), and ﬁeld emission scanning probe lithography (FE-SPL). Dedicated instru-
ments have been developed and are commercially available, such as the TERA-Fab 
from TERA-print using beam pens for maskless photolithography [64], NanoFrazor 
from Heidelberg Instruments as a table-top T-SPL system [85], ZyVector from 
Zyvex Labs to provide atomic-precision STM lithography based on HDL [86], 
and FE-SPL tool from nano analytik [87]. However, the shortcomings of tip-based 
nanofabrication remain, i.e., limited patterning depth, limited patterning area, and 
limited patterning speed. The persistent developers of these tip-based technologies 
hoped that one day it would play a role in nanoscale IC manufacturing. However, it 
may never be materialized, as can be seen in Chap. 2 on how advanced the optical 
lithography 
for 
IC 
manufacturing 
has 
progressed. 
Nevertheless, 
tip-based 
nanofabrication can have many applications other than competing for IC 
manufacturing. New patterning techniques and capabilities using scanning probes 
have been continuously developed, and they have played important roles in many 
areas of science and technology, particularly in the development of nanoscience and 
nanotechnology.

Chapter 6 
Nanoimprint Lithography 
6.1 
Introduction 
In addition to photons or charged beams (electrons and ions) or scanning probes, 
lithography can be done by replication from a mold. This was the idea proposed in 
1995 when Stephen Y. Chou ﬁrst reported sub-25 nm holes made in PMMA 
polymer with an imprinting mold, and the word “nanoimprint” was coined 
[1]. Nanoimprint lithography (NIL) was much less complicated than optical or 
electron beam lithography and has been the subject of active research and develop-
ment ever since it was proposed nearly 30 years ago. Vast number of papers as well 
as dedicated books have been published [2–4]. A wide spectrum of applications 
employing NIL as a patterning technique has been reported, such as quantized 
magnetic recording disks [5, 6], DNA electrophoresis chip [7], photodetectors [8], 
polarizer [9], quantum device [10], etc. As a lithographic method, any patterning 
processes that involve optical or e-beam lithography can be done by NIL in principle 
without using those expensive tools. 
NIL is a parallel process, just as the optical lithography through a mask, but 
without the diffraction-induced limit on imaging resolution as the optical lithography 
has. Resolution wise, it is on par with e-beam lithography, but without the proximity 
effect that e-beam lithography suffers. NIL had achieved sub-10 nm resolution 
patterning in its early development [11]. This was particularly attractive for IC 
manufacturing at the time when optical lithography struggled to achieve feature 
size below 100 nm. NIL was indeed positioned to replace optical lithography as one 
of the NGL (next generation lithography) candidates for IC manufacturing by the 
International Technology Roadmap for Semiconductors (ITRS) in 2003 [4]. How-
ever, optical lithography moved on with its immersion, multi-patterning, and the 
latest EUV technology (Chap. 2). NIL never got the chance to enter the mainstream 
IC manufacturing. One reason is that the state-of-the-art ICs have many layers 
overlaying to each other, while the process of pressing a mold into the resist in 
NIL can cause distortions or deformations, leading to misalignments between the
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_6
201

[Page 202]
different layers. The other reason is that optical lithography has established mature 
technology infrastructure and supply chain, while there is only one company world-
wide to develop the NIL for IC manufacturing over the past decades, initially the 
Molecular Imprints since 1999 [4, 12] and then the Canon Nanotechnology which 
acquired Molecular Imprints in 2014. With its continued efforts, by 2018, the 
company developed a cluster NIL system (FPA-1200NZ2C) capable of patterning 
300-mm-size wafer at a throughput of 90 wafers an hour and sub-20 nm resolution at 
3.4 nm overlay accuracy [4, 13]. Canon Nanotechnology plans to begin mass 
producing 3D NAND ﬂash memory ICs using NIL by 2025.
202
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Another area where NIL might shine was for manufacturing magnetic recording 
disks. The idea was to make magnetic recording bits as individual units by NIL 
patterning method, called bit-patterned magnetic recording (BPMR). However, 
novel magnetic materials were being developed rapidly, ﬁrst came the perpendicular 
magnetic recording (PMR) and then the heat-assisted magnetic recording (HAMR). 
These new materials and technologies allowed magnetic storage to exceed 1 Tb/in2 
(terabits per square inch) [14], while at such data storage density, the bit size should 
be at least 12.5 nm if it is to be made by patterning method [15]. NIL technique has 
tremendous obstacles in stamp manufacturing and defects control for such small 
pattern size [16]. Nevertheless, recent progress in NIL for IC manufacturing pro-
moted the renewed interest in BPMR. Western Digital, one of the world largest 
magnetic recording companies, demonstrated that the UV-NIL technique could 
make ~5.5 nm features with ~12 nm pitch using a template created from a random 
nanoparticle pattern, which would be equivalent to a recording density of 4 Tb/ 
in2 [17]. 
While NIL has very limited success in getting into the IC volume manufacturing 
and high density magnetic recording, the area where NIL has found huge opportu-
nities in industrial scale applications is in photonics. According to a recent article in 
Semiconductor Engineering [18], photonics is a burgeoning industry driven by 
increasing global demand for light-enabled systems which are essential for a variety 
of 
products 
and 
services, 
including 
telecommunications, 
data 
networks, 
bio-photonics, consumer electronics, automotive, and others. These vertical markets 
rely heavily on optics and photonics components, such as LEDs and laser chips, 
optical glass, detectors and image sensors, lenses, prisms, optical ﬁlters, gratings, 
optical ﬁbers, and much more, representing a global market expected to reach nearly 
$2 trillion by 2025. Photonics components use fewer layers than traditional ICs, 
which play into NIL’s strength, i.e., its ability to create single layer high-resolution 
periodical nanostructures with excellent reproducibility and scalability on rigid or 
ﬂexible substrates. NIL offers a cost-effective and efﬁcient way to fabricate complex 
nanostructures below 100 nm, which is critical for sub-wavelength photonic devices 
such as photonic crystals, waveguides, and grating couplers. 
Over the past 30 years, many forms of NIL methods have been developed. The 
original thermal nanoimprinting required relatively high temperature to soften the 
polymer and relatively high pressure to press the mold into the polymer. Heating and 
cooling cycle prolongs the nanoimprinting process, which is not ideal for high 
throughput manufacturing. Requirement for high pressure can increase the wear of

[Page 203]
stamp and is restricted in certain applications. Although nanoimprinting based on 
thermal press is still being widely used today, several other forms of NIL have 
moved away from high temperature and high pressure processes. Notably among the 
derivatives of NIL is the UV cured nanoimprinting. Liquid instead of solid polymers 
are used, and once a liquid polymer is molded into shapes, it is solidiﬁed by 
photopolymerization. Some other techniques further moved away from the original 
concept of nanoimprinting. Techniques such as microcontact and soft lithography 
can no longer be called imprinting at all because the stamps are not pressed into a 
polymer medium to produce a surface relief pattern. In these techniques, a pattern of 
new material is transferred to a substrate surface by simply bringing the stamp in 
contact with the surface. It is a printing rather than imprinting process. However, one 
thing in common for all the derivatives, including the original form of 
nanoimprinting itself, is the replication process and the dependence on a master. 
This chapter intends to cover major aspects of NIL technology, particularly on 
patterning sub-100 nm features, the various ways to achieve this dimension scale 
and limitations of each technique. 
6.2
Thermal Nanoimprint
203
6.2 
Thermal Nanoimprint 
Nanoimprint relying on heating and pressing was the ﬁrst proposed replication 
process at nanometer scale [1]. It can often be confused with hot embossing, as 
hot embossing refers to the same replication process that relies on heating and 
pressing a stamp into plastics or other ductile surfaces. The general consensus is 
that hot embossing refers to thermal imprint of large structures which is above a 
micrometer or tens of micrometers, while thermal nanoimprint refers to making 
small structures often less than a micrometer. Therefore, the process to make plastic 
microﬂuidic devices, which are normally of tens or hundreds of micrometers in 
structure dimension, is called hot embossing, not nanoimprinting. Nanoimprint has 
much broader meaning, because some of the nanoimprint techniques may not 
involve heating and pressing at all, as introduced later in this chapter. 
The basic process of thermal nanoimprint lithography (T-NIL) can be schemat-
ically illustrated by Fig. 6.1. A thin layer of polymer (100–200 nm thickness) coated 
on a ﬂat substrate is ﬁrst heated to about 50–100 °C above its glass transition 
temperature (Tg) so that it becomes soften. A stamp with surface relief structures is 
pressed into the thin polymer layer at pressure of around 50–100 bars 
(725–1450 psi), depending on the viscosity of molten polymer. The imprint depth 
is slightly less than the polymer layer thickness so that the stamp surface does not 
have hard contact with the substrate to prevent any damage to the stamp. The next 
step is to decouple the stamp from the polymer layer (demolding), while it is still 
soft. The temperature and pressure at different stage of imprinting are plotted in 
Fig. 6.2. Normally at about 10 °C below the Tg, the imprinting pressure is released. 
When cooling down to about 50 °C, the stamp and the imprinted sample are 
separated. After the separation of stamp, an impression of the stamp pattern is left

[Page 204]
in the polymer layer. The ﬁnal step is to remove the residual polymer inside the 
imprinted pattern areas by reactive ion etching, to lay bare the substrate surface. The 
polymer patterns obtained after removal of residual polymer are in the same form as 
those obtained by optical lithography or e-beam lithography of resists. There are a

number of key factors which are related to faithful replication of sub-100 nm features 
in the thermal press nanoimprinting process, such as the stamp, the polymer, the 
separation of stamp and polymer (demolding), and alignment if multilevel 
nanoimprinting is needed. These factors are discussed in the following sections.
6.2
Thermal Nanoimprint
205
6.2.1 
Nanoimprint Stamps 
As a master in the replication process, the stamp plays the key role in replicating 
nanoscale structures in polymer, because the replicated structures are only as good as 
the master structures. For T-NIL, the stamp is made of hard material and should be 
able to withstand heating and pressure without signiﬁcant weariness. Nanoimprint 
stamps can be made of many materials. However, they have to be compatible with 
conventional lithography process. The commonly used materials for stamps are 
silicon, silicon dioxide, silicon nitride, or various metals. For nanoimprinting of 
sub-100 nm features, the stamps are exclusively made by e-beam lithography 
patterning of resist and then transfer into substrate materials. Making sub-100 nm 
patterns by e-beam lithography has its own problems, such as proximity effect, 
which has been extensively discussed in Chap. 3. Once the resist patterns are made, 
they can be transferred by reactive ion etching (RIE) to silicon, or silicon dioxide, or 
silicon nitride layer on silicon. They can also be transferred by lift-off to make metal 
structures on silicon (see Sect. 8.3 in Chap. 8). A range of metallic materials can be 
used, such as gold, aluminum, chrome, or nickel. For high-resolution e-beam 
lithography, PMMA is the best choice of resist. However, PMMA is known to 
have poor selectivity in RIE. In this case, PMMA can be used as an intermediate 
imaging layer. After development of PMMA, a lift-off of thin chrome layer (~20 nm) 
is carried out to transfer PMMA pattern into chrome pattern (be aware of the change 
of pattern polarity). Then the chrome pattern is used as etch mask to etch into silicon, 
silicon dioxide, or silicon nitride up to 400 nm depth [19]. 
Stamps made of lift-off metal do not last very long because the metal patterns do 
not always stick well to silicon substrate. Stamps made of oxide or nitride are much 
more durable. However, silicon substrate is a brittle material. It can be easily broken 
under a nonuniform pressure load. The best and most durable stamp is made of 
nickel. The nickel stamp fabrication process is schematically illustrated in Fig. 6.3. 
The fabrication starts with e-beam lithography of PMMA resist on standard silicon 
wafer. After development of exposed PMMA, a thin metal layer (10–20 nm) of 
either gold or nickel is coated to cover the whole surface of PMMA layer. Then 
electroplating of nickel is carried out using the metal covered silicon wafer as the 
cathode. The electroplated nickel ﬁlls up the PMMA pattern cavities, and continuous 
electroplating makes the total thickness to 100–200 μm. Finally, the nickel sheet is 
separated from the silicon wafer by peeling off the silicon wafer if the nickel sheet is 
thick and strong enough or by dissolving the PMMA layer in an acetone bath. The 
nickel sheet can be ﬁxed onto a metal holder plate to serve as the stamp. As nickel 
sheet has certain ﬂexibility, a nickel stamp can be rolled onto a cylinder to form

[Page 206]
a roller stamp so that roll-to-roll (R2R) nanoimprinting can be carried out [20]. Fig-
ure 6.4 shows a large size nickel stamp sheet made by the author’s group for R2R 
nanoimprinting.

Stamps can also be made without the pattern transfer step, if the patterned 
medium is hard enough. The HSQ resist, which has been introduced in Chap. 3,  is

[Page 207]
of such property. As previously described, HSQ resist can turn into silicon dioxide 
upon electron beam exposure or heating at high temperature. 20 nm line patterns 
with 60 nm period have been fabricated in 30-nm-thick HSQ by e-beam exposure. 
With a post-development baking at 500 °C, the HSQ becomes essentially silicon 
dioxide and is hard enough to be used as a stamp for nanoimprint [21].

Thermal Nanoimprint
207
It must be noted that the above stamps are only for T-NIL. Other nanoimprinting 
techniques may use different stamps. For example, UV cured nanoimprint has to use 
transparent stamps, and microcontact printing may use soft stamps. These other 
stamps will be described in later sections. 
6.2.2 
Nanoimprint Polymers 
Although a high-resolution stamp is the prerequisite for high-resolution NIL, it is all 
down to the polymers to realize the resolution capability of the stamp. The basic 
requirements on a nanoimprinting polymer are low glass transition temperature (Tg) 
to enable low imprinting temperature, low viscosity to facilitate easy ﬂow of 
polymer during ﬁlling stamp cavity process, low shrinkage after imprinting to 
maintain pattern ﬁdelity, and high resistance to RIE to serve as a good mask for 
pattern transfer by RIE. A number of factors can inﬂuence the ﬁnal outcome of 
nanoimprint, such as applied pressure, heating temperature, thickness of polymer 
layer, pattern density, and feature size on the stamp. An imprinting polymer, once 
heated above its Tg, has to ﬁll the cavities of stamp by compressed ﬂow under 
applied pressure. The force F required for the squeezing ﬂow can be expressed by 
Eq. 6.1 [22]: 
F = - 3πR4 
4h3 
0 
dh 
dt η0
ð6:1Þ
where R is the radial ﬂow distance, h0 is the initial thickness of polymer layer, dh/dt 
is the speed of polymer layer being pressed in depth direction, and η0 is the viscosity 
of polymer. The viscosity of polymer depends on both its molecular weight and 
heating temperature. The higher the molecular weight, the more viscous the polymer 
becomes. The viscosity of 350 kg/mol PMMA can be more than three orders of 
magnitude higher than 25 kg/mol PMMA at the same temperature [23]. With the 
increase of heating temperature, the viscosity of PMMA decreases exponentially. 
Figure 6.5 shows the viscosity of 110 kg/mol PMMA vs. temperature [24]. 
The above formula tells that high pressing force is required to achieve high speed 
of imprinting, which is also necessary when imprinting either thin polymer layer or 
large stamp cavity or high viscosity of polymer. High imprinting temperature is also 
preferred. The direct consequence of low imprinting temperature is the prolonged 
imprinting time at certain pressure. Imprinting, which may be completed in a few 
seconds at 200 °C, can take hours at 140 °C  [25]. Thick polymer layer can make the

[Page 208]
imprinting easier. However, imprinting with thick polymer layer will leave too much 
residual. The ﬁnal RIE step to remove the residual will take longer time. Because the 
RIE process is indiscriminative, it etches both the residual and the imprinted polymer 
pattern structure, resulting in pattern damage and distortion with excessive RIE. To 
have the appropriate thickness of residual layer, the amount of polymer should 
ideally be calculated according to the cavity volume of stamp to make sure the 
cavities are not underﬁlled in the meantime leaving the minimum thickness of 
residual layer.

6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.5 Viscosity of

Fig. 6.6 Voids in imprinted 
structure due to nonuniform 
distribution of patterns. 
(Reprinted from [26] with 
permission) 
Smaller features are easier to imprint than larger features. For example, 500 nm 
periodical lines can be imprinted at 100 bars pressure and 140–150 °C temperature in 
5 minutes. At the same pressure and temperature, 100 μm periodical lines cannot be 
imprinted at all, unless the temperature rises to 200 °C  [25]. In this aspect, 
sub-100 nm periodical features are actually easier to imprint because the squeezing 
ﬂow distance of polymer is much less. The most difﬁcult imprinting task is the 
imprinting of both large and small patterns. While small feature can be imprinted 
quickly, large feature cavities may not get ﬁlled up completely, leaving voids as 
shown in Fig. 6.6 [26]. The dependence of polymer ﬁlling on feature dimension and

[Page 209]
density is very much like the proximity effect in e-beam lithography. In fact, it can be 
called the imprinting proximity effect [27]. 
6.2
Thermal Nanoimprint
209
Fig. 6.7 Imprint with 
different stamp structures: 
(a) positive stamp with less

required; (b) negative stamp

(a) 
(b) 
The stamp design has serious impact on the imprinting process if the imprinting 
proximity effect is to be avoided. An example is shown in Fig. 6.7. For the same 
pattern structures, the positive stamp structure in Fig. 6.7a (protrusions) causes much 
less displacement of polymer during imprint, compared to the negative stamp 
structures in Fig. 6.7b (cavities). In general, pattern design for NIL stamps should 
make sure that the cavities and protrusions are uniformly distributed over the entire 
stamp area, to avoid large difference in polymer squeezing ﬂow distance. This can 
reduce the occurrence of incomplete ﬁlling and variation of residual layer thickness. 
Optimizing the pressure, temperature, and imprinting time in combination with 
appropriate polymer material property can sometimes minimize the proximity effect 
and achieve uniform ﬁlling of stamp cavities with dimension from sub-100 nm to 
millimeter [28]. 
PMMA is the commonly used polymer in nanoimprint, because it is readily 
available in e-beam lithography. The other polymer, also widely used in NIL, is 
polystyrene (PS). PS is a common thermoplastic polymer and has much lower 
viscosity than PMMA at the same temperature [29]. The Tg of PMMA is 95–105 ° 
C and the Tg of PS is 110 °C. These polymers are adopted for NIL because of their 
commercial availability, but they may not be the best suited materials. In addition to 
PMMA and PS, over a dozen of other polymers have also been tried for T-NIL 
[18, 30]. A range of dedicated polymers speciﬁcally for T-NIL are commercially 
available, such as the NXR-1000 series [31] and mr-I 7000R series nanoimprinting 
polymers [32]. These commercial T-NIL polymers are tailored to have high-
resolution capability, wafer-scale uniformity, fast response and excellent ﬂow, low 
processing temperature and pressure, strong adhesion to substrates, and also stability 
at room temperature. They are generally better in pattern transfer with RIE or liftoff.

[Page 210]
210
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Thermoplastic polymers can either deform plastically or elastically under heating 
and pressure. Plastic deformation results in permanent change of shape, which is 
required for imprinting. If it is elastic deformation, the polymer can recover to its 
original shape. There is no clear boundary between plastic and elastic deformations. 
It can be controlled either by the heating temperature or the pressing time. In 
nanoimprinting of thermoplastic polymers, the recovery can cause residual layer 
reshapes after the separation of stamp and sample. In the extreme case, it may 
recover almost to the level of the initial layer thickness. The recovery phenomenon 
is particularly noticeable at low imprint temperature and for large imprinted 
features [33]. 
Small imprinted features may be free from the elastic recovery problem. They 
however cannot avoid the internal stress induced by pressing of stamp. Free standing 
nanoscale polymer structures are known to be unstable. There are simulations 
indicating a strong reduction in the fundamental elastic moduli of the material inside 
a nanostructure when the width of the nanostructure decreases below 50 nm [34]. On 
the one hand, the polymer structures at sub-100 nm become very weak. On the other 
hand, large shear force can exert on the polymer structures during stamp pressing 
into the polymer. Internal stress can build up in these small polymer structures, 
weakening their rigidity and leading to structure distortion. The imprinting-induced 
internal stress can be manifested when the polymer structures are annealed at 
temperatures near the Tg of the polymer, at which temperature the internal stress 
can be released, if there are any, but low enough not to cause any visible ﬂow of 
polymer. Pattern structure decay in the form of reduced height and enlarged base 
width has been observed at the annealing temperature. One example showed that line 
patterns of 210 nm high and 140 nm wide in 15 kg/mol PMMA decayed into 55 nm 
high and 180 nm wide after being annealed for 46 minutes at temperature of 
Tg + 15  °C [35]. The loss in height does not correspond to a proportional increase 
in width because the loss in height shows up as an increase of the residual layer 
thickness. The imprinting-induced internal stress is a general phenomenon in ther-
moplastic polymers. High molecular weight polymers are more viscous; therefore, 
they can build up more internal stress [36]. Shorter imprinting time also tends to 
create more internal stress [37]. With the internal stress and the elastic recovery, the 
pattern structure shape by T-NIL can become time dependent. 
T-NIL using thermoplastic polymers needs heating and cooling which takes time. 
The throughput is relatively low comparing to the UV-curable resins. The advantage, 
however, is that these polymers are readily available at low cost and in wide variety, 
while UV-curable resins are generally expensive because they must contain specially 
designed monomer, photoacid generator, solvent to reduce viscosity, and other 
ingredients (see Sect. 6.4.2). The solvents normally contain volatile organic com-
pound (VOC) which are environmentally hazardous and have to be handled with 
extra care [30]. Originally, the polymers for T-NIL only serve as mediums, like the 
resists in optical or e-beam lithography. Subsequent pattern transfer has to be 
performed, such as etching or deposition, in order to obtain the replicated structures 
in functional materials. In the past decades, many organic and inorganic functional 
materials have been found to be suitable for T-NIL, so that nanostructures could be

[Page 211]
made in the materials without additional pattern transfer process [38]. Even metals 
can be imprinted. Direct nanoimprinting on thermally softened metals, such as Al, 
Ag, and Cu, has been investigated. By heating up 100-μm-thick metal foils to 
500–650 °C and pressed with quartz stamp at 20 MPa pressure for 1 hour, 350 nm 
grating patterns were replicated in the metal foils [39]. 
6.2
Thermal Nanoimprint
211
6.2.3 
Demolding 
A high quality stamp and good compatible polymer imprinting medium cannot 
guarantee good quality nanoimprinting pattern structure unless the demolding pro-
cess is also successful. Demolding is to separate the stamp from imprinted polymer 
structure after cooling down below the glass transition temperature of polymer. 
Distortion or damage of imprinted patters can happen if the polymer sticks to the 
stamp surface, or there is a friction drag due to roughness of stamp sidewalls, or there 
is a trapping of polymer due to negative slope of stamp sidewalls. 
Polymer sticking to stamp is the common problem encountered in NIL because of 
the large surface contact area and high shear force between polymer and stamp 
during demolding. There are three ways to reduce the surface adhesion: 
(1) To choose a stamp material which has low surface energy, such as diamond like 
carbon (DLC). DLC is known to have low surface energy. DLC ﬁlm can be 
patterned to serve as the nanoimprint stamp which is not only of low polymer 
adhesion but also of high wear resistance [40]. 
(2) To mix mold release agent with polymer [41]. 
(3) To coat the surface of mold with an antistiction layer. 
Teﬂon™ or Teﬂon-like ﬂuorocarbon or ﬂuorosilane release agent [26, 42] are all 
able to modify the mold surface to make the surface hydrophobic and low energy. 
Among the three methods, the antistiction coating is most widely used. The coating 
can be done in liquid phase or vapor phase. However, the antistiction layer become 
less effective as the mold cavity becomes very small, for example, a few tens of 
nanometers, where only a few polymer macromolecular chains are in action during 
imprinting. 
The sidewall angle of mold has critical inﬂuence on demolding, hence on the ﬁnal 
imprinted polymer structures. Figure 6.8 shows three different proﬁles of imprint 
stamp. It is obvious that the mold proﬁle shown in Fig. 6.8a is preferable because it is 
much easier to pull out the mold from imprinted polymer layer. The demolding of 
the mold proﬁle shown in Fig. 6.8b will depend on the surface roughness of 
sidewalls because sidewalls exert high shear force on polymer structure during 
demolding. The mold proﬁle shown in Fig. 6.8c is the most undesirable proﬁle. 
Imprinted polymer structures are in effect trapped inside the mold structures. Dis-
tortion and damage to the imprinted polymer structures are inevitable. Molds made 
from RIE or liftoff are mostly of tapered or vertical sidewall proﬁles and generally do

[Page 212]
not have problem in demolding as long as the sidewall surface is treated with 
antistiction agents.

Nanoimprint Lithography
(a)

Fig. 6.8 Proﬁles of nanoimprint stamp: (a) tapered sidewall, (b) vertical sidewall, (c) undercut

Fig. 6.9 (a) Illustration of stress concentration during demolding, (b) PMMA patterns after stamp 
release. (Reprinted from [44] with permission) 
For molds with vertical sidewall proﬁle such as the one shown in Fig. 6.8b, the 
higher the pattern aspect ratio (height to width), the more difﬁcult it becomes to 
demold. High aspect ratio structures have large sidewall contact surface. There is a 
high shear stress at the vertical interface between mold and polymer. During 
demolding, the stress is concentrated at the base area of polymer pattern [43], as 
illustrated in Fig. 6.9a. As demolding takes place at temperature lower than the 
polymer Tg, polymer becomes brittle at the low temperature. Pattern fractures can 
happen around the base area, as the experimental result shown in Fig. 6.9b. 
Using polymers with low molecular weight (Mw) to facilitate imprinting, because 
of its low viscosity, is generally in conﬂict with the requirement of safe demolding, 
because low Mw polymer is also low in shear modulus. This can become a serious 
issue when low Mw polymer is used for high aspect ratio nanoimprinting. Therefore,

[Page 213]
for the sake of safe demolding, the pattern aspect ratio by nanoimprint rarely exceeds 
3:1. For higher aspect ratio nanoimprint, one solution is to use double-layer poly-
mers, as schematically shown in Fig. 6.10. The double-layer polymer system 
consists of high Mw as the base layer and low Mw as the top layer. As it is shown, 
when the double-layer polymers squeezed into the stamp cavity, the high Mw 
polymer is at the base region. Because of the high shear modulus of high Mw 
polymer, it can withstand the high shear force during demolding, keeping the high 
aspect ratio polymer pattern structure intact. Figure 6.11 shows the high aspect ratio 
patterns by imprinting of the double-layer polymer system.

Thermal Nanoimprint

Nanoimprint Lithography
6.2.4 
Alignment 
If multilevel nanoimprint patterning is required or nanoimprinting patterns need to 
overlay with an existing pattern on substrate surface, the overlay alignment becomes 
a big issue. The masters used in T-NIL are generally made of silicon or nickel. They 
are nontransparent. In order to align the master to an underlying pattern, an open 
window can be fabricated in the master, and the alignment can be carried out through 
the open window. However, to etch through a silicon wafer to open the alignment 
window, the etch-through hole has to be a few tens to a hundred micrometers. 
Alignment through such a large hole cannot achieve high alignment accuracy. 
Another alignment technique is to fabricate alignment marks at the back of a 
master. Before moving the master over to a substrate, a video camera takes the image 
of alignment marks on the substrate which is stored in a computer. Once the master is 
placed on top of the substrate, an optical microscope connected to a video camera 
will take the picture of alignment marks on the back of the master which are 
compared with the substrate alignment marks already stored in computer. Both 
alignment mark images are displayed on a computer screen. By moving around 
the master relative to the substrate, while aligning the marks on computer screen, an 
accurate alignment can be achieved. In the early version of NIL systems when the 
NIL apparatus was a simple hot press without dedicated alignment facility, the 
aforementioned alignment was done in a mask aligner with a special holder. Once 
the alignment between the stamp and substrate was done, they were clamped 
mechanically and manually placed into the imprinter stage for imprinting. With 
extreme care, sub-micrometer alignment accuracy could be achieved over a 4″ 
substrate [45]. Later versions of nanoimprinters have the built-in online alignment 
facility [46]. Though the alignment accuracy is still at sub-micrometer level, the 
alignment process has become automatic. 
The third alignment technique for NIL with nontransparent stamp is to insert a 
miniaturized camera between the stamp and substrate prior to pressing down the 
stamp [47], as illustrated in Fig. 6.12. In this way, the alignment marks do not have to 
be on the back of stamp, and the alignment is in situ and automatic. The alignment 
technique has been applied to step and stamp imprinting lithography (SSIL). SSIL
(a)                                                           (b) 
Stamp 
Alignment camera 
Fig. 6.12 Alignment scheme in step and stamp imprint lithography: (a) step, (b) stamp

[Page 215]
utilizes a small stamp to imprint a large size substrate [48]. As shown in Fig. 6.12, by  
moving the stage which holds the substrate, the stamp imprints onto the substrate in 
step and repeat fashion similar to an optical stepper. Dynamic alignment is ensured 
in each step.
6.3
Room Temperature Nanoimprint
215
Apart from the limit in optical imaging resolution during alignment, there are 
other fundamental limits in the alignment accuracy for T-NIL: 
1. Misalignment caused by mechanical pressure. This may be caused by 
nonparallelism between top and bottom platens or non-ﬂatness of the substrate 
such as bowing induced by internal stress from previous processing steps. If such 
nonparallelism exits, a pre-aligned position before applying imprinting pressure 
can be offset once a pressure is applied between top and bottom platens. 
2. Misalignment caused by different thermal expansions between a master and a 
substrate. If the master and substrate are made of different materials, their 
different thermal expansion coefﬁcients can result in misalignment once they 
are heated to high temperature. For example, an oxide stamp to imprint on a 4″ 
size silicon substrate can have 100 nm/°C thermal expansion difference at the rim 
of 4″ silicon wafer. The effect becomes more signiﬁcant when a silicon stamp is 
used to imprint on a GaAs substrate [49]. Therefore, for high alignment accuracy, 
it is imperative to avoid high temperature and high pressure. 
6.3 
Room Temperature Nanoimprint 
Thermal press nanoimprint is not always a desirable lithography technique. In some 
applications, the substrate materials cannot withstand high pressure or may change 
property at high temperature. For example, cobalt platinum magnetic thin ﬁlm is 
stable only below certain temperature, InP is very fragile at high uniaxial stress, and 
most of biological samples cannot survive high temperature and high pressure. From 
the throughput point of view, high temperature nanoimprinting platen needs to cool 
down, which consumes most of the time in an imprinting cycle. As long as the 
thermoplastics are used as the imprinting medium, the requirements for high tem-
perature and high pressure are inevitable. Alterative imprinting medium is the key 
for the realization of room temperature nanoimprint lithography (RT-NIL), for 
example, using polymers with low glass transition temperature. One of such poly-
mers is Hydrane HS2550 [50]. This polymer has glass transition temperature at 
0–10 °C and melting transition point at 30 °C, which makes the polymer very 
suitable for low temperature NIL. There is also a report using ultrasonic energy to 
hammer soft metals (gold and tin) into anodic aluminum oxide (AAO) templates at 
room temperature [18, 51]. 
The breakthrough in RT-NIL was the use of hydrogen silsesquioxane (HSQ) as 
the imprinting medium [52]. HSQ has been introduced in e-beam lithography. It is in 
liquid form at room temperature and only becomes hardened when heated to dry 
up. Figure 6.13 shows the imprinted depth in HSQ when the resist has undergone

[Page 216]
prebaking at different temperatures [53], indicating that it becomes harder to imprint 
when the HSQ layer becomes drier. Once it is dried at 250 °C, the HSQ layer cannot 
be imprinted at all. In practice, the HSQ layer is normally given 50–100 °C 
prebaking before carrying out RT-NIL, so that it is no longer “wet.” The increased 
viscosity caused by mild prebaking can facilitate the ﬁlling of mold cavities. The 
plots in Fig. 6.13 also show that there is no signiﬁcant increase of hardness for 
prebake temperature less than 100 °C. After imprinting, a post-baking is normally 
carried out to further dry up the HSQ layer. 
216
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.13 Inﬂuence of 
prebaking (PB) temperature 
on imprinted depth in HSQ 
(imprinting pressure: 
220 bars). (Reprinted from 
[53] with permission) 
HSQ is known to be difﬁcult in spin-coating large thickness. In order to make 
high aspect ratio imprinted patterns or to obtain desired resist undercut proﬁle for 
lift-off, multilayer RT-NIL techniques have been developed. In the multilayer resist 
systems, HSQ is the top layer serving as the imprinting or imaging layer. The bottom 
layers can be any other resists as long as they can be etched away in RIE. An 
example of the multilayer nanoimprinting resist system is HSQ on top of PMMA 
[54]. The nanoimprinting of HSQ/PMMA bilayer resist system and transfer of 
imprinted HSQ pattern into bottom PMMA layer by reactive ion etching (RIE) are 
schematically shown in Fig. 6.14. The bilayer resist system is formed by ﬁrst 
spinning 150-nm-thick PMMA on a silicon substrate. After baking the PMMA 
layer in oven at 180 °C for 1 hour, the HSQ resist was spun on top of the PMMA 
layer at 9000 rpm spin speed, resulting in a 40 nm thickness of HSQ top layer. The 
HSQ/PMMA resist stack was then baked on a hotplate at 150 °C for 2 minutes and 
followed by oven bake of 20 minutes at 180 °C. Nanoimprinting of the bilayer resist 
was done by a silicon stamp at 440 bars followed by oxygen RIE to transfer the HSQ 
pattern into the PMMA layer. A minimum of 70 nm line features have been 
achieved. Normally a thin residual layer remains after imprint, which has to be 
removed by RIE. In the aforementioned process, such a step was not necessary. This 
may be due to the fact that the HSQ was so thin and had been hard baked, and there 
was the PMMA layer underneath to cushion the press of stamp. The stamp just broke 
through the thin HSQ layer into the PMMA resist. Other polymers have also been

[Page 217]
used as the bottom layer, such as photoresists or poly(vinel alcohol) [53] or spin on 
glass (SOG) [55] or polyelectrolyte multilayer ﬁlms [56]. The imprinting and pattern 
transfer processes are similar to what is illustrated in Fig. 6.14. 
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
217
(a)
(b) 
O2 RIE 
Stamp 
HSQ 
PMMA 
Silicon 
Imprint 
Fig. 6.14 Schematic of HSQ/PMMA bilayer resist for RT-NIL: (a) imprinting, (b) pattern transfer 
by RIE 
The above bilayer system offers a number of advantages over conventional 
T-NIL of thermoplastic polymers or RT-NIL of single layer HSQ [54]: 
(1) Feature proﬁle is independent of imprinting. High aspect ratio can be achieved in 
RIE of PMMA layer. 
(2) The high etch resistance of HSQ to oxygen plasma allows very thin HSQ layer to 
be used as the imprinting layer. 
(3) No need to remove residual HSQ after imprinting, resulting in better feature 
ﬁdelity. 
(4) The underneath PMMA can act as a buffer layer to provide a soft landing for 
silicon stamp, which reduces the chance of damaging the stamp. 
(5) No intermediate layer is needed, because there is no intermixing between the 
HSQ and PMMA. 
(6) There is no need to coat release agent on silicon stamp because only 40-nm-thick 
HSQ needs to be penetrate through. The stamp can be repeatedly used without 
additional cleaning process. 
6.4 
UV Cured Nanoimprint 
Although nanoimprinting can be performed at room temperature as described in the 
previous section, it is still not a low pressure process. A truly room temperature and 
low pressure process is the UV cured nanoimprint lithography (UV-NIL). 
Figure 6.15 schematically illustrates the basic steps of UV-NIL. It is almost the 
same as the T-NIL shown in Fig. 6.1. The key differences are the use of transparent 
stamp and UV-curable polymer. The UV-curable polymers are in liquid form at 
room temperature with viscosity ranging from 50 to 200 mPa.s. Little pressure

[Page 218]
(< 1 bar) is needed to press the stamp into the liquid polymer. The solidiﬁcation of 
liquid polymer is done by UV illumination through the transparent stamp. UV-NIL 
was ﬁrst proposed in mid-1990s [57]. The technology has since gained more and 
more popularity as it has solved many problems associated with the T-NIL.

6
Nanoimprint Lithography

components, (b) UV

6.4.1 
Transparent Stamps 
For UV-NIL, the stamp should be transparent. In the early days of development, 
quartz (fused silica) substrate was the choice of stamp material. However, direct 
patterning resist layer on quartz by e-beam lithography met charging problem. 
Therefore, a thin layer (10–20 nm) of chrome is coated on the surface to act as 
both a discharging layer and a masking layer for quartz etching [58]. There was also 
a report of using indium-tin-oxide (ITO) coating as etch mask, which is both 
conductive and transparent [59]. Reactive ion etch (RIE) of quartz is known to be 
more difﬁcult than RIE of silicon. The quartz etch rate in a conventional RIE system 
is only 30–50 nm/min. Even with a high power density induction coupling plasma 
(ICP) etcher, the etch rate is about 150–350 nm/min. Other issues such as sidewall 
angle and surface roughness also exist. Optimization of etching conditions is 
necessary for each particular stamp design in order to achieve the best quality of 
fabrication.

[Page 219]
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
219
Quartz stamp is expensive in terms of both the material and the stamp fabrication 
process. The alternative is to fabricate stamps in polymers, as long as they are 
transparent to UV wavelength, and the polymer stamps can be replicated from a 
master silicon stamp in large quantity just like copying compact disks. As the result, 
the polymer stamp becomes so cheap that it can be for single use and thrown away. 
In this way, the master stamp can last much longer. There are two types of polymers 
commercially available for copying the disposable stamps. One is the Intermediate 
Polymer Stamp (IPS™) from Obducat AB in Sweden [18, 60], which is a polymer 
ﬁlm that users can make their own stamps from a master stamp by a T-NIL process. 
The other one is the OrmoStamp® from MicroResist Technology in Germany [61], 
which is UV-curable organic-inorganic hybrid polymer in liquid form. The stamp 
made from IPS ﬁlm can be used for simultaneous thermal and UV imprint, while the 
stamp made from OrmoStamp polymer is only for UV-NIL. A range of other 
polymers have also been investigated, such as a ﬂuororesin material [62], which is 
>95% transparent to UV light down to 200 nm wavelength. The added beneﬁt of  
this ﬂuororesin is its natural hydrophobic surface. No antistiction coating of stamp is 
needed. Some ﬂuorinated polymer stamps were repeatedly used for 200 times 
without obvious deterioration of surface hydrophobic property [63]. Other newly 
developed polymers from Optool in Sweden also do not need antistiction treatment 
[64]. A distinctive beneﬁt of the polymer-based stamps is its “self-cleaning” process. 
If there are any particulate defects on the master stamp, the copying process can 
remove theses defect. As seen in Fig. 6.16, particles are simply embedded into the 
polymer stamp and removed during copying the polymer stamp. They would not 
pose any risk in the next round of copying. 
As mentioned in Sect. 6.2.2, the biggest difﬁculty for NIL is to pattern features 
with large dimensional differences. While small feature cavities in a stamp can be 
ﬁlled quickly, large features require more volume of polymers and longer ﬂow
Fig. 6.16 Self-cleaning

[Page 220]
distance. Defects are often created due to partial ﬁlling of large stamp cavities. A 
hybrid stamp was developed to solve this problem, which takes advantage of 
UV-curable polymers, i.e., the polymer can be washed away if the UV irradiation 
is blocked. The hybrid stamp is schematically shown in Fig. 6.17a, which has both 
the transparent cavities for imprinting and opaque patterns for blocking the UV light 
[65]. The opaque features are formed by depositing and patterning a metal layer. 
When the hybrid stamp is pressed into the polymer resist and the UV light shines 
through the stamp, part of the UV irradiation is blocked by the opaque features on the 
stamp. After demolding the stamp, an additional development process is carried out 
so that in those areas where the UV light is blocked, the polymer resist will be 
washed away in developer solution, leaving both the imprinted features and the 
developed features as shown in Fig. 6.17b. It was demonstrated that both 350-nm-
thin dense lines and 200 μm large square patterns could be made by a hybrid 
stamp [65].

Developed resist 
Conventional UV-NIL always leaves an unwanted residual layer which has to be 
removed by RIE. A hybrid stamp shown in Fig. 6.18a can remove the residual layer 
without using RIE [66]. By making the end of protrusion features opaque, the UV 
irradiation is blocked, and no curing of polymer takes place at these areas. The 
subsequent removal of residual layer can be done simply by wet development in a 
developer solution or acetone.

[Page 221]
