Fig. 6.18 (a) Hybrid stamp with opaque layer at the end of protrusion features. (b) Imprinted and 
developed polymer structures 
UV-NIL does not have to use a transparent stamp. If the UV-curable polymer is 
coated onto a transparent substrate, a normal silicon stamp can be used, and the UV 
curing is done by illuminating through the transparent substrate, as schematically 
shown in Fig. 6.19. A thermal press NIL of SU-8 photoresist, combined with UV 
illumination from backside, has been able to produce high aspect ratio imprinted 
dense lines, as the NIL pattern shown in Fig. 6.20. Heating above the Tg of SU-8 
promotes a ﬂowable state of SU-8, which helps to ﬁll the dense and deep cavity in 
silicon stamp. The extra UV curing solidiﬁes the SU-8 and forms the ﬁnal NIL 
pattern [67]. 
6.4.2 
UV-Curable Polymers 
The key enabler of UV-NIL is the UV-curable polymers. UV-curable polymers have 
existed for a long time as standard polymer products. There are UV glues and UV 
dental sealants. They all rely on photopolymerization process to turn from liquid or 
paste state into solid state upon UV light irradiation. For the UV-curable polymers to 
be used in UV-NIL, there are a number of specially required properties. The most 
important properties are as follows:
•
Low viscosity so that it can ﬁll in the stamp cavity quickly and completely via 
capillary force
•
High resolution to replicate sub-100 nm patterns
•
Fast UV curing to reduce the cycle time of UV-NIL
•
Low shrinkage to ensure pattern ﬁdelity of replication
•
High dry etch selectivity to ensure reliable pattern transfer into substrate materials 
These desirable properties can be obtained by appropriate polymer synthesis. A 
UV-curable polymer for nanoimprint commonly consists of three basic constituents:

[Page 222]
222
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.19 Schematic of UV 
curing of polymer through a 
transparent substrate 
Fig. 6.20 High aspect ratio 
dense lines made in SU-8 
photoresist by combining 
thermal press with UV 
curing nanoimprint

[Page 223]
resin monomer, photoinitiator, and additives. To synthesize a suitable polymer is 
like a cooking process. Basically, different ingredients are added together so that the 
ﬁnal mixture possesses ﬂavors of each ingredient in various extents. To meet the 
above requirements, the UV-curable polymers contain vinyl ethers which are the key 
ingredient for photopolymerization, organic acrylates to provide the low viscosity of 
polymer mixture, organic cross-linker for thermal stability and mechanical strength, 
and photoinitiator which dissociates upon UV irradiation to form radicals which 
promote the polymerization process [68]. Some polymers may have additives such 
as silicon-containing acrylate monomers to enhance the dry etch selectivity or 
ﬂuorinated compounds to facilitate mold release. For example, it is found that a 
ﬂuorine based additive (1H, 1H, 2H, 2H perﬂuorooctyl triethoxysilane) can migrate 
to the surface of polymer ﬁlm during spin-coating, which can lower the surface 
energy and create an antistiction surface to make demolding of stamp easier [69].
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
223
The viscosity of UV-curable polymers is preferably below 15 mPa.s. In practice, 
it is normally a few tens or a few hundreds of mPa.s. It is considerably lower than 
those used in T-NIL, which is at least in the order of 104 Pa.s [29]. Low viscosity can 
make the polymer ﬁling of stamp cavity faster via capillary force. Imprinting 
pressure can be reduced and residual layer is thinner. The polymeric molecule size 
poses no limit on its high-resolution capability as the molecular chains are only 
1–2 nm. The resolution of UV-NIL is primarily limited by the structure dimension of 
stamps. The UV curing time depends on the sensitivity and concentration of 
photoinitiator. Normally, the required energy for curing the polymers is in the 
range of a few hundreds of mJ/cm2 . The curing time is from a few seconds to a 
minute depending on the power of UV lamp. 
One of the critical issues for UV-curable polymers is the shrinkage. UV-curable 
polymers, once it has been solidiﬁed, have typically 5–15% volume shrinkage 
[57]. The shrinkage is very much dependent on the molecular structures. It is 
possible to reduce the shrinkage to 3–6% by selecting high molecular weight of 
polymer components [70], or add a volume expanding monomer to compensate the 
shrinkage of conventional volume-shrinking epoxy monomers, which reduced the 
shrinkage to 1.86% and reduced the demolding force by 69% [71]. One thing should 
be noted that the polymer shrinkage is usually measured on a planar polymer ﬁlm 
under uniform pressure and UV irradiation. The measured shrinkage in this way may 
not be equal to actual shrinkage in replicated pattern structures. For example, the 
measured shrinkage for planar ﬁlm is 9%, whereas the measured dimensional 
variation for 100 nm line features shows no change [72]. One possible explanation 
is that those small features are under higher than uniform planar pressure during UV 
curing. The UV curing under higher pressure may result in a lower shrinkage 
because of the in-built internal stress. 
The acrylate based UV-curable polymers have high UV sensitivity but are also 
sensitive to ambient oxygen. Oxygen molecules dissolved in the polymer can 
scavenge free radical species and thus inhibit the polymerization process at the 
polymer surface at the onset of exposure. This can prolong the required exposure 
time. For example, a UV-curable polymer has minimum UV exposure dose of 
700 mJ/cm2 in vacuum but needs 1.4 J/cm2 when exposed in air [70]. A different

[Page 224]
UV-curable polymer system based on cationic cross-linking of cycloaliphatic 
epoxies was developed to overcome this problem, because cationic polymerization 
is not prone to oxygen inhibition, as compared to the free radical polymerization of 
acrylate monomers. The polymer also exhibits a very good dry etching resistance 
because of the high silicon content. Furthermore, it has very low shrinkage after 
curing (only a fraction of that of the acrylate system) [73]. 
224
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
The replicated polymer structures have to be transferred to substrate. There are 
two stages of RIE involved. First is the etch removal of residual polymer ﬁlm which 
is normally less than 100 nm. Second is the RIE of substrate material using the 
replicate polymer pattern as mask. For removal of residual ﬁlm, oxygen plasma is 
usually used. For pattern transfer to substrate, different etching gases are used 
depending on the etching chemistry of substrate material. As mentioned earlier, 
polymers with silicon-containing components can have high resistance in RIE. 
Reducing the inorganic content may result in much higher etching rate during 
removal of residual ﬁlm or pattern transfer that requires oxygen plasma. Figure 6.21 
compares the pattern structures of conventional silicon-containing photoresist 
(SiBER from Shipley containing 19% silicon by weight) and a UV-curable polymer 
(containing 9% silicon) etched in oxygen plasma. The polymer with low content of 
silicon fared poorly in oxygen plasma etching. For this particular polymer, a 
completely different etching chemistry with NH3 plasma has to be used, which 
exhibits selectivity of 5:1 to the etching of underneath antireﬂection coating. 
High content of silicon may be good in pattern transfer, but making the removal 
residual ﬁlm difﬁcult. For example, a UV-curable polymer containing inorganic 
components has dry etch rate of only 10 nm/min in pure oxygen plasma. Five percent 
of CF4 gas has to be added so that the plasma with O2/CF4 gas mixture can increase 
the polymer etch rate to 80 nm/min [69]. The advantage of pure organic polymer is 
the ease in removal of residual layer and especially in cleaning of the mold stamp if 
there are any residues of polymer sticking to the mold stamp. By tuning the polymer 
components, it is possible to formulate pure organic UV-curable polymer while
Fig. 6.21 Pattern proﬁles etched in oxygen plasma: (a) SiBER photoresist from Shipley, (b)  a  
UV-curable polymer. (Reprinted from [74] with permission)

[Page 225]
maintaining etch selectivity to silicon at around 2.5 in C4F8/SF6 plasma [70]. There 
are commercial UV-curable polymers available such as the NXR-2000 series from 
Nanonex [31] and mr-NIL200 series from Micro Resist Technology [32], which 
have been optimized for both high-resolution UV-NIL and good etch resistance in 
pattern transfer.
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
225
6.4.3 
Jet and Flash Imprint Lithography (J-FIL) 
J-FIL originated from the step and ﬂash imprint lithography (SFIL) which was ﬁrst 
proposed in 1999 [12], and it is essentially a double-layer UV-NIL process, as 
schematically shown in Fig. 6.22a–d. The original UV-NIL, ﬁrst proposed in 1996 
[57], could only work with a thin layer, low viscosity, and UV-curable polymer ﬁlm. 
Such a thin polymer ﬁlm made it difﬁcult for high aspect ratio pattern transfer into 
substrate material. In the SFIL process, a polymer resist, called transfer layer 
(or planarization layer), is coated onto a silicon substrate ﬁrst. Then a drop of low 
viscosity and UV-curable silicon-containing polymer liquid, called etch barrier, is 
dispensed on top of the transfer layer. A transparent stamp made of quartz in the size
Fig. 6.22 Schematic of 
S-FIL: (a) layer structure, 
(b) imprinting and UV 
curing, (c) demolding, (d) 
RIE into transfer layer; 
schematic of J-FIL (e) 
intelligent resist dispensing 
F 
(a) 
(b) 
(c) 
(d) 
Transparent 
stamp 
Transfer layer 
Substrate 
Release layer 
Imprint resist 
(e) 
Inkjet resist 
dispenser 
Imprint resist

[Page 226]
of 1.5 inch and coated with release layer is brought close to the transfer layer surface. 
The liquid polymer is then ﬁlled into the stamp cavities by capillary action. UV 
irradiation through the transparent stamp cures the liquid polymer and leaves an 
imprinted pattern of the stamp. A subsequent RIE transfers the imprinted pattern into 
the transfer layer which serves as the etch mask for further pattern transfer into the 
substrate. Same as UV-NIL, it is a room temperature and low pressure imprinting 
technology. Similar to the step and stamp imprint lithography (SSIL) as shown in 
Fig. 6.12, the SFIL imprints large size substrates by step and repeat of the stamp. 
Unlike the SSIL which stamps over a substrate already coated with a polymer layer, 
the liquid drop of etch barrier in SFIL is only dispensed at the location where the 
stamp is about to make an imprint.
226
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
One of the key factors preventing NIL from entering mainstream IC manufactur-
ing is the high defects level. Apart from the defects caused by dust particles, defects 
can be created by insufﬁcient ﬁlling of resist in a stamp. This is a particular issue for 
UV-NIL because the resist ﬁlling relies on capillary force, not on pressure and 
temperature as for T-NIL, and the ﬁlling characteristics are directly related to 
geometrical shapes and density of stamp patterns [18, 75]. On the one hand, too 
little resist leaves voids in the imprinted patterns. On the other hand, too much resist 
leaves excessive residual layer thickness. To enable an intelligent dispense of 
imprint resist to suit speciﬁc pattern structures, jet and ﬂash imprint lithography 
(J-FIL) was developed [76]. Different from SFIL, J-FIL uses a drop-on-demand 
inkjet resist dispenser to deliver pico-liter-sized resist droplets in a spatially selective 
manner such that the dispensed resist volume is distributed to match that of the 
template spatial volume distribution, as illustrated in Fig. 6.22e. This ensures that all 
the cavities are fully ﬁlled after imprinting while leaving the residual layer extraor-
dinarily thin (typically <15 nm) and uniform. To avoid air trapped inside the stamp 
cavity, the stamp is pressed in the center with a bow of 10 ~ 15 μm using air pressure 
to ensure that the stamp makes ﬁrst contact with the resist drops at its center and then 
pressed down completely, so that the resist ﬁlling starts from the center and spread 
out to entire stamp area [77]. 
Because inkjet dispense is integral to J-FIL, it is important that the resist has low 
viscosity between 10 and 20 mPa.s as well as high boiling point to ensure continuous 
jetting from inkjet printer without clogging the nozzles. Other requirements include 
that the resist should be easily separated from stamp without residuals adhered to the 
stamp and must have minimal shrinkage during curing to prevent image placement 
errors and to prevent signiﬁcant changes to the height or linewidth of the pattern 
[78]. All the technical issues have been more or less solved during the 20 years of 
development, starting from Molecule Imprints, Inc. (MII) which was the only 
company determined to push nanoimprint lithography into volume IC manufactur-
ing, until in 2014 when the company was acquired by Cannon to become Cannon 
Nanotechnologies. The resist structures shown in Fig. 6.23 were made by MII in 
early development, demonstrating the capability of this technology. 
It must be admitted that J-FIL is the nearest analog to optical lithography. It is a 
parallel process as well as working in step and repeat mode with the stamp size of 
26 mm × 33 mm same as the ﬁeld size of standard 193 nm DUV lithography. The

[Page 227]
stamp is transparent, which allows high precision alignment. In fact, J-FIL has a 
number of advantages over optical lithography: 
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
227
Fig. 6.23 (a) 50 nm dense lines, (b) 20 nm lines with ﬁne line edge, (c) 60 nm dense dots, (d) 
imprinted 3D structure. (Reprinted from [79] with permission)
•
Because no light wave diffraction is involved during imprinting, the only limit in 
resolution is the resolution of stamp structures, which at current level of e-beam 
lithography, as described in Chap. 3, is about 5 nm. Without light diffraction, all 
the optical proximity effects mentioned in Chap. 2 do not exist. Therefore, pattern 
in the stamp is straight design pattern without any needs of complicated OPC 
features.
•
The line edge roughness (LER), which has become a big problem in optical 
lithography at sub-50 nm resolution, does not exist in J-FIL, as shown in 
Fig. 6.21b.
•
The template can be made into 3D shape through multilevel or gray scale 
lithography process. Replication can be done no matter what the shape of 
template is. Three-dimensional pattern structure can be made easily in one step 
of imprint, as shown in Fig. 6.21d.
•
There is no complex optical imaging system such as the 193 nm immersion 
optical lithography or light source such as in EUV lithography. For 20 nm dens 
feature lithography, the cost of process and cost of ownership for J-FIL are less 
than 50% of double patterning process by 193 nm immersion lithography [80].

[Page 228]
228
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
As mentioned at the Introduction, the latest J-NIL system (FPA-1200NZ2C) from 
Cannon Nanotechnologies performs the entire patterning in four-chamber cluster 
conﬁguration, enabling a throughput of 90 wafers at 300 mm size per hour, which 
can almost match the throughput of mainstream 193 nm DUV lithography and has 
been designated for memory IC production [13]. 
6.4.4 
Substrate Conformal Imprint Lithography (SCIL) 
The stamps used for J-FIL are made of quartz which is rigid. For a large size rigid 
stamp, the ﬂatness and waviness are difﬁcult to control within a few micrometers, 
whereas the height of imprinted structures is not more than a few hundred nanome-
ters. For this reason, the quartz-based stamps are never made in large size. For 
example, the aforementioned J-FIL stamp is 26 mm × 33 mm. With the small stamp, 
the imprinting of a 300 mm wafer is done by step and repeat fashion. This works for 
IC manufacturing because it is the size of an individual die and no ﬁeld stitching is 
needed. For photonic applications, an optical diffractive surface can be much larger, 
and nanoscale structures are required to be continuous over the entire surface. By 
step and repeat to patch up a large area with a small stamp will inevitably encounter 
the ﬁeld stitching issue by which a continuous feature could be disrupted due to 
stitching error. The large area NIL is done by a soft stamp, which is the substrate 
conformal imprint lithography (SCIL). 
The stamp used in SCIL is made by casting liquid silicone rubber (poly-dimethyl-
siloxane, PDMS) onto a glass template. The transparent PDMS stamp can be as large 
as 150 mm. Figure 6.24a shows such a SCIL stamp. The SCIL is done by pneumat-
ically pushing down the soft stamp with a small pressure of 20mBar from one end 
and sequentially over the entire area as shown in Fig. 6.24b. In this way, no air 
bubbles will be trapped inside the imprinting area, and stamp cavities are ﬁlled with 
liquid resist by capillary force. After UV curing of resist, the stamp is peeled off 
sequentially by vacuum suction force. Because the stamp is soft, it can truly conform 
to the substrate, even over a protruded particle as shown in Fig. 6.24c [81]. 
Although highly conformal, a soft stamp can pose problem in patterning dense 
sub-100 nm features such as gratings because of pattern collapse. A composite stamp 
has been developed to solve the problem. The composite stamp consists of three 
layers: a thin layer (<100 μm) of high modulus (80 MPa) PDMS on top of 0.5-mm-
thick low modulus (15 MPa) PDMS and supported by a thin glass (200 μm), as 
schematically shown in Fig. 6.25. The nanoscale pattern structures are made in the 
hard PDMS layer, which can effectively prevent pattern collapse. The soft PDMS 
layer acts as a cushion to provide the conformal property. The glass supporting layer 
is thin enough to be bendable. With the composite stamp, a grating of 25–30 nm 
linewidth with a pitch of 60 nm as well as 20–30-nm-diameter holes with a pitch of 
50 nm can be made over 150 mm wafer size area [82]. 
Comparing to hard stamp UV-NIL, the soft stamp SCIL has a number of 
advantages [82]:

[Page 229]
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
229
Fig. 6.24 (a) Soft PDMS stamp for 150-mm-size wafer SCIL. (b) Sequential imprinting steps. (c) 
Conformal imprinting over a particle on the substrate. (Reprinted from [81] with permission)
•
Conformal contact can be made over large areas without the use of a high 
pressure as the ﬂexible stamp follows the substrate curvature.
•
Microscale air bubbles and partially ﬁlled features are avoided as silicone rubber 
has a high permeability for solvents and gasses, which allows trapped air to 
diffuse into the stamp.
•
Particle contaminants are less problematic as the rubber can locally deform 
around a particle, avoiding damage to the stamp or substrate.
•
Release of a rubber stamp from a rigid imprinted pattern is aided by the fact that 
the rubber temporarily deforms on release. This avoids damage to features in the 
resist or stamp and enables the replication of features with a high aspect ratio, in 
contrast to UV-NIL in which the aspect ratio is often limited to below 1.
•
PDMS has an inherently low surface energy and is chemically nonreactive, and 
therefore, an anti-sticking layer is not needed.

[Page 230]
230
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.25 Structure of tri-layer SCIL stamp 
The SCIL was jointly developed by Philips Research and SUSS MicroTec. The 
technology has now become commercially available as a tool kit of SUSS mask 
aligner (MA/BA6 and MA/BA8). Up to 6″ full-ﬁeld imprints with sub-50 nm 
resolution can be performed using the mask aligner with the exposure time of only 
2 min if no alignment is needed (imprint 30 sec, separation 60 sec, manual wafer 
handling 1.5 min) [83]. As mentioned in the Introduction, the industrial scale 
application of NIL is for photonics. SCIL can meet the requirements of substrate 
size, resolution, and throughput for such applications [84]. 
6.4.5 
Alignment Through Transparent Stamps 
Apart from room temperature and low pressure processes, another key advantage of 
UV-NIL is its high alignment accuracy. As described in Sect. 6.2.4, thermal press 
imprint only has alignment accuracy of submicron because the stamp is opaque. 
Now UV-NIL uses transparent stamps. Alignment marks can be seen through the 
stamp. The alignment scheme of UV-NIL is similar to that of contact optical 
lithography in a mask aligner. However, it potentially has much higher alignment 
accuracy than either contact or projection optical lithography. Figure 6.26 schemat-
ically compares the alignment schemes for projection and contact optical lithography 
with that of J-FIL. In an optical projection system (Fig. 6.26a), alignment is 
performed in several steps: (i) to align the mask relative to the mask stage, (ii) to 
align the wafer relative to the wafer stage, and (iii) to align the mask and the wafer 
relative to each other using multiple reference mirrors. Thermal, vibrational, and 
mechanical noises can all contribute to alignment errors. In a contact optical 
lithography (Fig. 6.26b), mask and substrate have to keep a large gap in order the 
mask to have free movement during alignment. The large gap deteriorates the 
imaging of alignment mark on the substrate. UV-NIL, in particular the J-FIL, can 
allow much small gap (<0.25 μm) between the stamp and substrate because of the 
much small size of stamp (~ a square inch). There is a thin layer of liquid between the 
gap, which acts both as lubricant and damping medium. Imaging of alignment mark 
through such small gap is much clear and sharp using a conventional optical 
microscope. 
As for the type of alignment marks, conventional optical mask aligners use box-
in-box or cross-in-box alignment marks. Because of the similarity between contact

[Page 231]
lithography using a mask and UV-NIL using a template, such alignment scheme has 
also been used for SCIL and is able to achieve alignment accuracy within 1 μm 
[82]. Further improvement in accuracy is limited by the resolution of optical 
microscope used for this type of alignment. A much high-resolution alignment can 
be achieved through interferometric alignment [86]. The interferometric alignment 
relies on gratings at both the stamp side and the substrate side to generate an 
interference pattern, called Moiré fringes. The Moiré fringes are formed when two 
gratings are overlaid at an angle or have slightly different periods. If the grating 
period on substrate is P1 and that on stamp is P2, then the period length of Moiré 
pattern can be calculated by Eq. 6.2 [87]: 
6.4
UV Cured Nanoimprint
231
Fig. 6.26 Alignment 
schemes: (a) projection 
optical lithography, (b) 
contact optical lithography, 
(c) SFIL. (Reprinted from 
[85] with permission) 
PM = 
P2 
1 - P2=P1 
ð6:2Þ
The interference pattern has much larger period length than the alignment grat-
ings on both the stamp and the substrate. For example, if P2 = 1.96 μm and 
P1 = 2 μm, then the Moiré period length is 98 μm. Figure 6.27 is a video image

[Page 232]
taken during alignment of stamp and substrate. The formation of Moiré pattern is 
seen as the dark fringes. If there is a misalignment Δ between stamp and substrate, 
then the Moiré period will shift a distance of d = Δ(PM/P2). A 150 nm misalignment 
between stamp and substrate will show up in Moiré pattern by 7.5 μm shift, which is 
much easy to see from an optical microscope. By optimizing the design of gratings, 
the Moiré fringe method can magnify position errors between the two gratings up to 
a factor of ~3000. Sub-10 nm alignment accuracy has been achieved for SCIL over 
30 × 30 mm2 area [82]. For J-FIL, the production tool from Cannon Nanotechnol-
ogies has achieved sub-5 nm overlay accuracy across 300 mm wafer [13]. Theoret-
ically, the method has the ultimate alignment accuracy of ~0.5 nm [86]. Although 
Moiré fringes can achieve sub-100 nm alignment accuracy, it is normally used in 
ﬁne-tuning step. Conventional box-in-box alignment technique is still used at coarse 
alignment step.

6.5 
Reverse Nanoimprint 
So far, the nanoimprint processes introduced are all using a structured stamp to press 
into a polymer-coated rigid ﬂat substrate. To imprint on a ﬂexible polymeric 
substrate, the coating cannot be done easily. For T-NIL, heating of the polymer 
layer can also soften the polymer substrate. In such situations, the imprinting can be 
done reversely [88]. The reverse nanoimprint process is schematically illustrated in 
Fig. 6.28. Instead of spin-coating polymer onto a substrate, the polymer is spun-
coated onto a structured stamp to ﬁll up all the stamp cavities (Fig. 6.28a). A short 
time soft bake is normally carried out to evaporate solvent in the polymer. The 
molded polymer structure is then transferred to a ﬂat substrate either by thermal press

[Page 233]
(Fig. 6.28b1) if the polymer is PS or PMMA or by UV curing (Fig. 6.28b2) if the 
polymer is UV curable and the mold is transparent [89]. The UV curing can also be 
performed from substrate side if the substrate is transparent and the mold is opaque 
[90]. A pressure is needed to achieve intimate contact between the polymer-coated 
stamp and substrate surface. After demolding, the polymer structures molded by the 
stamp is transferred onto the substrate (Fig. 6.28c), which is equivalent to that 
obtained by normal imprint. The key condition to the success of reverse nanoimprint 
is to make sure that the surface energy of substrate is greater than that of the mold, so 
that the structured polymer can easily come off the mold but ﬁrmly stick to the 
substrate. Ferroelectric polymer nanostructures have been successfully made by 
reverse nanoimprint over large areas on ﬂexible substrates [91]. 
6.5
Reverse Nanoimprint
233
Fig. 6.28 Reverse 
nanoimprinting process: 
(a) spin-coating stamp with

imprinting onto substrate by 
UV curing; (c) demolding

(b1)
(b2) 
(c) 
Because polymer is poured into mold cavities, instead of pressing into mold 
cavities, high aspect ratio replication can be more easily achieved than conventional 
thermal press nanoimprint. Figure 6.29 shows replicated PMMA pillars with 17.5:1 
aspect ratio by pouring the PMMA into a mold [92]. There are two difﬁculties facing 
the high aspect ratio replication: one is how to get the polymer into such deep and 
narrow cavities, and second is how to pull the high aspect ratio polymer structure out 
of the mold. The ﬁrst difﬁculty can be overcome by using a polymer liquid with very 
low surface tension. Polymers diluted in solvent such as toluene or chloroform is 
suitable for this purpose. For example, PMMA powder is dissolved in toluene in 1: 
40 weight ratio. A further vacuum baking can be performed to ensure there are no air 
bubbles trapped inside the deep cavities. The demolding is less a problem in reverse 
nanoimprint than in thermal press nanoimprint, because polymer molecules get into 
the mold cavity purely by gravity and capillary action. There is no external force

[Page 234]
applied to squeeze the polymer into cavity. Therefore, there is no shear stress created 
in the molded polymer structures. With proper antistiction treatment of mold surface, 
demolding of polymer structure should be easier, even when they are of high aspect 
ratio. Besides, UV curing process or drying of solvent can result in some shrinkage 
of polymer volume, which also facilitates the demolding process. Though the 
polymer structures shown in Fig. 6.29 are much bigger, the same process has been 
demonstrated to be able to mold sub-100 nm pattern structures in PMMA and 
polycarbonate (PC) [93]. 
234
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.29 High aspect ratio 
PMMA pillars replicated by 
cast molding. (Reprinted 
from [92] with permission) 
The reverse imprinting can be done either on a ﬂat substrate or a substrate that 
already has some structures, as schematically shown in Fig. 6.30. An example of 
such transfer is to use a SiO2 mold which has 350-nm-deep grating with a 700 nm 
period. To assist polymer transfer to the substrate, the mold is coated with 
1H,1H,2H,2H-perﬂuorodecyl-trichlorosilane, which results in a very low-energy 
surface. The mold is then spin-coated with a polymer solution whose concentration 
is chosen so that the mold surface is planarized, having a surface proﬁle of oscillating 
waves with the maximum peak height less than 100 nm. After spin-coating, the mold 
is soft baked at 80 °C for 5 min to remove residual solvent. The coated mold is then 
pressed against a pre-patterned SiO2 substrate in a preheated hydraulic press. The 
typical imprinting pressure is 5 MPa. Cooling is initiated after 5 min, and the 
pressure is sustained until the temperature has decreased to below the glass transition 
temperature (Tg) of the polymer. The mold is then separated from the patterned 
substrate, leaving polymer patterns on top of the protrusions on the substrate. By 
repeating the process, three layers of PMMA patterns were stacked up, as shown in 
Fig. 6.31 [94]. Reverse imprint was also demonstrated using a polymer (PVA) mold 
to transfer up to six layers of polymer (M-PDMS) nanostructures onto a ﬂexible 
polymer (PET) substrate [95].

[Page 235]

Soft Lithography

6.6 
Soft Lithography 
The previous section has shown that molded polymer pattern structures can be 
transferred from the stamp to a substrate by pressing the stamp in contact with the 
substrate. The same technique can be used to transfer liquid “ink” from the stamp to 
substrate. In fact, the concept of stamping ink onto a surface was proposed before the 
advent of nanoimprint. In 1993, Kumar and Whitesides reported using an elastomer 
stamp, made by casting PDMS (polydimethylsiloxane) into a master mold, to 
transfer alkanethiol ink to a substrate coated with gold thin ﬁlm [96]. The alkanethiol 
ink pattern then acts as a gold etching mask to transfer the ink pattern into a gold thin 
ﬁlm pattern on a substrate. The process is schematically illustrated in Fig. 6.32. 
The PDMS (from Dow Corning with trade name Sylgard 184) is ﬁrst mixed with 
curing agent (Sylgard curing agent 184) in 10:1 volume ratio. A vacuum de-airing is 
normally performed to remove air bubbles introduced during mixing. The mixed

[Page 236]
liquid is then cast onto a patterned master surface. The curing can be done at room 
temperature (23 °C) for 24 hours, or 65 °C for 4 hours, or 100 °C in 1 hour, or 150 °C 
in 15 minutes. After curing the rubber-like PDMS sheet is peeled off and soaked 
with thiol solution (inking). The stamp is then brought into contact with a substrate 
coated with thin gold ﬁlm. Upon contact, the thiol ink is transferred from the stamp 
protrusion surface to the gold ﬁlm. The thiol pattern serves as an etching mask 
during gold wet etch. In this way, the stamp pattern is transferred into a gold thin ﬁlm 
pattern on the substrate. The initial work reported in 1993 might not be perfect. The 
choice of PDMS for making the stamp might just be because it was cheap and easy to 
shape into a stamp. However, the concept has been taken up by many research 
groups around the world in the following decades [97]. Though the word “soft 
lithography” was coined in 1998 [98], many variations of soft lithographic technique 
have been developed since, such as microcontact printing (μCP), replica molding 
(REM), microtransfer molding (μTM), micromolding in capillaries (MIMIC), and 
solvent-assisted micromolding (SAMIM) [99]. It has become an important replica-
tion technique parallel to the NIL technology, particularly for biotechnology appli-
cations [100, 101]. 
236
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.32 Schematic of soft stamp fabrication and soft lithography processes 
6.6.1 
Soft Stamps 
Soft stamps are the key components in the whole array of soft lithography tech-
niques. Why soft? It is because a soft stamp allows intimate contact between stamp 
surface and substrate surface even when the substrate surface is rough or non-ﬂat. 
Unlike nanoimprint in which a stamp embosses into a polymer layer by external 
pressure or capillary force as has been introduced in Sects. 6.4.3 and 6.4.4, soft

[Page 237]
lithography transfers the ink pattern simply by surface contact. PDMS was the 
earliest and still is the primary choice of material. PDMS has already been intro-
duced as a stamp material in substrate conformal imprint lithography (SCIL) (Sect. 
6.4.4). Here some of the properties are reiterated as they are particularly well suited 
for soft lithography [102]: 
6.6
Soft Lithography
237
1. PDMS is deformable and can easily make conformal contact with a nonplanar 
surface at micrometer scale. The elastic nature and low surface energy (~22 mJ/ 
m2 ) also allow it to be peeled off easily. On the other hand, the surface energy can 
be increased by oxygen plasma treatment so that surface binding becomes 
irreversible. 
2. PDMS is chemically inert. It is resistant to many diluted acids, stable against 
many solvents, and a good etch mask for RIE of silicon. It is also optically 
transparent, thermally stable, and permeable to gases. 
3. PDMS stamps are easy to make at low cost. 
However, the very fact of softness of PDMS also causes several drawbacks in soft 
lithography, particularly when trying to pattern sub-100 nm features. As schemati-
cally shown in Fig. 6.33, the low tensile modulus (~1.8 MPa) can lead to pattern 
structure collapse if h/a is too large or to pattern sagging if w/a is too large [103]. One 
can imagine that for sub-100 nm stamp structures, the standing features are weaker 
than larger features and the depth in the stamp is shallower, all of which can lead to 
the aforementioned collapse and sagging. In fact, PDMS stamp structures tend to 
merge or collapse during inking and printing for structure dimension smaller than 
1 μm [104]. 
The problem of soft PDMS stamp in printing sub-micrometer patterns is best 
demonstrated in Fig. 6.34, where the printed dense dot array from 250 nm down to 
80 nm is compared using stamps with two different hardness [105]. One stamp was 
made of commercial PDMS (Sylgard 184) with modulus of 2 MPa, and the printed 
dot array is shown in Fig. 6.34a. The other stamp was made of modiﬁed PDMS 
material with modulus of 9.7 MPa, and the printed dot array is shown in Fig. 6.34b. 
The Sylgard 184 stamp can only print out 250 nm dot array, while the hard PDMS 
can print features down to 80 nm. Even for the largest 250 nm dots, soft stamp is
(a)
(b) 
h 
a
w
 
Fig. 6.33 Distortion of PDMS stamp: (a) pattern collapse due to high aspect ratio and close packed 
structures; (b) pattern sagging due to large span and shallow cavity features

[Page 238]
printed out in round shape, while hard stamp is printed in square. In general, 
commercial Sylgard 184 is only suitable for pattern structures above 500 nm [102].
238
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Fig. 6.34 Comparison of dot array pattern printed by (a) normal PDMS stamp and (b) hard PDMS 
stamp; dot sizes range from 250 nm (lower right quadrant) down to 80 nm (upper left quadrant), and 
structure height is 100 nm. (Reprinted from [105] with permission) 
In addition to the short range distortion, there is long range distortion in the form 
of pattern displacement error. Unsupported stamps made of Sylgard 184 showed 
nonuniform pattern displacements up to 20 μm between two successive prints over 
areas of 5 × 5cm2 [104]. The use of a rigid backplane can overcome such long range 
distortion problem [106], such as the composite stamp shown in Fig. 6.25, which is 
of tri-layer structure as described in Sect. 6.4.4. The glass back plate can increase the 
overall rigidness to avoid long range distortion, while the patterned hard PDMS layer 
can eliminate the short range distortion. A minimum of 23 nm features have been 
imprinted with the tri-layer ﬂexible stamp on a 4″ size substrate [107]. Other hybrid 
mold approach using modiﬁed polymer as hard structured layer in combination with 
PDMS also demonstrated 50 nm UV-NIL results [108]. It is also possible to tailor the 
length of molecular chains in polyurethane acrylate (PUA) so that the tensile 
modulus of stamp can be tuned to either 320 MPa (hard stamp) or 19.8 MPa (soft 
stamp). Such a stamp can be either used for hard press imprint or soft contact 
imprint [109]. 
Apart from mechanical instability problem, there are other problems associated 
with PDMS soft stamps: PDMS shrinks about 1% after curing; it can swell when 
absorbing some solvents such as toluene and hexane [99]. All the above problems 
make it much harder for the soft lithography to achieve resolution as good as the 
nanoimprint techniques described in previous sections. 
6.6.2 
Microcontact Printing 
Microcontact printing (μCP) is an alternative name for soft lithography. μCP is 
possible because of two essential components: one is the PDMS stamp and another is 
the alkanethiol self-assembled monolayer (SAM) system. The previous section has

[Page 239]
emphasized on the soft aspect of contact printing. This section will focus on its 
printing characteristics. PDMS has an important property, apart from those men-
tioned previously, that is, the permeability to some solvents. As a stamp, PDMS has 
to absorb inks ﬁrst before it can stamp an ink pattern onto a substrate. The ink is 
normally alkanethiol dissolved in a solvent, and PDMS can absorb the solvent like a 
sponge. In this case, aqueous ink (water based) is not suitable because alkanethiol 
has low solubility in water and PDMS does not soak up water well (low permeabil-
ity). The commonly used solvent is ethanol. Although ethanol is not the only solvent 
that can permeate PDMS, it causes less swelling in PDMS than others. The optimum 
concentration of thiols in ethanol is between 0.1 and 1 mM, depending on the 
resolution of stamp structures. Normally, low concentration is preferable for printing 
small features [99]. 
6.6
Soft Lithography
239
Inking of a PDMS stamp is normally done by placing a drop of thiol solution 
(about 1 μL per mm2 area of stamp) onto the patterned stamp for 30 seconds, as 
shown in Fig. 6.35a. The drop is then blown away with N2, and the stamp is dried for 
10 seconds under a continuous stream of N2. The inked stamp is then used to print 
within ~15 seconds after inking. Another “wet inking” method is to dip the PDMS 
stamp into thiol solution for a few minutes and blow dry under a N2 stream, as shown 
in Fig. 6.35b. Although there was attempt to attach a reservoir of thiol solution to a 
PDMS stamp to provide constant supply of thiol ink, it did not succeed because of 
the swelling of PDMS stamp which was constantly soaking up with the ethanol 
solvent [110]. 
Fig. 6.35 (a) Wet inking by 
ink drop; (b) wet inking by 
dipping into an ink tank; (c) 
dry inking by contact with 
an ink pad 
(a) 
(b) 
(c) 
Ink drop 
Ink tank 
Ink pad

[Page 240]
240
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
It is obvious that both of the “wet inking” methods (Fig. 6.35a, b) have thiols 
homogeneously distributed over all the surface of stamp, including sidewalls and 
bottom of stamp cavities. When the PDMS stamp is pressed down to print, two 
things can happen in addition to printing a stamp pattern: one is the diffusion of 
thiols from cavity sidewalls to the substrate surface, which broaden the printed ink 
feature; another is the evaporation of thiol-containing solvent from the cavity onto 
the substrate area where there is not supposed to be an ink pattern. The evaporated 
traces of thiol can affect the contrast of printed pattern because at a later stage of 
etching, any traces of thiol can deter the etching of substrate material. The conse-
quence of “wet inking” of stamp is the difﬁculty to achieve high-resolution printing 
features. To overcome the above problems, there is a third inking method, as shown 
in Fig. 6.35c. It uses an ink pad which is a piece of ﬂat PDMS soaked up with thiol 
solution. The inking of stamp is done by direct contact of stamp with the inking pad. 
The amount of ink transferred onto the stamp surface is controlled by the concen-
tration of thiol in the solvent. In this way, only the pattern protrusion surfaces have 
inks. The printed ink pattern will be exactly the same dimension as the pattern 
structures on the stamp. With the contact-inking method, 100 nm gold line features 
have been produced [110]. One thing should be noticed that a PDMS stamp with 
“wet inking” can be used repeatedly without further inking (~ 10 times per inking) 
because the absorbed ink in PDMS stamp can slowly diffuse out, whereas stamps 
inked by direct contact can only be used once per inking. 
As mentioned earlier in this section, μCP cannot work without the alkanethiol 
SAM system. Two aspects of the alkanethiol SAM system make it unique in μCP: 
one is that the thiol molecules can self-assemble into a monolayer on the surface of 
coinage metals such as Au, Ag, Cu, and Pd. The monolayer-forming process is fast 
and takes place at room temperature. This is important for making a uniform ﬁlm 
with low defect density. Although PDMS stamp is elastic and can form an intimate 
contact with substrate surface, it can never be guaranteed that the ink can be 
transferred onto substrate uniformly. Particularly, if the ink molecules congregate 
into clusters once on another material surface, pinhole type of defects can be easily 
formed, rendering the ink pattern useless as an etching mask. The self-assembling of 
thiol molecules on a gold surface is fast and automatic, only needing the inked stamp 
in contact with the gold surface for a short time between 10 seconds and a minute 
[99]. Longer contact time between an inked stamp and substrate can further increase 
the density and coverage of SAM, but it also can induce diffusion of thiol molecules 
outside the boundary of stamp patterns, making the printed feature larger than 
intended. The diffusion limits the resolution of μCP technique to above 100 nm. 
Special ink such as immobilized enzyme can be used, which transfers the stamp 
pattern by catalytic reaction when in contact with acid coated surface. With enzy-
matic ink, the resolution of μCP is only limited by the range of motion of the 
immobilized catalyst, which is about 2 nm [111]. 
In addition to the self-assembling property, another unique property of thiol-
based SAM is its very high resistance to wet chemical etching. For example, gold 
surface protected by a monolayer of hexadecanethiol (HDT) is 106 times slower 
etched in cyanide (gold etchant) [112]. The two unique properties make the thiol

[Page 241]
SAM a perfect masking material. Unfortunately, the thiol SAM system only works 
with coinage metals; among them, thin ﬁlm gold is the most used material. Never-
theless, one can always use the patterned gold ﬁlm as a mask to further transfer into 
other materials. For example, μCP has been used to pattern PMMA resist layer, in 
which a 20 nm Au ﬁlm was coated on top of PMMA resist layer. After contact 
printing by a PDMS stamp, the HDT inked pattern was transferred into the Au ﬁlm. 
Then the Au ﬁlm was used as etch mask to pattern the PMMA resist layer by oxygen 
plasma etching [113]. The same concept can be applied to many other multilayer 
systems as long as the Au or Ag ﬁlm can act as a masking material in subsequent 
pattern transfer processes. 
6.6
Soft Lithography
241
6.6.3 
Replication by Capillary Force 
The unique property of PDMS also enables some other ways of replicating micro-
and nanostructures. One of the earliest reported, apart from the μCP, was 
micromolding in capillaries (MIMIC) [114]. The principle of MIMIC is quite 
simple, as schematically shown in Fig. 6.36. A PDMS stamp is brought to intimate 
contact with a ﬂat substrate. A low viscosity liquid polymer is fed into the channels 
formed by cavities in the stamp. By capillary action, the liquid polymer ﬁlls in these
(a) 
(b)                                                             (c) 
Fig. 6.36 Schematic of micromolding in capillary (MIMIC)

[Page 242]
channel cavities (Fig. 6.36a, b). A UV curing or thermal curing solidiﬁes the 
polymer inside the cavities. After peeling off the PDMS stamp, a polymer replication 
of the channel pattern is left on the substrate (Fig. 6.36c). Instead of feeding liquid 
polymer from the side of stamp, the polymer can also be coated on a ﬂat substrate 
and is heated above its Tg, while a PDMS stamp seals from the top. When the 
polymer lowers its viscosity at high temperature, it can also ﬁll up the stamp cavities 
with capillary action [115]. Alternatively, the PDMS stamp can be directly placed 
onto a freshly spin-coated polymer ﬁlm while it is still wet. Liquid polymer gets into 
the stamp cavities by capillary force, if the PDMS surface is properly treated and can 
be wetted. The PDMS stamp can absorb all the solvent contained in the polymer ﬁlm 
because of the solvent permeability of PDMS. For example, PDMS can absorb 
PGMEA (propylene glycol mono ether acetate) up to 27 wt% of its weight, and 
30 wt% of absorbed PGMEA will diffuse into air in 1 hour, which creates a partial 
vacuum inside the PDMS cavity and helps to draw in liquid polymer [116]. Other 
examples of ﬁlling mold cavity by capillary action are the J-FIL and SCIL tech-
niques which have been described previously. Analysis has shown that the capillary 
pressure is greater than 1 bar when the stamp feature size is smaller than 440 nm. The 
smaller the stamp feature, the higher the capillary pressure becomes [117].
242
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Another form of replication using capillary force is the solvent-assisted 
microcontact molding (SAMIM) [118]. The SAMIM process is illustrated in 
Fig. 6.37. A polymer ﬁlm is coated onto a substrate and baked. A patterned PDMS 
stamp is wetted with a matching solvent for the polymer and then brought into 
contact with the polymer surface. The solvent on the stamp dissolves the polymer, 
and this dissolved polymer is molded into the channels or cavities formed between 
the stamp and the substrate by capillary action. The matching solvent for PMMA is 
acetone, and for photoresists, it is ethanol. The molded polymer structure becomes 
solidiﬁed in a few minutes after evaporation of solvent, while the stamp is still in 
conformable contact with the substrate. There are two problems associated with the 
SAMIM technique. The ﬁrst is that the surface of the patterned polymer is highly 
textured because of kinetic movement of polymer molecules during solvent 
dissolving process. The other is that the pattern formed by SAMIM gets distorted. 
In particular, the base of a rectangular stripe becomes wider than the top. 
PDMS is not only permeable to solvent but also permeable to gas. The gas 
permeability coefﬁcient of PDMS is higher than other polymers. Table 6.1 compares 
permeability coefﬁcients of PDMS with two other common polymer materials for 
some typical gases. 
It is apparent that PDMS has very high air (N2 +  O2) permeability. This property 
has been utilized to replicate polymer pattern structure through PDMS molds in a 
so-called micro-aspiration assisted lithography (MAAL) technique [120]. The 
MAAL is illustrated in Fig. 6.38. It uses a double-layer mold structure. The top 
layer consists of thick PDMS (5 mm) with a matrix of channels leading to an outlet 
which is connected to a vacuum pump. The bottom layer is the actual PDMS mold 
consisting of various pattern cavities and is separated from the top layer by a thin 
PDMS membrane (20 μm). Here is the working principle of MAAL: when the 
composite mold is brought in contact with the polymer-coated ﬂat substrate

[Page 243]
Polymers
(no prebaking of spin-coated polymer), it seals off the cavities with the liquid 
polymer layer; a low vacuum (870 mbar) is created by evacuating air from the 
channels in the top PDMS layer; the pressure difference between the sealed mold 
cavities and the channels helps air permeating (“aspirating”) through separation 
PDMS membrane, resulting in a vacuum in the mold cavities; the liquid polymer 
is then drawn into the cavities by the ambient atmospheric pressure. After ﬁlling the 
mold cavities, a thermal or UV curing process can solidify the polymer, and a 
replication of polymer pattern is formed after peeling of the PDMS mold.

Soft Lithography

The mold ﬁlling process is not assisted by capillary action, but by the air 
permeability of PDMS and the pressure difference created across the PDMS mem-
brane. The time required to ﬁll the mold cavities depends directly on the air

[Page 244]
permeability coefﬁcient of PDMS and the pressure difference. If the polymer cannot 
wet the PDMS mold surface, or the PDMS surface is hydrophobic to the polymer 
liquid, then the pressure difference has to be greater than the negative capillary force 
which resists the polymer ﬂowing into the PDMS mold cavity. The polymer ﬁlling 
speed also depends on the viscosity of polymer and geometry of the mold cavities. 
Nevertheless, MAAL is a much faster process than previously described capillary 
force lithography. For the same mold area, MAAL only needs 2 minutes to ﬁll up the 
entire mold cavity while capillary force ﬁlling needs 20 minutes, providing the 
polymer is a wetting material to PDMS [120]. Then for non-wetting polymer, 
MAAL also works. Despite its advantages, MAAL may have difﬁculty to ﬁll mold 
cavity of small geometry if the PDMS surface appears to be hydrophobic to the

ﬁlling polymer, because the smaller the cavity geometry, the greater the negative 
capillary force. If the PDMS surface is hydrophilic to the ﬁlling polymer, ﬁlling can 
be accomplished even without the vacuum assistance.
6.7
Roll-to-Roll Continuous Nanoimprint
245
6.7 
Roll-to-Roll Continuous Nanoimprint 
The replication nature of nanoimprint determines that the technology is useful only if 
there is a demand for large number of same pattern structures to be made. The 
patterning is low cost only if the same patterns are replicated many times, because 
making the mold is expensive. Scientiﬁc research does not have many patterns 
which require replication in large numbers; therefore, it makes no sense to use 
nanoimprint for research. As mentioned in the Introduction (Sect. 6.1), the technol-
ogy has found applications in large scale manufacturing of photonic structures in 
recent years. The photonic structures refer to single layer periodical surface relief 
patterns at the dimension of sub-100 nm or a few hundreds of nanometers to a few 
micrometers, which generate optical effects such as antireﬂection, light guiding or 
diffusion, light extraction and propagation, holographic imaging, etc. [121]. They 
usually require large area in the form of ﬂexible thin ﬁlms, which facilitates roll to 
roll (R2R) type of continuous nanoimprint [122]. 
R2R continuous nanoimprint can be done in two different manners—ﬂat 
nanoimprint and roll nanoimprint—depending on the type of mold. In ﬂat 
nanoimprint, the mold is a ﬂat plate with surface relief structures. The substrate is 
ﬂexible plastic ﬁlm. As illustrated in Fig. 6.39a, the ﬁlm substrate is rolled in from 
the unwinding roller, and the mold is pressed down to make the imprint, and then the 
imprinted ﬁlm is rolled out by the rewinding roller. It is not a truly continuous 
process, as the mold can only be pressed in step mode. The process is often called
Fig. 6.39 (a) Flat 
nanoimprint in reel to reel 
fashion; (b) roller imprint 
using a ﬂat mold 
(a) 
(b) 
Unwinding roller 
Imprinting 
Mould
Film substrate 
Rewinding roller 
Flexible substrate 
Flat mould 
Impression roller 
Rewinding roller
Unwinding roller

[Page 246]
reel to reel process. Although the imprint can be done on a long roll of ﬁlm, the 
difﬁculties are how to achieve uniform pressure over the entire imprinted surface 
each time as the mold is pressed down and how to avoid air bubbles being trapped on 
the imprinted surface. The solutions include using an airbag to apply the pressure 
instead of mechanical rigid device and placing the imprint section in a vacuum to 
ensure no trapped air during imprinting. An option to avoid the above problems is to 
use an impression roller to press down the ﬁlm substrate to a ﬂat rigid mold, as 
shown in Fig. 6.39b. At any moment of imprinting, only a very narrow region of the 
mold is imprinted. In this way, trapping of air bubbles can be avoided and a uniform 
pressure can be achieved.
246
6
Nanoimprint Lithography
Alternatively, large area ﬂat nanoimprint can be achieved by using a ﬂexible mold 
pressed by a rigid roller onto substrate. The setup is illustrated in Fig. 6.40. Instead of 
using a rigid ﬂat mold, surface relief structures are made on a roll of ﬂexible ﬁlm 
which serves as the mold. A rigid roller is pressed down on the ﬁlm mold and rolled 
over to make the mold imprinting into the underneath substrate which in this case is a 
rigid substrate. As the imprint is only performed at the area where the rigid roller is in 
contact with the ﬂat substrate, the contact area is very small, and it is much easier to 
achieve uniform pressure, as well as much less chance to trap air inside the imprinted 
area. 
The true R2R continuous nanoimprinting is to use a rigid roller mold continu-
ously rolling over a roll of ﬂexible thin ﬁlm substrate. A simple setup of continuous 
R2R nanoimprint is illustrated by Fig. 6.41. A photo of real R2R thermal
Fig. 6.40 Flat nanoimprint

[Page 247]
nanoimprint (T-NIL) system is shown in Fig. 6.42 [123]. Flexible thin ﬁlm substrate 
is continuously fed through, while the roller mold is pressed onto the thin ﬁlm, with 
the support of supporting roller. The roller mold is equipped with heating device 
which softens the polymer coated at the surface of ﬁlm substrate, enabling thermal 
imprint into the polymer layer. This type of R2R can also be used to imprint on both 
sides of polymer ﬁlm if the supporting roller is also a roller mold [30].
6.7
Roll-to-Roll Continuous Nanoimprint
247
Fig. 6.42 Photo of R2R 
thermal nanoimprint system: 
(a) roller mold, (b) heating 
device to heat up the roller, 
(c) supporting roller, (d) thin 
ﬁlm substrate [123] 
As previously mentioned, thermal nanoimprint is not an efﬁcient process because 
of the time required for heating and cooling. The best option for R2R continuous 
nanoimprint is UV cured nanoimprint. Typical UV cured R2R nanoimprint is 
illustrated as in Fig. 6.43. It consists of four basic components: (1) unwinding and 
rewinding system to provide continuous feeding of ﬁlm substrate, (2) coating device 
to coat UV-curable liquid polymer layer onto the thin ﬁlm substrate, (3) imprinting 
roller mold which continuously rolls over the coated thin ﬁlm substrate and 
impressed into the liquid polymer layer, and (4) UV curing light source which 
illuminates the thin ﬁlm substrate from backside, assuming the thin ﬁlm substrate 
is transparent, to cure the polymer while the roller mold is still impressed in the 
polymer layer. 
There are a number of challenges in R2R continuous nanoimprint: 
1. Making the roller mould. For ﬂat mould, any previously introduced lithographic 
techniques can be used, such as optical lithography, e-beam lithography and FIB

[Page 248]
248
6
Nanoimprint Lithography

Roller mould
Polymer 
coating device 
Rewinding roller 
UV light source 
Thin film substrate 
Fig. 6.43 Setup of UV cured R2R continuous nanoimprint

(a)
(b) 
Rotate 
Electron 
beam 
Fig. 6.44 (a) Proximity photolithography of roller mold; (b) e-beam continuous exposure of 
roller mold 
direct machining. To make continuous patterns on a roller is difﬁcult. For large 
pattern structures diamond turning tools can be used to cut grooves into the roller 
surface. The minimum features which can be achieved by diamond turning are 
limited to above 10μm, unless the diamond tip is made by focused ion beam 
milling [124]. Besides, the pattern types made by diamond turning on a rotating 
roller are also limited to certain periodical features. It cannot make arbitrary 
patterns. A roller mould is usually made on a ﬂexible ﬂat substrate ﬁrst and 
then wrapped over the roller, such as the mickle sheet mould shown previously in 
Fig. 6.4. Although a ﬂat mould sheet is easily to make and can be made with high 
resolution arbitrary patterns, the drawback is that it is not seamless and there is 
always a gap when the mould sheet wraps over a roller. 
There have been a few attempts to make seamless roller mold with ﬁne 
features. The reported techniques include proximity photolithography [125], 
laser direct write [126] and e-beam lithography [127], or self-assembled pattern-
ing using anodic aluminum oxide (AAO) [128]. Figure 6.44a illustrates how 
proximity photolithography is done over the roller surface. A ﬂat photomask is 
placed within the proximity of a roller. UV light shines through a narrow slit to

[Page 249]
illuminate the mask, while the roller rotates in step motion. Line exposure is 
performed at each rotating step, and the whole surface of roller mold can be 
exposed. Microlens array has been made onto a roller mold by this method 
[125]. However, the method cannot make continuous patterns such as long 
continuous grating because of its step mode. Continuous patterns can be made 
by continuous exposure on a continuously rotating roller. Such continuous 
exposures include laser or e-beam direct write, such as shown in Fig. 6.44b. 
Seamless roller mold with 120 nm line features has been reported by e-beam 
exposure of rotated roller [129]. For seamless elastic roller mold such as those 
made in PDMS, it can be made ﬁrst in the inner surface of a cylinder with 
photoresist and internal optical lithography and then cast liquid PDMS within 
the annular space between two cylinders [130]. 
2. Large area uniform coating of polymer. Nanoimprint, whether on rigid substrates 
or ﬂexible thin ﬁlm substrates, requires uniform coating of thermally impressible 
or UV-curable polymer layer. So far in this book, only one type of coating has 
been introduced, which is the spin-coating. Spin-coating is only suitable for 
round shape and rigid substrates such as silicon wafers. Spin-coating on a square 
substrate will generate nonuniform coating at the four corners. Large area con-
tinuous coating of liquid materials is a mature industrial process. There are many 
types of coating processes that have been applied to NIL, such as rod coating, dip 
coating, doctor blade coating, air knife coating, slot die coating, gravure coating, 
screen coating, and inkjet coating [122]. The challenge is to get very thin coating 
uniformly over a large area. The minimum thickness for conventional coating 
processes is normally above 1 μm. In recent years, development of organic and 
printed electronics such as organic light emitting diodes (OLED) and organic 
photovoltaic (OPV) placed great demands on thin layer coating with precisely 
controlled thickness. Signiﬁcant progresses have been made in reducing the 
coating thickness. Both slot-die [131] and micro gravure [132] coatings can 
achieve minimum 20 nm thickness. 
3. Dynamic alignment and overlay with high accuracy. If more than one layer of 
patterns is to be made on substrate, R2R continuous nanoimprint will require 
dynamic alignment and overlay, which is much hard than static alignment and 
overlay. The usual method is to capture the alignment marks on a continuous 
rolling substrate with a camera. Any deviations of substrate from roller mold will 
feedback to winding system to adjust the rolling speed and to roller mold control 
to ﬁnely adjust its lateral position. This can place great demands on the system 
control, which is not easy to achieve. Presently, the best dynamic overlay 
accuracy on continuous R2R nanoimprint is around 10 μm, which has severely 
restricted the overall patterning accuracy of nanoimprint.
6.7
Roll-to-Roll Continuous Nanoimprint
249
Due to the aforementioned challenges, nanoimprint in R2R continuous mode is 
hardly in nanoscale regime. Although R2R imprinted 100 nm features have been 
reported in laboratory [133], such patterning dimension has not been realized at 
industrial scale. Fortunately, most photonic applications do not require very small

[Page 250]
feature dimensions and do not require overlay alignment, for which the current R2R 
continuous imprint processes and technologies have been adequate and played 
important roles [122].

Chapter 7 
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching 
7.1 
Introduction 
The lithography process is not ﬁnished until a functional pattern structure has been 
made on or into a substrate material. There are of course many ways of making a 
pattern directly out of a material. Some of them have already been introduced in 
previous chapters. For example, focused ion beam can directly sputter etching a 
nanoscale pattern into a substrate material, or make a structure on top of a substrate 
by gas assisted deposition of a material, as described in Chap. 4. Electron beam, 
though not able to sputter etching, can also deposit materials with gases, as described 
in Chap. 3. Both of the techniques do not need a mask, because the charged particle 
beams can be focused and scanned into any patterns. The same is true for patterning 
by a scanning probe. Etching or deposition of materials can be done by a probe 
interacting with a substrate, which has been explained in detail in Chap. 5. However, 
the majority of nanofabrications involve lithographic patterning of polymer resists. 
Generally, the polymeric patterns have no other functions except serving as a mask. 
The patterns in the mask have to be transferred to underlying layers to form 
functional structures. 
There are many ways of transferring a polymer resist pattern into a substrate. 
These processing techniques can be grouped into either subtractive processes or 
additive subtractive processes, i.e., removing underlying material from the openings 
of polymer resist mask or adding a new material through the openings of polymer 
resist mask. This chapter will introduce the subtractive pattern transfer techniques. 
The additive pattern transfer will be introduced in Chap. 8. Figure 7.1 illustrates the 
subtractive pattern transfer processes. Figure 7.1a is pattern transferring to a thin ﬁlm 
deposited on a substrate by etching through the resist mask opening, whereas 
Fig. 7.1b shows pattern transferring directly into a substrate by etching through the 
resist mask openings. 
Subtractive pattern transfer is mostly characterized by various etching techniques, 
whether wet or dry etching. In wet etching processes, the etchants are liquid form of
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_7
257

[Page 258]
chemicals. In dry etching, the etchants are gas form of chemicals or in some cases are 
energized ions. Lithography and etching are the two most important processes which 
go hand in hand in most micro- and nanofabrication processes. The main character of 
IC manufacturing is the repeated processes of lithography and etching, which leads 
to the buildup of multilevel circuit pattern layouts in silicon. Making any other 
micro- and nanostructures, as long as processing by planar technology, cannot 
escape one form or another of lithography and etching processes. This chapter will 
only focus on the etching techniques that enable pattern transfer at sub-100 nm 
dimension.
258
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
(a)
(b) 
Fig. 7.1 Schematic of subtractive pattern transfer processes: (a) etching into a thin ﬁlm; (b) etching 
into substrate 
Regarding etching, no matter wet or dry, there are two key parameters which 
characterize all the etching techniques. 
(i) Selectivity. Etching is a process of removing substrate material or thin ﬁlm 
material on a substrate through mask openings. The mask can be a polymer 
resist or another thin ﬁlm material such as silicon dioxide and silicon nitride. 
The thin ﬁlm mask is also made by lithography and etching. Selectivity is the 
ratio of etching rate for substrate or thin ﬁlm over-etching rate for the mask. 
High selectivity means there is little loss of masking material and the mask can 
endure long etching in an etchant. 
(ii) Anisotropy. The purpose of etching is to transfer the pattern in mask into the 
material underneath the mask. Ideally, etching should transfer the exact dimen-
sion of mask pattern. In practice, there are always distortions, or lateral etching 
happens. The lateral etching can be caused by excessive etching of masking 
layer, which is to do with the aforementioned selectivity issue; the lateral 
etching can also be the result of lateral etching of the underneath material 
whether it is the substrate or the thin ﬁlm on a substrate. The degree of lateral 
etching is characterized by anisotropy. Figure 7.2 illustrates three different

[Page 259]
anisotropies in etching. It ranges from completely non-anisotropic or called 
isotropic etching to partially anisotropic etching and to completely anisotropic 
etching. In general, anisotropic etching transfers mask pattern in its exact 
dimension into a substrate. However, there are exceptions that anisotropic 
etching may not follow the mask pattern boundary, such as the alkaline-based 
wet chemical etching of crystalline silicon. On the other hand, isotropic etching 
may still be able to transfer the exact dimension of a mask pattern, if the etching 
depth is extremely shallow. 
7.2
Wet Chemical Etching
259
Fig. 7.2 Schematic of anisotropy of etching: (a) isotropic, (b) partially anisotropic, (c) completely 
anisotropic 
Ideally, a mask pattern should be faithfully transferred into another material 
without any distortions. In practice, this is a difﬁcult task. Apart from aforemen-
tioned anisotropy issue, each pattern transfer technique has its own capability and 
limitation, particularly when dealing with sub-100 nm pattern transfer. Some of the 
pattern transfer techniques are inherently not able to replicate the dimension of mask 
patterns. Even those techniques which are capable of nanoscale pattern transfer are 
still highly dependent on various process conditions to achieve desired accuracy and 
structure proﬁle. The principles of these techniques are described here, and particular 
attentions are drawn to the conditions at which nanoscale pattern transfer becomes 
possible. 
7.2 
Wet Chemical Etching 
7.2.1 
Wet Isotropic Etching 
Wet chemical etching is generally isotropic, i.e., etching progresses in depth as well 
as in lateral direction. Even though there is a mask, liquid etchant can penetrate 
underneath the mask, resulting in the etched pattern larger than the mask pattern, as 
illustrated in Fig. 7.2a. For this reason, wet chemical etching is normally not used for

[Page 260]
high resolution pattern transfer. A typical example is the fabrication of photomasks. 
Photomasks for above 0.25 μm IC technology are made by wet acidic etching of 
chrome layer using a patterned polymer resist as masking layer. For below 0.25 μm 
IC technology, various resolution enhancement techniques are used, which include 
phase-shifting masks (PSM) and optical proximity correction (OPC) masks. PSM 
and OPC masks both contain sub-resolution pattern features which are in the order of 
100 nm. Wet chemical etching is no longer capable of transferring these 
sub-resolution features faithfully into the chrome layer. Reactive ion etching (RIE) 
has to be used. 
260
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
However, the etching in lateral direction is only as fast as in the depth direction. If 
the etch depth is very small, the lateral etching can be negligible. Therefore, high 
resolution pattern transfer is still possible. Wet chemical etching has indeed found its 
way into sub-100 nm pattern transfer applications when self-assembled monolayer 
(SAM) is used as a resist. Not all SAMs are qualiﬁed as a resist for nanoscale pattern 
transfer. The only SAM that has found applications in nanolithography is the family 
of alkanethiol SAM systems. Patterning of SAM can be done by microcontact 
printing (μCP), as described in Chap. 6, or by a scanning probe (dip-pen 
nanolithography) as described in Chap. 5. The layer thickness of a SAM pattern is 
only 1–2 nm. Transfer of SAM pattern has to be carried out in wet chemical etching 
for two reasons: one is that the SAM is so thin that it cannot withstand ion 
bombardment in a RIE process; the other is that there are no effective RIE processes 
suitable for etching coinage metals. To realize sub-100 nm scale pattern transfer in 
wet chemical etching, one needs to make the metal ﬁlm extremely thin. 20 nm lines 
have been produced in 10-nm-thick gold ﬁlm using wet chemical etching of the gold 
ﬁlm [1]. Although only coinage metals can be patterned using the SAM resist as 
mask, the pattern can be further transferred into other materials using the patterned 
metallic ﬁlm as a mask. For example, various silicon structures have been generated 
by anisotropic etching of silicon with patterned gold or silver as the etching 
masks [2]. 
Wet chemical etching is inherently isotropic and generally carried out in acidic 
solutions. For isotropic etching of silicon, the most widely used acidic etchant is the 
mixture of HF, HNO3, and acetic acid, commonly called HNA etchant. The mech-
anism of etching silicon by HNA is that HNO3 ﬁrst oxidizes silicon and HF then 
dissolves the silicon dioxide. Pure HF acid has almost no etching on silicon. Acetic 
acid here functions as a buffer solution and can be replaced by water. The etch rate 
depends on the concentration ratio of each of the acids and is represented by a three-
phase diagram [3]. HNA has been used as standard etchant in semiconductor 
industry since the 1960s, mostly for cleaning of silicon wafers. High etch rate is 
not needed in most silicon IC production processes. Silicon dioxide is another 
important material and can be isotropically etched. The etchant for silicon dioxide 
etching is mainly HF acid. HF without dilution (49% weight) has etching rate up to 
1.8 μm/min. In practice, buffered HF is often used. Buffered oxide etchant (BOE) 
consists of seven parts of NH4F and one part of HF (49% weight) and has etch rate of 
70 nm/min for LPCVD oxide. The higher proportion of buffer solution, the lower the 
oxide etch rate.

[Page 261]
7.2
Wet Chemical Etching
261
When carrying out wet etching with polymer resist mask, one should be aware of 
the residual resist issue. Lithographically patterned and wet developed polymer resist 
would normally leave a thin layer (a few nanometers’ thick) of polymer residues on 
substrate surface. Polymers which are mainly hydrocarbons are generally resistant to 
acidic solutions. Even a very thin layer of polymer resist residue (scum) can stop the 
wet etching of substrate. Therefore, a descum process usually follows the develop-
ment of resist pattern before proceeding to acidic etching of substrate. Descum is 
normally done by a short oxygen plasma etching of developed resist pattern, 
assuming that the residue layer is very thin. For example, of the wet etching pattern 
transfer in photomask making process, there is always a descum step before the wet 
etching of chrome layer. The descum etching by oxygen plasma can, however, affect 
the resist pattern proﬁle. So it has to be brief, usually less than 30 seconds at low 
plasma density. Another caution is the effect of descum on substrate surface. In 
principle, the descum etching should not damage the underlying thin ﬁlm or sub-
strate surface. 
7.2.2 
Wet Anisotropic Etching 
Although wet chemical etching in general is isotropic, an exception is silicon etching 
by alkaline-based etchant, which is dependent on crystalline orientation; therefore, it 
is anisotropic. The anisotropy comes from the fact that alkaline dissolves silicon in 
different rates at different crystalline planes. The most commonly encountered 
crystalline planes of silicon wafers are <100>, <111>, and < 110>, which are 
illustrated in Fig. 7.3. The etch rate decreases in the order of <110>, <100>, 
and < 111> for the reason that they have different density of atoms on these planes. 
The etch rate in <110> plane is twice higher than the <100> plane, and they both 
are hundreds of times higher than the <111> plane. Typically, the ratio of etch rates 
for the three crystalline planes <110>/<100>/<111 > are 400/200/1 [4]. Because 
different crystalline planes intersect in different angles, the etch proﬁle of silicon in 
alkaline etchant can be quite complicated. For example, etching of <100> oriented 
silicon wafer proceeds fast in <100> direction but slow in <111> direction.
Fig. 7.3 Three main lattice structures of single crystal silicon

[Page 262]
Because the <111> plane intersects <100> plane at an angle of 54.74°, the etched 
proﬁle is cone-shaped with the slope of 54.74°. For limited thickness of silicon 
wafer, the eventual hole etched through the silicon wafer will have large opening at 
the top and small opening at the bottom, as illustrated in Fig. 7.4. The etch depth and 
opening can be calculated by Eq. 7.1.

of single crystal silicon at 
<100> plane
