Chemical etching in <110> direction is also anisotropic. The etched proﬁle in 
<110> direction is more complicated because <111> and <110> intersect in 
different angles from different directions. Figure 7.5 illustrates the proﬁle etched at 
<110> plane. There are eight <111> planes which intersect with <110> plane. 
Four of them are perpendicular to <110>, and the other four intersect with <110> 
plane in 35.26° angle. The eight <111> planes themselves are not perpendicular to 
each other but with angles. Therefore, the etched structures from <110> plane have 
some sidewalls vertical but some are not. For the <100> oriented silicon wafer, 
there are four <111> planes intersecting with <100> plane in 54.74° angle. The 
four <111> planes themselves are vertical to each other. Etching through a square

[Page 263]
or rectangular mask opening will result in a square or rectangular opening in the 
<100> plane. However, this is not so for <110> plane. A square or rectangular 
mask opening does not produce a square or rectangular etched hole in <110> 
oriented silicon wafer. 
7.2
Wet Chemical Etching
263
Fig. 7.6 Computer simulated alkaline etching of <110> silicon wafer with a rectangular mask 
pattern in different orientations: (a) perfectly aligned mask, (b) slightly misaligned mask, (c) largely 
misaligned mask 
The dependence on crystal planes highlights a critical issue in wet chemical 
etching of silicon, i.e., the alignment of etch mask with crystalline axis. 
Misalignment of mask pattern edge with crystal axis will result in the etched edge 
following the crystal axis, not the mask pattern edge. Figure 7.6 is a group of 
simulated examples. The simulation was done using the AnisE software which is a 
part of IntelliSuite software package [5]. The silicon substrate is <110> oriented. 
The mask pattern is a rectangular opening. Figure 7.6a, b, c are simulated etched 
structures for three different alignments of the mask with <110> direction. Only in 
Fig. 7.6a the mask is perfectly aligned to crystal axis, which results in vertical 
sidewalls along the mask edge. Figure 7.7 is the experimental proof of such 
misalignment effect. Figure 7.7a is the etched trench from misaligned mask, where 
roughness and slanting of sidewall along the trench are apparent. Figure 7.7b is the 
etched trench when mask is accurately aligned to <110> axis. The sidewall along 
the trench is smooth and vertical. Recently, a report showed that by aligning the 
edges of patterned holes to the <111> plane of Si <110> wafer, the KOH wet etch 
could eliminate the scalloping roughness of sidewall while avoiding measurable 
lateral etching to the walls [6]. 
In order to achieve accurate alignment, one must ﬁrst be aware of the crystalline 
orientation of a silicon wafer. The commercial silicon wafers come in two crystal 
planes, <100> plane and < 111> plane. They also have n-type doping and p-type 
doping. The standard markings of crystal plane and doping type are shown in 
Fig. 7.8. The <100> oriented wafer means the <100> axis is perpendicular to the 
wafer surface, whereas the <110> axis is parallel to the primary cut ﬂat. When 
aligning a mask, one should make sure the edge of square patterns is aligned to the 
primary cut ﬂat. The marking by wafer vendors is not always accurate. It is better to 
measure the crystal plane orientation experimentally. For example, one can carry out 
etching of a series of trenches along different directions and ﬁnd the trench which

[Page 264]
best aligned to the crystal axis and use the trench as the mark for mask alignment. 
This method can minimize the misalignment to less than 0.01° [7]. 
264
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.7 Experimental results of etching in <110> plane: (a) slightly misaligned mask, (b) 
perfectly aligned mask 
Fig. 7.8 Standard markings 
of silicon wafer orientation 
and doping type 
There are three common alkaline etchants for wet chemical etching of silicon, the 
KOH (potassium hydroxide), EDP (ethylenediamine pyrocatechol), and TMAH 
(trimethyl ammonium hydroxide). For etching large structures, all the three etchants 
are ﬁne. There are some differences in selectivity and etch rate. For example, KOH

[Page 265]
has higher silicon etch rate than the other two etchants, but also higher etch rate to 
oxide. More importantly, KOH etching of silicon creates rough surface, which is 
highly detrimental when the etched structure itself is at sub-100 nm dimension. EDP 
has lower oxide etch rate and is less anisotropic because it etches <111> plane faster 
than KOH does, but it is very toxic and special caution has to be taken when using 
it. TMAH is the best suitable alkaline etchant for nanoscale etching of silicon 
because the etched surface is ten times smoother than etching by KOH. 40 nm 
deep grooves with 50 nm width and 30 nm high ridge with 40 nm width have been 
fabricated on Si<100> surface with TMAH etchant [8]. The other option is to use a 
mixture of KOH and IPA (isopropyl alcohol), which can also signiﬁcantly reduce the 
surface roughness [9]. The additional beneﬁt of using TMAH is that it does not 
contain alkaline metals like the KOH. KOH is not compatible with semiconductor 
fabrication due to the alkaline metal potassium which can threaten transistor gate 
oxide reliability. 
7.2
Wet Chemical Etching
265
Alkaline etching of silicon is a bulk silicon micromachining process which is 
generally used to create large structures of tens or hundreds of micrometers. With 
a < 110> silicon wafer, the technique can be employed for nanoscale pattern 
transfer as long as the pattern edges are aligned to certain crystalline planes and 
the etch depth is limited. Line patterns less than 40 nm have been transferred into 
silicon by alkaline wet etching on <110> silicon wafer, which creates vertical 
sidewall proﬁle with more than 5:1 aspect ratio and nearly atomic smooth sidewall 
surface [10]. In conventional bulk micromachining of silicon, large quantity of 
silicon has to be etched away. A good masking material with high selectivity is 
necessary. Silicon nitride is preferred because it has higher etching resistance in 
alkaline etchant than silicon dioxide. For nanoscale wet etching of silicon, the 
selectivity of masking material is less of an issue because the etch depth is so 
small. Oxide mask is generally used because an oxide mask pattern can be directly 
generated by local oxidation using an AFM scanning probe [11]. Otherwise, an 
oxide mask pattern is generated by electron beam lithography of PMMA resist and 
subsequent isotropic etching by buffered HF or conventional reactive ion etching 
process [9]. Only a very thin oxide masking layer is needed for such nanoscale 
etching. 
7.2.3 
Metal Assisted Anisotropic Etching 
Metal assisted chemical etching (MACE) is a simple process to achieve anisotropic 
etching of silicon by utilizing some noble metals as catalysts. A thin layer of noble 
metal (Au, Pt, or Au/Pd alloy) sputtered on the surface of a Si substrate catalyzed the 
etching of Si in a mixed solution containing HF and H2O2. The Si beneath the noble 
metal is etched much faster than the Si without noble metal coverage. As a result, the 
noble metal sinks into the Si substrate, generating pores in the Si substrate. The 
detailed geometries of the resulting Si structures depend mostly on the initial 
morphology of the noble metal coverage [12]. The process is illustrated in

[Page 266]
Fig. 7.9a, b where initial metal discs on the Si surface sunk into the Si to form pores. 
If the metal covers the silicon surface with holes, the end result of MACE is pillars 
standing in the etched pit, as illustrated in Fig. 7.9c, d [13]. 
266
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.9 Schematic of 
metal assisted chemical 
etching process: (a) initial 
metal discs; (b) etched pores 
with the metal discs at the 
bottom; (c) initial metal ﬁlm 
with holes; (d) etched pit 
with standing silicon pillars 
[13] 
Fig. 7.10 Patterned silicon 
pillars by MACE [14] 
It is generally considered that the noble metals act as catalysts to dissociate the 
H2O2 into oxidants which oxidize the silicon and then the silicon oxide is dissolved 
in HF acid. Once the silicon oxide is removed, the fresh surface of silicon undergoes 
new oxidation and etching. The cycle repeats, and the silicon underneath the noble 
metal is etched away to form deep pores. In this case, the metal ﬁlm acts as a mask 
with negative tone, i.e., silicon is etched where there is a metal pattern. A metal 
pattern can be formed in many ways. For example, Fig. 7.10 shows silicon pillars

[Page 267]
made by MACE, which used self-assembled polystyrene (PS) spheres as mask to 
pattern silver (Ag) ﬁlm into dots array and used the Ag dots array as masks to etch 
silicon [14]. Nanoscale silicon structures can be fabricated by MACE with nanoscale 
noble metal patterns. One of reported work showed vertical silicon nanowires with 
individual nanowires at 20 nm diameter and height of 4400 nm [15]. Another work 
demonstrated 25 nm lines with 25 nm spacing were etched in silicon to a depth of 
1.26 μm, which has an aspect ratio of ~50:1 [16]. In addition to the well-known 
noble metal catalysts, such as Ag, Au, and Pt, carbon-based catalysts, such as 
graphene, graphene oxide, and carbon nanotubes, have been successfully applied 
as catalysts for MACE, making the process CMOS-compatible. The MACE has also 
been applied to semiconductors other than Si to fabricate various nanostructures, 
such as Ge, GaAs, AlGaAs, GaN, GaP, InP, SiC, and Ga2O3 [17]. 
7.3
Reactive Ion Etching (RIE)
267
However, as a wet etching technique, MACE suffers from stiction and agglom-
eration of structures during drying due to van der Waals and capillary forces between 
adjacent surfaces of dense etched structures. Post etching drying steps, such as CO2 
based critical point drying, are usually required. For this reason, gas phase MACE 
has been developed. The gas phase of etchant can be created by a vapor of water-
diluted hydroﬂuoric acid and a continuous airﬂow, which works both as an oxidizer 
and as a gas carrier for reactive species. The etching of very deep (up to 107 μm) 
nanostructures (vertically aligned nanowires with diameter in the range of 10~100 
nm) was achieved in the Si <100> direction with very high aspect ratio (up to 
10,000:1) and high etching rate (up to 25 μm/h) [18]. 
7.3 
Reactive Ion Etching (RIE) 
Reactive ion etching (RIE) is the mostly used pattern transfer technique in 
microfabrication and nanofabrication. The most distinctive feature of RIE is that 
the etching takes place in plasma [19]. There are no wet chemicals involved. 
Therefore, the other name for RIE is dry etching, though dry etching includes 
much broad range of gas phase etching techniques and RIE is only one of them [20]. 
7.3.1 
Principle of RIE 
RIE involves both physical and chemical processes. The physical process includes 
ionization of reactive gas molecules and ion sputtering of sample surface, both of 
which take place in a plasma. Plasma can be loosely deﬁned as partially ionized 
gases. In a conﬁned space such as a vacuum chamber, the ionized particles collec-
tively set up a long range electrostatic ﬁeld which has important inﬂuence on the 
behaviors of these charged particles. The electric ﬁeld is created because electrons in 
the plasma tend to move much faster than ions. The fast moving electrons hit the 
chamber wall before ions do, causing the wall to charge up negatively. The

[Page 268]
negatively charged wall surface pushes other electrons away from the wall, creating 
a layer of positively charged region where an electrical ﬁeld is created to accelerate 
the ions toward the wall surface [21]. 
268
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.11 Planar electrode 
plasma system and potential 
distribution 
In a practical RIE system with planar electrode conﬁguration, as schematically 
shown in Fig. 7.11, one of the electrodes is the cathode which is connected to radio 
frequency (RF) power source by capacitance coupling. The other electrode is the 
anode which is grounded, including the reaction chamber walls. The RF power 
source provides a high frequency electrical ﬁeld between the anode and cathode, 
which accelerates and resonates a small number of free electrons. These electrons 
acquiring enough kinetic energy in the RF ﬁeld will collide with gas molecules and 
ionize the gas molecules. More free electrons become available and more gas 
molecules are ionized, leading to an avalanche of free electrons and gaseous ions. 
The gas ﬁlled space between cathode and anode changes from initially an insulator 
to a conductor. Electrical current ﬂows through the space between the two elec-
trodes. The electrical potential distribution between cathode and anode is shown in 
Fig. 7.11. A large part of the region is equipotential region, which is also called glow 
region where electrons and ions recombine to form molecules. The gas molecules 
returning from excitation state to normal state through recombination process release 
their energy in the form of photons. A glowing light is seen in the plasma chamber. 
Local electrical ﬁelds near the cathode and anode are formed because of the fast 
moving electrons and charging of the electrode surface. The region near the cathode 
which is not grounded establishes higher ﬁeld than that of the anode, which 
accelerates ions toward the cathode. A sample placed on the cathode electrode will 
experience bombardment of high energy ions. 
Although there is an electrical ﬁeld which accelerates ions toward the sample 
surface, the physical sputtering is not a dominant phenomenon in RIE. The dominant

[Page 269]
W
etching process is caused by chemical reaction. Most RIE processes use 
halogen-containing gases, such as ﬂuorine (F), chlorine (Cl), bromine (Br), and 
iodine (I), to generate the plasma, because they are the most chemically reactive 
and erosive gases to various inorganic materials. Fluorine is widely used for etching 
silicon and silicon compounds. Chlorine is mainly used for etching Ga-, Al-, and 
As-containing III–V compounds. For InP and other In-containing compounds such 
as InGaAs and GaInAsP, the volatility of etched product in chlorine plasma at room 
temperature is low. Either the substrate has to be heated to more than 150 °C or  
bromine- or iodine-containing gases are used (but such gases tend to attack the 
vacuum system aggressively) [22]. There are, however, cases that non-halogen-
containing gases (hydrogen or hydrogen/methane mixture) were used to etch com-
pound semiconductors such as GaAs and InP [23].

Reactive Ion Etching (RIE)

The halogen compounds that can be used in RIE and the materials which can be 
etched are listed in Table 7.1 [24]. One can notice that for etching of some of the 
materials, there are several choices of etching gases. The gases listed in Table 7.1 
only serves as references. There can be many more choices. Each gas has its own 
advantages and drawbacks. The choice of a particular etching gas is very much 
dependent on the requirements of a speciﬁc application. For example, ﬂuorine 
compounds and chlorine gas or compounds can both be used for etching silicon. F 
atoms react with Si spontaneously, and they induce isotropic etching and cause 
undercutting below the mask. Cl2 etching is anisotropic since it is an ion assisted 
process rather than a spontaneous etching process. However, Cl2 etching has much 
lower etch rate and poorer selectivity than ﬂuorine based etching [25]. 
The compound forms of halogen gas, though chemically erosive, are rarely able 
to etch a material or to etch at very low rate. They only become chemically reactive 
and erosive when these halogen atoms are dissociated from the compounds or lose 
their electrons to become free radicals. Here the energetic ions play an important role 
to produce the radicals. An interesting experiment demonstrated that when silicon 
surface was exposed to XeF2 gas or Ar+ beam alone, the silicon was removed at less

[Page 270]
than 1 nm/min. However, if the XeF2 gas and Ar+ beam were both present, the etch 
rate jumped to 6 nm/min [22]. The RIE process is the action of four simultaneous 
processes, as schematically shown in Fig. 7.12 [22]: 
(a) The ions sputter the substrate surface, which helps initially to remove any 
hydrocarbon contaminants and native oxide layer to create a fresh material 
surface, promoting absorption of gas molecules onto the material surface. Ion 
sputtering can also remove material atoms as purely physical sputtering etching. 
(b) The ions, if they are reactive species, can directly react with material atoms to 
form volatile compounds which escape from the substrate surface. 
(c) The ions can dissociate the absorbed gas molecules to produce radicals on the 
substrate surface. 
(d) The radicals can move around at the substrate surface and react with the material 
surface atoms to form volatile compounds leaving the surface. All the volatile 
compounds are pumped away, so that no redeposition occurs during the etching 
process. 
270
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.12 Schematic of RIE 
process: (a) ion sputtering; 
(b) reactive etching; (c) 
radical formation; (d) 
radical etching 
Substrate

(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)

Gas

Radical 
It is clear that there are three factors that lead to the RIE: the energized ions, the 
reactive gas, and the formation of volatile compounds. First, if there are only 
energized ions without reactive gas, for example, of etching in pure Ar plasma, 
then the etching is pure physical sputtering. Ion milling is a typical sputter etching 
technique conducted in argon plasma. Second, if there are only reactive gas mole-
cules without energized ions, the radicals are only generated in plasma not on the 
surface. The etching is a pure chemical reaction. For example, if the sample is placed 
on the anode shown in Fig. 7.11, there is little electrical ﬁeld to accelerate the ions. 
Or there are no electrodes at all, such as in a barrel etcher, the ion bombardment of 
surface is negligible. The etching is isotropic and similar to aforementioned wet 
chemical etching. The third factor is the volatility of etched products. If there are no

[Page 271]
volatile compounds formed during etching, the removed material by etching will 
redeposit somewhere else in the vicinity of etched pattern. 
7.3
Reactive Ion Etching (RIE)
271
The volatility of halogen compounds can be characterized by their boiling 
temperature. The boiling temperatures of halogen compounds for some most com-
monly encountered elements in micro- and nanostructure fabrication are listed in 
Table 7.2. [26]. The data in Table 7.2 can serve as a guideline for selecting 
appropriate halogen gas when etching a particular material. The general trend is 
that when going from ﬂuorine to chlorine and bromine chemistry, etch rates decrease 
and etching needs more ion assistance. An example has already been described 
previously about etching silicon with ﬂuorine and chlorine gases. Fluorine plasma 
etches silicon faster but is less anisotropic. Chlorine plasma etches silicon slower but 
is more anisotropic. This behavior, which is in favor of an easier proﬁle control, is in 
part due to the reduction of the etch product volatility. For compound materials such 
as GaAs, there is generally not one etch product for the compound material, but one 
etch product per constituent, each having its own volatility. This complicates the 
choice of the chemistry and has strong effects on the etching mechanisms and on the 
chemical composition of the etched surface. To increase the volatility of those etched 
products with high boiling temperatures, the sample has to be heated to high 
temperature. In some cases, adding hydrogen gas into plasma works better because 
some hydrides have much higher volatility than the halogen compounds, as shown in 
Table 7.2. 
7.3.2 
Process Control in RIE 
RIE is a complex process. The above description is only a qualitative and simplistic 
description of what is happening inside the plasma etching chamber. So far there are 
no theories that can satisfactorily describe the complete RIE process, though there 
have been various attempts to establish theoretical models of plasma etching pro-
cesses [21]. Carrying out pattern transfer by RIE is highly experimental and empir-
ical. There are RIE parameters which can be externally controlled, such as gas ﬂow 
rate, RF power, chamber pressure, substrate temperature, substrate bias voltage, etc. 
There are also measurable indicators that indicate the quality of etching, such as the 
aforementioned etching selectivity and anisotropy, as well as etch rate and etch 
uniformity. The control of an RIE process is to adjust these parameters experimen-
tally to match them with a desirable outcome from the above indicators. Though 
theoretically predicting a plasma etching process is difﬁcult, there are some broad 
guidelines with which a user should be able to anticipate what may happen when 
certain process parameters are changed. These guidelines are brieﬂy described in the 
following: 
(1) Flow rate of reactive gas 
The basic process in RIE is gaseous ions reacting with substrate surface and 
converting the surface material into volatile products. The reaction rate is directly

linked to the supply rate of reactive gases. However, fast ﬂow rate of reactive gases 
at a constant pressure can lead to short dwelling time of gas molecules in reaction 
chamber, because the constant pressure is maintained by high pumping speed. If 
plasma discharge power is not increased accordingly, the number of gaseous ions 
generated in reaction chamber will decrease, resulting in reduction of etching rate. 
On the other hand, if the gas ﬂow rate is too low, the consumed reactive gases cannot 
be replenished sufﬁciently, which again can lead to drop of etching rate.

(2) RF power 
Increasing RF power will increase electron energy, hence increasing ionization 
probability. Therefore, etching rate generally increases with the increase of RF 
power, particularly when gas ﬂow rate has increased or chamber pressure has 
increased. As explained earlier, high energy electrons not only generate more ions 
but also build up a high negative ﬁeld near the cathode surface, called self-bias ﬁeld, 
which accelerates ions toward cathode. The potential difference at cathode region is 
200–500 volts. Accelerated ions bombarding cathode (platen) where the sample is 
situated can also increase the etching rate. In addition, the anisotropy of etching can 
be improved because ion sputtering is highly directional and almost has no lateral 
etching. However, ion sputtering is a physical process, irrespective of mask or 
substrate. Too much of ion sputtering will speed up the consumption of masking 
layer and reduce etching selectivity. Figure 7.13 shows the Si etch rate and selec-
tivity as functions of platen power which represents the energy of bombarding ions 
in an induction coupling plasma etching system [23]. Although high platen power is 
favored for high etch rate, the etch selectivity suffers at the high platen power. 
(3) Chamber pressure 
RIE is normally taking place at low pressure condition. The chamber vacuum is 
usually at 10-3 ~  10-1 Torr. At low pressure, the density of gas molecule is low in 
the chamber. Electrons have longer mean free path and can acquire more accelera-
tion energy between two collisions with gas molecules, which can increase

[Page 274]
ionization probability. Another beneﬁt of low chamber pressure is less lateral etching 
and improvement of anisotropy. This is due to fewer collisions between ions and gas 
molecules, resulting in more directional movement of ions toward substrate. Low 
chamber pressure also means high pumping speed, which helps to quickly move 
away the volatile products from etched surface. The reaction rate is improved, hence 
the etching rate. In general, at low chamber pressure, RIE is dominated by ion 
sputtering etching, because chemically active gaseous molecules are less and ion 
energy is higher. At high chamber pressure, the etching is dominated by chemical 
reaction, because the mean free path of ions is reduced and the number of chemically 
active ions is large. At higher pressure, the etching is more isotropic. However, if the 
pressure is too low, the plasma cannot start or sustain. There exists an optimum 
pressure at which both etch rate and selectivity are maximum, as an example shown 
in Fig. 7.14, where both Si etch rate and selectivity are decreased when chamber 
pressure is too low or too high [23].

(4) Substrate temperature 
In general, high temperature can promote chemical reaction and also help to 
remove reacted products from substrate surface. As described earlier, etching of 
indium-containing III–V semiconductor compounds with chlorine plasma requires 
high substrate temperature, because of the low volatility of indium-chlorine com-
pounds. In RIE, ion bombardment of substrate will naturally cause the rise of 
substrate temperature. The substrate surface temperature can reach 100–200 °C, 
depending on the duration of etching. However, high substrate temperature can 
soften masking material if it is photoresist, leading to pattern distortion and higher 
photoresist etch rate. Using other inorganic materials instead of photoresist as mask 
will avoid the problem. Another effect of high substrate temperature is the increase 
of lateral etching, because the mobility of radicals is enhanced at high surface 
temperature and hence the chemical etching which is by nature an isotropic etching. 
For long duration of etching, the sample holder has to be cooled to reduce the 
substrate temperature.

[Page 275]

(5) Gas additives 
Although halogen compounds are the main gases for etching inorganic and 
metallic materials, additives are often used to enhance some aspect of the chemistry. 
For example, the addition of 10% O2 to CF4 increases the etch rate by ten times. In 
the plasma, CF4 is broken down into radicals CF3 and CF2. In the absence of oxygen, 
these radicals can recombine (both in the gas phase and on surfaces) into stable 
molecules, e.g., 
CF3 þ CF3 →C2F6 and CF3 þ F →CF4 
With oxygen present, it can react with the radicals to form CO, CO2, and COF, 
thus releasing ﬂuorine radicals: 
CF2 þ O2 →COF þ F, COF þ O →CO2 þ F 
Figure 7.15 shows the Si etch rate and selectivity as the functions of ﬂow rate of 
O2 additive [23]. Addition of oxygen has clearly enhanced the silicon etch rate. 
However, the selectivity is substantially reduced because oxygen is an effective 
etching gas to etch polymer resists which is the masking material in this case. 
Nevertheless, 
addition 
of 
oxygen 
also 
brings 
the 
beneﬁt 
of 
eliminating 
micromasking effect and helps to produce smooth etched surface. 
Apart from oxygen gas, argon gas is often added into plasma. While Ar + can 
participate in physical sputter etching, they can also help to stabilize the plasma. For 
example, SF6 is an electronegative gas. It absorbs electrons upon ionization, which 
may reduce the total number of electrons in the plasma. The addition of argon can 
add electrons to the plasma when they are ionized and so helps to produce a stable 
plasma [22]. However, adding too much argon can dilute the concentration of 
reactive gas and also cause excessive physical sputtering, which leads to reduction 
of both etch rate and selectivity.

[Page 276]
276
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
On the one hand, RIE is a complex process and is inﬂuenced by many parameters. 
On the other hand, there are more degrees of freedom to control the process 
conditions and to optimize. It is possible to ﬁne-tune process parameters to reach a 
compromise between etch rate and selectivity and to achieve a particular etch proﬁle. 
Figure 7.16a–c are three different proﬁles of etched silicon tips. Although the basic 
etching chemistry is based on SF6 plasma, different shapes of etch proﬁle can be ﬁne 
controlled by adding CHF3, O2, and Ar gases [27]. The sharp end of these silicon tips 
can be controlled to less than 50 nm in diameter. 
The control of anisotropy in RIE is tricky because etching by halogen radicals in 
plasma is nondirectional. It causes the etching to advance both in depth and laterally, 
resulting in the pyramid shape of etched proﬁle as shown in Fig. 7.16. Only the ion 
bombardment of substrate is directional. However, halogen radical etching is very 
sensitive to polymers. A thin layer of polymer or carbon compounds can stop the 
etching. A rough guide to control the anisotropy of RIE is to ﬁnd a way to create a 
passivation ﬁlm on the etched sidewall to stop halogen radicals etching laterally, 
while any passivation on the bottom of etched structure can be removed by direc-
tional ion bombardment with the progressing of etching process. It is not difﬁcult to 
ﬁnd carbon compounds in RIE because most of the etching processes employ 
polymer resists as the etching mask layer. The carbon compounds in the plasma 
can deposit onto the sidewall of etched structures, forming the passivation layer. It is 
also possible to form the passivation layer by introducing carbon compound gas, 
such as CHF3, into the plasma. Sub-50 nm silicon nanowires have been produced 
with aspect ratio up to 50:1 using mixture of SF6 + CHF3 etching gases [28]. With 
the assistance of CHF3, sub-100 nm silicon structures can be etched in RIE even 
without the use of a polymer masking layer. Figure 7.17 shows etched silicon 
nanostructure of 50 nm in width and 350–400 nm in height with near vertical 
sidewall proﬁle in which 10 nm chrome ﬁlm was used as masking layer. The etching 
was done with CHF3 and SF6 gas mixture at RF power of 250 W and chamber 
pressure of 30mTorr [29]. 
7.3.3 
RIE by Inductively Coupled Plasma (ICP) 
It has been demonstrated that conventional RIE is perfectly capable of etching 
sub-100 nm structures. The only limitation is its relatively low etch rate, which is 
normally less than 200 nm/min for silicon [30]. The etching rate in RIE directly 
depends on plasma density. For a conventional RIE system as shown in Fig. 7.11, 
plasma density increases with increasing RF power. However, increasing RF power 
will also increase the self-biasing voltage on the cathode where the etching sample is 
situated. The consequence is the increase of ion bombardment energy, hence dete-
rioration of etching selectivity. This becomes a particularly serious problem in 
sub-100 nm RIE, because the masking layer is always thin to enable photon or 
e-beam patterning of polymer resist at sub-100 nm feature dimension. It is also a

[Page 277]
7.3
Reactive Ion Etching (RIE)
277
Fig. 7.16 Controlled 
etching to achieve different 
shapes of silicon tips from 
very sharp tips (a) to  
medium sharp tips (b) and to 
blunt tips (c)

[Page 278]
serious issue for deep structure etching as longer etching at high etching rate 
consumes masking layer faster.
278
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.17 Sub-100 nm 
etched silicon structure 
using RIE with CHF3 and 
SF6 gas mixture and chrome 
ﬁlm as etching mask 
Fig. 7.18 Schematic of 
inductive coupling plasma 
source 
Inductively coupled plasma (ICP) system has cleverly solved the problem. In an 
ICP source, the plasma generation is separated from etching chamber, as shown in 
Fig. 7.18. RF power is coupled into plasma chamber by an induction coil from 
outside. The sample stage is connected to a second RF power source as an auxiliary 
RF source to enhance the production of plasma. Electromagnetic ﬁeld generated by 
inductive coupling coil can sustain electron cycling movement in plasma for a long 
period, which has greatly increased ionization probability while keeping the pressure

[Page 279]
low in the etching chamber. As the sample stage has an independent input of RF 
power, the self-biasing voltage can be independently controlled. Therefore, an ICP 
system can produce very high plasma density (>5×1011 /cm3 ) compared to conven-
tional RIE (108 –1010 /cm3 ) and maintain low ion bombardment energy. The conﬂict 
between high plasma density and high etch selectivity encountered in conventional 
RIE systems has been resolved. The current best ICP systems can produce about 
20 μm/min silicon etching rate which depends on the area size to be etched on silicon 
wafer. The selectivity between silicon and photoresists can be as high as 100:1. 
Selectivity between silicon and oxide is over 200:1. With such high plasma density 
and high etching selectivity, high etching rate and deep reactive ion etching (DRIE) 
become possible. 
7.4
Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE)
279
Because of its high density plasma, ICP is not only widely used for deep silicon 
etching but also an efﬁcient etching tool for other materials, such as quartz [31], III– 
V semiconductors [32], silicon carbide [33], and even tungsten which can be etched 
to the depth of more than 300 μm at an etch rate of 2.73 μm/min using patterned 
aluminum as the etch mask [34]. Commercial ICP etchers are readily available from 
a number of vendors worldwide [35–38]. 
7.4 
Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE) 
To fabricate silicon MEMS (microelectromechanical systems), the bulk of silicon 
needs to be removed. Bulk silicon micromachining is used to be done with alkaline 
wet etching as described in Sect. 7.2.2. With ICP technology, bulk silicon 
micromachining as well as a range of other materials becomes possible by RIE 
due to its high etch rate. Most MEMS devices not only require to remove large 
quantity of silicon but also require high aspect ratio structures to enhance their 
electromechanical performance. However, conventional RIE is inherently not highly 
anisotropic. There are always lateral etch when the etch goes deeper. Deep reactive 
ion etching (DRIE) requires high anisotropy to achieve high aspect ratio etched 
structures. 
In addition to MEMS applications, semiconductor industry also requires high 
aspect ratio silicon etching to fabricate through-silicon-via (TSV). TSVs are deep 
holes etched into silicon and ﬁlled with conductive metals to serve as electrical 
interconnects. The primary beneﬁt of TSVs is the reduced interconnect length. The 
short vertical connections through thinned silicon die enable reduced latency, lower 
capacitance, lower inductance, and permit higher speed communications, higher 
numbers of interconnections, and lower power level of communication links 
between circuits. With the help of TSVs (Sect. 11.2 of Chap. 11), thinned die and 
stacked die are possible, which permit miniaturization of integrated multi-chip 
systems and have enabled today’s mobile communications and system-on-chip 
(SoC) ICs [39]. For 3D ﬂash memory (NAND) chips, DRIE has become an essential 
fabrication technology [40].

[Page 280]
Process parameters
Passivation
Etching
280
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
7.4.1 
Bosch Process 
To ensure high anisotropy of DRIE, a gas switching process called Bosch process 
was invented in 1992 [41], which deliberately passivates the sidewall to stop lateral 
etching. The typical Bosch process conditions are listed in Table 7.3, and its steps are 
schematically illustrated in Fig. 7.19. It is a repeated etching-passivation process by 
introducing alternating gases. The etching gas is SF6 and passivation gas is C4F8. 
C4F8 in plasma can form a ﬂuorine carbon compound which once deposited on 
silicon surface can prevent F+ from further reacting with silicon. Etching and 
passivation are switched over every 5–15 seconds a cycle. Although pure SF6 gas 
reacting with silicon results in isotropic etching, the etched surface is soon passiv-
ated by C4F8 after a few seconds of C4F8 reaction. In next round of SF6 gas being 
introduced, ion bombardment in depth direction removes the passivation ﬁlm to

only proceeds in depth direction and eventually forms vertical sidewalls. In this way, 
highly anisotropic etching can be achieved, as the examples of etched MEMS and 
TSVs shown in Fig. 7.20.
7.4
Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE)
281
Fig. 7.20 Examples of Bosch process: (a) a silicon MEMS gyroscope; (b) cross-sectional image of 
TSVs ﬁlled with tungsten in a silicon wafer (reprinted from [39] with permission) 
Fig. 7.21 (a) Scalloping effect created in “Bosch” process; (b) scalloping effect in etched 
sub-100 nm silicon structures (reprinted from [43] with permission) 
Though it is capable of etching very deep structures with near vertical sidewall 
proﬁles, the Bosch process has a fundamental problem: the scalloping effect, e.g., 
ripples are formed over the sidewall surface due to the switching between etching 
and passivation, as the example shown in Fig. 7.21a. The surface roughness due to 
the scalloping is in the order of more than 100 nm [42]. The surface roughness 
caused by the scalloping effect may be tolerable if the feature dimension is tens or 
hundreds of micrometers, which is the case in MEMS devices. However, it cannot be

[Page 282]
tolerated if the feature dimension is in sub-100 nm range. An example is shown in 
Fig. 7.21b where the passivation time is 5 seconds and etching time is 6 seconds. 
There are eight cycles of gas switching, which can almost be counted from the 
number of scallops on the sidewall [43]. 
282
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.22 High aspect ratio silicon nanostructures etched by non-switching DRIE process (16 nm 
line width) (reprinted from [48] with permission) 
Many investigations have been carried out to reduce the scalloping effect. For 
large structure dimension, the scalloping may be smoothed by post etch polishing in 
wet chemicals such as KOH + IPA [44, 45]. For sub-100 nm feature dimension, the 
wet chemical polishing may distort the structure. Other techniques have been 
developed, including prolonging the passivation time [46], using a Teﬂon coated 
sample bed [47], or simply mixing the SF6 etch gas with the C4F8 passivation gas 
without switching. Figure 7.22 shows etched 16 nm silicon line structures using the 
non-switching SF6 + C4F8 DRIE process [48]. By optimizing the etching parameters 
and process conditions, a simple mixing of SF6 and C4F8 gases has also achieved 
sub-10 nm vertical silicon nanowires with aspect ratio of ~50:1 and smooth side-
walls [49]. Smooth sidewalls can be achieved even with standard ICP etchers by 
modifying both SF6 pulse time and lowering the substrate holder temperature. The 
sidewall scallop depth achieved by a modiﬁed Bosch process was reduced from 
271 nm to 42 nm via the generic process optimization [50]. 
Nonetheless, the typical surface roughness by Bosch process is in excess of 
200 nm. However, when etching narrow trenches (in the range below 5 μm wide), 
this roughness drastically reduces to levels below 25 nm, and it eventually vanishes 
with increasing aspect ratios during the process. Very high aspect ratios up to 160:1 
on trenches of 250 nm width have been achieved using the Bosch process, which

[Page 283]
indicates that surface roughness induced by the cyclic nature of the Bosch process is 
signiﬁcantly attenuated when increasing the aspect ratio of deep trenches, which is 
especially true for the submicron trenches [51]. 
7.4
Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE)
283
7.4.2 
Cryogenic Process 
Another sidewall passivation technique is low temperature etching of silicon, also 
called cryogenic process. During etching of silicon, SF6 gas reacting with silicon 
forms volatile compound SiF4. At room temperature, the compound is quickly 
removed from reaction chamber by vacuum pumping system. However, at low 
temperature (<-100 °C), the compound can form a 10–20 nm protective ﬁlm 
over silicon surface, such that the reaction of ﬂuorine free radicals with silicon is 
greatly weakened because of this protective ﬁlm. Also weakened is the reaction with 
photoresist or oxide. Both the sidewall protection and high selectivity of mask to 
silicon are realized by the cryogenic process, resulting in excellent anisotropic 
etching of silicon [52]. Introducing oxygen into the low temperature chamber can 
also help to form a protection layer on the sidewall, which prevents the sidewall from 
lateral etching. 
The use of photoresist masking layers is difﬁcult with cryogenic etching since the 
resist is a polymer and can crack with the low temperatures. For this reason, oxide 
mask is preferred. The mask selectivity to silicon is approximately 150 to 1 for a 
silicon dioxide and 70 to 1 for photoresist [53]. The advantage of cryogenic etching 
for deep structure is that there is no scalloping of the sidewalls of the etched features. 
The optimum low temperature is -110°C~–140 °C, at which no lateral etching of 
silicon was found. Below -140 °C, the entire silicon etching stops because the 
etching gas SF6 starts to freeze on the cold substrate surface [54]. The main 
disadvantage of cryogenic etching is the low process temperature. Since the substrate 
is at such a low temperature, it attracts particulates and contaminates in the process 
chamber that can lodge onto the surface, thereby creating micromasking effects as 
the defects in the etched structures. 
Table 7.4 compares the performances of three non-switching DRIE process for 
etching silicon nanostructures: HBr process, room temperature SF6 + C4F8 process 
(RT F-base), and cryogenic process [48]. Each process has different etch rate and 
selectivity, but all are capable of producing very smooth sidewalls (<10 nm 
roughness). 
7.4.3 
Critical Issues in DRIE 
When using ICP-based DRIE for pattern transfer, there are a number of critical 
issues one has to be aware. These issues include loading, microtrenching,

[Page 284]
micromasking, notching, and ion induced damage, which will directly inﬂuence the 
etch uniformity, control of etch proﬁle, or damage of substrate materials.

Fig. 7.23 Loading effects in DRIE: (a) macroloading; (b) microloading; (c) aspect ratio dependent

7.4.3.1 
Loading Effect 
Reactive ion etching rate depends on the supply rate of chemically active species. 
When the consumption of radicals surpasses the supply of them while the gas ﬂow 
rate and other conditions remain the same, the etching rate will decrease, causing the 
so-called loading effect. There are three types of loading effects—macroloading, 
microloading, and aspect ratio dependent etching (ARDE) [55, 56]—as illustrated in 
Fig. 7.23. They all mean the same thing, i.e., nonuniform etching due to the 
difference in supply and consumption of reactive radicals at the etching surface. 
Macroloading effect is the decrease of overall etch rate due to the increase of total 
area of materials to be etched away, as shown in Fig. 7.23a, which poses the problem 
that a well-characterized etching process for one set of the pattern design may not be 
applicable to another set of design if the overall etching areas are different. 
Microloading effect is related to localized depletion of reactive species or accu-
mulation of etching production as a result of local variation of pattern density, as 
schematically illustrated in Fig. 7.23b. High density pattern areas demand high rate

[Page 285]
supply of reactive species as well as high rate removal of etched volatile compounds 
than low pattern density areas. Such differences in the rates of radicals supply and 
etched product removal can cause difference in etch depth between high density 
pattern area and low pattern density area. Generally, isolated patterns are etched 
deeper than dense patterns. 
7.4
Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE)
285
ARDE is strongly dependent on feature dimensions. Other names for the ARDE 
are etch lag or aperture effect. As illustrated in Fig. 7.23c, the smaller the feature 
dimension (or pattern opening to etching), the less is the etch depth under the same 
etching condition. The cause of ADRE is very complicated. There are as many as 
eight different mechanisms that may explain the ADRE phenomenon [55], but it is 
believed to be mostly determined by the mean free paths of the species since this has 
a signiﬁcant impact on their transport in narrow trenches. The most direct method for 
modifying the mean free path is to adjust the process pressures. Since the Bosch 
DRIE process is composed of two parts wherein the pressures of each part can be 
individually controlled, the etch lag can be reduced by increasing the process 
pressure during the passivation step relative to the pressure during the etch step. 
Additionally, it has been found that decreasing the temperature of the substrate 
during processing from 40 °C to 0  °C can enable the etch to exhibit nearly lag free 
behavior over a range of aspect ratios [57]. 
Loading effect is most signiﬁcant in DRIE because of prolonged etching and 
large etching depth. The longer the etching time, the more signiﬁcant is the loading 
effect. There can be 100% variation in etching depth [56]. Conventional RIE also has 
the problem of aperture effect, when both large and small features have to be etched 
on the same substrate. For example, the etch rate of SiO2 has shown to drop from 
32 nm/min to 12 nm/min when the etched feature size changes from 20 μm to  52 nm  
[58]. There are no very effective measures that can eliminate the loading effect. 
Generally, high pumping speed (low chamber pressure), diluting gases, or using 
pulsed plasma can reduce to some extent the loading effect [59]. These measures 
help to remove the etched products fast or to reduce local nonuniformity of reactive 
species. Alternatively, the pattern layout can be modiﬁed to reduce or eliminate the 
nonuniformity in feature density or feature dimension, for example, introducing 
dummy features purely to balance the feature density or subdividing large features 
in multistep etching [45]. If the pattern design cannot be easily altered, one has to 
decide what is the critical feature dimension in a design layout if different feature 
sizes coexist in the design. Then the RIE condition should be optimized for the 
critical feature dimension, allowing variation in etch depth for other noncritical 
features. 
7.4.3.2 
Microtrenching Effect 
Microtrenching is a phenomenon that the etch rate is larger near the corners of a 
trench compared to the center of the trench. Typical microtrenching in etched 
features is shown in Fig. 7.24a where deep grooves appear adjacent to the sidewalls 
of etched structure. Microtrenching is believed to be produced by the impact of high

[Page 286]
energy particles (mostly ions) at grazing angles (>80°) on the sidewalls and then 
reﬂected toward the bottom of the etched surface, as illustrated in Fig. 7.24b. These 
ions retain a large fraction of their energy and directionality. It is like a “focusing” of 
the high energy particles at the base of the sidewalls of the feature, resulting in higher 
etch rates at those locations [60]. Such a mechanism has been proven by Monte 
Carlo simulation of ions sliding at sloped sidewalls [61]. Both sidewall slope angle 
and incident angle of ions can signiﬁcantly inﬂuence the microtrenching formation. 
Generally, the deeper the etch depth, the more signiﬁcant is the microtrenching 
because a larger sidewall surface becomes available for reﬂecting ions. Negative 
charging of sidewalls can also contribute to microtrenching, because the incident 
ions can be deﬂected by the negative ﬁeld toward the sidewall [62]. By deliberately 
introducing ﬂux of electrons into the etched trenches, the local electrical ﬁeld can be 
neutralized and the microtrenching effect can be reduced [63]. 
286
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
(a)
(b) 
Ion 
Fig. 7.24 (a) Microtrenching effect in RIE; (b) mechanism of microtrenching 
7.4.3.3 
Notching Effect 
Notching is the phenomenon of lateral etching at the foot of etched structure, which 
occurs when etching nonuniform size of features in silicon-on-insulator (SOI) sub-
strates. Typical notching effect is shown in Fig. 7.25a [64]. SOI wafers are special 
silicon substrates in which a thin SiO2 layer is sandwiched between a thick silicon 
wafer (handle layer) and a thin silicon wafer (device layer) [65]. Theoretically, the 
SiO2 insulator underneath the device layer provides an excellent etch stop layer 
which ensures all features, no matter big or small, are all etched to the same depth

[Page 287]
(the thickness of device layer). There should be no microloading effect or ARDE. 
However, it is found that big features are continuously etched laterally at the foot of 
trenches even though the etching has stopped in depth direction. Small features can 
also have notches if they are over-etched. The notching, or called “footing,” is 
believed to be caused by positive charging at the bottom insulating layer, as 
illustrated in Fig. 7.25b. Positive charges accumulated at the SiO2 layer create a 
local positive ﬁeld which deﬂects the incoming ions toward sides of the surface, 
causing the lateral etching [66]. There is nothing one can do to prevent the notching, 
unless the positive charge can be conducted away [67]. The most effective measure 
to prevent notching is to apply a low frequency pulsed bias power to the substrate 
stage to give the insulating layer a chance to dissipate the accumulated charge during 
the bias-off period [68]. Fig. 7.26 shows the etched trench on a SOI wafer by Bosch 
process at 13.56 MHz frequency (Fig. 7.26a) and 380KHz frequency pulsed biasing 
process (Fig. 7.26b). Notch-free DRIE of SOI wafer has been achieved [69].

Deep Reactive Ion Etching (DRIE)

mechanism of notching during etching of SOI substrate (reprinted from [67] with permission)

Fig. 7.27 SEM image of 
“black silicon” 
7.4.3.4 
Micromasking Effect 
DRIE of silicon often encounters the “black silicon” phenomenon. The etched 
surface looks black color instead of shiny bright color. Close observation of the 
black surface ﬁnds “grasslike” structures, as the SEM image shown in Fig. 7.27, 
which absorbs most light with little reﬂection. Such grassy silicon is formed by 
micromasking effect [70]. During DRIE, masking layer whether photoresist or oxide 
can be etched due to sputtering. Droplets of masking material land on the silicon 
surface, which prevent the silicon underneath to be etched. So the spikes of silicon 
like grass are formed. By adding O2 or CHF3, as well as slightly tuning either bias 
power or substrate temperature, the spikes can be removed. The basic idea is to 
adjust the etching gas constituents and etching condition to increase the isotropic 
etching, so that these “grasses” can be cut off. On the other hand, the black silicon is 
a perfect antireﬂection surface. Its reﬂectivity can be less than 0.001% [71], which is 
a valued property for a wide range of applications such as solar cells, image sensors, 
photodetectors and thermal imaging cameras, and even antibacterial surface [72]. 
7.4.3.5 
Ion Induced Damage Effect 
Ion sputtering during RIE can introduce damage to the substrate. Though the energy 
of ions is much lower than focused ion beam sputtering, in the range of 50–500 eV, 
and the penetration depth is only a few nanometers, it can change the surface 
conductivity and the mobility of charge carriers with depth up to 100 nm [22]. The 
much deeper damage range is due to the ion channeling effect, e.g., ions can travel 
much larger distance in a crystalline material if the incident ions happened to be in

[Page 289]
the path of low index lattice (Sect. 4.4 of Chap. 4). It is generally believed that this 
damage is due to the introduction of traps, vacancies, and interstitial atoms into the 
crystalline lattice. For single crystalline materials such as silicon, the ion induced 
damage can always be reduced or eliminated by post etching annealing. However, 
high annealing temperature can cause decomposition of compound semiconductors 
such as GaAs. Reducing ion energy is the most effective way of reducing the 
damage. For example, it is found that when RF power increased from 20 W to 
40 W, the carrier mobility and concentration at the etched surface decreased by 
13.8% and 29.2%, respectively [73]. Use of ICP etchers can certainly help to control 
the ion bombardment energy, because the substrate stage bias voltage can be 
independently controlled. However, detailed study found that even with ICP, one 
should avoid generating atomic ions in the plasma because etching with atomic ions 
such as Si+ showed more damage than with molecular ions such as (SiCl)+ [22]. On 
the other hand, reducing RF power may have to involve changing other conditions in 
order to maintain necessary etch rate, selectivity, and anisotropy. As a general rule, 
the use of highly reactive chemistries and molecular constituents within the plasma is 
best for reducing the effects of damage. 
7.5
Atomic Layer Etching (ALE)
289
7.5 
Atomic Layer Etching (ALE) 
Previously introduced RIE has several processes taking place simultaneously as 
illustrated in Fig. 7.12. Atomic layer etching (ALE) only allows to remove one 
atomic layer a time. This self-limited surface reaction is realized by a cyclic 
absorption and reaction process which is illustrated in Fig. 7.28. The process starts 
with a fresh substrate (Fig. 7.28a). In the ﬁrst reaction step, a precursor gas is 
introduced into the reactor for a short time (Fig. 7.28b). The chamber is then 
exhausted to remove the chemical reactants (Fig. 7.28c), so that only a monolayer 
of precursor molecules is absorbed on the substrate (Fig. 7.28d). Afterward, a beam 
of low energy ions is introduced to bombard the precursor covered surface, which 
induces a chemical reaction with the substrate atoms to form volatile compounds 
(Fig. 7.28e). These volatile compounds are pumped away (Fig. 7.28f) in second 
purge, resulting in a monolayer of substrate to be etched (Fig. 7.28g)  [74]. 
To enable successful ALE, the following requirements should be met: (1) the 
precursor is chosen so that upon reaction with substrate atoms volatile products can 
be formed; (2) the precursor does not spontaneously etch the substrate; (3) energies 
for ion or neutral beam bombardment are typically limited to 100 eV or lower to 
minimize physical sputtering and surface damage; and (4) the timing of purge should 
be precisely controlled so that the substrate thickness only loses about one mono-
layer per cycle. The self-limited surface reaction is ensured by absorption of 
monolayer reactant molecules and by minimizing both spontaneous chemical etch-
ing and physical sputtering. In previously described RIE, SF6 is the dominant etchant 
chemical compound for silicon etching. However, spontaneous etching takes place 
at room temperature for the F/Si system, which can only be suppressed at cryogenic

[Page 290]
temperatures. For this reason, ALE of silicon adopts the Cl2/Si system because the 
reaction of monolayer absorption of Cl2 on silicon only takes place with the aid of 
low energy ion (Ar+ at 20 eV) bombardment at room temperature [75]. Although the 
surface reaction of Cl2/Si system can also be thermally initiated, it requires signif-
icantly high temperatures (>600 °C). Use of an Ar plasma can lower the temperature 
requirement to below 200 °C [76]. The advantage of thermal ALE is that the removal 
of material is isotropic, which implies that it can be used to thin down materials in 
almost any geometry. 
290
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
Fig. 7.28 Process sequence of ALE: (a) fresh substrate; (b) introducing precursor gas; (c) purge the 
precursor gas; (d) a monolayer absorption of reactant on substrate; (e) low energy ions bombarding 
the absorbed monolayer; (f) volatile compound is purged; (g) an atomic layer of substrate is etched 
ALE was ﬁrst reported in 1990 [77], which was initially developed for GaAs 
etching to avoid damages caused by ion bombardment in conventional RIE. In the 
three decades of research and development, over two dozen materials have been 
shown capable of being processed with this technique [76]. It remained only as a 
topic of scientiﬁc interest because the etch rate is too low to draw industrial attention. 
This has changed in recent years as the mainstream semiconductor fabrication 
processes moved to sub-10 nm nodes [78]. At such length scales, all of lithography, 
material deposition, and material removal need to be capable of a few nanometer 
resolution, and this is the reason for the semiconductor industry’s interest in atomic 
layer processes. As the atomic layer deposition (ALD) has been widely used for 
material deposition in the latest IC manufacturing, ALE is being driven by the 
semiconductor industry as an alternative to conventional RIE and is viewed as an 
essential counterpart to ALD. Because lateral shrinkage of device features also 
brings about vertical diminution of layer thicknesses, ALD and ALE are the only 
two processes that are commercially available for such purposes. Apart from atomic 
precision removal of materials, ALE has other advantages over conventional RIE, 
including no or very little ion bombardment-related damage to the critical gate 
region and signiﬁcantly reduced process variability (both across the wafer and 
die-to-die).

[Page 291]
7.6
Ion Milling
291
7.6 
Ion Milling 
Etching at sub-100 nm dimension is also possible by ion milling. Ion milling is a 
pure physical ion sputtering process. Ion milling is needed because not all materials 
can be etched by RIE. Only those materials which chemically react with reactive ions 
and form volatile compounds can be etched by RIE. For etching other materials, ion 
milling is the alternative. 
Argon gas is commonly used to generate gaseous ions in ion milling. As argon is 
an inert gas, argon ions do not react chemically with any sample materials. There are 
different physical reactions between Ar + and sample surface, depending on the 
Ar + energy. If Ar + energy is less than 10 eV, surface absorption or surface damage 
is the dominant process. Ion damage to sample surface conﬁnes to only a few atomic 
layers. If Ar + energy is over 10 keV, ions can penetrate deep inside sample and 
become ion implantation. Ion milling only occurs at Ar + energy between 10 and 
5000 eV. It has been shown in previous sections that there is always ion sputtering 
taking place in RIE. However, ions in a RIE system are generated in the etching 
chamber and only accelerated by the negative ﬁeld near the sample surface 
(Fig. 7.11). The sputtering efﬁciency is low, and material removal by pure ion 
sputtering is not the dominant etching mechanism. 
Figure 7.29 is the schematic of a typical ion milling system. The ion source is a 
plasma source which has been described in Sect. 4.2.2 of Chap. 4. In the ion source, 
electrons emitted from a thermionic cathode are accelerated by the ﬁeld between 
cathode and anode. Argon molecules are ionized by energized electrons, and plasma 
is formed in the ion source chamber. The argon ions are then extracted from ion 
source chamber in the form of a broad beam and accelerated toward the sample in the 
sputtering chamber. Therefore, ion milling is also called ion beam milling or ion 
beam etching (IBE). It is different from the focused ion beam that has been 
introduced in Chap. 4, but the sputtering mechanism is the same. Because Ar + is 
chemically inert to sample materials, the etch rate by ion milling only depends on the 
sputtering yield of a particular sample material. Typical ion milling rate is 100~3000 
A/min at 1000 eV Ar ion energy and 1 mA/cm2 ion current density. For sputtering an 
insulating material, an electron gun, which is a hot ﬁlament as shown in Fig. 7.29, is  
employed to provide ﬂood exposure of electrons for neutralizing positive charge 
accumulated at the sample surface. 
In principle, ion milling can etch any materials. Its independence of material types 
makes the etching technique a good supplement to RIE and other etching techniques. 
It opens up the possibility to fabricate nanostructures in a much broader range of 
materials other than those commonly used in microelectronic industry. For example, 
many magnetic materials can be etched by ion milling [79, 80]. However, one must 
be aware of the following issues when using ion milling for nanoscale pattern 
transfer: 
(1) Physical sputtering by Ar ions is basically nonselective to materials. The etch 
selectivity only depends on the difference of sputtering yield for different 
materials, which are relatively small. Table 7.5 shows the ion milling rates for

[Page 292]
Materials

)
90
some commonly used material in electronic industry. It is apparent that photo-
resist, which is the basic masking material in most RIE processes, is only 
marginally suitable as mask for ion milling of gold ﬁlm, but not for nichrome 
or alumina.

For some materials, the selectivity of photoresist to the etched material can be 
improved by introducing certain chemical gases, so that the ion milling becomes 
partially reactive. Figure 7.30 has compared ion beam milling of hafnium oxide 
(HfO2) as the function of ion energy with and without adding CHF3 gas [81]. Without 
the participation of CHF3 in the milling process (Ar only), the etching rate of 
photoresist (PR) is higher than that of HfO2. By mixing two parts of CHF3 with

[Page 293]
one part of Ar, the etch rate of HfO2 is much enhanced, whereas the etch rate of 
photoresist is reduced. In this way, the photoresist serves as an ion milling mask 
better. It should be noted that the reactive ion milling is by no means a true sense of 
RIE because the etched product is still nonvolatile.

 HfO2-Ar

(Ei (keV)) 1/2 
For sub-100 nm pattern transfer by ion milling, e-beam resists have to be used as 
the masking layer because of the use of e-beam lithography for patterning. Though 
PMMA is the popular high resolution e-beam resist, it is not a good masking material 
for ion milling [80]. The negative tone inorganic resist HSQ works better. Pattern 
features as small as 30 nm have been made with HSQ by e-beam lithography and 
served as ion milling mask to etch gold nanostructures [82]. The gold nanostructures 
produced by combining negative tone HSQ mask and ion milling are equivalent to 
those fabricated by lift-off (see next chapter) in positive resist mask but have much 
smoother edges than those done by lift-off. The thickness of HSQ masking layer has 
to be similar to the thickness of metal layer to be etched, because the selectivity of 
HSQ in ion milling of gold is still low [82]. 
In general, the etch depth by ion milling is limited because of the fast consump-
tion of mask material. As mask thickness is reduced toward the end of etching, the 
ﬁnal etched feature may deviate from its original design. The most pronounced 
proﬁle distortion encountered in ion milling pattern transfer is faceting. Faceting is a 
phenomenon that top corners of a masking pattern are eroded faster than its planar 
surface, as illustrated in Fig. 7.31. Figure 7.31a is the initial mask pattern structure 
before ion milling. Because of the fast sputtering at the pattern corner, the mask 
pattern becomes sloped in the course of ion milling, so does the underlying etched 
pattern structure as shown in Fig. 7.31b. The faceting is most severe for polymer 
resist mask because much thick masking layer has to be used. To reduce the faceting 
problem, the masking layer should be made very thin. Hard metallic masking 
materials can be used, such as tungsten, which has been proven to alleviate the

[Page 294]
faceting problem [83]. However, patterning the hard mask needs an additional 
fabrications step, which inevitably complicates the pattern transfer process. 
294
7
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Etching
(a)
(b) 
Faceting 
Fig. 7.31 Faceting in ion milling: (a) initial mask pattern proﬁle; (b) faceting of etched proﬁle 
caused by ion sputtering 
(2) Ion milling is a physical etching process and does not form volatile compounds. 
The sputtered material can redeposit everywhere, particularly onto adjacent 
patterns [81]. By rotating while tilting the sample during sputtering, it helps to 
clean up effectively the redeposition of sputtered debris on the structure side-
walls [80]. A gentle wet acidic etch after ion milling can also help to clean up the 
redeposition, though the acid that removes the redeposited material can harm the 
etched structures as well. To overcome the dilemma, some reactive gases can be 
introduced into the ion milling process, which may produce some nonvolatile 
compounds dissolvable in acid but without attacking the pattern structures. For 
example, by ion milling of HfO2 with Ar/CHF3 gas mixture, the reaction product 
is hafnium ﬂuoride which can be dissolved in HCl acid, but the HCl does not 
react with HfO2 [81].

Chapter 8 
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition 
8.1 
Introduction 
Although subtractive etching is the most used pattern transfer process, not all 
materials can be etched either by wet chemical or dry plasma. Metals in particular 
are difﬁcult to etch by reactive plasma process except only a few. Though wet acids 
can etch metals, the isotropic nature of wet etch means it is difﬁcult to obtain high 
resolution as well as high aspect ratio metallic structures. Pure physical sputtering 
such as the ion milling introduced in Chap. 7 is too slow and has stringent require-
ments on the mask. Therefore, etching alone cannot perform all the pattern transfer 
tasks in nanofabrication. The other option is to transfer resist patterns by deposition. 
It is an additive process, i.e., adding materials through the resist pattern openings. 
The emphasis in this chapter is the additive pattern formation through a mask, as 
opposed to direct additive pattern formations introduced in previous chapters, such 
as patterned depositions by electron beam or focused ion beam or scanning probes. 
For masked pattern transfer where the mask is a polymer resist pattern created by 
lithography, whether using optical or electron beam or nanoimprint, patterns of 
deposited materials can be realized by liftoff as shown in Fig. 8.1a or by plating as 
shown in Fig. 8.1b. Other additive techniques include pattern transfer by shadow 
masks or by direct printing. 
8.2 
Thin Film Deposition 
The additive pattern transfer process involves depositing thin ﬁlms into the opening 
of a polymer resist mask. A thin ﬁlm can be deposited by physical vapor deposition 
(PVD), chemical vapor deposition (CVD), atomic layer deposition (ALD), molecule 
beam epitaxy (MBE), oxidation, plating, spin or spray coating, blade coating, or 
printing. PVD and CVD are the two most commonly used processes [1]. PVD
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_8
299

[Page 300]
includes thermal evaporation deposition and plasma sputtering deposition. The 
thermal evaporation can further be divided into resistive heating evaporation and 
electron beam bombardment heating evaporation. The plasma sputtering is differ-
entiated according to how the plasma is generated. It can be a DC plasma sputtering, 
magnetron plasma sputtering, or radio frequency (RF) plasma sputtering. CVD 
includes low-pressure CVD (LPCVD), plasma enhanced CVD (PECVD), atmo-
spheric pressure CVD (APCVD), and metal organic CVD (MOCVD). ALD is a 
pure chemical process by which single layer atoms are deposited by self-limited 
surface chemical reaction [2]. The main features of PVD, CVD, and ALD thin ﬁlm 
deposition techniques are summarized in Table 8.1.
300
8
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition
(a)
(b) 
Fig. 8.1 Schematic of additive pattern transfer processes: (a) liftoff, (b) electroplating 
One of the immediate concerns, when thin ﬁlm deposition process is used for 
additive pattern transfer, is the substrate temperature. As described earlier, polymer 
resist which is the masking material for the pattern transfer cannot withstand 
temperature higher than its glass transition temperature (Tg). As it is known from 
the previous chapters, at above Tg temperature, polymers become soften and 
ﬂowable. Distortions become inevitable particularly for small dimension and high 
aspect ratio patterns. The requirement for low substrate temperature leaves only the 
PVD as the suitable thin ﬁlm deposition technique for additive pattern transfer. 
Another requirement is the directionality of deposition. Additive pattern transfer 
by liftoff needs the thin ﬁlm deposition to have poor step coverage, so that the ﬁlm 
deposited onto the resist masking layer can be easily removed by dissolving the 
polymer resist. Good step coverage will connect the ﬁlm between top and bottom of 
polymer resist patterns, making the liftoff difﬁcult if not impossible. Figure 8.2 
illustrates the effect of step coverage on the ﬁnal outcome of liftoff. With a poor step 
coverage, the resist layer can be easily removed by solvents together with deposited 
ﬁlm on top of it, as shown in Fig. 8.2a, whereas a good step coverage prevents 
solvent from penetrating into the resist layer, making the complete removal of resist

Because of its high directionality, the deposition rate of thin ﬁlm by thermal 
evaporation depends on the distance and relative position between substrate and 
evaporation source. It follows the Knudsen’s cosine law, as expressed in Eq. 8.1

In the above equation, R is the deposition rate which is the function of evapora-
tion source to the sample distance r, the angle (θ) of sample to the axis of evaporation 
source, and the orientation of sample surface ϕ. The source position relative to a 
sample is schematically indicated in Fig. 8.3a. The rate also depends on the vapor 
pressure of source material Ps which is the function of heating temperature and the 
pressure in the evaporation chamber Pc. 
It is apparent that a ﬂat sample placed at certain distance from the evaporation 
source will have nonuniform ﬁlm thickness at different locations of the sample. 
Assuming the ﬁlm thickness at the center of sample (on the axis of evaporation 
source) is t0, the thickness at an off-center location with distance x is tx, the height of 
sample above the source is h, and the size of evaporation source is assumed to be 
much smaller than the source to sample distance (h), and then tx can be worked out 
from Eq. 8.2, according to the Knudsen’s cosine law [3].

Eq. 8.2 indicates that ﬁlm thickness at an off-center location on a ﬂat sample is 
always less than at a close-to-center location. Such difference can only be reduced if 
the sample is further away from the evaporation source. In order to achieve uniform 
deposition, the sample size should be small, and the evaporation chamber should be 
big. All of these measures are at the price of low deposition rate and less efﬁcient use 
of source material. 
It is possible to obtain uniform ﬁlm thickness, provided that the sample is placed 
on a spherical surface, as illustrated in Fig. 8.3b. In practice, samples which are 
usually small in size can be placed on spherically shaped sample holder. At any point 
of the spherical surface, the angles θ and ϕ are equal. Assuming the sphere has radius 
of r0, then the distance between any point on the spherical surface and the source can 
be expressed as r = 2r0 cos θ = 2r0 cos ϕ. Replacing the distance r in Eq. 8.1, the 
deposition rate at the spherical surface becomes Eq. 8.3:

Now the deposition rate only depends on the radius of sphere r0. The ﬁlm 
thickness should be uniform as long as the sample is placed at a position which 
coincides with the surface of a sphere and the evaporation source is located at the 
edge of this sphere as indicated in Fig. 8.3b.

Pumping 
Another widely used PVD technique in additive pattern transfer is the plasma 
sputtering deposition. An important advantage of sputter deposition is that even 
materials with very high melting points can be easily sputtered while evaporation of 
these materials in a thermal evaporator is problematic or impossible. Sputtered ﬁlms 
typically have a better adhesion on the substrate than evaporated ﬁlms. A sputter 
coating system is normally in diode conﬁguration, as schematically shown in 
Fig. 8.4. Two parallel electrodes are mounted in a vacuum chamber with a distance. 
One is the stage on which a sample is placed, and another is the target from which 
comes the deposition material. A direct current (DC) voltage of several kilovolts is 
applied between the electrodes, with the sample stage as anode and target as cathode. 
Inert gas, normally argon, is introduced into the vacuum chamber. At high electrical 
ﬁeld, free electrons are accelerated and collide with Ar atoms which become ionized. 
More free electrons are available in the chamber because of the ionization. These 
electrons acquire energy from the electrical ﬁeld between the diode electrodes, 
which causes more Ar atoms to be ionized. The Ar+ ions are accelerated toward 
the target, sputtering off target atoms. These free atoms with initial kinetic energy ﬂy 
everywhere and deposit on the sample surface as well as other surrounding surfaces. 
Plasma sputtering in the DC diode conﬁguration is characterized by slow deposition 
rates, high voltages, and low currents. It is also inadequate for the deposition of 
dielectric ﬁlms because of charging, arcing, and very low deposition rates. The 
improvement was to replace the DC power supply, shown in Fig. 8.4, with a RF 
power supply, generally operating at the radio frequency (RF) of 13.56 MHz. This 
change eliminates the charging and arcing problems when sputtering dielectrics and 
also leads to slightly higher deposition rates. The oscillating RF potential applied to 
anode and cathode results in a modiﬁcation to the electron motions, which provides 
better energy coupling to the electrons and produces higher plasma density [4]. 
Further evolution from the RF sputtering system was the magnetron cathode 
system. This type of tool uses magnetic conﬁnement of electrons in the plasma, 
which results in a much higher plasma density than in either the RF or DC diode 
systems. In a magnetron plasma system, the electric ﬁeld is always oriented normal 
to the surface of the cathode. The transverse magnetic ﬁeld is conﬁgured perpendic-
ular to electric ﬁeld, so that the trapped and drifting electrons are constrained to 
circulate many times around the cathode face, which greatly enhances the Ar 
ionization rate and hence the deposition rate [5].

[Page 305]
8.2
Thin Film Deposition
305
The deposition by plasma sputtering is also characterized by the Knudsen’s 
cosine law. However, various departures from the cosine distribution can happen, 
depending on the ion energy and the target material structure. The sputtering rate of 
low energy ions is higher at larger emission angle, and the sputtering rate of high 
energy ions is higher at smaller emission angle. The sputtering rate is also dependent 
on speciﬁc crystalline orientations of the target. The size of sputtering target is much 
larger than the size of an evaporation source. The sputter-off target atoms in the 
chamber space are frequently scattered by gas atoms before reaching the sample 
surface. All the above factors imply that the movement of atoms has a very broad, 
almost isotropic nature. This is the key reason why plasma sputtering deposition is 
less directional than thermal evaporation deposition. There are ways to partially 
improve the directionality of plasma sputtering deposition. Two techniques have 
been developed to ﬁlter out the angular distribution of the sputtered atoms to achieve 
a more normal-incident deposition process: one is long-throw deposition and another 
is collimated deposition [5]. 
Long-throw deposition is simply to increase the distance between the target and 
the sample holder plate. It is a mimic to the thermal evaporation deposition whereby 
the emission source is far apart from the sample. The reason for long throw is to 
decrease the pressure in the plasma chamber. It is known that to sustain a plasma, the 
electrode spacing L and chamber pressure P have to satisfy the condition of 
L•P > 0.5 (cm•Torr). Then to maintain plasma while keeping low pressure, the 
electrode spacing has to increase. The above condition is for DC plasma. For RF and 
magnetron plasma, electrons acquire extra energy from extended circling. The 
plasma can be sustained at much lower pressure than in a DC system. At 25 cm 
throw distance, the working pressure in a magnetron system can be in the low 
0.1 mTorr range. As the mean free path of an atom in ambient gas can be expressed 
as Eq. 8.4, low pressure means long mean free path for target atoms. 
λ ≈ 5 × 10 -3 
P Torr
ð
Þ
 
cm
ð
Þ
ð8:4Þ 
With the throw distance equal to the cathode diameter, the ballistic and 
unscattered atoms will have the maximum angular divergence of about ±45°. 
Atoms traveling at such large angles will be unable to reach the sample, which 
effectively increases the directionality of deposition. The drawback of long-throw 
deposition is some loss of deposition material because large angular atoms deposit 
onto the chamber walls instead of the sample. 
Directional deposition can also be obtained by inserting an array of collimating 
tubes between the magnetron cathode and the sample [6], as schematically shown in 
Fig. 8.5. The collimator functions much like the sidewalls of the chamber. It collects 
atoms which are traveling laterally to the cathode/sample plane. These atoms are 
deposited on the sidewalls of the collimator cells and hence not deposited onto the 
sample. Geometrically, the use of a collimator does not require any signiﬁcant 
increase in throw distance over conventional sputter deposition, thus removing the

[Page 306]
very low-pressure constraint of long-throw sputtering. The aspect ratio of the 
collimator can be readily changed without signiﬁcantly altering its overall dimen-
sions. For practical manufacturing applications, collimator cell diameters are of the 
order of 1 cm, and therefore the height of a 2:1 collimator is only 2 cm. The overall 
throw distance increase is then of the order of 3–4 cm. The directionality of 
deposition can be increased by increasing the aspect ratio of collimating tube. 
However, the deposition rate will be signiﬁcantly reduced due to the collimation. 
Generally, the deposition rate is reduced by a factor of about 35% for each unit 
increase in the collimator aspect ratio. The reduction of deposition on the sample 
becomes the deposition onto the collimator tubes. Eventually this leads to either 
closure of the collimator holes, or peeling and ﬂaking of thick deposits, neither of 
which is desirable. In practice, it is necessary to change the collimator about two to 
three times more often than changing of cathode, which unfortunately can signiﬁ-
cantly add to overall cost.
