8.3 
Pattern Transfer by Liftoff 
The concept of patterning high resolution metallic structures by liftoff was proposed 
more than 40 years ago [7], and soon afterward sub-20 nm metal lines were made by 
the technique using patterned PMMA resist [8]. Liftoff has now become the primary 
technique for patterning metallic thin ﬁlms, because for many metallic materials, 
there are no other means to pattern them except the liftoff. Liftoff process has been 
illustrated in Fig. 8.1a. A polymer resist layer is patterned by optical or electron 
beam lithography ﬁrst. Metallic thin ﬁlm is then deposited onto the patterned resist 
layer. Chemical solvent, normally acetone, dissolves the resist layer, which also lifts 
off the metallic thin ﬁlm on top of the resist layer from the substrate. Only the 
metallic ﬁlm deposited through the resist pattern opening onto the substrate remains. 
In this way, the resist pattern is transferred onto the substrate as a metallic pattern of 
reverse polarity. 
The resolution capability of liftoff process is in principle the same as the resist 
patterning process, whether the patterning is done by optical lithography, electron 
beam lithography, or nanoimprinting lithography. Sub-10 nm liftoff metal patterns 
have been demonstrated [9]. From thin ﬁlm deposition point of view, the only

[Page 307]
limitation on the ultimate resolution of liftoff patterns is the grain size of deposited 
thin ﬁlms. A continuous line pattern may be broken if the grain size is comparable to 
the linewidth. As seen in Table 8.1, each deposition technique has its own range of 
grain size. Pattern transfer at sub-100 nm feature dimension requires small grain size 
which depends on the deposition conditions. Generally, high deposition rate, 
whether by thermal evaporation or plasma sputtering, will result in small grain 
size in the deposited ﬁlm. 
8.3
Pattern Transfer by Liftoff
307
(b)
(a) 
Fig. 8.6 Proﬁles of thin ﬁlm coverage over a patterned resist structure with (a) thermal evaporation 
deposition and (b) plasma sputtering deposition 
The key to the successful liftoff is to make sure that the liquid solvent can 
penetrate into the resist layer and dissolve it. This requires the metal ﬁlm on top of 
the resist layer to disconnect with the metal ﬁlm inside the resist pattern opening. The 
discontinuity of ﬁlm is only possible if (1) the deposited ﬁlm thickness is much less 
than the resist layer thickness and (2) the deposited ﬁlm has poor step coverage over 
the patterned resist layer. The ﬁrst condition can easily be met by using thick resist 
layer. For successful liftoff, the resist layer should be at least three times thicker than 
the deposited thin ﬁlm. The second condition depends on the directionality of thin 
ﬁlm deposition process. As described in the previous section, thermal evaporation 
deposition has the best directionality than any other deposition methods. Figure 8.6 
compares the deposition proﬁles for thermal evaporation (Fig. 8.6a) and sputtering 
deposition (Fig. 8.6b). For this reason, whenever possible, liftoff should always be 
done through thermal evaporation deposition of thin ﬁlm. 
However, in terms of ﬁlm quality, thermally evaporated ﬁlm is the poorest among 
other methods. More importantly, thermal evaporation is not best suited for depos-
iting alloy materials, because each constituent in an alloy may have different vapor 
pressure and hence different evaporation rate. The composition of deposited ﬁlm 
may deviate from the stoichiometry of original alloy in the evaporation source. 
Plasma sputtering deposition, on the other hand, has more or less the same sputtering 
rate for different constituents in an alloy. Although different materials have different 
ion sputtering yield as indicated in Table 4.1 of Chap. 4, the sputtering only takes 
place at the target surface, which would quickly deplete the atoms of constituent of 
high sputtering yield, leading to decrease of its sputtering rate until all the atoms of 
other constituents in the surface layer are sputtered off and a new fresh layer is

[Page 308]
revealed. In this way, the overall sputtering rates for all the constituents in an alloy 
become similar. Although thin ﬁlm deposited by plasma sputtering is better, its poor 
directionality makes the liftoff much more difﬁcult than using thermal evaporation 
deposition. In order to ensure the discontinuity of thin ﬁlm over patterned polymer 
resist topography, it is highly desirable to create an undercut proﬁle in the patterned 
resist structure, as is schematically drawn in Fig. 8.7. With an undercut proﬁle, in 
addition to a thicker resist layer, the poor directionality of thin ﬁlm deposition can be 
offset, making liftoff possible even with plasma sputtering deposition. 
308
8
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition
Lift-off 
Substrate 
Resist pattern 
Undercut profile
Thin film deposition 
Fig. 8.7 Undercut proﬁle of resist layer facilitates liftoff 
It is difﬁcult to produce an undercut resist proﬁle in optical lithography, because 
the absorption of photons in photoresist is always higher at top part of the photoresist 
layer, resulting in an overcut proﬁle after development. In electron beam lithogra-
phy, undercut resist proﬁle is possible. As described in Chap. 3, electrons scatter in a 
resist layer, and electron energy deposition becomes broader as electrons going 
deeper in resist. This feature can be conﬁrmed by Monte Carlo simulation of electron 
scattering and resist exposure. Figure 8.8a is the computer simulated resist proﬁles 
exposed with 20 keV electron beam. The resist is a 0.5-μm-thick PMMA and the 
pattern is single 50 nm line. The pear-shaped proﬁle is the result of electron 
scattering. Three resist proﬁles are shown, representing three different exposure 
doses. The higher the exposure dose, the broader the undercut range. As described 
in Chap. 3, electron beam lithography at low energy does not have high resolution, 
particularly for dense patterns. When patterning thick resist at high resolution, 
especially for sub-100 nm features, high beam energy is preferred. However, high 
beam energy makes it difﬁcult to produce an undercut proﬁle in a single layer resist, 
as is seen in Fig. 8.8b. Increasing exposure dose makes little difference in increasing 
the undercut range. 
The above problem can be solved by bilayer resist systems. Figure 8.9 illustrates 
how the bilayer resist system for liftoff works. The commonly used bilayer resist 
system for liftoff is the combination of PMMA and its copolymer [10]. They are both 
sensitive to electron exposure. High molecular weight PMMA, which is of low 
sensitivity to electron exposure, forms the top layer. Low molecular weight copol-
ymer P(MMA-MAA), which is of high sensitivity to electron exposure, forms the 
bottom layer. They are both developed in the same developer solution after e-beam 
exposure. Because the bottom layer resist is more sensitive to e-beam exposure than 
the top layer resist, it develops faster with the same exposure dose than the top layer, 
which creates the undercut proﬁle.

[Page 309]

Pattern Transfer by Liftoff

cm2 ; (b) single layer PMMA at 100 keV beam energy, three proﬁles at exposure dose of 300 μC/ 
cm2 , 400 μC/cm2 , and 500 μC/cm2 
The bottom layer does not have to be an e-beam sensitive polymer. As long as the 
top layer is sensitive to e-beam, resist patterning can be performed only on the top 
layer. Once the top layer is exposed and developed, the bottom polymer can be 
dissolved by a different solvent through the opening of top layer. By controlling the 
extent of dissolution for the bottom layer polymer, the length of undercut can be 
independently controlled. The process is illustrated by Fig. 8.10. One of such

[Page 310]
polymers especially designed for liftoff process is the liftoff resist (LOR) based on 
polydimethylglutarimide (PMGI) polymers which is a standard product available 
from many resist vendors. Using LOR as the bottom layer in a bilayer resist stack can 
produce precisely controlled undercut proﬁle as seen in Fig. 8.11a, where three 
undercut proﬁles are shown. The length of undercut is controlled only by dissolution 
time. Figure 8.11b shows deposited metal thin ﬁlm over the patterned bilayer resist 
stack before liftoff [10]. With the proper undercut proﬁle, the metal ﬁlm on top of the 
resist stack and at the substrate surface is well disconnected.

The same concept can be applied to various other combinations of resists or 
materials to allow undercut to be independently created. For example, a PMMA/

[Page 311]
aluminum bilayer for metal liftoff patterning has been demonstrated with PMMA on 
top as an imaging layer [11]. The Al underlayer works in the same way as the 
aforementioned LOR. Al can be dissolved in an alkaline solution, which does not 
attack the PMMA pattern. The added beneﬁt of using Al underlayer is the ability to 
carry out e-beam lithography on an insulating substrate, because the aluminum 
coating can effectively prevent electron charging of the insulating substrate. Silver 
pattern features with minimum 40 nm linewidth have been produced on a quartz 
substrate by this process. 
8.3
Pattern Transfer by Liftoff
311
It is always difﬁcult to have a completely clean liftoff of metal ﬁlm, particularly 
when the pattern area is small and most of the resist layers are covered by metal ﬁlm. 
The solvent has to penetrate the metal layer around the gaps at resist pattern edges 
and dissolve the resist underneath. Once the resist is dissolved, the bulk of metal ﬁlm 
must be cleaned away from the sample surface with the assistance of a pressured 
spray of solvent. The process is slow, and sometimes the metal ﬁlm can redeposit 
onto the sample surface once the resist layer is gone. If the metal ﬁlm is deposited by 
plasma sputtering which is known to have poor directionality, the ﬁlm may hang 
over the pattern edges here and there, resulting in many defects on the sample surface 
after liftoff. A technique which used a stream of solid CO2 particles (dry ice), or 
called carbon dioxide snow, was developed to remove the metal ﬁlm on top of resist 
layer prior to a solvent stripping of the resist [12]. The mechanism is to create a 
thermal expansion mismatch between the metal ﬁlm and the resist layer because of 
the cold solid CO2 below -60 °C. This can cause the metal ﬁlm to crack and 
delaminate. The loosened metal ﬁlm is then cleared away by a CO2 snow jet with 
high speed up to 40 m/s, and the resist layer is stripped off in a solvent. The sample 
backside is usually heated to +60 °C during the process to promote the thermal 
mismatch and delamination of metal ﬁlm. Because the metal ﬁlm is cleared away 
before the resist layer is dissolved in solvent, the sample surface is well protected by 
the resist layer, which can prevent any metal ﬁlm patches from redepositing onto the 
sample surface. 
Most of nanoscale metal pattern liftoff was done by electron beam lithography of 
positive resist, notably the PMMA resist of either single layer or multilayer. How-
ever, there are applications requiring negative tone liftoff, whereby metal thin ﬁlm is 
required to cover majority of a substrate surface. In this case, negative tone resist 
process is preferred because liftoff by positive tone resist such as PMMA will require 
exposure of large areas and consume long e-beam lithography time. However, 
negative resist is known to produce overcut proﬁles, which makes liftoff very 
difﬁcult. Although some negative photoresists have been used for negative tone 
liftoff process and undercut proﬁles can be produced by overdevelopment of the 
resist structure [13], the process is, however, only suitable for much larger pattern 
dimension. For high resolution negative tone liftoff, the bilayer concept is still 
applicable. A HSQ/PMMA bilayer resist process has been developed [14], as 
illustrated in Fig. 8.12. 
HSQ is spin-coated on top of PMMA, and electron beam lithography is 
performed on the HSQ layer. After development, HSQ negative tone resist patterns 
are formed. The PMMA will not be affected during the development of HSQ top

[Page 312]
layer because of the use of incompatible developer, even though it has been exposed 
by e-beam irradiation through the HSQ layer. The HSQ patterns are then used as an 
etching mask to transfer into the PMMA underlayer. By controlling the etching 
parameters, undercut proﬁle of the underlayer PMMA resist can be controlled 
independently. Figure 8.13 shows the SEM image of HSQ/PMMA resist proﬁle 
after etching of PMMA underlayer. The negative tone undercut proﬁle is clearly 
demonstrated. Metal liftoff can be easily done with resist pattern structure. 
Figure 8.14a shows chrome metal pattern in which each metal line is separated by 
50 nm gap which is formed by liftoff of HSQ/PMMA resist lines. Figure 8.14b

8.4 
Pattern Transfer by Plating 
Despite its many advantages in creating metallic patterns, liftoff technique is not 
suitable to produce high aspect ratio pattern structures. One reason is that the 
deposited ﬁlm thickness has to be at most less than one-third of resist depth in 
order to ensure discontinuity of ﬁlm, hence a successful liftoff. The other reason is

[Page 314]
that gradual accumulation of deposited material overhanging at top of resist pattern 
edge can close up the opening if the resist structure is too narrow and too deep, as 
illustrated by Fig. 8.15. High aspect ratio metallic micro- or nanostructures are 
commonly produced by plating. 
314
8
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition
Fig. 8.15 Overhang of 
deposited materials causing 
close-up of resist pattern 
opening 
Plating, either electroplating or electroless plating, is a well-established industrial 
manufacturing process. Building microstructures by plating started in the 1970s 
when magnetic storage devices were made by plating copper and permalloy through 
molds [15]. The molds were photolithographicly deﬁned resist patterns. For 
electroplating, a seed layer is needed, and electroplating of metallic patterns is 
done by patterning a resist layer on top of the seed layer and then ﬁlling up the 
patterned resist mold with metal, as illustrated in Fig. 8.1b. Electroless plating is 
mainly used for conformable coating of a surface. Only a limited number of metals 
and alloys can be deposited by electroless plating, and the deposition rate is much 
lower than electroplating [16]. In contrast, many metals and alloys can be 
electroplated. The major applications of electroplating in microstructure patterning 
are in thin ﬁlm head manufacturing for magnetic storage devices [17] and copper 
ﬁlling of via hole for integrated circuits [18]. Many other areas are also using 
electroplating as an effective method to build high aspect ratio metallic microstruc-
tures. Notably, electroplating is one of the main fabrication techniques to make 
microelectromechanical systems (MEMS). 
Electroplating, or electroforming, is one of the key components in the LIGA 
technique which is a German acronym of “Lithographie (LI) Galvanoformung 
(G) Abformung (A),” meaning lithography + electroforming + molding [19]. By 
hard X-ray exposure of very thick polymer resist, microstructures with high aspect 
ratio have been made by electroplating nickel into the resist mold [16]. Thick 
photoresist can also be patterned by UV light to form the mold. Though the aspect 
ratio is not as high as those made by X-ray LIGA, the UV based process is a low cost

[Page 315]
version of LIGA and particularly useful for making micromechanical structures 
[20]. Another main application area where electroplating plays a major role is 
making various magnetic microdevices by electroplating of magnetic materials, 
notably the permalloy. Microstructures made of permalloy can be actuated and 
manipulated by external magnetic ﬁelds, for example, magnetic micromirrors 
which can be deﬂected by applying a magnetic ﬁeld externally [21]. The magnetic 
force exerting on the magnetic microstructures is proportional to the volume of 
magnetic material. Electroplating is the effective way to build up bulk magnetic 
materials. 
8.4
Pattern Transfer by Plating
315
The aforementioned applications using electroplating are mainly for making 
microscale structures with dimension larger than micrometer or a few hundreds of 
micrometers. Electroplating for making sub-100 nm structures is primarily in two 
areas: ﬁrst area is in making masks and zone plate lens for X-ray lithography or 
X-ray imaging; second area is in making stamps for nanoimprinting, which has been 
described in Sect. 6.2.1 of Chap. 6. For X-ray lithography, heavy metal structures act 
as absorbers in an X-ray mask. They have to be thick enough to absorb all the X-ray 
radiation, as have been described in Sect. 2.4.3 of Chap. 2. As X-ray is 1:1 proximity 
imaging, to pattern sub-100 nm structures by X-ray lithography, the absorber pattern 
on the mask has to be sub-100 nm as well. Micro zone plate (MZP) is a common type 
of imaging lens to focus X-ray radiation. The resolution of a MZP lens can be 
expressed as Eq. 8.5 [22], which is similar to that of optical imaging (Eq. 2.3 in 
Chap. 2): 
Rx = k1 
λ 
NAMZP , 
where NAMZP = 
λ 
2ΔrMZP 
ð8:5Þ 
where λ is the X-ray wavelength, NAMZP is the numerical aperture of the zone lens, 
and ΔrMZPis the outmost zone width. k1 is illumination-dependent constant. Then the 
resolution can be expressed as 2k1 ΔrMZP. With an optimized X-ray illumination 
system, the k1 factor can be less than 0.5 [23]. It is apparent that the imaging 
resolution is solely dependent on the outmost zone width. The smaller the zone 
width, the higher the imaging resolution. Making high resolution MZP lens is a great 
challenge, and electroplating is the only method to achieve high aspect ratio 
(requirement of opacity to X-ray) heavy metal structure. 
The material for high aspect ratio electroplating at sub-100 nm dimension is 
mainly gold. Electroplated gold is in the form of polycrystalline structure which can 
have many grain boundaries and imperfections. The polycrystalline grains may grow 
faster in a particular orientation. For example, gold plating is faster in the <111> 
direction [24]. The grains usually grow perpendicular to the surface. Therefore, 
surface roughness is the common feature of electroplated patterns. Figure 8.16 
shows a part of MZP lens which is made from electroplated gold. The linewidth of 
outmost ring is 25 nm. Polycrystalline grains are visible from the surface of MZP. If 
the resist pattern dimension is comparable to the grain size, voids or even break can 
happen to the electroplated lines if the height of feature is much greater than its width 
[24]. Figure 8.17 shows a MZP with outmost nominal line width of 15 nm. As seen

[Page 316]
from the inset SEM image, the gold lines are wider than its nominal 15 nm width, 
which is the result of proximity effect in e-beam lithography. In addition, some of the 
gold lines are visibly broken, which is most likely due to gain size effect in gold 
electroplating.

A critical issue in pattern transfer by electroplating is the uniformity of 
electroplated structures. As the deposition rate in electroplating is highly dependent 
on plating current, the uniformity of deposition thickness depends on the uniformity 
of current density distribution. The current density is always higher at the location 
where there are asperities such as the boundary of a substrate or sharp edge of a 
pattern structure. The consequence of such nonuniform distribution of current 
density is the nonuniformity of the thickness of electroplated features. Generally, 
large electroplated features may be thicker at the edges. Isolated narrow lines are 
thicker than densely packed narrow lines [16]. For a large substrate with many 
different sizes of features, it often happens that some features have reached the 
correct thickness while others may have been over plated.

[Page 317]
8.4
Pattern Transfer by Plating
317
An important additive pattern transfer process in IC manufacturing is the dama-
scene process. The name damascene came from its similarity to ancient jewelry 
making process which inlays metal wires and patterns. IC manufacturing involves 
multilevel interconnecting transistors to make a circuit functioning. Interconnects 
were done by depositing a metal ﬁlm (Al or Cu) and patterning the metal ﬁlm into 
connecting lines. Between different levels of interconnects, via holes are etched and 
ﬁlled with metal to connect the top and bottom levels of metal lines. In original 
single-damascene process, via hole ﬁlling and metal line patterning are done sepa-
rately. The single-damascene process is suitable for Al interconnects because Al can 
be easily etched by RIE. With interconnects moving from Al to Cu, the poor dry 
etchability of Cu made the single-damascene process unattainable. The dual-
damascene process was then developed, which uses a single metal deposition 
process to simultaneously form the metal lines and metal vias [26]. 
The schematic cross section of copper interconnects and vias is shown in 
Fig. 8.18a [27]. It starts ﬁrst with nanoscale vias and trenches etched in an interlayer 
dielectric (typically SiO2) and followed by deposition of diffusion barrier layers. A 
thin Cu seed layer is then deposited by plasma sputtering, followed by Cu 
electroplating. Finally, excessively plated Cu in the ﬁeld regions is removed by 
chemical mechanical polishing (CMP). Figure 8.18b is the cross-sectional SEM 
image of interconnected IC showing the ﬁlled oxide dielectrics and the copper vias 
as well as interconnects. In recent years, ICs are developing toward 3D stack and 
system on chip (SoC) which need through-silicon-via (TSV) to interconnect differ-
ent levels of circuits. The TSVs normally have depth from 10 μm to more than 
100 μm and aspect ratio >20:1. Fig. 7.20b in Chap. 7 has shown such a TSV which 
is 95 μm deep and ﬁlled with tungsten. At such high aspect ratio, the TSVs have to be 
etched by DRIE process, and the seed layer has to be deposited by ALD. Copper or 
tungsten then ﬁlls the TSV by electroplating or CVD process [28].

(b) 
Fig. 8.18 (a) Schematic cross section of integrated circuit with vias and interconnects. (b) Image of 
copper interconnects in an integrated circuit. (Reprinted from [27] with permission)

8.5 
Pattern Transfer by Stencil Mask 
Metallic patterns can also be transferred directly onto a substrate by a stencil mask. 
The process is illustrated in Fig. 8.19. A stencil mask is a framed membrane with 
etch-through patterns. Pattern transfer by stencil mask is to use a stencil mask to 
partially block material deposition on a substrate. Deposited material forms a pattern 
on a substrate, which is the same as the etch-through pattern on the stencil mask. Or 
rather, it leaves a shadow of the mask on substrate during material deposition. So it 
can also be called shadow mask patterning. To ensure the shadowing effect, the 
deposition has to be directional. Therefore, thermal evaporation deposition is the 
only viable deposition technique to combine with the stencil mask pattern transfer 
process. 
Nanoscale pattern transfer through stencil mask was ﬁrst reported in 1999 
[29]. The stencil mask was made of a suspended silicon nitride membrane with 
holes of 5–10 nm. Thermally evaporated metal dots of 15 nm were made through the 
membrane mask. Later large area patterning was developed [30, 31]. Stencil mask 
patterning has the following advantages.
•
A thin ﬁlm pattern can be deposited at any local position, because the stencil mask 
is not in contact with substrate. Pattern deposition can also be done on topo-
graphical surface.

[Page 319]
8.5
Pattern Transfer by Stencil Mask
319
•
Thin ﬁlm patterns are formed in one step, without resist patterning and liftoff 
processes like conventional lithography. Because no resist patterning is needed, 
material can be deposited directly on fresh substrate surface, avoiding potential 
surface contamination. As the deposition is carried out in vacuum, the possibility 
of substrate surface oxidation can also be excluded. Mechanically fragile or 
chemically functionalized surface can be structured due to the noncontact and 
process-free nature of the pattern transfer.
•
Because no optical or e-beam lithography is involved, there is no proximity effect 
caused either by light diffraction or electron scattering. Large and small patterns 
can be formed simultaneously. For example, it has been reported that on the same 
stencil mask, both sub-100 nm features and above 100 μm features were made in 
one step deposition [31]. 
However, when transferring patterns by stencil masks, some cautions have to be 
taken as there are limitations in this technique.
•
The technique can only deposit single layer pattern. For multilayer deposition, 
there is an alignment issue, unless the multilayer materials are deposited on the 
same location.
•
For thick ﬁlm deposition or repeated use of stencil mask, the deposition of 
material around the edge of stencil pattern can close in, resulting in smaller 
feature dimension on the mask, and eventually clog some small holes completely. 
Figure 8.20a shows schematically the ideal deposition without clogging, and 
Fig. 8.20b is the deposition with clogging at the stencil mask aperture. The 
eventual close-up of mask aperture is demonstrated by the SEM image of real 
stencil mask in Fig. 8.20c. Nevertheless, it is possible to remove clogging by wet 
chemical or reaction ion etching after each time use. It is the clogging during the 
deposition that imposes a limit on the amount of material to be deposited in a 
single process step.
•
Because there is a gap between stencil mask and substrate surface, there are 
always blurs like halos around the main pattern edge, due to molecules arriving in 
angular direction through the stencil mask opening. Further blurs are caused by 
kinetic movement of molecules, because the molecules that evaporated thermally 
from the material source have kinetic energy and will move laterally before 
landing on the substrate surface. The blur, however, is much thinner than the 
main deposited pattern structure and can be corrected by an extra etching after the 
stencil mask pattern transfer [32].
•
For large size stencil mask, there is a thermal stability issue. Deposition of metal 
by thermal evaporation can generate a lot of heat from evaporation source. 
Radiation of heat can cause expansion of stencil mask, causing pattern shift and 
distortion. The deposition induced stress can also deform the stencil membrane.
•
The fabrication of stencil mask involves other lithographic techniques and reac-
tive ion etching, which limits the smallest feature achievable on the mask. For 
large area stencil mask, the minimum mask features are around 100 nm. For small 
size stencil mask, focused ion beam sputtering can be used to make very small 
holes in membrane.

[Page 320]

8
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition

[Page 321]
8.6
Pattern Formation by Printing
321
8.6 
Pattern Formation by Printing 
The additive pattern transfer technologies described in the previous sections are all 
based on transfer through a mask, either a polymer resist mask or a stencil mask. 
Functional materials can also be deposited into patterns by printing, such as inkjet 
printing, screen printing, and gravure printing. Patterning a polymer resist mask 
needs to go through a lengthy lithography process, including spin-coating resist, 
lithographic patterning (optical or e-beam), developing the exposed resist, etc. 
Making a stencil mask is even more complicated, which needs not only resist 
patterning but also metal etching. Printing, especially inkjet printing, does not 
need a mask but deposits functional materials directly into designed patterns. 
Additive pattern formation by printing has a number of advantages over 
lithography-based technology:
•
Printing can deposit materials on much larger substrate than conventional lithog-
raphy which is so far limited to 300 mm size of a silicon wafer.
•
Printing has no restriction on the type of substrate to deposit materials, particu-
larly on various ﬂexible substrates which conventional lithography cannot 
accommodate.
•
Printing is a low cost material patterning process, not only because the printing 
equipment is low cost but also because of its high throughput capability which 
greatly reduces the unit cost of large volume production.
•
For inkjet printing, there are added beneﬁts that the pattern formation is entirely 
digital without the need for a mask, and it is on-demand deposition of materials 
with little wastes. 
Figure 8.21 schematically compares inkjet printing with shadow mask deposition 
for manufacturing organic light emitting diode (OLED) display panels. While 
thermal evaporation of colored pixels (red-blue-green) through a shadow mask 
wastes 90% of precious organic materials, inkjet printing performs on-demand 
deposition which saves 90% of the material. Conventionally, all the OLED TV 
panels are made by shadow mask deposition for the color pixels. In May 2023, TCL, 
the leading display panel manufacturer in China, unveiled its inkjet-printed 65-inch 
ﬂexible OLED TV, which demonstrated the capability of printing to replace tradi-
tional additive pattern formation technologies. In fact, printing functional materials, 
such as conductors, semiconductors, or dielectric materials, to fabricate electronic 
devices and circuits has become a new engineering discipline in recent years, called 
printed electronics [34]. 
From the nanofabrication point of view, the pattern formation with traditional 
printing is not of nanoscale. The existing printing techniques, including inkjet, 
gravure, gravure offset, or screen printing, print features in micrometers or hundred 
micrometer dimension. Taking the example of inkjet printing, traditional inkjet 
printers, as we use in ofﬁce in everyday, are only capable of printing features no 
less than 50 μm. It relies on jet out ink droplets from a nozzle by piezoelectric 
pushing, as schematically shown in Fig. 8.22a. The dimension of printed feature

[Page 322]
depends largely on the size of ink droplets which in turn depends on the size of 
nozzle. However, the inkjet nozzle cannot be made very small because of the ink 
clogging problem. The smallest nozzle that can be safely used is about 20 μm, which 
determines the low limit of feature dimension that can be printed by this type of 
inkjet printers. The small driving force by piezoelectric pushing in a traditional inkjet 
printer also permits only low ink viscosity. 
322
8
Nanoscale Pattern Transfer by Deposition
Fig. 8.21 Color pixel formation through (a) a shadow mask and (b) by inkjet printing 
The demands for high resolution printing in printed electronics greatly stimulated 
the development of new printing methods. An advance in inkjet printing technology 
is the development of aerosol jet printing [35], as schematically shown in Fig. 8.22b. 
In an aerosol jet printer, inks are atomized into aerosol which is blown out by high 
speed airstream. At the exit of the nozzle, a ring of compressed air aerodynamically 
focuses the aerosol stream into a much ﬁner jet. Although the diameter of nozzle can 
be 200 μm, the focused jet stream is as small as 5 μm, ensuring much ﬁner printed 
features. The much larger nozzle used in the aerosol jet printer can also avoid the 
clogging problem due to high viscosity ink or dispersed nanomaterials such as 
carbon nanotubes or metal nanowires in an ink. The breakthrough in improving 
the 
resolution 
capability 
of 
inkjet 
printing 
is 
the 
development 
of 
electrohydrodynamic jet (E-jet) printing [36]. The principle of E-jet is illustrated in 
Fig. 8.22c. The ink droplets are pulled out by an electric ﬁeld between the nozzle and 
substrate. A ﬁne jet is achieved because a liquid under the inﬂuence of electric ﬁeld 
can form a cone shape with very sharp end. Further increasing the ﬁeld can pull out 
the apex of liquid cone as ﬁne droplets or a stream. With E-jet printing technology, 
high aspect ratio patterns with 50 nm lateral resolution have been demonstrated. 
Printing can be considered as a different kind of nanomanufacturing process, not 
characterized by its patterning resolution but by its ability to deposit patterned

[Page 323]

Pattern Formation by Printing

[Page 324]
nanomaterials. Although a wide variety of nanomaterials have been developed over 
the past decades, including a variety of nanoparticles and nanowires, carbon nano-
tube, graphene, and a range of 2D nanomaterials, the applications of these 
nanomaterials in electronic devices have not been very successful. One of the key 
reasons is the integration issue. The extraordinary functions of nanomaterials only 
work on individual entity, such as single nanowire, single carbon nanotube, or a 
single layer graphene. Although excellent working devices have been demonstrated 
by integrating single entity of nanomaterial into devices in laboratories, they cannot 
be done in large scale or as mass manufacturing process. Printing offers a solution 
[37]. By formulating the nanomaterials into inks and printing them, large scale 
integration of nanomaterials into devices becomes possible. Though the printing 
does not have ﬁne resolution as conventional nanofabrication, many electronic 
devices, such as sensors, displays, or solar cells, do not have to be as small as 
those in an integrated circuit chip. Once the nanomaterials are printed, they become 
part of electronic devices and play a key role with their nanoscale properties. The key 
aspect of printing is that large scale manufacturing can be done with high throughput 
and low cost. It opens a new avenue for widening the application of nanomaterials.

Chapter 9 
Indirect Nanofabrication 
9.1 
Introduction 
The nanofabrication technologies introduced in the previous chapters so far have a 
common feature, that is, the dimension of a structure or pattern is deﬁned directly by 
a lithographic means, whether through direct patterning (electron beam, focused ion 
beam, laser beam, scanning probes, inkjet printing), or through a mask (photomask 
or stencil mask) or by a mold (nanoimprint or soft lithography), and/or combined 
with pattern transfer such as etching or liftoff. Though each lithographic technique 
has its own capability and limit, most of aforementioned techniques are capable of 
sub-10 nm patterning resolution after years of development both in lithography 
equipment and in patterning materials as well as processes [1]. 
It should be aware that to achieve such ultimate resolution capability, one is 
assumed to have access to the expensive state-of-the-art equipment, which may be a 
luxury that many do not have. Some nanofabrication practitioners may have to go 
with whatever equipment they can access and try their best to explore the potential of 
their existing fabrication facility or to ﬁnd ways to get round of the resolution limit of 
their own equipment. The indirect nanofabrication technologies to be introduced in 
this chapter are those clever ways of getting round of the limits of existing fabrica-
tion equipment. They are indirect because the pattern dimension is not predeﬁned by 
a direct lithographic technique. Here is a simple example; if one cannot make a very 
narrow line pattern, say sub-10 nm linewidth, by optical or e-beam or scanning 
probe, one can start with a wide line pattern and take away bit by bit from both sides 
until it becomes very narrow. This is the sort of indirect nanofabrication technique to 
be discussed, and the principle can be applied to many other ways of narrowing 
down a pattern structural dimension. 
It is difﬁcult to categorize each indirect nanofabrication techniques because there 
can be many diverse methods to overcome the existing fabrication limits. The above 
example can be considered as an extension of etching techniques introduced in 
Chap. 7. It is a lateral etching instead of vertical etching, without direct involvement
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_9
327

[Page 328]
of lithographic patterning. There are also methods utilizing material deposition to 
create pattern structures whose lateral dimension is not deﬁned by vertical lithogra-
phy but by the thickness of deposited ﬁlm on the sidewall of a patterned structure. 
Generally, indirect nanofabrication is not a straightforward “lithography plus pattern 
transfer” process but rather more complex, and several process steps may be 
involved. In most cases, they do not have the ﬂexibility and versatility enjoyed by 
direct nanofabrication technologies, for example, making arbitrary shapes of patterns 
can be difﬁcult with indirect techniques. Nevertheless, indirect nanofabrication is a 
research area where many tricks have been played to push down the limit of existing 
nanofabrication capabilities.
328
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
9.2 
Sidewall Lithography 
Sidewall lithography was the name given to a technique which achieves narrow 
width of pattern structure by thin ﬁlm coating on the sidewalls of supporting 
structures and selectively etching away the supporting structures. The technique is 
schematically explained in Fig. 9.1. The supporting pattern structures are ﬁrst made 
with low-resolution direct lithographic process, for example, optical lithography 
(Fig. 9.1a). The second step is to conformably coat a thin ﬁlm covering the 
supporting structure (Fig. 9.1b). The thin ﬁlm has to be a material different from 
the supporting structure. A selective RIE process removes the top thin ﬁlm cover of 
the supporting structure (Fig. 9.1c). The ﬁnal step is to selectively etch away the 
supporting structure, leaving the sidewall thin ﬁlm standing alone as the ﬁnal pattern 
(Fig. 9.1d). Figure 9.2 shows the SiO2 structures created by conformal coating SiO2 
onto silicon supporting structure and selective etching [2]. It is apparent that the 
lateral width of ﬁnal pattern structure depends only on the thickness of thin ﬁlm 
coated on the sidewalls of supporting structures. So is the name of sidewall lithog-
raphy. There are two critical requirements that enable the sidewall lithography:
(c) 
Substrate 
(b) 
Substrate 
Conformable 
coating
Supporting 
structure 
(a) 
(d) 
Substrate 
Final 
structure 
Fig. 9.1 Schematic of sidewall lithography process: (a) patterning supporting structure; (b) 
conformable coating; (c) removal of top cover thin ﬁlm by RIE; (d) removal of supporting structure

[Page 329]
(1) the supporting structures must have a vertical sidewall, so that when the top cover 
is removed by RIE, there is a minimum removal of the sidewall coating material; 
(2) the two materials for the thin ﬁlm and the supporting structure have to be 
incompatible in RIE or chemical wet etching, so that the supporting structure can 
be removed without damaging the sidewall thin ﬁlm structure.
9.2
Sidewall Lithography
329
Fig. 9.2 (a) Circular SiO2 structure and (b) SiO2 grating structure made from removing silicon 
supporting structures. (Reprinted from [2] with permission) 
Fine resolution structures created by sidewall deposition were ﬁrst reported in 
1983, which was then called edge deﬁned technique [3]. It consisted of repeatedly 
applied CVD deposition, reactive ion etching, and selective isotropic etching with a 
proper choice of materials and etching chemistry. Each cycle doubles the number of 
lines in the pattern. Gratings with ~40 nm period was then fabricated by doubling 
the number of lines in a 160 nm period grating twice. Later a much simple process 
was proposed, in which the supporting photoresist was silylated to replace the 
conformable thin ﬁlm coating step [4]. A minimum of 50 nm wide and 200 nm 
high silicon nitride patterns had been fabricated by RIE transfer of sidewall patterns, 
which was inconceivable at the time by any other lithographic techniques. 
Since it was reported, sidewall lithography has been continuously employed till 
present day to make ﬁne pattern structures without using high resolution lithography 
tools [5, 6]. The basic concept is the same. The only variations are different 
combinations of thin ﬁlm and supporting structure materials. There have been 
reports using platinum coating on amorphous silicon to make 50 nm Pt patterns 
[7], using SiO2 as the supporter and Si3N4 as the thin ﬁlm to make 100 nm line and 
space grating for nanoimprint [8], using polysilicon and SiO2 combination to make 
sub-10 nm SiO2 pattern structures as masks to etch into silicon [9], or using patterned 
silicon as supporting structures and thermal oxidation process to modify the sidewall 
surface of the silicon supporters while protected by Si3N4 at the top [10]. Whatever 
the combination of materials is, as long as the two aforementioned critical require-
ments are observed, sub-100 nm patterns can be made with low cost, albeit a slight 
increase in process complexity. However, the ﬂexibility of making any shapes of 2D

[Page 330]
pattern layout is restricted with the technique, because the ﬁnal pattern layout relies 
on the pattern of supporting structures as shown in Fig. 9.2. 
330
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
Fig. 9.3 Multiple patterning by self-aligned spacer process. (Reprinted from [12] with permission) 
The most prominent example of sidewall lithography is the application to IC 
manufacturing. As described in 2.7.5 of Chap. 2, deep UV optical lithography with 
193 nm wavelength is only able to pattern at 130 nm resolution. With immersion lens 
technique, the resolution can be pushed down to 38 nm. Further down scaling from 
14 nm node IC manufacturing onward, multiple patterning processes were used 
when EUV lithography was not ready. One of the dominant multiple patterning 
processes is self-aligned spacer technology which is exactly the same as the sidewall 
lithography illustrated in Fig. 9.1, if comparing it with the self-aligned double 
patterning (SADP) process in Fig. 2.40. With the help of spacer which is the sidewall 
deposition, pattern density can be doubled [11]. Figure 9.3 is the demonstration that 
38 nm half-pitch (hp) line pattern by single 193 nm immersion lithography was 
reduced to 19 nm hp. by self-aligned double patterning (SADP) and further reduced 
to 9.5 nm by self-aligned quadruple patterning (SAQP) [12]. By self-aligned octuplet 
patterning (SAOP), the half-pitch was eventually reduced to 5.5 nm [13]. 
While the sidewall lithography relies on selective removal of supporting struc-
tures to leave the sidewall thin ﬁlm as the ﬁnal pattern structures, the supporting 
structures can be left where they are, and the sidewall thin ﬁlm serves as a nanoscale 
insulator. Figure 9.4 illustrates the concept of adhesion lithography which creates 
nanoscale insulating gaps between metal electrodes [14]. The process starts with 
depositing the supporting structure which is a metal (M1) as indicated in Fig. 9.4a.  A  
metallophilic self-assembled monolayer (SAM) is then deposited to cover the whole 
substrate including the M1 structure (Fig. 9.4b). A second metal (M2) is deposited to 
cover the whole substrate again (Fig. 9.4c). Now an adhesive tape is applied to the 
whole substrate surface (Fig. 9.4d), and then part of M2 which is in contact with the 
SAM region is peeled away due to the weak adhesion with M1 because of the SAM 
coating (Fig. 9.4e). Finally, the substrate is left with two metallic structures (M1, 
M2), as seen in Fig. 9.4f where the SAM layer in between serves as an insulator 
which is only 12 nm wide averagely. Such nanoscale gaps are created without costly 
equipment and under ambient conditions on a timescale of seconds. The insulating 
layer can also be deposited by atomic layer deposition (ALD) on a patterned

[Page 331]
substrate and metal deposition followed by peel-off using an adhesive tape, which 
have created the insulating gap as small as 1 nm [15]. Using adhesive tape to replace 
liftoff was also applied to make a variety of metallic patterns including sub-15 nm 
nanogaps in the so-called “sketch and peel” lithography [16]. Conformal metal 
coating on nonvertical silicon cones followed by peel-off was able to make metallic 
micronozzles as shown in Fig. 9.5 [17]. 
9.2
Sidewall Lithography
331
Fig. 9.4 Adhesion lithography to create nanogaps between metal electrodes (steps from a to f are 
described in the context) 
Fig. 9.5 SEM photo of 
metallic micronozzle array 
made by conformal coating 
and peel-off

Indirect Nanofabrication
(a) 
GaAs 
AlGaAs 
(b) 
Fig. 9.6 Selective etching of MBE grown alternating layers: (a) MBE growth and sample cleaving; 
(b) selective etching 
An extension of sidewall lithography is to planarly grow GaAs and AlGaAs 
alternative layers by molecular beam epitaxy (MBE) with precisely controlled 
thickness and then selectively etch away one of the materials, leaving the other as 
the ﬁnal pattern structure. In order to get etchant solution into the buried layers, the 
substrate after growing the layers should be cleaved so that the alternating layer 
structures are exposed to wet chemical etchant, as schematically shown in Fig. 9.6a. 
Selective removal is possible because GaAs and AlGaAs have the property that they 
can be etched in different acidic solutions. For example, GaAs can be etched away in 
the mixture of citric acid and H2O2 (5:1 mixture) without affecting the 
Al0.32Ga0.68As [18], or Al0.7Ga0.3As can be etched away in a diluted solution of 
hydroﬂuoric acid without affecting the GaAs [19]. Acidic etching removes one of the 
crystalline materials and leaves the other alternating layers as the densely packed line 
pattern structure such as the one schematically shown in Fig. 9.6b. It is apparent that 
these line patterns will have the width equal to the thickness of MBE grown layers. A 
grating at 6 nm half-pitch was fabricated by cleaving AlGaAs superlattice grown on 
GaAs with molecular beam epitaxy and selectively etching away the AlGaAs layers 
in dilute hydroﬂuoric acid, which was then used as a nanoimprint mold [20]. 
9.3 
Dimensional Subtraction 
Taking away bit by bit from sideway of a wide line to make it narrow is the idea of 
indirect patterning by lateral subtraction. As introduced in Chap. 7, RIE always has 
certain degree of isotropic etching. While the etching is going deeper, it is also going 
wider laterally. The anisotropy can be controlled by tuning the plasma condition. 
Figure 9.7 shows a series of SEM photos of etched silicon stripes [21]. It demon-
strates how the stripes are narrowed with the progression of etching time. Eventually, 
the structure dimension at the top can be made inﬁnitely small. That is how very 
sharp silicon tips are made, as shown in Fig. 9.8 [22].

[Page 333]
9.3
Dimensional Subtraction
333
Fig. 9.7 Lateral subtraction of silicon line pattern in RIE: (a) etching 1 min; (b) etching 2 min; (c) 
etching 3 min 
Fig. 9.8 Silicon tips made 
by RIE with nanometer 
scale apex 
(a)
(b)
(c) 
Fig. 9.9 Schematic of oxidation sharpening process: (a) initial tip geometry; (b) oxidation; (c) ﬁnal 
tip geometry after removal of oxide layer 
Another lateral subtraction technique, particularly suitable for scaling down 
silicon structures, is oxidation sharpening. Oxidation sharpening technique was 
ﬁrst reported in 1991 [23]. It is known that silicon can be turned into silicon dioxide 
in thermal oxidation process. Once becoming oxide, it can be etched away in 
hydrogen ﬂuoride (HF) acid. The process is schematically illustrated in Fig. 9.9.

[Page 334]
An initial large silicon cone structure (Fig. 9.9a) is thermally oxidized. The thermal 
oxidation “consumes” away the silicon from surface down (Fig. 9.9b). The longer 
the thermal oxidation process, the more the silicon is converted into silicon dioxide. 
After the thermal oxidation, a HF wet etching removes the oxide “shell,” leaving a 
sharpened silicon tip (Fig. 9.9c). Generally, a “fat” structure can be “slimmed” down 
by the oxidation and HF etching. It should be noted that the oxidation slows down 
with the oxidation layer growing thicker. Multiple oxidation-etching may be 
performed if signiﬁcant amount of silicon has to be removed. Atomically sharp 
silicon tips, with a tip radius of <1 nm, have been made with two consecutive 
oxidation and HF etching processes [23]. Thermal oxidation requires temperature 
more than 950 °C. Alternatively, silicon substrate can be wet oxidized by immersion 
into HNO3 solution. A saturated oxide layer about 1.8 nm thick can be formed in 
5 min, which is then stripped away in HF solution. By repeated oxidation and HF 
etching, a fat silicon structure can be thinned down. In this way, 40 nm silicon lines 
were reduced to 10 nm after 15 cycles of oxidation and etching [24].

9
Indirect Nanofabrication
silicon
nitride
oxide 
(a)
(b) 
(c)
(d) 
Nanochannels 
Fig. 9.10 Schematic of nanochannel fabrication by lateral oxidation and selective etching 
Instead of stripping oxide, the oxide layer can remain while the silicon is etched 
away, which results in holes or trenches whose dimension is determined by the 
amount of remaining silicon. The fabrication process is a combination of anisotropic 
etching in potassium hydroxide (KOH) solution, local oxidation of silicon 
(LOCOS), and plasma etching of silicon. Such a process has been utilized to 
fabricate sub-100 nm ﬂuidic channels on a silicon substrate [25]. The process is 
schematically shown in Fig. 9.10. It starts from patterning Si3N4 mask of much 
wider dimension (2.5–3 μm) on a < 100> silicon substrate. This can be done by 
standard low-resolution contact photolithography. The lines are patterned parallel to 
<110> directions (Fig. 9.10a). Once the Si3N4 lines are deﬁned, wet chemical 
etching of silicon by KOH is performed at the bare silicon area (Fig. 9.10b). The 
next step is to oxidize the silicon by LOCOS at 1100 °C to grow SiO2 to a thickness 
of more than 500 nm in both depth and lateral directions (Fig. 9.10c). The ﬁnal step

[Page 335]
is to remove the Si3N4 mask and etch away the silicon sandwiched between the oxide 
walls (Fig. 9.10d). Thin channels as narrow as 40 nm can be fabricated without using 
any expensive lithography tools. This is a typical lateral subtraction process which 
takes advantage of the fact that silicon, nitride, and oxide can each be selectively 
etched without harming the others. 
9.3
Dimensional Subtraction
335
Fig. 9.11 Magnetic read/ 
write head trimmed by FIB. 
(Reprinted from [27] with 
permission) 
The lateral subtraction also has a name called “post-trimming.” In fact, post-
fabrication trimming is a widely used technique for feature size reduction [1]. For 
example, RIE has been used to trim electron beam exposed HSQ resist structure to 
achieve sub-10 nm pattern resolution. With CF4 plasma trimming, an initial 21 nm 
HSQ line feature can be reduced to 5.3 nm [26]. Focused ion beam (FIB) is best 
suited to perform the trimming task, due to its precisely controlled scanning and 
sputter etching nature. FIB is ﬂexible to sputter away materials from a very precise 
location. The best example of FIB trimming is the manufacturing of magnetic read/ 
write heads, whereby the FIB trimming has become a standard volume manufactur-
ing process. A magnetic read/write head initially patterned by optical lithography 
can be further trimmed down to sub-100 nm dimension by FIB sputter etching. 
Figure 9.11 is an example of FIB trimmed magnetic head [27]. An initial square-
shaped pattern has been trimmed into a thin line. 
There can be many clever ways to realize feature size reduction. Figure 9.12 
illustrates how overexposure of positive-tone resist can produce much reduced size 
of negative-tone resist image. The standard mask pattern looks like what is shown in 
Fig. 9.12a. By overexposure, each dot expands laterally (white regions). After 
development, the remaining resist becomes the pattern of reversed tone with much 
reduced dimension (black dots inside white region), as shown in Fig. 9.12b. The size

[Page 336]
and period of reversed-tone dots array can be varied by varying the overexposure 
dose and the initial mask pattern design. It has been shown that for initial 125 nm 
positive-tone dot design, the exposure at 230 mJ/cm2 produced 107 nm negative-
tone dots, against the normal exposure dose of 110 mJ/cm2 . At 350 mJ/cm2 , the 
negative-tone dots were reduced to 53 nm [28]. 
336
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
(a)
(b) 
Fig. 9.12 Feature size reduction by overexposure of positive-tone resist: (a) initial positive-tone 
mask pattern; (b) ﬁnal negative-tone pattern (four little dots in the middle) after overexposure of the 
pattern in (a) 
(a)
(b) 
Fig. 9.13 Wet chemical etching of silicon to create nanoscale see-through hole: (a) reverse 
pyramid-shaped cavity is made in the top layer silicon of a SOI wafer; (b) removal of silicon and 
oxide from bottom side to reveal the hole 
Subtraction can also be performed vertically to create nanoscale see-through 
holes or slits. An example is to utilize anisotropic wet chemical etching of silicon. 
As described in Chap. 7, wet chemical etching of silicon in alkaline solution is 
dependent on crystalline orientation, i.e., etching fast in one direction but slow in 
another direction. A typical example is the etching of <100> silicon in KOH. The 
slow etching in <111> direction creates an etched proﬁle with sloped sidewall. By 
controlling the mask opening size and etch depth, a reverse pyramid-shaped cavity 
can be created as shown in Fig. 9.13a. In this case, the reversed cone is made in a SOI 
substrate, and the etching is controlled to stop just when the tip of the reversed cone 
reaches the SiO2 insulating layer. A second deep RIE of silicon from the bottom side

[Page 337]
removes the silicon underneath the pyramid tip. A further etching of the SiO2 layer 
reveals the cone tip which becomes a see-through hole, as shown in Fig. 9.13b. 
Square nanopore arrays with an average pore size of 60 nm as well as the smallest 
nanoslits of 13 nm have been made by this simple technique [29]. 
9.4
Dimensional Addition
337
9.4 
Dimensional Addition 
While subtraction can produce narrow lines, the opposite is to produce narrow gaps 
by lateral addition. Conventional technique to create a narrow gap is direct e-beam 
lithography of resist and pattern transfer by RIE or direct FIB cutting of a continuous 
line. The lateral addition technique is to start with a wider gap and then narrow it by 
adding material bit by bit to close in the gap. The initial gap can be produced by 
conventional lithography and etching, as illustrated in Fig. 9.14a. Subsequent 
deposition of material closes the gap by hanging over the gap (Fig. 9.14b). By 
carefully controlling the initial gap distance and material deposition, 2–3 nm gap 
between two gold electrodes has been created [30]. The most ﬂexible lateral addition 
process is to utilize electron beam or focused ion beam induced deposition, by which 
the amount of material deposited and the location of deposition can be precisely 
controlled. Figure 9.14c shows a sequence of process to produce a narrow gap by 
electron beam induced deposition of platinum. The initial 120 nm gap has been 
narrowed to 5 nm by gradual deposition of material from both sides [31]. 
Another additive method is to utilize the fact that oxidation can make materials 
grow “fatter,” which can indirectly reduce the distance between two originally 
“slim” structures. Figure 9.15 illustrates such a scheme. The process starts with 
standard optical or e-beam lithography to pattern gold and chrome double-layer 
structure (Fig. 9.15a). The chrome is then allowed to oxidize to become CrO, which 
expands and overhangs on top of gold pads (Fig. 9.15b). Evaporation of gold 
blanketly covers the overhanging CrO pads and the rest of substrate (Fig. 9.15c). 
A liftoff to remove the CrO pads together with gold on top leaves the surface with 
gold patterns having gaps which are deﬁned by the overhanging CrO structures 
(Fig. 9.15d). Using this nanomasking method, gold electrode gaps of 7.4 nm with a 
standard deviation of 2.2 nm have been produced [32]. 
Instead of oxidation, shrinking of lateral dimension can be realized by material 
deposition. As illustrated in Fig. 9.16, the initial holes or gaps made by optical 
lithography may be big (Fig. 9.16a). By depositing thin ﬁlm, the diameter of the 
distance between adjacent patterns can be reduced, depending on the thickness of 
thin ﬁlm (Fig. 9.16b). Plasma etching back removes the thin ﬁlm from the top and 
bottom (Fig. 9.16c) but leaves the sidewall thin ﬁlm intact. A subsequent pattern 
transfer by RIE results in much smaller holes or gaps in the substrate than original 
photoresist pattern (Fig. 9.16d). It is very much like the spacer concept shown in 
Fig. 9.1, but here the spacer acts as an etching mask instead of the pattern structure 
itself. A similar approach was used to make nanoscale sealed channels as illustrated 
in Fig. 9.17a. Conformal coating not only reduced the diameter of circular channel to

[Page 338]
338

from [31] with permission) 
Fig. 9.15 Nanomasking process to produce gold electrode gaps: (a) initial Cr on top of Au pattern; 
(b) oxidization of Cr; (c) blanket coating of gold; (d) removal of CrO cap

20 nm but also completely sealed it from top, as the SEM image shown in 
Fig. 9.17b [33].
9.4
Dimensional Addition
339
(a)
(b) 
(c)

Fig. 9.16 Creation of nanoholes by lateral addition of material: (a) original photoresist pattern; (b) 
thin ﬁlm deposition; (c) plasma etching back; (d) transfer to substrate 
Fig. 9.17 (a) Circular channels with reduced diameter and sealed by ALD; (b) SEM image of 
fabricated nanoscale channels. (Reprinted from [33] with permission) 
It has been found that ion irradiation of a freestanding thin ﬁlm can induce free 
movement of atoms in the thin ﬁlm. If there is a hole in a freestanding thin ﬁlm, ion 
beam irradiation of the hole and surrounding area can cause the hole to shrink, even 
close up completely. This so-called “ion beam sculpting” technique has been used to 
produce nanopores of less than 10 nm in thin membranes [34]. Conventionally, 
nanopores are made by RIE or direct FIB etching of a membrane. The direct 
technique is always limited by the resolution of lithography tool and process. The 
smallest hole etched by focused ion beam in a 30-nm-thick silicon nitride membrane 
is around 30 nm [35]. Further reduction of nanopore size beyond the resolution limit 
of FIB can be realized by the ion beam sculpting process. For example, an initial 
100 nm hole in a 100-nm-thick silicon nitride membrane has been reduced to 15 nm 
after 18 scans of Ga ion beam in a 1–2 μm2 area surrounding the hole. The complete 
process takes about 1 min. Figure 9.18a–d shows a series of SEM photos of a

[Page 340]
nanopore, representing gradual reduction of pore size from 100 nm to 5 nm through 
scanning of focused ion beam. The nanopore can be made even smaller if electrons 
instead of ions are used. By employing 200 keV electron beam in a transmission 
electron microscope (TEM), the 5 nm nanopore shown in Fig. 9.18e has been 
reduced to 1 nm [35]. The exact mechanism of beam induced nanopore shrink is 
still not clear. It is believed that high energy ions or electron irradiation of the thin 
membrane has increased the mobility and diffusivity of atoms surrounding a hole. 
The “melting” of material causes the close-up of nanopore. Nanopores can also be 
created by melting prepatterned polymeric materials because heating can induce 
reﬂow of polymers. Pore size as small as 6 nm was produced in SU-8 membrane by 
heating up the initially nanoimprinted holes in the polymer membrane [36]. 
340
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
Fig. 9.18 Reduction of nanopore size by ion beam irradiation: (a) 100 nm, (b) 40 nm, (c) 20 nm, 
(d) 10 nm, (e) 5 nm (TEM image). (Reprinted from [35] with permission) 
9.5 
Indirect Fabrication of Nanogaps 
Nanogaps are a class of nanostructures which have drawn many interests from many 
ﬁelds of applications. In the last 6 years since 2018, there are more than 600 research 
papers published on the topic of nanogaps, more than 100 papers each year, 
according to the Web of Science database. Fabrication of nanogaps is also a research 
area where many ingenious indirect methods have been created, particularly the 
techniques on making metallic nanogaps [37]. The previous sections of this chapter 
already introduced some examples of indirect fabrication of nanogaps, such as the 
methods shown in Figs. 9.4, 9.10, 9.14, 9.15, and 9.16. Here are some more methods 
to realize extremely small gaps. 
In addition to the sidewall lithography, dimensional subtraction or addition, 
electromigration effect has been utilized to create nanogaps whereby atoms migrate 
under the inﬂuence of electric ﬁeld. When a conductive track is sufﬁciently narrow, 
electromigration can cause the broken track, which has been the major cause of 
interconnect failures in integrated circuits and other electronic devices [38]. How-
ever, this phenomenon can be used to make small gaps. 2–3 nm gaps between two 
electrodes have been made by this method [39]. FIB milling can also induce 
breaking of single grain boundary in polycrystalline material, resulting in nanogaps 
as narrow as 1–2  nm  [40]. While the electromigration breaks a continuous electrode 
to create nanogaps, ﬁeld emission current can reduce a large gap by accumulated

[Page 341]
atomic migration. An initial gap separation of 50–71 nm was uniformly reduced to 
<10 nm by the accumulation of nickel atoms between two nickel tips at the ﬁeld 
emission current of 300 nA [41]. Small gaps can also be produced by mechanically 
breaking a wire. Mechanical cracking is carried out with stress concentration in 
prepatterned notches and necks deﬁned by lithographic patterning [1]. For example, 
thin gold lines were made on a silicon wafer, and the silicon wafer was mechanically 
bended to generate a tensile stress in the gold lines. A breakage occurred at the 
narrowest part the line and made gaps as narrow as 0.8 nm [42]. 
9.5
Indirect Fabrication of Nanogaps
341
Fig. 9.19 Laser-induced 
oxidation of Ti and selective 
wet etching to create 5 nm 
nanogaps. (Reprinted from 
[43] with permission) 
While a nanogap can be produced by deposition from both sides of a large gap as 
seen in Fig. 9.14, it can also be created by modifying the material property from both 
sides of a large gap and subsequently selective etching to remove the middle part. 
Figure 9.19 illustrates such a scheme based on laser-induced thermal oxidation effect 
[43]. Instead of exposing photoresist by a laser beam, titanium (Ti) is used as the 
imaging layer which can be oxidized by laser-induced thermal effect. The threshold 
energy for laser oxidation of Ti is about 127.3 J/cm2 [44]. When scanning with two 
laser beams close enough, Ti on the scanning path is oxidized but leaves a gap where 
Ti is not oxidized because there is an energy “valley” which is below the oxidation 
threshold. A nanogap is then obtained by wet etching to remove Ti in the middle 
because of the high contrast selective etching rate between Ti and TiO2 with the 
etchant of HF: (25%)/H2O2 (30%)/H2O = 1:1.5:100 [43].

[Page 342]
342
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
9.6 
Super-resolution Patterning 
It has been introduced in Chap. 2 that the resolution of optical lithography is limited 
by diffraction. Super-resolution is the imaging technique that can break or overcome 
the diffraction limit, which is also called subdiffraction techniques. If closely 
examining the light intensity distribution shown in Fig. 2.4 of Chap. 2, one can 
see that the intensity shape is always narrow at the top and broadened downward. If 
the exposure of photoresist is only conﬁned to the top section of the intensity, then 
the obtained resolution would be much higher. One of the developments to achieve 
super-resolution has been the two-color photolithography [45]. The basic idea is to 
use two wavelengths (two colors) of photon beams. One wavelength beam performs 
normal photoresist exposure (activation), and the other wavelength beam stops the 
photoresist exposure (inhibition). By surrounding the activation beam with the 
inhibition beam, the exposure is conﬁned to only very high intensity part which is 
of much narrow distribution, hence very high resolution. 
There are two ways to realize such scheme. One is to use an absorbance 
modulation layer (AML) as illustrated in Fig. 9.20. The AML is a photochromic 
layer which is initially transparent but becomes opaque when illuminated by λ2 
wavelength beam. The photoresist underneath the AML only responds to λ1 wave-
length beam. The λ2 beam exposure makes the transparent opening of AML only at 
the nodal point which allows only the peak point of λ1 beam intensity distribution to 
expose the photoresist underneath. Super-resolution optical lithography is then 
achieved. With λ1 = 325 nm and λ2 = 633 nm, minimum 30 nm line features 
were produced in PMMA resist, which is only one-tenth of the illuminating wave-
length [46]. The other way to realize two-color photolithography is to formulate the 
photoresist whose polymerization is inhibited at one wavelength but activated at 
another wavelength. With a femtosecond laser (λ1 = 800 nm) as the activation beam
Fig. 9.20 Super-resolution 
optical lithography enabled 
by absorbance modulation 
layer 
Substrate 
Photochromic layer
λ1 λ2 λ1 
Photoresist layer

[Page 343]
and a doughnut-shaped continuous laser (λ2 = 375 nm) as the inhibition beam, the 
smallest line feature of 9 nm and two-line pitch of 52 nm were achieved 
[47]. Recently, the method was employed to make 3D optical disk with petabit 
(1015 bits) storage capacity [48].
9.6
Super-resolution Patterning

In proximity optical lithography, i.e., 1:1 optical imaging by a mask aligner, there 
is an interesting phenomenon that the light intensity distribution changes its shape 
with changing gap distance between mask and substrate. Figure 9.21 shows the 
simulated light intensity distributions at two different gap distances between mask 
and substrate (photoresist surface). If the gap distance is inﬁnitely small (assumed 
0.001 μm in simulation), a square-shaped light distribution has almost the same 
width as the mask feature opening (1 μm). When the mask moves away from the 
photoresist surface, the light distribution will change due to diffraction. However, 
there is a gap distance (1 μm in this case) at which the top section of the distribution 
is much narrower than the opening width of mask feature. Further increasing the gap 
distance, the distribution becomes ﬂat and the high resolution peak disappears. If the 
mask-substrate gap is controlled at this optimum distance and the exposure dose is 
controlled to enable only the top section of light intensity to participate the photo-
resist exposure, then one can expect the photoresist image to be much smaller than 
the mask feature width. In the simulation example shown in Fig. 9.21, the line 
feature, obtained by separating the mask from photoresist surface with 1 μm gap and 
utilizing only the top part of light intensity, will be only 300 nm instead of 1 μm of  
the mask design. 
Such phenomenon has been utilized in X-ray lithography, since X-ray lithogra-
phy is also a proximity imaging [49]. Figure 9.22 compares the SEM image of X-ray 
mask features which is measured to be 152 nm line opening (Fig. 9.22a) and exposed 
line image in SAL605 negative-tone resist which is measured to be 61 nm

[Page 344]
(Fig. 9.22b). The demagniﬁcation is around 2.5. As aforementioned, to produce high 
resolution patterns by a low-resolution mask in proximity X-ray lithography, not 
only should the mask-substrate gap distance be carefully controlled and optimized 
but also the careful control of exposure dose and development time. The 61 nm lines 
in SAL605 negative-tone resist were obtained by 1 min 45 s development time. The 
same resist feature became 43 ~ 46 nm if developed for 2 min [49]. The longer 
development time has tailored the exposure level toward higher threshold intensity at 
which the distribution is narrower. A simulated example is shown in Fig. 9.23 where 
the intensity proﬁle is generated by optical interference lithography. By controlling 
the threshold intensity at 1.5, the exposed lines can be much narrower. 
344
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
Fig. 9.22 Demagniﬁcation of X-ray proximity printing: (a) mask feature at 152 nm opening width; 
(b) exposure resist feature at 61 nm line width. (Reprinted from [49] with permission) 
Controlling exposure dose and development time to acquire different exposed 
feature dimension in resists is also a common practice in electron beam lithography. 
It is a well-known fact that the highest resolution e-beam lithography is only 
achievable in resists having the lowest sensitivity such as PMMA, HSQ, and some 
inorganic resists. The reason is that an electron beam has a Gaussian distribution 
both in beam current and in electron energy and that only the high energy electrons 
and peak intensity of beam current, which is very narrow in distribution, can expose 
a low sensitivity resist. The resultant feature will be narrow because the broad 
portion of the beam, which mainly consists of low energy scattering electrons and 
low number of electrons, will have negligible effect on the polymer molecular chains 
in the resist. This also true for laser direct write lithography because a laser beam also 
has a Gaussian-shaped energy distribution. By laser-induced thermal oxidation of Ti 
ﬁlm, which is of much lower sensitivity to laser energy than exposure of photore-
sists, 40 nm feature size was produced by a 532 nm wavelength laser [44]. 
The same practice can be applied to focused ion beam (FIB) as well. Although the 
beam current distribution in FIB is generally Gaussian like, it has a larger tail than an 
electron beam because of the high energy spread in a gallium liquid metal ion source.

[Page 345]
This is one of the reasons that the resolution of patterning by FIB is generally not as 
high as that by an electron beam. However, by utilizing the beam proﬁle, it is 
possible to fabricate structures much smaller than the beam size. Figure 9.24 
shows how a narrow gap can be made by FIB sputter etching [50]. The computer 
simulated contours of FIB sputtering in Fig. 9.24a represent the successive 
sputtering proﬁles in the Au ﬁlm as the FIB sputtering dose increases (Au ﬁlm of 
27 nm thickness on top of SiO2 with a 1.6 nm Ti seed layer). There is a sputtering

Fig. 9.24 (a) Simulated contours of FIB sputtering of Au-Ti ﬁlm; (b) SEM image of 3 nm nanogap

[Page 346]
dose at which the Au-Ti ﬁlm is just broken through by the FIB etching and a narrow 
gap is created in the ﬁlm. Figure 9.24b is the SEM image of an actual 3 nm nanogap 
created by FIB sputtering with a beam diameter of ~12 nm. To produce this nanogap, 
it is very critical to be able to control the FIB sputtering to stop just at the moment 
when the beam tip breaks through the Au-Ti ﬁlm. To do this, an in situ measurement 
of current ﬂowing through the electrode is performed, which is connected to the FIB 
scan controller. The beam sputtering scan is switched off immediately when the 
current ﬂow through the thin ﬁlm electrode drops to zero, which indicates that the 
electrode has been cut through by FIB sputtering.
346
9
Indirect Nanofabrication
Fig. 9.25 Nanopore produced by FIB sputtering and ion milling: (a) FIB sputtering from top side 
of a membrane; (b) ion milling from bottom side of membrane 
The Gaussian distribution of a FIB determines that a hole sputtered by FIB will 
also be in the shape of a Gaussian proﬁle, as shown in Fig. 9.25a. Then taking away 
materials layer by layer from the other side would eventually reveal the tip of this 
Gaussian-shaped hole and create a very small pore which diameter is determined by 
the Gaussian distribution width as illustrated in Fig. 9.25b. The key step in making 
the nanopore is the precise control of ion milling depth in the backside. The ion 
milling has to stop just as the membrane has been broken through. To achieve this 
precise control, an ion counter can be mounted on the other side of membrane. When 
ions start to ﬂow through and are detected by the ion counter, it signals the 
breakthrough of the membrane and the time to stop the ion milling. The size of 
nanopore can be further reduced by low dose exposure of ions after a hole is created, 
which promotes the mobility of atoms surrounding the hole and shrinks the hole, i.e., 
the ion beam sculpting mentioned previously. Nanopore with diameter as small as 
1.8 nm have been created in Si3N4 membrane by the technique [34].
