Chapter 10 
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly 
10.1 
Introduction 
The nanofabrication technologies described in the previous chapters so far can be 
characterized as conventional technologies, in the sense that they always involve one 
way or another lithographic patterning and pattern transfer. They are still the same 
as, or not too much different from, the technologies for manufacturing integrated 
circuits, which originated more than 60 years ago when the ﬁrst patent on planar 
integrated circuit was granted, though today’s technologies can make pattern struc-
tures a thousand times smaller. It is a “top-down” approach that complex structures 
are built up by patterning layers upon layers from the surface of a planar substrate. 
The capabilities of top-down nanofabrication technologies have been amply dem-
onstrated in previous chapters. The smallest features which can be made by top-
down approach are, however, always limited by the available fabrication tools, being 
either lithography or pattern transfer. On the other hand, nature has demonstrated its 
ability to produce extremely complex living organisms by self-organization and self-
construction for billions of years, all of which are taking place at molecular level 
which is an order of magnitude smaller than a nanometer. One surely could ask the 
question why a desired nanostructure cannot be made by molecular self-assembly, 
which is a “bottom-up” approach where molecules autonomously “grow” into 
nanostructures. 
Such an attempt had started more than 30 years ago, pioneered by, not surpris-
ingly, chemists and biologists [1]. To them, manipulation of molecules is a familiar 
subject. In some areas, nanostructures constructed by molecular self-assembly have 
been quite successful. For example, self-assembled monolayers (SAMs) have been 
widely used in nanoscale patterning. Creation and application of carbon nanotubes 
(CNT) is another example how nanostructures and objects can be grown in control-
lable manner. DNA molecular strands have been used as basic scaffolds to construct 
nanostructures. Controlled aggregation of colloidal particles is being widely utilized 
in formation of functional patterns and structures. There are other examples which
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_10
349

[Page 350]
demonstrate certain 2D or 3D pattern structures that can be formed by guided self-
assembly or self-organization from the interaction of molecular force or other 
interparticle forces.
350
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
The nanofabrication by self-assembly is inherently advantageous over conven-
tional top-down technologies in two aspects: one is the ability to construct structures 
in true nanoscale, i.e., at molecule level or at least below 10 nm at which current 
patterning technologies are struggling or unable to achieve; another is to make 
nanostructures cheaply. Nanostructuring by molecular self-assembly should be, in 
principle, low cost, because there are no complicated and expensive fabrication 
equipment involved. For self-assembly to replace current lithography processes, 
the grand challenge is how to control the synthesis and assembly of molecules into 
desired nanostructures. As Professor George M. Whitesides of Harvard University 
put it in 1995, “Although there are countless examples of self-assembly all around 
us—from molecular crystals to mammals—the basic rules that govern these assem-
blies are not understood in useful detail, and self-assembling processes cannot, in 
general, be designed and carried out ‘to order.’ Many of the ideas that are crucial to 
the development of this area, ‘molecular shape,’ the interplay between enthalpy and 
entropy, the nature of non-covalent forces that connect the particles in self-
assembled molecular aggregates, are simply not yet under the control of investiga-
tors” [2]. Gladly, such uncertainty for self-assembly to be one of the nanofabrication 
technologies has largely been eliminated after 30 years of continuous research 
efforts. Self-assembly processes can be designed and carried out to order. 
One has to be aware that the current level of nanofabrication by self-assembly is 
still very primitive. Most of the nanostructures made from self-assembly are array 
like, which is originated from the formation of crystal lattices by molecular attrac-
tions. In majority of cases, the self-assembly has to be guided or to take place on a 
template, which requires assistance from conventional top-down nanofabrication 
processes. The combination of top-down and bottom-up approaches, or the so-called 
top-down meets bottom-up, has been, and will be in the future, the main feature in 
the development of self-assembly nanofabrication. 
10.2 
Self-Assembly Processes 
Self-assembly has a very broad meaning. Any spontaneous formation of an organi-
zation from individual parts without human intervention can be called self-assembly. 
Self-assembly can take place either at molecular level or meso-/macroscopic level, 
from the formation of crystals to snowﬂakes and to universe. There are a number of 
distinctive features in self-assembly process: 
1. Self-assembly is spontaneous, automatic, and autonomous. Autonomous means 
that once the conditions for self-assembly to occur are satisﬁed, the assembly 
process starts automatically and spontaneously without human intervention.

[Page 351]
10.3
Self-Assembly of Atoms and Molecules
351
2. Self-assembly is the result of interaction of interfacial forces between individual 
entities. At molecular level, the interfacial forces involve non-covalent bonding 
force, typically van der Waals force, or weak covalent bonding force such as 
hydrogen bonds. At mesoscopic level, self-assembly may involve electrostatic 
and surface tension forces. In macroscopic scale, gravitational force, capillary 
force, or external electric or magnetic force may come into play [3]. 
3. The individual entities taking part in a self-assembly process have to be mobile in 
order for the self-assembly process to occur. Therefore, self-assembly process 
normally takes place in liquid phase or on a smooth surface so that the basic 
building blocks can move around and interact with each other. 
4. The formation of an ordered structure is usually the result of equilibrium state, at 
which individual parts keep equal distance to each other, not a random aggrega-
tion. The self-assembled structures are long range ordered beyond a few mole-
cules once the interaction reaches equilibration. 
5. The actual formation of an organization or ordered structure is inﬂuenced by 
environment, either the input of external energy or conﬁned geometrical 
boundaries. 
A self-assembly process can be static or dynamic depending on if there are 
external inﬂuences. In the absence of external inﬂuences, building blocks self-
assemble statically, driven by energy minimization to form static equilibrium struc-
tures. In the presence of outside inﬂuences, a dynamically self-assembling system 
may prevail that can adjust to its surrounding environment by residing on an 
energetic minimum which is caused by the inﬂux of energy in the system. It reduces 
entropy by absorbing energy from the environment. This gradient in entropy 
between the organism and the environment can be maintained only as long as energy 
is driven from the environment into the organism [4]. A familiar example about how 
environment inﬂuences the self-assembly process is the formation of snowﬂakes. 
Water molecules crystallize from liquid phase to solid phase when temperature is 
below 0°C. The ice crystals are of many different shapes, depending on temperature, 
pressure, air current, and humidity, as the examples shown in Fig. 10.1. Generally, 
colder temperatures produce snowﬂakes with sharper tips on the sides of the crystals 
and may lead to more branching of the snowﬂake arms (dendrites). Snowﬂakes that 
grow under warmer conditions grow more slowly, resulting in smoother, less 
intricate shapes. 
10.3 
Self-Assembly of Atoms and Molecules 
Self-assembly can take place at either atomic level or molecular level. There are 
typically two routes to realize atomic or molecular self-assembly: vapor phase or 
solution 
phase 
[6]. 
Most 
thin-ﬁlm 
deposition 
processes 
encountered 
in 
nanofabrication can be characterized as vapor phase self-assembly, such as physical 
or chemical vapor depositions (PVD or CVD), which allows atomic species to

[Page 352]
deposit on a surface where they attain mobility at certain temperature to assemble 
into crystal-like structures. They can also assemble through vapor phase in free space 
to form 3D nanostructures. The typical examples are how the carbon nanotubes and 
C60 are formed. The vapor phase self-assembly tends to form covalent bonds 
between atoms, which requires input of high energy from environment. 
352
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.1 Various shapes of snowﬂakes from crystallization of water molecules. (Reprinted from 
[5] with permission) 
The self-assembly processes that are of interests in nanostructure formation are 
via solution phase. Highly ordered nanostructures can be fabricated by the sponta-
neous arrangement of atoms, molecules, colloids, or molecular units in solution or 
liquid phase into ordered structures via local interactions (e.g., electrostatic, van der 
Waals, hydrophobic, etc.) between the molecular units. Solution-based self-assem-
bly methods offer numerous beneﬁts for fabricating nanostructures such as potential 
for tunability of nanostructure properties by modifying concentrations of constituent 
molecules and ease of controlling shape and size of nanostructures. These self-
assembled structures are easy to prepare at room temperature and form quickly 
and spontaneously from solutions of molecules. They are molecularly ordered and 
robust under many conditions of use. They are thermodynamically stable and tend to 
reject defects. They permit control of layer thickness to within approximately 
1 nm [7]. 
10.3.1 
Self-Assembled Monolayer 
Solution-phase self-assembly of molecules was discovered more than a hundred 
years ago with the creation of Langmuir-Blodgett (LB) ﬁlms [8]. While the LB ﬁlms 
are still widely used in biological applications, the most successful molecular self-
assembly in nanofabrication is the formation of self-assembled monolayer (SAM)

[Page 353]
that exhibited higher mechanical resistance and chemical stability than LB ﬁlms 
[9]. SAMs are ordered molecular assemblies formed by the adsorption of an active 
surfactant on a solid surface. The order in these two-dimensional systems is pro-
duced by a spontaneous chemical synthesis at the interface, as the system approaches 
equilibrium. SAMs are usually prepared by immersing a substrate in the solution 
containing a ligand that is reactive toward the surface or alternatively by exposing 
the substrate to the vapor of the reactive species. The most investigated SAMs are 
alkanethiolates CH3(CH2)nS- on Au(111) surface [10]. Alkanethiols absorb spon-
taneously on a gold surface from solution and form adsorbed alkanethiolates. 
Though the organosulfur compounds also absorb very strongly to silver, copper, 
platinum, mercury, iron, nanosized γ-Fe2O3 particles, colloidal gold particles, GaAs, 
and InP surfaces [9], the SAM on gold is of the best quality and mostly investigated 
and used. Figure 10.2 illustrates the SAM formation process. 
10.3
Self-Assembly of Atoms and Molecules
353
Fig. 10.2 Schematic of self-assembling monolayer on gold 
The ﬁrst step in creating the SAM is the absorption process where the sulfur atom 
chemisorbs to the Au(111). This is possible since sulfur compounds have a strong 
afﬁnity to transition metal surfaces, such as Au, due to their ability to form multiple 
bonds with the surface metal clusters. Sulfur atoms bonded to the gold surface bring 
the alkyl chains into close contact; these contacts freeze out conﬁgurational entropy 
and lead to an ordered structure. The kinetics that drive this ordering are related to 
chain disorder, the dipole-dipole and van der Waals forces of the chain-chain 
interaction, and the surface mobility of the chains. The kinetics is faster for longer 
alkane chains, probably due to the increased van der Waals interactions. Highly 
ordered SAMs of hexadecanethiolate on gold can be formed in a few minutes by 
immersing a gold substrate in a solution of hexadecanethiol in ethanol (ca. 2 mM). In 
microcontact printing using a soft stamp, as described in Chap. 6, the transfer of thiol 
molecules onto a gold surface to become SAM takes only a few seconds [10].

[Page 354]
354
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.3 Schematic of 
SAM enabled molecular 
recognition 
ligand 
receptor 
SAM 
gold film 
Si substrate 
The SAMs of thiolate compounds have many attractive features and offer several 
advantages over other SAM systems. They provide the highest structural order by 
densely packing long alkanethiolate chains on the surface and forming crystalline or 
quasicrystalline monolayers. They are easy to prepare, have density of defects low 
enough to be useful in many applications and good stability under ambient labora-
tory conditions, and, most importantly, are resistant to acid erosion. For example, 
silver surfaces with octadecanethiolate monolayers could be kept in ambient condi-
tions without tarnishing for many months, and copper surfaces coated with the same 
monolayer sustain nitric acid attack. Gold surface protected by a monolayer of 
hexadecanethiol (HDT) is 106 times slower in cyanide (gold etchant). Such a 
property makes the SAM ﬁlm an excellent ultrathin resist or passivation layer for 
pattern transfer with nanometer dimension. SAMs are also very useful in surface 
engineering, i.e., modiﬁcation of surface properties such as wetting, adhesion, 
lubrication, corrosion, nucleation, protein adsorption, and cell attachment. 
Once the gold surface is covered with organosulfur SAM which consists of long 
chain polymer molecules, other organic molecules can be added to it. So the surface 
becomes functionalized. The SAM surface can be functionalized in such way that it 
has receptor-terminated alkanethiolate groups which only recognize certain other 
molecules, as schematically illustrated in Fig. 10.3, forming a molecular recognition 
system [11]. Speciﬁc recognition systems can be deﬁned by different interfacial 
binding property of molecules. It is like a “lock and key” system. One receptor only 
recognizes a speciﬁc group of molecules [12], which is a very useful technique to 
construct sensors [13]. 
10.3.2 
Self-Assembled Supermolecules 
Not only the SAMs, the power of chemical synthesis also provides access to a 
potentially vast range of functionally and structurally diverse molecular building

[Page 355]
blocks, which can be linked through different types of relatively weak, non-covalent 
interactions (predominantly hydrogen bonds and metal-ligand interactions) to yield 
organized supramolecular architectures with tailor-made properties [14]. Figure 10.4 
shows how groups of molecules are linked by hydrogen bonds. There are three 
groups of molecules, each of which is linked by two hydrogen bonds. Within each 
group, two similar molecular structures are linked by four hydrogen bonds. The 
hydrogen bond is a special chemical bond which is stronger than van der Waals force 
but much weaker than covalent bonds (1/20 of covalent bond strength) or ionic 
bonds [15]. Because of the low energy required for hydrogen bonding to occur, 
molecules can be linked to assemble from simple molecular structures to supramo-
lecular structures at normal conditions, provided those molecules are mobile. Supra-
molecular engineering has enabled endless possibilities to create complex 
nanostructures from simple molecular building blocks [16], which has found appli-
cations in a variety of ﬁelds, such as in high-performance supercapacitors [17]. 
10.3
Self-Assembly of Atoms and Molecules
355
Hydrogen bonds 
Fig. 10.4 Supramolecular structure self-assembled by hydrogen bonds 
Although hydrogen bonding is able to form supramolecular crystals or supramo-
lecular compounds in solution, which relies on molecular mobility in solution phase 
and chemistry of intermolecular non-covalent bond, nanofabrication is more about 
constructing structures on a substrate surface. To construct supramolecular structure 
on a surface, the inﬂuences of the substrate atomic lattice and substrate electronic or 
chemical properties on non-covalent bonds also play critical roles [18]. The param-
eters crucial for this type of self-assembly are the surface mobility of molecules, their 
lateral interactions, and their coupling to the surface atomic lattice. Though there are 
more constrains in assembly of molecules on a solid surface than in a liquid solution, 
it provides the possibility to tune the surface property and to produce tailor-made 
molecular structures. One example is the construction of surface supported metal-
organic coordination networks, resulting from the interaction of organic linker 
molecules and transition metals at surfaces with the coordination behavior of 
benzene polycarboxylic acid species and iron (Fe) atoms on a copper Cu(100) 
surface [19]. It involves sequential deposition of polyfunctional organic molecules 
and Fe atoms on an atomically clean Cu(100) substrate. Another example is the 
construction of single-atom transistor, as schematically shown in Fig. 10.5 [20]. It is

[Page 356]
a supramolecular structure with a cobalt atom at the center of metal-organic coordi-
nation network. The cobalt atom becomes the quantum island which enables electron 
tunneling from source to drain electrodes in the transistor.

Fig. 10.5 Single-atom transistor constructed by self-assembly process 
10.3.3 
Evaporation Induced Self-Assembly (EISA) 
While the SAM process produces a uniform dense layer, the same solution-phase 
self-assembly can also create mesoporous layer with highly regular nanostructures 
via evaporation induced self-assembly (EISA) method [21]. The EISA process 
involves the natural and spontaneous ability of surfactants to organize solutes into 
different shapes due to interactions such as van der Waals and electrostatic forces, 
hydrogen bonding, and π-π interactions. In the formation of mesoporous thin ﬁlm by 
the EISA, as illustrated in Fig. 10.6 [22], an initial homogeneous solution consisting 
of surfactant (structure agent), soluble metal alkoxide or metal salt (inorganic 
precursor), and solvent such as alcohol or ethanol, water, and often acid (usually 
HCl) are mixed thoroughly. The solution is cast on a substrate by spray, spin, or dip 
coating. Evaporation of the volatile components in the liquid ﬁlm takes place at the 
interface between air and the ﬁlm. The repulsion force between molecular units due 
to the surfactants keeps them in equal distance. Final removal of organic surfactant 
by heating at calcination temperatures of 40–550 °C leaves a latticelike porous 
inorganic network on the substrate surface [23]. 
The mesoporous structures made by EISA have found a variety of applications 
including as nanoreactors, as adsorbents for removing volatile organic compounds, 
for removal of toxic metal ions, as organic dyes, and as bulky proteins. In addition to 
applications as catalysts and adsorbents, silica nanoparticles with ordered mesopores 
have attracted considerable attention as nanometer-sized containers for intracellular 
drug delivery; ordered mesoporous carbon materials have been investigated as 
metal-free catalysts for oxygen reduction reactions; mesoporous transition metal 
oxides such as titania and tin oxide have been used as electrodes in dye-sensitized 
solar cells, lithium-ion batteries, gas sensors, and supercapacitors [24].

[Page 357]
10.3
Self-Assembly of Atoms and Molecules
357
Fig. 10.6 The most representative steps of evaporation induced self-assembly (EISA). (Reprinted 
from [22] with gratitude to the authors and InTech) 
10.3.4 
Self-Assembly of Metal-Organic Frameworks (MOFs) 
Another family of organic-inorganic hybrid porous nanomaterials created by 
solution-phase self-assembly of molecules is the metal-organic framework (MOF), 
which represents a revolution in chemistry due to the immense chemical and 
structural possibilities this family of materials can offer [25]. MOFs, also called 
porous coordination polymers, are self-assembled structures composed of metal 
nodes (metal ions or clusters) and polydentate organic ligands (mainly carboxylates, 
imidazolates, or phosphonates). The synthetic process generally consists of mixing 
the molecular building blocks, i.e., precursors, in a solvent (commonly methanol, 
dimethylformamide, or water). Then the mixture is either left undisturbed at room 
temperature for a period of time when the self-assembly process takes place or 
heated up by conventional heating or through other energy sources, such as micro-
wave or ultrasound, at an appropriate temperature for a speciﬁc time. The incorpo-
ration of a modulator agent (i.e., additive) into the synthetic mixture can achieve a 
ﬁne control over the self-assembly process to ensure the physical properties such as 
crystallinity, particle size, morphology, porosity, defectivity, and surface chemistry 
of MOFs. The topologies, porosities, functionalities, and surface properties of MOFs 
can also be manipulated by playing with different metal nodes and organic linkers 
and by means of post-synthetic modiﬁcations [26]. The resulting ordered structural 
motifs, similar to cage-like structures with pore size of 2–50 nm, provide MOFs with 
a high internal surface area and porosity, which are the key features exploited in the 
applications as diverse as gas storage and separation, molecular sieving, energy 
storage and conversion, sensing, catalysis, and controlled delivery of active species 
(e.g., drugs or fertilizers) [27].

[Page 358]
358
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
10.4 
Self-Assembly of Colloidal Particles 
In addition to molecular self-assembly, another broad category of self-assembly is 
the formation of lattice-like structures by self-assembled colloidal particles or 
spheres. The self-assembled structures are often called colloidal crystals [28]. Col-
loidal self-assembly refers to self-assembly of particles or spheres with diameter 
from micrometers to nanometers in a liquid suspension. Particles in such a dimen-
sional range have properties neither of individual atoms nor of bulk solid-state 
materials. There are three conditions which have to be satisﬁed to realize self-
assembly of colloidal particles. 
1. The colloidal particles must be able to move freely. These particles are normally 
dispersed in a liquid to form a colloidal suspension. That is how the name of 
colloidal particles came. 
2. The particles must be sufﬁciently small so that their movement in a liquid is a 
Brownian motion. The Brownian motion enables the particles to interact sufﬁ-
ciently, which is the basis for self-assembly to happen. Normally the size of 
particles should be less than 5 μm. If the particles are too big, gravitation force 
may come to play, which will disrupt the Brownian motion of particles. 
3. The shape of colloidal particles in the self-assembly process. It inﬂuences (i) the 
geometry of their close-packing, (ii) the directionality of the interactions, (iii) the 
selectivity of the interactions, (iv) the connectivity between objects in their 
assembly, (v) the attraction between them, and (vi) the dynamic aspects of their 
assembly, especially when the interaction is mediated by a ﬂuid [29]. Generally, 
the particles should be spherically shaped with uniform diameter. For 
nonspherical particles, it is possible to control the outcome of self-assembled 
structures via tuning of intermolecular interactions through a meticulous design 
of particle shape and other environmental conditions [30]. 
Self-assembly of colloidal particles is a physical process, and there are several 
different forces which can promote the formation of colloidal crystals. The forces 
include capillary force or surface tension force which drive the particles to aggregate. 
Electrostatic force due to electric charge carried by particles and van der Waals force 
will also make the particles attract to each other. One of the perfect examples of 
colloidal self-assembly by capillary force and surface tension force is the formation 
of “coffee ring” [31]. When a drop of coffee liquid dries on a solid surface, it leaves a 
ringlike stain which consists of coffee particles. Similarly, micron sized polystyrene 
spheres in liquid suspension can form 2D ordered aggregation when the liquid layer 
is drying on a solid surface. The process is illustrated in Fig. 10.7. Initially they 
randomly scatter in the liquid layer. As the liquid evaporates, the polystyrene spheres 
are pushed to the edge of liquid drop whose boundary is “pinned” to the surface, 
which means that when the liquid evaporates, the liquid drop cannot shrink in 
circumference but instead ﬂattens out. That ﬂattening motion, driven by surface 
tension, pushes liquid ﬂowing to the edge and carrying anything suspended in it, 
such as the polystyrene spheres or coffee particles, to its edges, as illustrated in

[Page 359]
Fig. 10.7a. Meanwhile, the spheres or particles coming closer to each other get a 
further push by capillary force when liquid surface lowers below the diameter of 
particles, as illustrated in Fig. 10.7b. By the time the liquid fully evaporates, which 
may take hours, most of the particles have reached the edge and closely packed. 
Figure 10.8 shows the optical image of orderly packed polystyrene spheres assem-
bled from its initially random distribution. The ordering is due to surface tension of 
suspension liquid and capillary force between the particles, which pulls the particles 
together. 
10.4
Self-Assembly of Colloidal Particles
359
Fig. 10.7 Schematic of self-assembly process of “coffee ring”: (a) surface tension driven aggre-
gation, (b) capillary force driven aggregation 
Fig. 10.8 Orderly packed 
polystyrene spheres formed 
by surface tension and 
capillary force driven self-
assembly. (Reprinted from 
[32] with permission) 
The “coffee ring” type of self-assembly has been used to create colloidal crystals 
in large area. The basic principle is the same, coating colloidal particle suspension 
liquid onto a surface and letting it dry. The coating can be done by spinning, inkjet 
printing, dip coating, or electrospraying [33]. As aforementioned, the self-assembly 
of colloidal particles only happens when liquid surface drops below the diameter of 
particles; controlling the drying process is the key to achieve high quality self-
assembled colloidal crystals. In addition, the concentration of particles in the sus-
pension liquid is also important for self-assembly of 2D colloidal crystal. Too low 
the concentration will result in many defects in the assembled lattice. Too high the 
concentration will create locally multilayer pilling of particles. Figure 10.9 shows the

[Page 360]
relation between drying time and concentration of colloidal particles [34]. There is a 
boundary of dry time and solute density, only within which an ordered self-assembly 
can be achieved. The same process for 2D assembly of colloidal crystal can be 
extended to produce 3D colloidal crystal. It is simply a repeating of 2D assembly 
layer upon layer to realize 3D assembly. In addition to the drying induced self-
assembly, the assembly can also take place with the assistance of Marangoni ﬂow 
which is the phenomenon of liquid ﬂow from regions of low surface tension to those 
of high surface tension [35]. 
360
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.9 Proper drying 
time and particle 
concentration (solute 
density) to produce ordered 
self-assembly [34] 
Fig. 10.10 Illustration of defects in self-assembly of colloidal crystal: (a) microscopic defects 
(vacancies), (b) macroscopic defects (dislocations) 
Self-assembly of colloidal crystals is not a totally controllable process. There are 
inevitably defects in the assembled structures. The defects can be classiﬁed into two 
types, microscopic and macroscopic defects, as illustrated in Fig. 10.10. Microscopic 
defects are the missing individual particles in the assembled crystal lattice, called 
vacancies, as seen in Fig. 10.10a. Macroscopic defects are the cracks appearing in

[Page 361]
the bulk of crystal, called dislocation, as seen in Fig. 10.10b. Examples of such 
defects in self-assembled colloidal crystal are shown in Fig. 10.11 where Fig. 10.11a 
is a 2D colloidal crystal with vacancy and dislocation defects [36], and Fig. 10.11b is 
a defected 3D colloidal crystal [37]. It is a fact that there are always defects in self-
assembled colloidal crystals. A high quality self-assembled colloidal crystal may 
have low level of defects. One can only control the defect density to its minimum, 
but cannot completely eliminate them. 
10.5
Guided Self-Assembly
361
Fig. 10.11 (a) Defects in 2D colloidal crystal, (b) defects in 3D colloidal crystal 
10.5 
Guided Self-Assembly 
Pure 
self-assembly 
or 
bottom-up 
approach 
cannot 
generate 
many 
useful 
nanostructures, apart from forming latticelike periodical arrays, as shown in 
Fig. 10.11, which may only have practical applications as photonic crystals 
[37, 38] or in magnetic storage [39, 40]. As described in the previous sections, 
self-assembly is strongly inﬂuenced by external energy and environmental condi-
tions, as those snowﬂakes shown in Fig. 10.1. By manipulating these conditions and 
external energy, one can direct the assembly to develop toward a designed path. Or 
in other words, the self-assembly process can be guided. 
10.5.1 
Surface Topography 
The obvious guided self-assembly is to use a topographical template. Conventional 
top-down fabrication can provide a template with patterned structures. The fabri-
cated topographical structures create physical boundaries to prevent self-assembly 
from spreading all over the surface. Patterned surface topography can be quite 
effective to trap nanoparticles. Figure 10.12a illustrates schematically how

[Page 362]
nanoparticles are guided into pre-patterned areas when the substrate is immersed into 
a nanoparticle suspension. This is only a simplistic illustration. The real process of 
trapping the nanoparticles is much complicated. These nanoparticles are not phys-
ically falling into the traps, because the gravitation force is too weak for these 
nanosized particles, but driven by capillary force of the suspension liquid. It only 
happens when the suspension liquid drop dries up by evaporation, as illustrated in 
Fig. 10.7, and the surface topography pins the retreating liquid surface as it shrinks, 
bringing the nanoparticles into the trap [41]. Figure 10.12b shows the SEM image of 
self-assembled nanoparticles in a groove [42]. Once they aggregate in the trap, 
electrostatic force, if the suspension is ionic solution, or interparticle van der 
Waals force will further secure the assembly and their attachment to the substrate 
before the drying completed. The assembly process can be sped up by heat induced 
interfacial convective ﬂow [43], which reduces the assembly time to a few minutes 
as comparing to leaving the suspension liquid drying up at ambient condition, which 
can take hours. 
362
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
(a)
(b) 
Nanoparticles 
suspension 
Patterned 
substrate 
Fig. 10.12 Guided self-assembly of nanoparticles by surface topography: (a) schematic illustra-
tion; (b) SEM image of assembled nanoparticles in a patterned groove. (Reprinted from [42] with 
permission) 
Alternatively, nanoparticle suspension can also be coated onto a topographical 
substrate by dip coating, i.e., the substrate is dipped into the suspension and then 
slowly pulled out, leaving a thin layer of liquid containing nanoparticles on the 
surface, like the formation of Langmuir-Blodgett ﬁlm. In this way, uniform assembly 
of nanoparticles into patterned grooves can be achieved by balancing the pulling 
speed and evaporation rate of the thin liquid ﬁlm [44]. The assembled structures are 
closely related to the geometry of topographical structures. The geometry of grooves 
must be much larger than the diameter of colloidal particles [45]. If their dimensions 
are comparable, then the geometry of topographical structure made by top-down 
nanofabrication must match the integers of particle diameter [46]. The ﬁnal assem-
bled structures are very much dependent on the top-down fabricated topographical 
structures [47]. Not only 2D array of colloidal particles can be guided by top-down 
fabricated holes, but more layers can be assembled on top of the initial 2D array. For 
example, up to four layers of 500-nm-diameter polystyrene (PS) nanospheres were 
assembled on patterned photoresist holes by controlling the concentration of PS 
nanospheres in the aqueous solution [48].

[Page 363]
10.5
Guided Self-Assembly
363
Surface topography can also be created by bubbles whose ridges deﬁne the 
boundaries of surface pattern structure. Silver nanowires were dispersed in water 
with a surfactant and a thickening agent. By stirring the mixture to generate a large 
number of bubbles, the silver nanowires were lining up along the bubble ridges. In 
this way, a transparent conductive layer was produced [49]. The bubbles can be 
further regulated by top-down fabricated surface topography so that the lineup of 
nanoparticles through self-assembly along the bubble ridges forms predesigned 
patterns [50]. 
10.5.2 
Surface Energy 
In addition to topographical template, the substrate surface can be physically or 
chemically modiﬁed so that colloidal particles can be selectively assembled. This 
can be achieved by changing the surface energy, i.e., the surface hydrophobicity. In 
this way, nanoparticles in a colloidal suspension will only assemble onto the 
hydrophilic surface area, not stick to the hydrophobic surface area. 
Surface hydrophobicity and hydrophilicity, or another name is wettability, is a 
very important branch of surface science. The wettability of a surface is deﬁned by 
water contact angle. A surface is hydrophobic when its static water contact angle θ is 
>90°and is hydrophilic when θ is <90° [51]. There are many ways to modify the 
wettability of a solid surface. Physical modiﬁcations include making a surface rough 
or porous to increase its hydrophilicity. Chemical modiﬁcations are mainly adding 
chemically active molecules to increase its surface energy. For example, silicon 
surface is naturally hydrophilic because a thin native oxide forms over time in air, 
and it becomes hydrophobic by removing the native oxide in hydroﬂuoric acid (HF). 
To guide the self-assembled colloidal particles into a pattern, the key parameter is the 
contrast between hydrophobicity and hydrophilicity of adjacent surfaces, which 
determines the contrast of self-assembled patterns [52]. One way to achieve the 
high contrast is to introduce a patterned hydrophobic self-assembled alkane phos-
phate monolayer (SAM) on SiO2 area. The pattern created with the hydrophobic 
SAM has the water contact angle of 110°, while the hydrophilic SiO2 area has 
contact angle of only <6° [53]. There are other ways to chemically pattern a surface 
such as by microcontact printing (μCP) which has been discussed in length in 
Chap. 6 (Sect. 6.6.2) or UV illumination through a mask to induce modiﬁcation of 
hydrophobic surface into hydrophilic surface [54]. 
10.5.3 
Electrostatic and Magnetic Forces 
At molecular level, electrostatic self-assembly is based on the attraction of oppo-
sitely charged ions where the interactions can be between cationic and anionic 
molecules or between zwitterionic molecules which comprise positively and

[Page 364]
negatively charged functional groups that are covalently bonded with each other. 
Two oppositely charged particles suspended in a ﬂuid will attract to each other. In 
conjunction with other aforementioned forces, such interactions only result in 
formation of microscopic structures such as supermolecules [55], rather than mean-
ingful macroscopic patterns. 
364
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.13 Two-
dimensional pattern formed 
by self-assembled 
polystyrene spheres; the 
inset image shows the 
overall appearance of the 
pattern (~600 μm). 
(Reprinted from [61] with 
permission) 
On the other hand, charged particle in a suspension ﬂuid will attract to electrodes 
which is oppositely charged. This is a long range force compared to aforementioned 
molecular attraction. Substrate with uniform surface charge can be made by immers-
ing clean gold surfaces in ethanol solution of alkanethiols for 10 min. The surface 
can be either positively charged or negatively charged, depending on the type of thiol 
groups in the solution. Patterned surface charge can be made by μCP with different 
thiol compounds as the ink [56] or contact electriﬁcation [57]. Polystyrene sphere 
(PS), which is the mostly used particles for controlled self-assembly, can also be 
made to carry electric charge. Carboxyl-terminated PS can be made to carry negative 
charge, and amidine-terminated PS can be made to carry positive charge. 
For electric force assisted self-assembly, the oppositely charged nanoparticles are 
attracted to the charged substrate surface in the same way as electrophoretic depo-
sition. However, they may not assemble into orderly packed colloidal crystalline 
structure, because these particles repel each other due to the same type of electric 
charge they carry. With an externally controllable electrical ﬁeld, an orderly packing 
can be formed, for example, by raising the applied voltage [58]. The exact nature of 
the assembly is not clear but is believed due to some electrohydrodynamic [59]  or  
electroosmotic [60] effect near the electrode surface at high ﬁeld. The behavior of 
charged nanoparticles can also be altered by UV illumination of a transparent 
electrode surface, which also helps the formation of colloidal crystal structures. 
Figure 10.13 is a SEM image of assembled polystyrene spheres (PS) by UV assisted 
electrophoretic deposition. The assembly is not perfect as there are only localized 2D 
crystalline lattice (ordered) structures within the patterned area. Each of the crystal-
line domain is around 10–20 μm. However, the overall goal of selective assembly of 
PS onto pre-patterned area has been achieved, as seen from the inset image.

10.5
Guided Self-Assembly
365
Fig. 10.14 Schematic of magnetically assisted self-assembly of magnetic particles 
Similar to electrostatic force, the magnetic force is also long range force. Mag-
netic particles can be assembled onto a magnetized surface. Unlike electric ﬁeld, 
magnetic ﬁeld has practically no effect on most molecules or chemical processes. In 
addition, while materials may become polarized by electric ﬁeld, magnetic ﬁeld can 
permeate uninhibited through most mediums, particularly through liquids and gases. 
Magnetically assisted self-assembly mainly refers to assembly of superparamagnetic 
colloidal particles whose magnetization is linearly related to the applied magnetic 
ﬁeld, i.e., no hysteresis exhibits in this type of materials. Superparamagnetic colloi-
dal particles remain stably suspended in the absence of magnetic ﬁelds because they 
are unmagnetized and thus experience virtually no magnetic interactions with other 
particles. Particles made of ferromagnetic materials becomes superparamagnetic 
when their size is smaller than the superparamagnetic critical size, which is roughly 
10 nm in diameter [62]. Superparamagnetic colloidal particles are typically 100 nm 
to 10 μm in diameters, composed of superparamagnetic nanoparticles encapsulated 
or 
dispersed 
inside 
silica 
or 
various 
polymers, 
such 
as 
polystyrene 
or 
polyvinyldibenzene. Increasing the magnetic content of the particles by encapsulat-
ing more magnetic nanoparticles inside each polymer bead results in only an 
increased magnetic response without altering its superparamagnetic properties. 
Magnetic force as an external power source has been exploited for magnetically 
induced self-assembly. The energy transmitted by an external magnetic ﬁeld to 
magnetic nanoparticles leads to the changes of orientation, migration, and aggrega-
tion to form microscopically ordered colloidal crystals, which is much rapid and 
reversible [63], compared to the conventional surface tension and capillary force 
induced self-assembly introduced in Sect. 10.5. For the formation of macroscopic 
pattern by superparamagnetic colloidal particle assembly, combined actions of 
external and local magnetic ﬁelds are required. As illustrated in Fig. 10.14, the 
particles suspended in a liquid are ﬁrst magnetized by an external magnetic ﬁeld. 
These particles then become magnetic dipoles aligning to the external magnetic ﬁeld 
lines. In the meantime, they are also subject to the local magnetic force generated by 
patterned magnetic elements on the template, which either attracts or repels these 
magnetic dipoles. These magnetic particles are then selectively assembled into the 
template patterns or trapped by the local magnets. Since each magnetic element on 
the template can be individually magnetized, just as the data recording on a magnetic 
disk, the assembling of magnetic particles can be programmed to form nonperiodic 
patterns [64].

[Page 366]
366
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
10.5.4 
DNA Templates 
Deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) is a long chain of polymer made from repeating units 
called nucleotides. Biologically, DNA contains the genetic instructions used in the 
development and functioning of all known living organisms. DNA becomes an 
attractive building block in nanofabrication because of its unique physical and 
chemical properties. It was long ago discovered that symmetrical DNA patterns 
such as squares and stars can be constructed by repeatedly folding the long natural 
DNA single strand and ﬁxing it with the short strand like origami [65]. The principal 
mechanism relies on the ability of DNA strands to cross over from one double-strand 
DNA helix to connect to another adjacent helix. These “crossovers” can be 
programmed to occur at speciﬁc locations using sequence design and base pairing 
of single-strand DNA [66]. The so-called DNA origami can be used to construct the 
most complex DNA nanostructures, which subsequently serve as a 2D template or 
3D scaffolds to assemble functional nanomaterials or molecules, such as metallic 
nanoparticles and nanowires and carbon nanotubes. DNA-templated nanofabrication 
is a rapidly developing discipline that produces custom-designed nanomaterials 
through the use of DNA molecules as self-assembly guides [67]. DNA guided 
assembly of conductive and semiconductive nanoscale entities has been particularly 
useful in constructing nanoelectronic devices [68] and 3D ordered inorganic 
frameworks [69]. 
10.6 
Nanosphere Lithography 
Two of the most studied colloidal particles for self-assembly are polystyrene (PS) 
and silica nanospheres. Apart from the assembled colloidal crystals being used as 
photonic crystals [70], they also can serve as masks for the so-called nanosphere 
lithography [71]. As illustrated in Fig. 10.15, self-assembly of PS spheres with 
uniform diameter leaves gaps (Fig. 10.15a, b). Materials deposited by thermal 
evaporation or plasma sputtering over the spheres-covered surface can only reach 
the substrate through these gaps (Fig. 10.15c). After removing the polystyrene 
spheres in CH2Cl2 acid, similar to the liftoff process introduced in Chap. 8, the 
substrate surface is left with array of triangular dots (Fig. 10.15d). Assuming the 
sphere diameter is D, the dot size formed through a single layer PS sphere mask is 
0.233D, and the distance between dots is 0.577D. A double layer of spheres can 
further reduce the gap [72], hence the size of dots which are calculated to be 0.155D. 
Instead of deposition in normal direction, which only produces dots, deposition in 
glancing angles can form surprisingly complicated patterns in the so-called shadow 
sphere lithography [73]. In addition to making dot array by material deposition, 
reactive ion etching can produce hole array through the nanosphere mask [74]. How-
ever, both the dot/hole size and the period of dot/hole array are restricted by the 
available size of polystyrene spheres which are normally above 200 nm for reliable 
assembly [75]. The lack of ﬂexibility is the major drawback of this technique.

[Page 367]
10.6
Nanosphere Lithography
367
Fig. 10.15 Nanosphere 
lithography steps: (a) 
suspension liquid of PS 
spheres casting on a ﬂat 
substrate surface; (b) self-
assembly of PS spheres into 
2D hexagonal-close-packed 
array; (c) deposition of a 
functional material through 
the gaps; (d) liftoff of PS 
spheres [71] 
Another kind of nanosphere lithography utilizes self-assembled polystyrene or 
silica nanospheres on a substrate surface as a lens array. By shining UV light onto the 
transparent nanosphere lens array, light spots are formed under each nanosphere, 
and a regular array of holes can be created if the underlayer is photoresist [76]. Fig-
ure 10.16 illustrates the process of nanosphere optical lithography. With illumination 
wavelength of 400 nm and conventional photoresist AZ5214, holes with minimum 
diameter of about 250 nm were formed in the photoresist layer using silica sphere of 
~0.97 μm diameter, which was about 0.6 times of the exposure wavelength. In

[Page 368]
addition to normal exposure, tilted exposure produced various shapes of feature in 
photoresist, and exposure in thick resist layer resulted in 3D structures due to the 
Talbot effect in Z direction [77].

10.7 
Block Copolymer Lithography 
Block copolymers (BCPs) consist of chemically dissimilar polymer chains cova-
lently linked to form a single molecule [78]. Under certain conditions, the dissimilar 
blocks tend to segregate into different spatial domains, called microphase separation 
[79], similar to oil-water separation. On the other hand, the spatial separation is 
constrained by the chemical connectivity of the blocks. As a result of these compet-
ing effects, self-organized periodic structures are created on a nanometer scale. 
Formation of any particular microdomain structures depends on relative volume 
ratio between blocks and chain architecture as well as the persistence lengths of the 
respective blocks. In the simplest case of noncrystalline ﬂexible coil AB diblock 
copolymers, the composition of the AB diblock (i.e., the volume fraction of block A 
and B) controls the geometry of the microdomain structure. Table 10.1 gives some 
possible spatial microdomain structures due to different volume fractions of block A 
in the diblock copolymer. More complex spatial microdomain structures can be 
formed with triblock copolymers. The nanoscale spatial structures that can be 
formed from block copolymers are almost endless.

[Page 369]
10.7
Block Copolymer Lithography
369
Table 10.1 Possible spatial microdomain structures for diblock copolymer with different volume 
fractions of one of the blocks 
Reprinted from [80] with permission 
The relevance of BCP to nanofabrication is its ability to form nanoscale ordered 
pattern structures in a very simple and low cost manner. They are polymers same as 
photoresists. The regular array of microdomains is equivalent to the patterns made 
by photolithography; therefore, it is a different kind of lithography, i.e., block 
copolymer lithography. After nearly 30 years of development, BCPs are now able 
to create polymer patterns with sub-10 nm resolution [81]. 
10.7.1 
Directed Microphase Separation 
Block copolymers, if allowed to self-assemble alone, will form some sort of ordered 
structures due to the microphase separation from aforementioned competing effects. 
However, the microdomains are formed randomly and grow as a polygranular 
texture with different blocks and having sizes of several tens of nanometers, and 
the periodic ordering maintained only over distances of several tens of lattice 
constant (i.e., a typical grain size of only 1–2 μm). This short range ordering is of 
no interests to nanofabrication. To achieve long range ordered microdomain sepa-
ration, both internal and external inﬂuences on the formation process should be 
applied. The internal control includes the choice of molecular weight, composition, 
and chain architecture. Table 10.1 has demonstrated how the microdomain structures 
vary simply due to the content of one of the blocks. The external control can be many

[Page 370]
ways. The most commonly used external inﬂuence is thermal annealing. At high 
temperature, the mobility of block polymer chains is enhanced, which facilitate the 
interaction between different blocks. For example, a polystyrene/polybutadiene (PS/ 
PB) diblock layer became ordered spherical holes after annealing at 110°C for 
1–12 h [82]. Other external inﬂuences include mechanical ﬂow ﬁeld [83] and electric 
ﬁeld [84] or by the inﬂuence of surface interactions [85], chemically patterned 
surface [86], or temperature gradient to create a preferred orientation or alignment 
of microdomains [80]. 
370
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.17 Dislocation 
defects in BCP self-
assembly of line and space 
pattern 
Like all self-assembly processes, self-assembly of BCP will inevitably have 
defects. An example of defects is shown in Fig. 10.17 [87]. They are dislocation 
defects in a line-space microphase separation. One strategy to avoid defect formation 
in the course of self-assembly and to facilitate their removal is to direct the self-
assembly by external ﬁelds such as aforementioned electric ﬁelds or shear ﬂow, 
which are however only effective to some extent. As the probability of generating 
defects is proportional to the area the assembly takes place, an effective approach to 
having the defect level under control is to conﬁne the assembly in a small area 
deﬁned by chemical or topographical boundaries. These two techniques combine the 
advantages of traditional lithography to fabricate the chemical or topographical 
guiding pattern with the copolymer’s ability to spontaneously form dense structures 
within the boundaries [88]. 
Self-assembly of BCP conﬁned within the boundaries deﬁned by surface chem-
ical property is called chemoepitaxy, as illustrated in Fig. 10.18a, which involves the 
creation of dense chemical patterns on a neutral substrate to generate preferential 
wetting sites for one of the polymer ingredients in the BCP. Many methods can be 
used to selectively tune the surface free energy of a neutral surface, such as

[Page 371]
photolithography, 
electron-beam 
lithography, 
masked 
oxygen 
plasma 
functionalization, scanning probe lithography, and those introduced in Sect. 10.5.2. 
10.7
Block Copolymer Lithography
371
Fig. 10.18 Schematics of (a) chemoepitaxy and (b) graphoepitaxy for guided BCP assembly [90] 
Self-assembly of BCP conﬁned within the boundaries deﬁned by surface topog-
raphy is called graphoepitaxy, as illustrated in Fig. 10.18b, which involves conven-
tional top-down lithography to make low density patterns and having the assembly 
only in the zones divided by these patterns. An example of graphoepitaxy is shown 
in Fig. 10.19 [89]. Figure 10.19a is the line pattern by 193 nm photolithography. By 
coating PS-PMMA block copolymer over the patterned surface and annealing the 
BCP ﬁlm, self-assembly took place between the patterned lines and added two more 
lines in between. The original pattern density was tripled, as seen in Fig. 10.19b. 
10.7.2 
Directed Self-Assembly for IC Manufacturing 
The reason that self-assembly of BCP has been continuously studied for 30 years is 
its potential to be employed as a lithography technique for IC manufacturing. In IC 
manufacturing, interconnecting lines and contact holes are the two most important 
structures requiring high resolution lithography, which also happens to be the most 
common patterns produced by BCP self-assembly. Directed self-assembly (DSA) of 
BCP offers a simple and low-cost solution to extend the photolithography resolution 
beyond its diffraction limit. As demonstrated in Fig. 10.19, pattern density is 
increased with the same effect as that of multiple patterning of optical lithography 
(Chap. 2). In 2007, DSA was for the ﬁrst time written in the International Technol-
ogy Roadmap for Semiconductor (ITRS) as one of the alternative lithography 
techniques for IC manufacturing.

[Page 372]
372
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.19 Combination of (a) conventional photolithography and (b) BCP self-assembly to 
produce high density line and space pattern [89] 
Among the many BCPs studied, polystyrene-block-poly-poly (methyl methacry-
late) (PS-b-PMMA) is the most important BCP suitable for IC patterning, not only 
because it can generate both hole and line structures but also because the PMMA can 
be selectively removed after microphase separation which leaves the PS pattern as 
etch mask for subsequent pattern transfer [91]. Although there have been attempts to 
integrate BCP assembly to IC manufacturing on 300 mm wafer scale for 7 nm IC 
node [92], there are many practical issues remaining to be solved before the IC 
industry can safely use it. The issues include high level of defects, long annealing 
time, and thin assembled polymer layer [93]. 
Self-assembly of BCP is promoted by thermal annealing, which is a long process 
because the microphase separation involves the reorganization of polymer chains. 
The annealing process is normally in the range of a few hours to tens of hours. In the 
example of BCP self-assembly of line/space patterns shown in Fig. 10.19, the 
assembly took 2 days at 190 °C temperature in vacuum. This long process cannot 
be tolerated in IC manufacturing. To meet the throughput target of 120 wafers per 
hour, BCP assembly time is estimated to be no more than 6 min [94]. Various 
schemes have been developed to shorten the annealing time, including rapid 
annealing at elevated temperature [95] or solvent annealing [96]. Solvent annealing 
is based on a solvent vapor swelling the polymer and causing chains to move apart, 
thereby allowing fast separation. Currently, the cycle time is about 10 min but 
expected to get down to 1–2 min [97]. 
As the assembled PS-b-PMMA layer is very thin, only less than 10 nm in order to 
realize high resolution and rapid formation of self-assembled patterns, a hard 
masking layer is always used, which is in most cases SiO2 or Si3N4. There are two 
steps of pattern transfer: selective removal of one polymer component (PMMA) and 
etching of SiO2 or Si3N4 using the PS as patterned mask. The etching chemistry can 
be optimized to have high selectivity to keep the PS intact while only etching the 
PMMA with PMMA:PS etch selectivity >20 [98]. Once the PMMA is removed, the

[Page 373]
PS pattern becomes the mask for second etching of underlying hard mask which is 
also very thin (~10 nm) [99]. 
10.8
Self-Regulated Porous Alumina
373
The most critical issue which stops the DSA to be adopted in IC manufacturing is 
its defect level. To be relevant for industrial manufacturing, the total defect density 
should be below one defect per 1cm2 pattern area, which was very difﬁcult to 
achieve. Primary contributors to the total defectivity are dislocations and bridges, 
formed during the self-assembly process [100]. Shortening the annealing time and 
conﬁning the DSA in narrower boundary can all help to reduce the defectivity. For 
example, only 3x pattern density multiplication is allowed by chemoepitaxy or 
graphoepitaxy. There are also continued research efforts toward developing block 
polymers incorporating highly incompatible segments (termed “high χ”) [81]. Cur-
rently, DSA can make patterns correspond to the 3 nm or 5 nm IC technology node. 
Combined with high-NA EUV, DSA may help to push the lithography further down 
to 2 nm node, as the 2022 edition of International Roadmap for Devices and Systems 
(IRDS™) envisioned [101]. 
10.8 
Self-Regulated Porous Alumina 
Anodization is an electrochemical process whereby certain metals (aluminum, 
niobium, tantalum, titanium, tungsten, zirconium) can be oxidized electrochemi-
cally. During anodization, the metal workpiece is the anode in an electrolyte bath. 
The cathode is any electrical conductor that does not react with the electrolyte. Once 
connected to a power supply, electrons attracted to the anode allow ions at the metal 
surface to react with water molecules in the electrolyte to form an oxide layer on the 
metal, and hydrogen gas is released from the cathode side. Normally, the electrolyte 
is selected in which the oxide is insoluble so that a barrier oxide is formed, which can 
serve as protection coating from corrosion or an electrical insulation layer. If the 
electrolyte is a diluted acid, the oxide layer will be partially dissolved and the 
oxidation surface becomes porous. This is how porous alumina or anodized alumi-
num oxide (AAO) is produced. Porous alumina possesses many desirable engineer-
ing properties such as excellent hardness and corrosion and abrasion resistance. The 
most visible application in everyday life is the durable coloring of aluminum surface 
in decorative and architectural applications [102]. 
Porous alumina became relevant to nanofabrication due to its ability to produce 
highly ordered pores with pore diameter of 10–400 nm and pore density of 108 –1010 / 
cm2 . It is a self-regulated process, very much like the previously introduced self-
assembly processes. Formation sequence of ordered porous alumina is schematically 
illustrated in Fig. 10.20. When the current starts to pass through the aluminum 
anode, a barrier layer of nonporous alumina begins to form (I). During anodization 
of aluminum in an acidic electrolyte, there are two competing processes taking 
place—one is the oxidation to form alumina layer, and another is the dissolution 
of alumina in acid. If there is no dissolution of alumina, the oxidation process will 
eventually stop because the surface becomes insulating as the oxide layer becomes

[Page 374]
thicker. As this layer grows thicker, the electrical ﬁeld across the oxide layer 
increases due to the increase of resistance. The high electrical ﬁeld causes small 
fractures on the top of the oxide layer (II). Nanopores are formed initially from these 
fractures in the alumina surface, where acid penetrates and dissolves alumina and 
some cracks develop further into large pores (III). Because the starting points of 
alumina dissolution are random, the nanopores initially formed are also random. 
However, as the oxidation and acid dissolution progress, a balance between the 
competition of oxidation and dissolution is reached, and ordered hexagonal struc-
tures start to emerge (IV). The formation of porous surface also decreases the 
resistance, which produces a voltage decrease when working at constant current 
density. Finally, a constant voltage is maintained due to the balance of oxidation-
dissolution at the bottom of the pores. Figure 10.21a shows highly ordered hexag-
onal holes formed in alumina, and Fig. 10.21b is the cross-sectional view of the 
porous 
alumina, 
showing 
very 
dense 
and 
high 
aspect 
ratio 
honeycomb 
structures [103]. 
374
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.20 Sequence of porous alumina formation. (Reprinted from [104] with permission) 
Fig. 10.21 Hexagonal nanopore array formed by aluminum anodization: (a) top view; (b) cross-
sectional view. (Reprinted from [103] with permission)

[Page 375]
10.8
Self-Regulated Porous Alumina
375
Fig. 10.22 Two-step anodization of aluminum for the formation of regular pores: (a) initial 
aluminum surface; (b) ﬁrst anodization; (c) oxide removal; (d) second anodization. (Reprinted 
from [107] with permission) 
The formation process described in Fig. 10.20 is too slow. For example, the 
nanopore array shown in Fig. 10.21 was formed after 160-h anodization of pure 
aluminum in 0.3 M oxalic acid and 40 V voltage at 0 °C temperature. High voltage 
and high electrolyte temperature, which results in high anodization current density, 
will cause the formation of nanopores faster. The growth rate of porous alumina as 
high as 10 μm/min was reported at voltage of 195 V and current density of 150 mA/ 
cm2 [105]. However, the pore size also grows bigger at higher current density. The 
best way to speed up the nanopore formation process while maintaining small pore 
size is to anodize in two steps [106], as schematically shown Fig. 10.22 [107]: the 
ﬁrst step is to anodize the aluminum to generate disordered pores (Fig. 10.22a, b); 
then the porous top part is removed in acid which reveals the textured surface with 
arrays of almost hemispherical concaves (Fig. 10.22c); the regular surface texture is 
then used as the starting points for second step anodization which ﬁnally forms the 
regular pores (Fig. 10.22d) with much faster formation rate of 1–2 μm/min 
[108]. The two-step anodization can reduce the pore formation time to one-tenth 
of the one-step process. Many factors can affect the geometry of porous alumina 
structure such as pore diameter, pore spacing, and pore density, which include 
anodization potential, electrolyte type and concentration, stirring of electrolyte, 
anodization temperature, and time [109]. 
Since the nanopores are initiated from surface dents, as illustrated in Fig. 10.22c, 
the surface can be deliberately textured by a conventional patterning technique, for 
example, by nanoimprint. Different from self-ordered two-step anodic anodization 
which only generates circular-shaped pores with close-packed honeycomb (i.e., 
trigonal) arrangement, artiﬁcially nanoengineering Al foil surface by hard imprint 
lithography can guide anodic anodization to form pores with desired structural 
parameters. Nanopores will be formed with controlled regularity and density. The 
pore size and spacing can be further regulated by unequal anodization rates at 
different anodization voltages [110]. An example is shown in Fig. 10.23, where 
nanoimprint was used to pattern the aluminum surface and subsequent anodization 
created sparsely distributed pores instead of densely packed pores [111]. However, 
the anodization condition has to be tuned to match the lattice constant of surface 
pattern. Otherwise, no regular nanopores will be formed.

[Page 376]
376
10
Nanofabrication by Self-Assembly
Fig. 10.23 Anodized pores 
of designed spacing with the 
assistance of nanoimprint 
patterning 
Fig. 10.24 (a) Cross section of ordered nanopores in alumina; (b) packed nanoparticles in the 
nanopores by electrophoretic force. (Reprinted from [117] with permission) 
The most useful AAO pore for nanofabrication is to grow them in thin Al ﬁlms 
deposited on substrates of choice, instead of bulk aluminum foils [112]. Pore sizes 
from 10 nm to 200 nm with aspect ratio as high as 400:1 have been made by 
depositing aluminum ﬁlm on top of silicon and subsequent anodizing [113]. Once 
the silicon is used as substrate, nanostructures of other materials can be produced 
using the AAO nanopores as a template. For example, nanowires or nanorods can be 
produced by ﬁlling up the nanopores with metals [114–116]. The porous alumina 
templates have also been used to assemble nanoparticles, whereby nanoparticles are 
packed into the nanopores by electrophoretic force. Figure 10.24a shows regular 
nanopores created by two-step anodization of aluminum ﬁlm, and Fig. 10.24b shows 
nanoparticles packed into the nanopores by electrophoretic force [117]. Combining 
with other top-down nanofabrication techniques, more exotic nanostructures have

[Page 377]
been created [118]. One must be aware that porous alumina is an insulator, as 
illustrated in Fig. 10.22d. Each nanopore is surrounded by Al2O3. If the porous 
alumina is made on a conducting substrate surface, any metals deposited into the 
nanopores will not be in contact with the substrate surface, unless the barrier layer is 
removed prior to the metal deposition. The barrier layer can be dissolved in 5% 
phosphoric acid, albeit the acid dissolution also reduces the thickness of porous 
alumina layer and widens the pore size.

Chapter 11 
Applications of Nanofabrication 
11.1 
Introduction 
The previous chapters have introduced various nanofabrication technologies. In 
practical applications, any fabrication technology will not be used alone. A structure 
or a device may not be made by one fabrication technology but a combination of 
several technologies. On the other hand, the same goal can be reached by different 
ways, and there are many factors which determine which speciﬁc technology to be 
used to achieve the ﬁnal goal. For example, one particular technology may be 
superior to others in improving or advancing a speciﬁc aspect of performance in a 
device, or the technology is simpler than others and can lower the fabrication cost. 
There are also situations when fabrications have to be done using existing equipment 
when no other options are available or affordable. 
Any new application of a nanoscale structure or device is likely to be the result of 
not only a novel design but also an adoption of new fabrication technology or a 
clever combination of different existing fabrication technologies. The developments 
of application and fabrication have always been interdependent to each other and 
advancing each other. The constant demand of new applications is the market pull to 
advance fabrication technologies to make something better and cheaper. The con-
tinuous progress in research of new ways of making structures or devices is the 
technology push to inspire designers to come up with new applications. For exam-
ple, the progress of integrated circuit (IC) has been the result of continuous advance 
of optical lithography and associated pattern transfer technologies in the last 
30 years. The applications of nanotechnology in a variety of ﬁelds have been the 
driving force to the development of many new nanofabrication techniques in the last 
20 years. 
This chapter intends to introduce the applications most relevant to the advances in 
nanofabrication technologies, including integrated circuits, photonics, and nanotech-
nology; describes how different fabrication techniques or processes are used in each 
ﬁeld; and cross-references them to the previous chapters of this book. These
© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2024 
Z. Cui, Nanofabrication, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-62546-6_11
383

[Page 384]
applications have best manifested how modern nanofabrication technologies play an 
important role and how fabrication and application rely on each other as well as 
advancing each other. It should be noted that nanofabrication cannot stand alone. It 
evolves from microfabrication and is often being used in combination with 
microfabrication. The boundary between microfabrication and nanofabrication is 
blurry, and this chapter will focus on those applications which require mostly the 
fabrication of sub-100 nm features or structures.
384
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
11.2 
Integrated Circuits 
Gordon Moore, one of the founders of Intel, famously predicted in 1965 that the 
number of transistors on a silicon chips would double every 18 months, which late in 
1975 was modiﬁed to every 2 years [1]. The “Moore’s law” has been more or less 
right in nearly 60 years, although the rate of doubling in transistor density was 
slowed down from 24 months to 33 months as shown in Fig. 11.1 [2]. In recent 
years, Moore’s law no longer implies simple shrinking of transistor dimensions. In 
fact, transistor itself has evolved from traditional 2D planar structure in Fig. 11.2a to 
2.5D ﬁn-like structure, such as the FinFET shown in Fig. 11.2b, because the gate 
electrode simply cannot effectively control the ﬂow of electrons on one side of the 
semiconductor channel as its length shrinks narrower and narrower. A FinFET has
Fig. 11.1 The transistor 
density evolution of Intel 
processors by year [2] 
Fig. 11.2 (a) Planer FET (ﬁeld-effect transistor), (b) FinFET, (c) GAA FET [4]

[Page 385]
its gate surrounding three sides of the channel, resulting in greatly enhanced 
controllability in that even the channel becomes narrower. From 22 nm technology 
node onward, all transistors in an IC chip have been FinFETs. It is the FinFET which 
saved the Moore’s law [3]. Further shrinking to below 7 nm technology node, even 
FinFET can no longer function efﬁciently. The transistor goes to 3D with the gate 
around four sides of a channel, as shown in Fig. 11.2c which is the gate-all-around 
transistor (GAA FET).
11.2
Integrated Circuits
385
The aforementioned “technology node” which corresponds to the minimum 
feature size of transistors originally came from the “National Technology Roadmap 
for Semiconductors” (NTRS). The ﬁrst edition of NTRS was published in 1992, 
which was based on the semiconductor industrial consensus that the transistor would 
shrink in size so that more transistors could be integrated in accordance to the 
Moore’s law. In 1999, NTRS became ITRS (International Technology Roadmap 
for Semiconductors) with the inclusion of worldwide semiconductor players. In the 
2001 edition of ITRS, the technology node was redeﬁned by minimum half-pitch 
(hp) size instead of minimum feature size (see deﬁnition of half-pitch in Sect. 2.7 of 
Chap. 2). In 2016, ITRS evolved to become IRDS (International Roadmap for 
Devices and Systems) to reﬂect the wide spectrum of electronics not just ICs [5]. 
One should be aware that the technology node which explicitly indicates the 
minimum half-pitch size of planar transistors is no longer valid after the 22 nm node, 
because of the evolution of transistor architecture to FinFET and GAA FET 
(Fig. 11.2). For example, the 5 nm node of Samsung’s IC actually has a minimum 
metal pitch of 36 nm, corresponding to the 18 nm (half-pitch) technology node 
deﬁnition according to NTRS/ITRS and IRDS nomenclature. This means that the 
semiconductor industry is in the 18 nm generation in 2020 if using the correct 
deﬁnition of technology node. However, IC manufacturers still insist in calling 
this technology “the 5 nm node” even though these node numbers do not correspond 
to any meaningful and measurable quantity related to transistor density on the wafer. 
For this reason, it is apparent that Moore’s law will still be valid for the next 10 years 
because the real sizes of IC features are far bigger than the node deﬁnition [6]. 
To be able to cram more and more transistors into a single silicon chip is entirely 
the result of continuous advances in fabrication technologies. Making IC chips is an 
enormous piece of system engineering [7]. It involves more than a thousand process 
steps, including materials, design, processing, packaging, and test. The essence of IC 
manufacturing is the planar technology, i.e., an IC structure, no matter how compli-
cated, is built layer upon layer from a substrate material up. The processes can be 
grouped into four basic cycling stages before ﬁnal packaging: 
Lithography
Thin films
Doping
Etching
Packaging 
There are two major types of thin ﬁlm: conductive thin ﬁlm and insulating thin 
ﬁlms. Conductive thin ﬁlms form the circuit interconnection system, and insulating 
thin ﬁlms separate different circuit functions. The methods for thin ﬁlm deposition

[Page 386]
include thermal evaporation, ion sputtering, chemical vapor deposition, and mole-
cule beam epitaxy. Doping forms the semiconducting channel of a transistor, which 
is the most basic element of ICs. The techniques for doping include ion implantation 
and diffusion. Doping is only needed for making transistors. Once the transistors are 
made on the ﬁrst level of processes, called front end of line (FEOL), the rest is to 
make interconnects, called back end of line (BEOL). Interconnects are conductive 
lines and holes piled up in many layers above the transistors, as illustrated in 
Fig. 11.3 [8]. Between layers the metal lines are connected vertically via holes 
(Fig. 11.3a), and the fully functional circuits are realized by both local and global 
interconnects (Fig. 11.3b). 
386
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.3 Interconnects of metal lines connecting transistors at local and global levels. (Courtesy of 
Larry Zhao, Semiconductor Engineering [8]) 
For all the circuit features, the key technology which enables the making of 
smaller and smaller transistors is lithography. Lithography deﬁnes the photoresist 
patterns of IC layouts. Etching technology transfers the photoresist patterns into 
various thin ﬁlms deposited on a silicon substrate. Lithography and etching/deposi-
tion thread through the whole IC manufacturing process. A complicated IC chip 
normally needs to go through 20–30 of lithography and etching/deposition steps, 
which is the core of IC fabrications. 
As the shrinking of feature size is solely dependent on the advance of lithography 
technology, each edition of the semiconductor roadmap (NTRS/ITRS and IRDS) has 
a dedicated section to chart the progress of lithography for future generations of 
IC. For example, the latest roadmap (IRDS 2022 Edition) predicted the lithography 
options for IC manufacturing in the next 15 years as shown in Table 11.1 [9]. It 
should be noticed that there are two nomenclatures about the node in the table. The 
“Logic node” follows the tradition of semiconductor industry in planar transistor era, 
which continuously reduces in dimension from the 3 nm in 2022 to 0.5 nm in 2037.

[Page 387]
However, such dimension does not have any physical meaning of a transistor on an 
IC chip as 0.5 nm is barely the size of a silicon atom, which is nonsense according to 
some expert [10]. While the semiconductor industry and general public still insist on 
using such nomenclature to mark the generation of ICs, the roadmap gives another 
deﬁnition: the Node. For 3 nm IC, the meaningful node is G40M24 which represents 
gate pitch of 40 nm and metal pitch of 24 nm. So the 3 nm generation of ICs have the 
actual 12 nm half-pitch minimum features to be patterned by lithography.

According to the roadmap (Table 11.1), the current 5 nm generation of IC is using 
high NA (0.33) EUV with single patterning. From 3 nm onward, multiple EUV 
patterning will have to be used, or higher NA(0.55) of single patterning. As the 
feature size gets smaller and smaller, multiple patterning using 0.55NA EUV will 
become the only option, or EUV with shorter wavelength (half of current EUV 
source) has to be developed. So far, all P-type and N-type transistors in a CMOS are 
made side by side on the same level. Future generations of IC will have to stack the 
transistors on top of each other. Table 11.1 tells that from 2028 at 1.5 nm generation 
ICs, the increase of transistor density is realized by two layers of transistors, which is 
marked by the node G40M16T2. Going to the future, even four layer (T4) or six 
layer (T6) transistors will be in a chip in order to keep up with the pace of 
development [10]. On the lithography side, EUV is the primary option whether 
high NA or multiple patterning. There is also a low-cost option utilizing direct self-
assembly (DSA). With this option, features of smaller half-pitch are taken care of by 
DSA of block copolymers (Sect. 10.7 of Chap. 10), while features of large half-pitch 
are done by DUV multi-patterning or EUV lithography.

[Page 388]
388
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.4 (a) Intel’s “10 nm” node FinFET. (Courtesy of Intel); (b) IBM’s “2 nm” node GAA FET. 
(Courtesy of IBM) 
One should notice that the minimum half-pitch stops at 8 nm for future generation 
ICs. From the nanofabrication point of view, there are already foreseeable lithogra-
phy solutions for IC manufacturing in the next 15 years. The fabrication challenge is 
more on 3D architecture and integration. Looking at the transistor structures of 
Intel’s “10 nm” node FinFET [11] and IBM’s “2 nm” node GAA FET [12] shown 
in Fig. 11.4, the fabrication challenges are not only about lithography but also about 
high aspect ratio etching and highly conformable deposition in deep holes without 
voids. Fortunately, these technologies are already available, such as deep reactive 
ion etching (DRIE) and atomic layer deposition (ALD)/etching (ALE). Three-
dimensional architectures are not only pursued at transistor level but also at circuit 
level. A typical example is the stacked ﬂash memory circuit (NAND). The latest 
NAND technology is able to stack 232 layers of memory circuits in a single chip, 
which offers data input/output speed of 2.4 GB/s and storage density of 14.6 
Gb/mm2 [13]. 
While the technology nodes go down smaller and smaller, the semiconductor 
industry also started to take different approach since 2009, which was the “more than 
Moore” approach as illustrated in Fig. 11.5. The traditional shrinking of CMOS 
circuit feature size has continuously followed the Moore’s law and has been con-
sidered as “more Moore” approach in Fig. 11.5. The “more than Moore” approach is 
to integrate diverse functionalities with the traditional ICs. There two ways to realize 
more than Moore, which are the system on chip (SoC) and system in package (SiP). 
Conventional ICs are classiﬁed as CPU, DRAM/ﬂash, ASIC, digital, or analog 
circuits. There are also microelectromechanical systems (MEMS) and various 
silicon-based sensors. The concept of SoC is to combine these different functional 
circuits or sensors into a single chip, so that the chip itself becomes an integrated 
system. SoC allows much smaller and compact electronic systems with multiple 
functions. There are many challenges to realize SoC because of the different types of 
circuits, particularly the MEMS type of sensors [14]. They require different fabri-
cation processes and different design methodologies. To put them all together may

[Page 389]
introduce interferences or reduce manufacturing yield, and the manufacturing cost is 
high because of incompatibility of different fabrication technologies. System in 
package (SiP) takes a different approach from SoC. Instead of putting multifunctions 
into a single die, SiP put different dies with different functions into a single packaged 
chip. Conventional IC package only contains a single die (chip). SiP is a single chip 
consisting of multiple dies stacking on top of each other, or side by side sitting on a 
silicon interposer, instead of different chips on a printed circuit board (PCB) 
[15]. The interconnection between stacked dies can be realized in two ways: one is
