[Page 390]
by through silicon via (TSV) and the other is by wire bonding or ﬂip-chip bonding. 
Examples of the two approaches are shown in Fig. 11.6. To put multiple dies into a 
single package, each die has to be thin down from its original a few hundreds of 
micrometers to a few tens of micrometers. The TSV is achieved by deep silicon 
etching which has been introduced in Chap. 7.
390
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
The third approach is chiplets [16]. The idea behind chiplets is to break apart the 
system on a chip (SoC) into its composite functional blocks, or parts. Sub-elements 
of a complex-function chip could be made as chiplets (small chips), where these 
sub-elements may include separate computational processor or graphics unit, an AI 
accelerator, an I/O function, or a host of other chip functions. A system comprised of 
chiplets is kind of like an SoC on a module. Chiplet technology can help extend 
Moore’s law by allowing the creation of more complex and powerful SoCs without 
having to ﬁt all of the necessary components onto a single, monolithic chip. Chiplet-
based architectures offer several beneﬁts over traditional monolithic SoCs, including 
improved performance, reduced power consumption, and increased design ﬂexibil-
ity. The chiplets can be packaged in the so-called “CoWoS” (Chip-on-Wafer-on-
Substrate) scheme, which is a 2.5D wafer-level multi-chip packaging technology 
incorporating multiple dies, particularly processors and memories, side by side on a 
silicon interposer to achieve better interconnect density and performance [17]. 
In early 2016, an article published in Nature pointed out that by 2020 the 
transistor will only be 2–3 nm according to the Moore’s law, which is just ten 
atoms across. At that scale, electron behavior will be governed by quantum uncer-
tainties that will make transistors hopelessly unreliable. The article then declared that 
the Moore’s law could not go on forever [20]. Now we know that transistors do not 
have to be that small. Even in 15 years from now, the minimum feature size in a 
transistor will still be 8 nm, as the IRDS reveals (Table 11.1). The scientiﬁc and 
technology communities of semiconductors used to worry about the physical limit of 
CMOS integrated circuits. Alternatives such as single-electron transistors or 
non-FET not based on charge transportation phenomena were proposed and inves-
tigated [21]. With the transistors going to 3D, the physical limit is no longer a key 
issue. The Moore’s law also evolved to not a simple count of transistors, but 
comprehensive scaling of Power, Performance, Area, Cost (PPAC) by which the 
transistor size is only one of the parameters (area). The new IC scaling of Moore’s 
law sets the targets as follows: for every 2–3 years, >10% more operating frequency 
at scaled supply voltage (Performance); >20% less energy per switching at a given 
performance (Power); >30% less chip area footprint (Area); and < 30% more wafer 
cost or 15% less die cost for scaled die (Cost) [22]. 
To keep up with the new Moore’s law, simple reduction of transistor size is no 
longer enough. Figure 11.7 shows the evolution of MOSFET architecture since the 
FinFET [23]. From FinFET to GAA FET or nanosheet FET (NSFET), the control-
lability of single transistor is greatly enhanced. However, a CMOS is composed of 
both pFET and nFET. So far, they are placed side by side on the same level as the 
Forksheet FET in Fig. 11.7c. Further scaling has to reduce the channel width, hence 
the reduction of transistor performance. The next generation of ICs is to stack up the 
P-type and N-type transistors, which is the complementary FET (CFET) shown in

[Page 391]
Fig. 11.7d. CFET allows maximization of the effective channel width without 
sacriﬁcing the chip area, hence the enhancement of transistor’s drive current and 
working speed. From the fabrication point of view, two possible schemes are being 
explored for enabling the vertical stacking: monolithic and sequential. A monolithic 
CFET starts with the epitaxial growth of the bottom channel and followed by the 
deposition of an intermediate sacriﬁcial layer. Next comes the epitaxial growth of the 
top channel [24]. Although this seems the most straightforward way to build CFETs, 
the stacking approach results in the fabrication of very high aspect ratio vertical 
structures. The fabrication of second layer transistors requires high annealing tem-
perature (900 °C) for doping diffusion, which can harm the transistors underneath. 
To avoid degradation of the bottom tier structures, the overall thermal budget of the 
top tier device as well as the layer transfer processing needs to remain low, typically 
below 500 °C. The sequential approach relies on bonding a blanket pre-doped 
single-crystalline wafer onto the bottom processed wafer prior to pursuing top tier 
fabrication process [25].

Fig. 11.7 Architecture evolution of MOSFETs. (a) FinFET. (b) NSFET. (c) Forksheet FET with 
horizontally spaced nFET and pFET structures. (d) CFET with vertical nFET and pFET stacking 
[23] 
11.3 
Photonics 
Photonics is the science and technology of light [26]. It encompasses generating, 
guiding, manipulating, amplifying, and detecting light. Lasers, optical ﬁbers, cam-
eras and screens in mobile phones, lighting in cars and homes, computer screens, and 
TVs are just a few examples of photonics. Photonics plays an important role in 
driving innovation across an increasing number of ﬁelds from optical data commu-
nications to imaging, lighting and displays, manufacturing sector, life sciences, 
healthcare, security, and safety. It is behind many of the innovations which have 
transformed the way we live in recent years [27]. The global market for light-enabled 
systems is about $1.4 trillion today and is expected to reach nearly $2 trillion by

[Page 392]
2025, in which the photonics components represent about 9% of this total share, or 
roughly $120 billion [28]. 
392
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.8 Illustration of (a) 1D, (b) 2D, and (c) 3D photonic crystals 
One of the most basic photonic devices/structures/materials is photonic crystals 
which can be 1D, 2D, or 3D, as illustrated in Fig. 11.8. Photonic crystals are also 
called photonic bandgap materials. It is well known that light is refracted or reﬂected 
at the interface of two different mediums. If one material is periodically embedded in 
another material as shown in Fig. 11.8, and each of them has different refractive 
index, light traveling inside the material will experience refraction and reﬂection by 
the periodically distributed structures, resulting in a light wave of a particular energy 
band completely blocked from going through the material, or a bandgap is formed 
for a particular wavelength of light. It is very similar to electrons scattering in a 
semiconductor crystal where periodical arranged atoms form different energy bands 
to the free traveling electrons. Therefore, photonic crystal is an electromagnetic 
wave transmitting material which is periodically embedded with a material of 
another refractive index [29]. 
The concept of photonic crystal was ﬁrst proposed in 1987 by researchers in Bell 
Labs [30]. The ﬁrst photonic crystal was a piece of microwave transmission material 
with 1-mm-diameter periodical holes fabricated by mechanical drilling. The drilled 
material was able to block microwave transmission in any direction in the material. 
The practical applications of photonic crystals in near infrared (wavelength 
780–3000 nm) and visible light (wavelength 450–750 nm) emerged 10 years later. 
For optical wavelength, the periodical structures have to be in sub-micrometer 
dimension, which was only possible by the progress of fabrication technology at 
the time. 
It must be pointed out that making photonic crystals is not just for blocking 
electromagnetic wave transmission but to control the transmission in desirable 
fashion, for example, to guide optical wave to travel along designed routes. To 
enable this application, the photonic crystal must contain designed defects. The 
so-called defects are the region where there are no periodical structures, therefore 
no blocking of waves. One of such examples is shown in Fig. 11.9 where apart from 
the periodical holes, there is a path without the holes [31]. Optical wave is blocked

[Page 393]
r 
by the photonic bandgap and only allowed to travel along the hole-free path which 
forms the wave guide. Introducing defects into a photonic crystal is not straightfor-
ward, and a careful design of fabrication process is required. Many fabrication 
technologies introduced in previous chapters can be employed to make photonic 
crystals, including conventional top-down lithography and pattern transfer [32, 33], 
as well as bottom-up self-assembly of colloid particles and nanospheres [34] o
block copolymers [35, 36]. For example, the 3D photonic crystal waveguide shown 
in Fig. 11.9 was fabricated by a combination of UV interferometric (holographic) 
lithography and electron beam lithography in thick photoresist utilizing the depth 
control by electron energy [32]. 
11.3
Photonics
393
Fig. 11.9 Example of optical wave guide realized by 3D photonic crystal structure 
Fig. 11.10 Example of 2D and 3D metamaterials (split ring resonators) 
Another 
class 
of 
manmade 
materials, 
similar 
to 
photonic 
crystals, 
is 
metamaterials which are engineered so that a property not found in naturally 
occurring materials could be exploited. Metamaterials consist of 2D or 3D periodical 
metallic or dielectric structures which are of negative index of refraction to incident 
electromagnetic waves. Classic refraction of electromagnetic wave through a 
medium material is right-handed. The refracted wave through metamaterials is 
however left-handed. The change of refraction direction has the possibility of 
making an object invisible [37], which has been the main drive to study this type 
of materials. The classic conﬁguration of metamaterials is metallic ring type of

[Page 394]
structures on an insulating surface, called split ring resonators (SRRs), as shown in 
Fig. 11.10 [38]. The dimension of SRR structures depends on the electromagnetic 
wavelength. The shorter the wavelength, the smaller the rings should be. The SRRs 
shown in Fig. 11.10 were for terahertz and far infrared wavelength. The fabrication 
of SRR is quite straightforward, basically lithographic patterning and metal liftoff. 
For small SRR, e-beam lithography can be used for patterning [39]. 
394
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
In recent years, the concept of metamaterial has evolved to metasurfaces which 
are thin 2D metamaterial layers that allow or inhibit the propagation of electromag-
netic waves in desired directions. The main advantages of metasurfaces compared to 
the existing conventional technology are their low cost, low level of absorption in 
comparison with bulky metamaterials, and easy integration due to their thin proﬁle 
[40]. Metasurfaces manipulate electromagnetic waves by scattering from the nano-
structured antennas on the surface, which enable anomalous reﬂection or transmis-
sion, in contrast to conventional elements such as lenses. They are one of the most 
promising candidates for replacing bulk optical components and devices, for exam-
ple, the optical lens in virtual reality (VR) headsets. The propagation characteristics 
of a wave that reﬂects from a metasurface depend strongly on the physical shapes 
which are of sub-optical wavelength dimension and arrangement of these antennas 
[41]. The key issue for widespread application of metasurfaces is the large scale and 
high-throughput fabrication of metasurfaces. Although optical and e-beam lithogra-
phy have been employed to fabricate metasurfaces, other low-cost high-throughput 
technologies have also been explored such as roll-to-roll nanoimprint [42, 43] and 
nanosphere lithography [41]. 
Microoptical elements are another application domain where nanofabrication can 
play a key role. The microoptical elements include refractive and diffractive 
microlenses, 
light 
diffusion, 
guiding, 
coupling 
or 
antireﬂection, 
and 
anticounterfeiting surfaces. This type of applications requires surface relief struc-
tures on quartz, glass, or plastic ﬁlms with dimensions equivalent to optical wave-
length. The fabrication techniques involved include mostly lithography patterning 
and reactive ion etching. To make large-area surface relief structures on plastic ﬁlm, 
roll-to-roll 
nanoimprint 
is 
commonly 
used. 
As 
introduced 
in 
Chap. 
6, 
nanoimprinting lithography is particularly suitable for fabricating this type of struc-
tures which are dimensionally uniform, periodically distributed, and only requiring 
single layer patterning. 
One particular application which has drawn wide attention is the patterned 
sapphire substrate (PSS) for light emitting diode (LED). Conventional LEDs have 
the light emission layer sandwiched between a sapphire substrate and a transparent 
electrode. Because of the difference of refractive index for these layers, light emitted 
from the emission layer may undergo total reﬂection between the transparent 
electrode and sapphire substrate and be trapped inside, as schematically shown in 
Fig. 11.11a, resulting in low light extraction efﬁciency. If the sapphire substrate 
surface is not ﬂat but rugged, the total reﬂection could be interrupted, and light 
extraction efﬁciency may be improved, as shown in Fig. 11.11b. There are many 
techniques to make the sapphire substrate surface rugged, such as wet chemical 
etching [44, 45] and reactive ion etching [46].

[Page 395]

Fig. 11.11 (a) Conventional sapphire substrate causing light trapping. (b) Rugged sapphire 
substrate increasing light extraction. (c) Comparison of light output of LEDs with conventional 
sapphire substrate (CSS) and patterned sapphire substrate (PSS) with 3-μm-diameter bumps, 2-μm-
diameter bumps, and 450-nm-diameter bumps (NPSS) 
The ruggedness can be controlled by patterning the surface with regular patterns. 
Figure 11.11c compares the light output for conventional sapphire substrate (CSS) 
and patterned sapphire substrate (PSS) with different pattern structures [47]. It 
demonstrates that the PSS can indeed enhance the light output and the best extraction 
is achieved with nanoscale patterned sapphire substrate (NPSS) or nano-micro 
complex PSS [48]. Many patterning techniques have been employed to create the 
PSS, such as optical lithography [49], nanoimprint [50], and self-assembly [51]. The 
shapes of the patterned surface relief structures can also be varied as pillars, 
hemispherical and conical, etc. [52]. The improvements in light extraction efﬁciency 
are also varied from 20% to 60%. However, it is hard to draw any ﬁrm conclusion 
about which type or structural parameter of PSS is the best for LED applications 
[53]. PSS has been widely employed in manufacturing for LEDs, and making PSS 
has become an industry itself. Not only LEDs, organic light emitting diodes 
(OLEDs) and solar cells also started adopting the PSS concept to improve either 
light extraction in the case of OLEDs or light adsorption in the case of solar cells. 
11.4 
Nanotechnology 
Nanotechnology is a very broad technology ﬁeld. If nanotechnology can be sum-
marized in one sentence, that is, “Nanotechnology is the understanding and control 
of matter at dimensions between approximately 1 and 100 nanometers (nm), where 
unique phenomena enable novel applications not feasible when working with bulk 
materials or even with single atoms or molecules” (quote from the US National 
Nanotehnology Initiative [54]). The ﬁrst person who predicted nanotechnology was

[Page 396]
Dr. Richard Feynman, the Nobel Laureate in Physics. In his famous speech in 1959, 
he predicted “there is plenty of room at the bottom,” meaning that many things 
remained to be discovered at the most basic level of matters. By the 1980s, the 
development of scanning tunneling microscope (STM) and atomic force microscope 
(AFM) enabled humankind to see and touch the atomistic world and to manipulate 
atoms. The discovery of carbon nanotubes in 1991 opened the world of 
nanomaterials and demonstrated their amazing properties. Since 2000, marked by 
the publication of National Nanotechnology Initiative by the US government, 
nanotechnology became the focus of worldwide scientiﬁc and technology commu-
nity. By the end of 2023, the US government has invested more than 40 billion US 
dollars in the R&D of nanotechnology [55]. With rapid advances in material science 
and fabrication technologies, the particularity of nanoworld becomes more and more 
evident. Material structures below 100 nm, either naturally formed or manmade, 
have displayed completely different properties from their bulk form. These proper-
ties have resulted in many applications previously impossible with bulk materials or 
with microscale structures. The global nanotechnology market was valued at $57 
billion in 2022 and will reach $183 billion by 2028 [56]. Nanotechnology encom-
passes many areas of applications. While nanofabrication itself is one of the subsets 
of nanotechnology, there are other application ﬁelds including nanomaterials, 
nanoelectronics, nanophotonics, and nanobioelectronics, where nanofabrication 
has played a key role in their development as described in the following. 
396
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
11.4.1 
Nanomaterials 
Nanomaterials are referred to a class of materials whose entities are of 1–100 nm in 
physical dimension, including 0D nanomaterials such as a variety of nanoparticles 
and quantum dots, 1D nanomaterials such as carbon nanotubes and a variety of 
nanowires, and 2D nanomaterials such as graphene and a variety of 2D semicon-
ductor materials. They are mainly inorganic such as metals, semiconductors, dielec-
trics, and metal oxides. While majority of nanomaterials are made by physically 
breaking a bulk material or chemical synthesis, there are nanomaterials which can be 
made by nanofabrication including previously introduced top-down and bottom-up 
technologies. For examples, silicon nanowires can be made by lithographic pattern-
ing of silicon substrate and subsequent etching/oxidation processes, as schematically 
shown in Fig. 11.12, which is a top-down approach. These silicon nanowires have 
found wide applications in making ﬁeld-effect transistors as shown in Fig. 11.12d 
[57] and sensors [57, 58]. 
The nanowires can also be made by bottom-up approach. Using gold particles as 
catalysts (Fig. 11.13a), vertical silicon nanowires can be made by vapor-liquid-solid 
(VLS) process (Fig. 11.13b)  [59]. Similar approach can be employed to grow carbon 
nanotubes (CNTs) at precisely controlled locations, because CNTs only grow on 
catalysts. Nickel as the catalyst material can be patterned by electron beam lithog-
raphy into an array of dots. Vertically aligned CNTs are grown on each of the Ni dot

[Page 397]
by PECVD process at 700 °C substrate temperature at ~150 nm/min growth rate 
[60]. If the Ni dot is sufﬁciently small (~100 nm), single CNT can be grown on each 
dot [61]. Figure 11.13c shows a single CNT grown inside a gated cavity, which 
serves as a ﬁeld electron emitter [62]. Nanoparticles and nanowires can also be made 
in templates which in turn are made by nanofabrication, for example, the anodic 
aluminum 
oxide 
(AAO) 
templates 
[63]. 
Graphene 
is 
another 
important 
nanomaterial. Apart from large single layer graphene sheets which have found 
applications as transparent conductive ﬁlms, graphene with particular shapes is 
needed to open up its energy bandgap. For example, graphene ﬂakes with zigzag 
shape have been found with interesting spintronic properties [64]. Patterning of 
graphene can be done with different lithographic techniques, such as e-beam lithog-
raphy, nanoimprinting, or contact printing, in combination with etching processes 
[65]. For the particular zigzag shape, oxygen plasma etching of circular pattern was 
found to be able to evolve into the zigzag shape [66]. 
11.4
Nanotechnology
397
Fig. 11.12 Making silicon nanowires by top-down process. (a) Lithographic patterning of mask on 
SOI substrate. (b) Etching into silicon top layer. (c) Oxidation treatment of silicon nanowire. (d) 
Field-effect transistors made of a silicon nanowire 
Nanofabrication not only can create nanomaterials but also is a means to apply 
nanomaterials additively. A single entity of nanomaterial, though having superior 
property than its bulk form, is difﬁcult to be integrated into a device or a system such 
as the example shown in Fig. 11.13c. The most successful industrial scale

[Page 398]
applications of nanomaterials are actually using their ink forms or powder forms and 
printing them [67]. Although printed pattern structures using conventional printing 
tools are not of nanoscale, their properties are of those of nanomaterials. One of the 
areas where printing nanomaterials becomes a key fabrication technology is the 
printed electronics [68]. Metallic nanoparticles and nanowires, graphene and carbon 
nanotubes, quantum dots, etc. can all be made into ink/paste and printed by inkjet, 
screen, and gravure printers into pattern structures or made into ﬁlms by coating. 
Thin ﬁlm transistors, light emitting diodes, and solar cells, as well as sensors and 
electronic circuits, can be made by printing nanomaterials. The additive nature of 
printing fabrication allows the electronics to be made on a variety of substrate 
materials, especially ﬂexible substrates, which becomes the key technology behind 
the burgeoning of ﬂexible electronics in recent years [69].

11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.13 Making silicon nanowires by bottom-up process. (a) Deposition of gold catalysts on 
silicon substrate. (b) VLS growth of silicon nanowires. (c) Single CNT grown inside a gated silicon 
cavity as a ﬁeld electron emitter. (Reprinted from [62] with permission)

[Page 399]
11.4
Nanotechnology
399
11.4.2 
Nanoelectronics 
In terms of physical dimension, transistors in the state-of-the-art integrated circuits 
are already of nanoscale as introduced in 11.2 of this chapter. Although the combi-
nation of 3D scaling and heterogeneous packaging techniques will enable the 
integration of a higher density of devices in a well-deﬁned area/volume for the 
next two decades, as predicted by IRDS [22], the physical, topological, or techno-
logical limits will eventually be reached. The nanoelectronics in this section refers to 
the transistors not using traditional silicon-based material or working on ﬁeld-
induced charge-transport principle. In fact, researches on new paradigms of transis-
tors and electronics have never stopped in the last 30 years. 
The most investigated new paradigm is the devices based on quantum mechanical 
principles, notably utilizing quantum conﬁnement effect which is promoted when 
the size of a material is comparable to the de Broglie wavelength of electron wave 
function. Size-dependent quantum conﬁnement can also enable energy band engi-
neering. A typical example is the single-electron transistor. Electron movement in a 
single-electron transistor is no longer continuous but quantized by individual elec-
trons known as Coulomb blockade [70]. Normally such quantum conﬁnement effect 
only becomes prominent at extremely low temperature (~5 K). By making ultrasmall 
Coulomb blockade devices with a silicon island of sub-5 nm, it can operate at room 
temperature (300 K). Another very promising new paradigm device is the tunnel 
ﬁeld-effect transistor (TFET) [71]. A TFET works by modulating quantum tunneling 
through the source-drain barrier instead of modulating thermionic emission over the 
barrier as in traditional MOSFETs. In classical MOSFETs, the ability of controlling 
transistor current by gate voltage (Subthreshold Swing, SS) is limited to 60 mV/dec 
because of the Fermi-Dirac distribution which describes the probability of occupying 
an energy level by electrons. This hampers the reduction of the supply voltage. A 
considerable increase in power consumption can be expected when the device 
dimensions are scaled down further. For the same device dimension, TFET offers 
sharper turn-on devices compared to MOSFET, i.e., SS lower than 60 mV/dec, 
which brings the beneﬁts of lower power consumption and higher working 
speed [72]. 
The key to the success of TFETs is to have the channel material of high charge 
mobility and low energy bandgap, for which silicon is not a suitable material. Since 
the discovery of graphene in 2004, a large family of 2D semiconductor materials 
with one or few atomic layer thicknesses have been developed and offered new hope 
for high performance TFETs. Particularly, the energy bands of these 2D materials 
can be engineered through nanofabrication. For example, although graphene is a 
promising electronic material due to its extremely high mobility, the zero bandgap 
limits its application in transistor devices. It has been found that nonzero bandgap 
can form in graphene nanoribbons which are tailored by nanofabrication [73]. Nev-
ertheless, it should be noted that the state-of-the-art performance of the experimen-
tally realized 2D TFETs remains less encouraging compared to their theoretical 
progress. This is primarily attributed to the limitation in materials and fabrication

[Page 400]
technologies including synthesizing high-quality 2D materials with controllable 
layer thickness, synthesizing high-k dielectric with a high-quality dielectric/2D 
material interface, fabricating low-resistance contacts on 2D materials, and fabricat-
ing abrupt doping proﬁle at tunneling junction. As the result, both average SS and 
on-state current in fabricated 2D TFETs so far failed to meet the desired levels 
needed for ensuring CMOS compatible performance [74]. 
400
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.14 (a) Schematic of bow-tie antenna which consists of two metal thin ﬁlm triangles 
oppositely placed. (b) Example of sub-10 nm bow-tie structures made by FIB milling 
11.4.3 
Nanophotonics 
Different from the photonics introduced in Sect. 11.3 of this chapter, which concerns 
light-matter interaction at the structural dimensions equivalent to optical wavelength, 
nanophotonics studies the ﬂow of light at length scales far below the optical 
wavelength. Using metallic and dielectric nanostructures precisely sculpted into 
2D and 3D nanoarchitectures, light can be scattered, refracted, conﬁned, ﬁltered, 
and processed in fascinating new ways that are impossible to achieve with natural 
materials and in conventional geometries [75]. 
One of the areas in nanophotonics, where nanofabrication is particularly relevant, 
is the plasmonic bow-tie antenna. The bow-tie antenna is two oppositely placed 
metal triangle pieces as schematically shown in Fig. 11.14a [76]. When the bow-tie

[Page 401]
antenna is illuminated with a laser light polarized to gap direction, it excites the 
plasmon resonance at the apex between metal triangles. The plasmonic resonance 
has been introduced in 2.8 of Chap. 2. It is a phenomenon of free electrons in a thin 
metal sheet becoming excited by light, which generates high intensity electric ﬁelds. 
The resonance intensity can be from several hundred to several ten thousand times 
higher than that of the incident light. The narrower the bow-tie gap, the stronger the 
plasmon resonance. For example, the enhancement factor enabled by a 3 nm gap was 
one order of magnitude higher than that of a 50 nm gap for surface-enhanced infrared 
spectroscopy [77]. To fabricate such narrow metallic gaps, many techniques can be 
used, including direct e-beam lithography, focused ion beam sculpturing or 
nanoimprinting [73], as well as a variety of indirect methods as described in 9.5 of 
Chap. 9. Figure 11.14b shows an example of bow ties with gaps of 5, 7, and 9 nm 
made by a Ga+ based focused ion beam milling [78]. By FIB induced grain boundary 
breaking, the gap can be made as small as 1–2 nm [79]. In fact, the entire ﬁeld of 
nanophotonics is dependent on the advances in nanofabrication technologies. 
Nanofabrication is the “unsung hero” in enabling advances in nanophotonics [80]. 
11.4
Nanotechnology
401
11.4.4 
Nanobiotechnology 
Nanobiotechnology is a discipline in which tools from nanotechnology are devel-
oped and applied to study biological phenomena. One of the most important tools 
from nanotechnology is nanofabrication, which creates nanoscale structures in a 
variety of ways to help the progress of biotechnology. Although majority of biolog-
ical applications in nanobiotechnology are utilizing wide spectrum of nanomaterials 
[81], nanoscale devices are also important helpers which crucially relies on 
nanofabrication. One such category is nanoscale biosensors employing nano-
electromechanical systems (NEMS). In NEMS, the key nanoscale entity is a 
mechanically movable element or structure which is a free standing thin beam, 
either ﬁxed at both ends (bridge) as shown in Fig. 11.15a [82] or  ﬁxed at one end 
(cantilever) as shown in Fig. 11.15b [83]. They can be driven electrically into 
vibration mode; therefore, they are usually called resonators. The main application 
of nanoresonators is as high-sensitivity biosensors to detect minute amount of bio-
molecules. At high resonant frequency, a tiny amount of mass increase on the 
resonator can change its resonant frequency, and the change can be detected. It has 
been reported that mass change as small as 10-18 g (attogram) can be detected by a 
cantilever nanoresonator, which is equivalent to a single DNA molecule [84]. A 
bridge type nanoresonator achieved mass resolution of ~zg (10-21 g) [85]. In recent 
years, nanowires/nanotubes and two-dimensional (2D) atomic layers such as 
graphene/phosphorene have been utilized for making nanoresonators, which offer 
ultralow mass, ultrawide frequency tuning range, broad dynamic range, and ultralow 
power consumption [86]. 
Fluidic platforms are essential for biology because most biological phenomena 
are taking place in liquid phase. While the advanced fabrication technologies

[Page 402]
enabled sophisticated integrated circuit (IC), the same technologies were also 
employed to fabricate miniaturized ﬂuidic platforms, i.e., microﬂuidics, marked by 
the ﬁrst miniaturized gas chromatograph fabricated on a silicon wafer over 40 years 
ago [87]. It opened the era of “lab-on-a-chip” which involves manipulating small 
volumes of ﬂuids, typically in the microliter or nanoliter range, within microscale 
channels etched or molded on the chip [88]. Then nanoﬂuidics emerged following 
the footsteps of microﬂuidics because of the progress in nanofabrication [89]. 
402
11
Applications of Nanofabrication
Fig. 11.15 (a) Bridge-type nanoresonator and (b) cantilever-type nanoresonator as high-sensitivity 
biosensors 
The three types of structures crucial to nanoﬂuidics are nanopores, nanochannels, 
and nanopipets, which are all relying on state-of-the-art nanofabrication technology 
[90]. Many fabrication techniques introduced in previous chapters can be utilized for 
making nanoﬂuidic systems [91], typically including e-beam lithography, focused 
ion beam milling, and etching and deposition. Where a direct nanofabrication 
method cannot meet the need, indirect fabrication can be employed. An example 
has been shown in Fig. 9.17 in Chap. 9 on how sealed nanoscale ﬂuidic channels are 
made. The fabrication process is as follows: a trench is ﬁrst patterned in silicon by 
lithography and etching processes; the trench is then etched by hydroﬂuoric acid 
(HF); subsequent atomic layer deposition (ALD) process deposits Al2O3 inside the 
tunnel walls as well as the top opening because of its conformable deposition; the 
deposition continues until the top opening is completely sealed off and in the same 
time the tunnel is narrowed down to nanometer size because of the accumulation of 
Al2O3 layer inside; eventually sub-10 nm sealed nanoﬂuidic channels were made by 
balancing the etching and deposition precisely [92]. Similarly, e-beam or ion beam 
sculpting can reduce the pore size from initial tens of nanometers to ~1 nm, as the 
example shown by Fig. 9.18 in Chap. 9. These nanopores played an important role in 
DNA sequencing applications [93]. Pores or channels made by traditional patterning 
in conventional membrane materials at sub-10 nm scale are always rough in sur-
faces. In recent years, a class of nanomaterials such as nanotubes and 2D materials 
made of carbon, boron nitride, and MoS2 are being investigated in terms of their 
ionic and ﬂuid transport, because of their unique crystallographic nature and

[Page 403]
electronic properties. Atomically ﬂat angstrom-scale slits can be made by removing 
a single atomic plane from a bulk crystal [94], which provides new platforms for the 
development of nanoﬂuidics [95].
